# Universal Factory — Brainstorm Corpus

_Divergent idea swarm (5 lenses x every aspect). Ideas, not plans. No logistics/feasibility filtering — that is the next pass. Everything provisional._

**27 aspects (12 added by the expander) · 851 curated ideas**

## Cross-cutting themes

- **Delete the dead shell: the artifact is alive and shippable from t=0** — The single most recurrent reframe across the set. The same move appears in at least seven aspects: 'The Contract IS the Executable' (contracts), 'Reverse the wave — green ceiling drips DOWN' (verification), 'The Artifact Is Always Shippable / no build, only a living organism' (graceful degradation), 'The Factory Never Stops — living reconciliation loop' (re-runs), 'The factory is a continuous foundry, not a run' (orchestrator), 'The Cockpit Is the Factory — no launch step' (cockpit), and 'leaves ship as typed stubs the moment the contract freezes.' The shared thesis: never produce a dead intermediate. Freeze contracts as runnable proxies so the whole plan-tree boots, is clickable, integration-tested, and deployable the instant contracts freeze; 'building' degrades from a discrete event into progressively swapping live mocks for real code with zero integration risk. This deletes the riskiest moment in the original hypothesis (stitch-time integration) and the second-riskiest (the all-or-nothing green wave) simultaneously.
- **Invert the source of truth: the spec/graph/manifest is canonical, code is a derived cache** — A cluster that relocates the center of gravity off the code files. 'The graph IS the build — code files are a read-only projection' (graph layer), 'The Build Manifest is the real artifact; code is a cache' (reproducibility), 'shared surfaces are DERIVED at stitch time, not writable files' (contention), and 'Bidirectional contracts — reality writes back up the tree' (renegotiation) all say the same thing: workers mutate typed semantic nodes/intents and a deterministic renderer emits the TSX/SQL/migrations. Consequences that were 'features to build' in the original hypothesis (replay, audit, content-addressed caching, drift-proofing, three-way-mergeable conflicts, contention-free shared files) all collapse into trivial reads/projections of one canonical object. As-built reality literally cannot drift from the contract because it is generated from it.
- **Replace declared categories with emergent market prices** — A recurring epistemics move: stop having an author or a classifier DECLARE a property; let a market of competing models PRICE it. 'The Insurance Market — a separate underwriter quotes a premium in token budget, and the premium IS the risk signal' (risk), 'The Escalation Auction — failures bid for help in an open market' (failure recovery), and 'No tree at all — a market of bids replaces the planner' (plan generation). The shared insight: risk, difficulty, and decomposition are emergent prices that aggregate many models' fear, surfacing cross-cutting danger no human taxonomy would ever have a column for. Risk stops being a tag anyone writes and becomes a price discovered by cross-model bidding.
- **Falsification over confirmation: a node is done when attackers give up, not when its own tests pass** — Reframes the success oracle itself. 'Green is a lie — replace the green wave with a RED-team climb' (the green oracle) is the purest statement, and 'The factory must not run tests at all — proof replaces execution' (trust boundary) is its rigorous twin. Both reject the builder's self-confirming signal. The red-team climb swaps confirmation bias (tests confirm themselves) for Popperian falsification (it survived everyone trying to kill it), structurally subsuming mutation testing, adversarial authorship, differential oracles, and human spot-checks as 'attacks.' The proof variant goes further — never execute untrusted code at all, discharge correctness by proof — which also resolves the secret-exposure and sandbox trust problems.
- **Dissolve the human/machine boundary: the human is a typed worker node, not a gate** — 'Make the human a worker node, not a gatekeeper' (human gates) refuses to soften, move, or learn away the approval gate — it deletes the human/machine boundary entirely, marking taste/brand/legal leaves human-only and dispatching them off the same manifest that dispatches code leaves to Codex. This rhymes with 'skill mitosis' (workers are emergent, not a fixed roster) and 'the factory grows its own workers': the worker population — human and AI and freshly-minted skills alike — is a uniform, self-extending pool addressed by one dispatch mechanism. The human stops being a turnstile in front of the swarm and becomes a tool inside it, pulled in only where judgment is irreplaceable.
- **The factory is a standing organism, not a bounded run** — Several aspects abolish the premise that 'a build' has a start and end. 'The factory is a continuous foundry — a never-sleeping standing army that perpetually re-derives the Tower from intent' (orchestrator), 'The Factory Never Stops — a living reconciliation loop, Kubernetes-controller model' (re-runs), and 'The factory dreams — offline self-play between runs, learning from synthetic intents it invents while idle' (learning) together make building a permanent ambient state. Nudging intent reflows the affected subtree like a spreadsheet recalcing a cell; idle quota becomes a permanently rising quality floor. Build-time, sync-state, and 'is it done?' collapse into one always-warm system. Ghost workers, speculative builds, and content caching become emergent behaviors rather than designed features.
- **The whole run is a reversible event log, not a one-way process** — Because every leaf builds in an isolated worktree against a frozen contract, the run becomes durable and replayable. 'Time-Travel Steering — rewind a subtree to any checkpoint and fork' (steering) makes high-altitude decisions reversible: a wrong call becomes a cheap undo + A/B race rather than a catastrophe. 'Counterfactual / knockout replay — re-run one leaf with ONE variable changed and diff the alternate timeline' (forensics) turns the post-mortem from narration into experiment, proving causes by re-running them green. Both treat the run as a queryable git-of-everything; forensics also feeds the router (every autopsy is evidence about which model should own which node-type).
- **Generality lives in the meta-layer: build the factory that builds factories** — The generality and self-improvement aspects relocate domain-agnosticism up a level. 'The Factory That Builds Factories — hand it an unseen domain and it forges a bespoke sub-factory (node-types, contracts, worker skills, verification graph), runs it, forgets it' (generality), 'skill mitosis — the factory authors/evals/attaches a brand-new skill on the spot' (skills), and 'The Factory Forks Itself and Runs an Evolutionary Tournament Against Itself' (self-improvement) all make the menu infinite and self-extending instead of an enumerated list. True domain-agnosticism means never enumerating domains; true worker coverage means never authoring a fixed roster. The factory scales its own capabilities with demand rather than with human authoring weekends.
- **Bias maximal then subtract: build the biggest plausible thing, then ask what to cut** — 'Auto-Classify Backwards — Build the Biggest Plausible Thing, Then Ask What to Cut' (altitude) and 'Delete recursion entirely — exactly two altitudes, never N' (recursion termination) both push against timid, incremental, unbounded decomposition. The altitude move inverts the scope gate from a permission-to-grow into a permission-to-prune (cheaper to cut from a maximal draft than to grow from a minimal one). The two-altitude move kills runaway tree growth by fiat. Together they encode a strong prior: ambition is the default, restraint is the explicit edit.

### Deepest reframes

- The deepest meta-pattern: nearly every boldest reframe DELETES its aspect rather than improving it. 'One model, no failover' deletes the cost subsystem; 'no tree at all' deletes the planner; 'the contract IS the executable' deletes building; 'delete recursion entirely' deletes tree growth; 'shared files do not exist' deletes contention; 'the spec can never be wrong' deletes renegotiation; 'proof replaces execution' deletes the test runner; 'no build, only an organism' deletes the run. The set's signature move is dissolution-of-the-problem, not optimization-of-the-solution. The boldest answer to almost any subsystem is to argue the subsystem is self-inflicted.
- 'Derive, don't write' is the universal mechanism that powers the most reframes. Code is derived from the graph; shared surfaces (schema/migrations/manifest/barrels/configs) are derived at stitch time; the manifest is canonical and code is its projection; parent contracts are derived live views of children's reality; stubs are derived from frozen contracts. Whenever something is DERIVED rather than authored, an entire class of problems (drift, contention, merge conflicts, integration risk, reproducibility) becomes structurally impossible instead of merely managed.
- Integration risk is the villain the whole set is organized around killing — and it gets killed three independent ways. (1) Executable contracts make every seam always-running, so there is no stitch moment. (2) Top-down stubbing makes the whole integrated, so integration is the always-on baseline not a final big-bang. (3) Graph-as-source-of-truth makes drift impossible because code is generated. The redundancy is itself a finding: the original hypothesis's single riskiest moment is so important that three different aspects independently route around it.
- Markets, auctions, and prices recur as the preferred coordination primitive over planners, classifiers, and schedulers — bids replace the planner, premiums replace the risk tagger, escalation auctions replace failure routing, an evolutionary tournament replaces a fitness function. Emergent price discovery across many competing models is repeatedly judged bolder than any centrally-declared property.
- Adversarial / cross-model opposition is load-bearing everywhere, in tension with the 'one model, no failover' cost reframe. Red-team climbs, the dreaming factory's adversarial attacker, fork-and-tournament self-improvement, the insurance underwriter, and 'Claude judges / Codex builds' all require at least two adversaries. The set wants both a single model (to delete the cost machinery) AND model plurality (as the engine of trust).

### Tensions to resolve later

- One model vs. two models — the deepest contradiction in the set. 'One model, no failover — the two-economy is the bug' (cost) argues the entire cost/router/failover subsystem only exists because two providers were chosen, and collapsing to one model deletes it. But the original hypothesis's two-model router, AND 'per-token Mixture-of-Experts' (routing), AND red-team falsification, AND 'the factory dreams' (Codex adversarially attacks), AND fork-and-tournament self-improvement, AND 'Claude architects / Codex builds & cross-reviews' all DEPEND on model plurality for their adversarial trust. You cannot both delete the second model to kill cost machinery and keep it as the engine of falsification. Either trust comes from a single model's self-proof (the 'proof replaces execution' camp) or from cross-model opposition — but not both cheaply.
- Top-down living shell vs. bottom-up bid market — two boldest reframes that fight at the root. 'Reverse the wave / green ceiling drips DOWN' and 'the contract IS the executable' REQUIRE a top-down frozen contract tree (you stub the whole tree top-down, then fill leaves). But 'No tree at all — a market of bids replaces the planner' deletes the very tree those reframes stub. A bid market has no pre-frozen top-down skeleton to drip green down through. The executable-contract organism and the planner-free bid market cannot both be the architecture.
- Frozen contracts vs. bidirectional contracts that reality rewrites. The executable-contract and time-travel-steering reframes lean on contracts being FROZEN (worktrees build against a frozen spec; you can replay because the contract didn't move). But 'Bidirectional contracts — reality writes back up the tree, the contract is a live view of children's as-built reality, the spec can never be wrong by construction' makes the contract a moving target authored from below. If the parent contract is continuously rewritten by child reality, 'build against a frozen contract' and 'replay against the frozen spec' lose their anchor. Frozen-prophecy and live-view-of-reality are opposite ontologies of the same object.
- Proof-not-execution vs. red-team-climb vs. the always-running executable organism — three incompatible definitions of 'done/trusted.' 'The factory must not run tests at all — proof replaces execution' forbids running code. 'Green is a lie — a node is done only when breaker-agents run out of ATTACKS' requires running code adversarially at scale. And 'the contract IS the executable / the whole app boots and is clickable on day one' requires running the (mocked) system continuously. Static proof, dynamic adversarial execution, and continuous live execution are three different trust regimes; a single factory must pick one as primary.
- Standing-organism continuous foundry vs. reversible replayable event-log. 'The factory never stops / continuous reconciliation loop / Kubernetes-controller' wants a forward-only living convergence toward current intent (like a controller reconciling to desired state). 'Time-travel steering / the run is a durable replayable event log you scrub backward and fork' wants the run preserved as discrete, rewindable history. A reconciler that perpetually overwrites toward the latest intent is in tension with an immutable event log you can rewind into — continuous convergence tends to erase the very checkpoints time-travel needs to fork from.
- Bias-maximal scope vs. the standing-army cost reality. 'Build the Biggest Plausible Thing, then ask what to cut' and 'bias BIGGER' spend swarm compute on a maximal draft before pruning, and 'the factory dreams' burns idle quota on synthetic self-play. These sit against the cost-collapse instinct ('the two-economy is the bug') and the whole budget/quota aspect. Maximalism-then-subtract and dream-while-idle are expensive priors; a system anxious about wallets and quota arbitrage would bias minimal-then-grow. The set wants both aggressive speculative spend and ruthless cost deletion.
- Human as dispatched worker-node vs. human as the irreplaceable steering authority. 'Make the human a worker node, not a gatekeeper' dispatches the human on-demand off the manifest like any other tool — the swarm pulls them in. But time-travel steering, the scope-preview gate, and mid-run interruptibility cast the human as the one who scrubs the timeline, forks subtrees, and approves spend — i.e., as the orchestrator's master, not its dispatched worker. Being dispatched BY the manifest and being the authority who STEERS the manifest are opposite positions in the control hierarchy.

## Ideas by aspect

### Plan-tree generation & recursion

_Current assumption: idea -> ~5 sections -> ~4 bullets -> recurse until a leaf is "Codex-ready"._

**Boldest reframe:** No tree at all — a market of bids replaces the planner

- **A written "Codex-ready" rubric, not a vibe** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the fuzzy "recurse until Codex-ready" stop condition with an explicit, checkable definition-of-leaf: a node is a leaf only if it has a named target file/path, a typed signature or schema delta, a one-sentence behavior, and at least one assertable acceptance check. If any field is blank, the node MUST recurse. Strengthen it further to acceptance-boundary closure: if two valid-but-incompatible implementations could both pass the node's contract, it is NOT a leaf yet and must split. **Why better:** "Codex-ready" is currently a subjective judgment the planner makes inconsistently — the single biggest source of under-decomposed leaves that one-shot poorly. A concrete checklist makes the stop condition deterministic, auditable, and machine-checkable, the same fields become the leaf's contract for free, and tying leaf-ness to deterministic satisfiability (not prose clarity) kills a whole class of stitch-time integration failures where divergent-but-correct answers don't compose.
- **Stop on build-cost budget, not on a fixed depth** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop the rigid "5 sections → 4 bullets" arithmetic and recurse based on an estimated build-cost budget per node (rough token/LOC estimate). A node splits if its estimate exceeds a one-shot ceiling; it stays a leaf if it fits comfortably under it. **Why better:** Fixed fan-out forces shallow features to be padded and deep features to be truncated. A budget-driven split adapts the tree shape to the actual work, so trivial features get 3 nodes and gnarly ones get 30 — matching where one-shot reliability actually lives.
- **Sibling de-duplication pass before freezing a level** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After generating a level's children, run one consolidation pass that detects overlapping or near-duplicate siblings (e.g. two bullets both touching the same util) and merges them, or hoists the shared piece into a new shared-leaf the others depend on. **Why better:** Naive top-down recursion repeatedly re-invents the same helper across branches, producing collisions at stitch time. Catching duplication at planning time — before contracts freeze — turns silent merge conflicts into one clean shared contract, the cheapest possible place to fix it.
- **A leaf can be 'too small' — fuse trivial siblings before the swarm spends** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Add a downward pressure to balance the existing upward split: after decomposition, run a fusion pass that MERGES sibling leaves whose combined size is still one-shottable and whose contracts are tightly coupled, so the swarm dispatches one worker instead of three that mostly pass types back and forth. **Why better:** The current model only ever splits and never asks if it split too far. Over-fine leaves multiply orchestration overhead, worktree/process cost, and stitch edges — three workers coordinating a shared type is slower and flakier than one worker writing all three. A fusion pass makes leaf granularity a two-sided optimization toward the one-shot sweet spot instead of a one-way ratchet toward fragmentation.
- **Re-decompose on first build failure (adaptive depth)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat a leaf that a worker fails to one-shot as a mis-classified non-leaf: automatically recurse it one more level and re-dispatch the children, rather than retrying the same too-big prompt. Cap the auto-splits to avoid runaway. **Why better:** The planner can't perfectly predict one-shot-ability up front. Letting a build failure feed back as a split signal makes the tree self-correcting — the cheap fix for "this leaf was secretly two leaves" instead of burning retries on an oversized prompt.
- **Two-pass plan: cheap skeleton compile, then typed link/elaborate pass** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Split planning into a compiler-style front-end and back-end. Pass 1 (Claude, cheap, whole tree): emit only node titles + one-line intents + edges — a 'plan AST' with no contracts, shown at the scope-preview gate. Pass 2 walks the AST and elaborates each node into a frozen contract, resolving cross-node references like a linker resolving symbols. Enrichment only ever runs on the approved structure. **Why better:** Single-pass recursion interleaves expensive contract-freezing with structural decomposition, so a wrong top-level split is discovered only after spending tokens detailing doomed subtrees, and the user can't see the shape cheaply. Separating 'what's the structure' from 'what's each contract' makes the scope gate near-free and isolates re-planning to one pass.
- **Pre-viz with gray-box nodes: storyboard the whole tree before any 'final' contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow the VFX previs/animatic stage: first generate the ENTIRE tree at low fidelity using cheap placeholder gray-box nodes (fake contracts, stubbed signatures, mock tests) so the full end-to-end shape and the seams are visible and walkable as one animatic. Only after the user approves the animatic at the scope gate do nodes get promoted from gray-box to frozen-contract. **Why better:** Freezing contracts top-down during the first recursion commits expensive, hard-to-unwind decisions before anyone has seen the whole shape — like locking VFX shots before the animatic. A gray-box previs pass lets the user catch a structural mistake when it's a one-line placeholder edit, not after contracts and failing tests are frozen across the tree.
- **Plan-tree as a typed grammar with a validating linter** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Define plan nodes as a strict typed grammar — node kinds (App, Floor, Feature, Component, Migration, Asset) each with required fields and legal child-kinds, exactly like Bazel rules in a BUILD file. A linter runs on every emitted subtree and rejects illegal shapes (a Migration can't be a child of a UI Component; an App must have ≥1 schema node). The router and skill-attachment read straight off node kind. **Why better:** Free-form 'idea → sections → bullets' produces inconsistent, untyped nodes the orchestrator must re-interpret to route. A typed grammar makes the tree machine-checkable, lets skills/workers attach deterministically by kind, and catches malformed plans before the swarm spends a cent — the leverage typed BUILD files give Bazel over shell scripts.
- **Two adversarial framings, then reconcile (capability-view vs data-flow-view)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Generate the plan-tree TWICE from different framings — Claude decomposes by user-facing capability, a second pass decomposes by data/contract flow — then diff them. Nodes BOTH trees agree on are high-confidence; nodes only one found are flagged as either missing work or over-engineering and resolved before freezing. **Why better:** A single decomposition has a single blind spot — the capability view misses cross-cutting data concerns, the data view misses UX seams. The current single 5→4 pass bakes one perspective's omissions in as ground truth. A two-framing diff surfaces exactly the dropped/ghost nodes that otherwise only appear as bugs at stitch-time, at the cost of one extra cheap planning pass.
- **Intent Holography (every leaf carries the whole)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every node is generated holding a compressed projection of the ENTIRE intent, not just its local slice. A leaf building one button knows the product's soul, the sibling it must harmonize with, the global invariants. Decomposition isn't cutting the intent into disjoint pieces — it's projecting the whole onto each fragment so any leaf could reconstruct its neighbors' purpose. **Why better:** Naive recursion produces context-starved leaves that drift — workers one-shot a node having forgotten why it exists, then stitch reveals seams. Holographic nodes mean every isolated worker builds something globally coherent on the first try, so the green wave climbs without integration surprises.
- **Live co-authored tree you prune like a bonsai** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The plan-tree isn't generated-then-approved at one scope-gate — it grows IN the cockpit in real time and the human (or a steering agent) can grab any node and prune it, graft a branch, drag scope up or down, or pin 'never touch this' — and the recursion instantly re-flows downstream around the edit. Decomposition becomes a duet, not a batch artifact you accept or reject. **Why better:** A single up-front scope-preview gate is binary — accept a tree you barely understand or reject and re-roll the whole thing. Continuous prunable growth lets intent get sharpened exactly where it matters with surgical edits, matching the cockpit's clarity-first ethos instead of a take-it-or-leave-it spend gate.
- **The Self-Rewriting Tree (arity = node entropy, no fixed fan-out)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the 5-sections/4-bullets constants entirely. The architect estimates each node's intrinsic coupling/uncertainty and emits as many children as it deserves — 1 child or 40, 2 levels deep or 9 — and a 'balanced-looking' tree is treated as a SMELL, not a goal. The whole tree breathes and re-balances mid-run: a leaf that turns out too big detonates into a subtree, a section that collapses to triviality is absorbed back into its parent. **Why better:** Fixed 5×4 fan-out is an arbitrary cage that mis-sizes almost every real intent — a CRUD form and a distributed scheduler get the same shape. Self-shaping arity matches structure to actual difficulty, so leaves are uniformly one-shottable, no worker is starved or drowned, and leaf-count tracks the real difficulty surface instead of an aesthetic.
- **Explicit dependency edges between leaves (the tree is really a DAG)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Allow the plan to carry sideways 'depends-on' edges (leaf A needs the type B exports) in addition to the decomposition hierarchy. Cross-cutting concerns (a shared type, the auth gate, a DB table, a design token) become first-class nodes that MULTIPLE parents point to, instead of being duplicated under each branch or jammed into a fake 'common' parent. Recursion still happens, but along edges, and a node can have several parents; build order and the stitch wave follow real dependencies. **Why better:** A pure tree pretends siblings are independent and silently duplicates shared concerns — exactly where mechanical stitch breaks (two leaves independently define the same type three ways). A DAG makes shared contracts singular and authoritative, lets the swarm topologically order work, and the green wave climbs real seams instead of invented hierarchy. This is also what graphify already wants reality to look like.
- **Adversarial co-decomposition (two models race to break the tree)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't trust a single decomposer. Claude proposes a plan-tree; Codex simultaneously proposes a rival decomposition AND attacks Claude's — hunting hidden coupling, missing nodes, fake leaves. The final tree is the negotiated survivor of the fight, with every contested seam flagged as a risk-hotspot the build phase watches closely. **Why better:** A single-pass recursion bakes in one mind's blind spots, and bad cuts only surface during the expensive parallel build. Forging the tree in an adversarial crucible (the forge-council pattern applied to STRUCTURE, not answers) catches the catastrophic mis-cut while it's still free to fix.
- **Seed recursion from a library of canonical decomposition templates** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before free-form recursion, match the node's type ('a new floor', 'a CRUD feature', 'an art campaign', 'a research report') against a small library of known-good decomposition templates and start from that skeleton, then let recursion adapt/specialize it. Novel intents fall back to fresh generation AND get saved as new templates. **Why better:** Decomposing a Tower 'floor' from scratch every time wastes the architect's best tokens on solved structure and produces inconsistent shapes. A template seed gives a battle-tested starting structure (route, server data, client UI, character panel, tests), so recursion sharpens a good tree instead of inventing one — and the library hardens every run.
- **The tree remembers every tree it ever grew (decomposition as retrieval + graft)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Decomposition becomes retrieval, not generation. graphify stores every plan-tree ever built across every run; a new intent first finds its nearest neighbors and GRAFTS proven subtrees — 'auth flow', 'Stripe checkout', 'CSV import floor' — wholesale, only generating novel structure for the genuinely new parts. The factory's 100th app decomposes in seconds because it has decomposed 99 things like it. **Why better:** Recursing from scratch re-derives the same auth/payments/CRUD skeletons forever and pays full token cost each run. A self-improving decomposition memory makes the factory get faster AND structurally better the more it's used — the tree compounds instead of resetting.
- **Content-addressed plan nodes (Bazel/Nx remote cache for thoughts)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hash every plan node by its (intent + frozen contract + child contracts), the way Bazel keys build artifacts by input hash. Before expanding or building any node, look it up in a global plan-cache; if an identical node was ever planned or built before — this run or last week, this user or another — reuse the subtree wholesale instead of re-recursing. The plan-tree becomes a cache lookup, not a fresh generation. **Why better:** Regenerating the whole tree every time re-pays tokens to re-think 'add a login form' for the thousandth time. Content-addressing turns repeated structure into instant cache hits, makes recursion incremental (only changed intent re-expands), and gives deterministic reproducibility — the same intent yields the same tree.
- **Edit-the-tree, not rebuild-it: structural diff + incremental re-plan** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Keep the plan-tree as a persistent, versioned artifact. When intent changes mid-run ('actually make it multi-tenant'), compute a structural diff against the prior tree and re-plan only the affected subgraph, invalidating downstream contracts the way Nx invalidates only affected projects. Unaffected subtrees and their in-flight workers keep running untouched. **Why better:** A fresh recursion per request means any mid-flight intent change tears down and re-plans everything, killing live workers. Treating the tree as editable state with affected-graph invalidation makes the factory responsive to changing intent without throwing away correct, already-built subtrees.
- **Critical-path planning: expand the longest dependency chain first (PERT/CPM)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't expand breadth-first or uniformly. Build a dependency DAG as you go and spend planning depth preferentially on the critical path — the longest chain of must-finish-before edges — the way assembly-line and project schedulers (PERT/CPM, Theory of Constraints) optimize the bottleneck. Shallow-plan the slack branches; deep-plan the chain that determines total time. **Why better:** Uniform recursion treats a throwaway settings page and the schema-migration spine as equally worth decomposing. Critical-path-first planning front-loads the risk and the contracts everything else blocks on, so the swarm starts on the bottleneck immediately and the scope-preview gate shows the real time-to-finish, not an average.
- **Merge plan-tree generation INTO contract-freezing — they're one act** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no separate 'generate the tree' phase. The only legitimate output of decomposition is a contract; so make the architect emit contracts directly and let the tree be a read-only VIEW computed from contract dependencies. You never edit the tree, you only edit contracts; readiness IS having a complete contract. **Why better:** Today the tree and the contracts can disagree (the tree says 5 children, the contracts imply 7). Collapsing them removes an entire class of sync bugs and the redundant 'recurse until Codex-ready' check — the structure can no longer drift from the only artifact the build actually consumes.
- **Decompose-by-counterexample (the tree is the negative space of failing tests)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the whole flow. Instead of idea → sections → bullets → then contracts/tests, generate the FAILING TEST SUITE first — the full set of 'this must be true' assertions / golden outputs that define done — and let the plan-tree fall out as the minimal partition that makes each cluster of tests independently passable. Recursion terminates on 'owns at least one assertion', not on 'small enough'; any code (however large) that turns a red cluster green is a valid leaf. **Why better:** Forward decomposition optimizes for a tidy-looking outline and bolts tests on at the contract freeze, so leaves can exist that no test covers (dead scope), assertions can exist that no leaf owns (gaps), and 'Codex-ready' stays a vibe. Tests-first makes the tree provably complete-and-minimal by construction, gives recursion a binary objective stop condition, and makes the contract the thing the tree is MADE of rather than a later artifact.
- **Speculative + lazy expansion (branch-prediction for plans)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat plan expansion like a speculative-execution workflow: lazily expand only the branches the build is about to need (just-in-time), keeping unexpanded subtrees as 'promise' nodes with just a contract stub — but speculatively pre-expand AND pre-warm builds on the 1–2 highest-uncertainty/most-likely children in parallel, discarding the losers. When a parent's real cut lands, correct guesses are already half-built. **Why better:** Eagerly recursing the entire tree to leaves up front spends tokens planning branches a scope gate or early failure will prune anyway, and the deepest leaves wait longest because nothing builds until the whole tree freezes. Lazy expansion plans the minimum tree to start the swarm and speculation hides planning latency behind build latency — the tree deepens only where reality demands it, collapsing the critical path.
- **Spike-driven decomposition (probe the riskiest leaf first)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Interleave planning and building: don't freeze the whole tree top-down before any code. Decompose one level, identify the highest-uncertainty node, send a throwaway spike to build it, and let what the spike learns REWRITE the rest of the tree before the swarm commits. The plan is a hypothesis the cheapest probe is allowed to falsify. **Why better:** Top-down freeze assumes the planner knows the shape before building reveals it — but the riskiest unknowns (an API's real shape, a perf wall, a schema constraint) are only discovered by touching them. Spiking first means the expensive parallel swarm runs against a tree that already survived contact with reality, instead of discovering at stitch-time that frozen contracts were wrong.
- **Fixed-point recursion (the tree decomposes itself into existence)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat decomposition as a fixed-point search, not a single descent. The factory generates a candidate tree, SIMULATES building it (cheap dry-run, token estimate, coupling check), feeds the simulated failures back as constraints, and re-decomposes — looping until the tree stops changing. The plan you build is the one that survived imagining its own construction N times. **Why better:** One-shot recursion commits to the first plausible cut and only learns it was wrong by spending real compute on the parallel build. A fixed-point that converges before any worker spawns means the structure is pre-validated against its own build dynamics — you pay cheap simulation tokens to avoid expensive build-and-discard.
- **Morphogen gradients: a global complexity field auto-tunes fan-out per region** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Instead of a constant fan-out, diffuse a scalar 'complexity/risk gradient' across the tree like a morphogen in embryo development. High-gradient regions (payments, auth, concurrency, schema) spontaneously sprout finer subdivision and more failing tests; low-gradient regions (static copy, a marketing floor) stay coarse. Fan-out is a local response to the field, not a fixed number. **Why better:** A flat ~4 bullets-per-node ignores that risk is wildly non-uniform. A gradient-driven tree concentrates decomposition and test-coverage exactly where mistakes are expensive and stays cheap where they're not — self-organizing the resolution to match the danger map, which a fixed fan-out can never do.
- **Kill the tree; keep a flat contract ledger** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete recursion entirely. The architect emits ONE flat list of leaf contracts (typed signatures + tests) with explicit dependency edges, never an intermediate idea→section→bullet hierarchy. The 'tree' was a human storytelling artifact; the build only ever consumes leaves and edges, so middle nodes are pure overhead that must be re-validated and re-stitched for nothing. **Why better:** The whole stitch-the-green-wave-up-the-tree machinery exists only because you built a tree. A DAG of leaves stitches once, parallelizes maximally, and has no phantom mid-nodes to drift out of sync with reality.
- **Grow bottom-up, never plan top-down (accretion)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Don't plan the tree then build leaves. Start by generating the smallest plausible working artifact (one screen, one endpoint, one slide), then let agents propose ADJACENT leaves it implies, accreting outward until the intent is covered. Structure emerges from working code rather than being imposed by a speculative outline. **Why better:** Top-down planning commits to a structure before any code proves it's buildable, so every wrong guess cascades. Accretion never plans a node that can't be built, and the 'plan' is always backed by something already green.
- **No tree at all — a market of bids replaces the planner** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Throw away centralized decomposition. Post the raw intent to a pool of worker agents; each BIDS to claim a chunk it thinks it can one-shot, declaring the contract it will satisfy. The 'plan' is the emergent set of accepted non-overlapping bids, negotiated by the workers themselves, not dictated by an architect. **Why better:** Central recursion forces one architect to predict what's one-shot-able for OTHER models/skills it doesn't fully understand. Letting workers self-select chunks they're confident in produces decomposition grounded in each worker's actual capability, and naturally load-balances.

### Contracts / the shell

_Current assumption: typed signatures + DB schema + failing tests frozen top-down; a golden per-stack template._

**Boldest reframe:** The Contract IS the Executable — leaves never get "built". Freeze the contract as a live, runnable artifact (typed proxies that synthesize values satisfying the tests) so the entire plan-tree boots and runs end-to-end, fully mocked, the instant contracts freeze. The whole app is clickable and demoable on day one of the run; "building" becomes progressively swapping live mocks for real code with zero integration risk because every seam was always executing. This is the boldest reframe because it doesn't tweak what a contract contains — it deletes the single riskiest moment in the entire factory (stitch-time integration) by collapsing spec and running system into one object. Every other idea makes the dead shell smarter; this one refuses to ship a dead shell at all.

- **Failing tests carry a one-line WHY** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every frozen failing test gets a mandatory `// CONTRACT: <plain-English assertion this guards>` comment on the assertion. The leaf worker reads intent, not just a red bar, so it builds to the spirit of the contract instead of gaming the literal assertion. **Why better:** A bare failing test tells a Codex leaf WHAT is red but not WHY; workers routinely satisfy the literal assertion while violating intent. The repo's own schema tests (`toEqual(arrayContaining([...]))`) are inscrutable without the why. A cheap one-line intent annotation kills the most common silent-miss failure mode.
- **Contracts fail VISIBLE, not loud — .passthrough() as factory law** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Adopt the codebase's brain-schema discipline factory-wide: leaf outputs validate against a Zod schema that uses `.passthrough()` and attaches `_contractViolation` rather than throwing. A leaf may over-deliver (extra fields/exports) freely; it only fails when the REQUIRED shape is missing, and the violation surfaces in the cockpit instead of crashing the build. **Why better:** A rigid 'must match exactly' contract makes parallel workers brittle and forces premature freezing of every field. The repo already proved `.passthrough()` + visible-error beats exact-match (`decision-schemas.ts` attaches `_validationError`, never throws). It lets the swarm move while keeping the green wave honest about what's actually missing.
- **Golden template ships as a fill-in-the-blanks skeleton, not prose** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The per-stack golden template is a real compilable file with `// FILL: <what goes here>` markers at every variation point, plus imports, the export signature, and a passing smoke test already wired. The leaf's only job is to replace the FILL blocks — never to invent file structure, import style, or test harness. **Why better:** A template described in the plan but never materialized lets every worker reinvent boilerplate and drift on conventions (this repo's GSAP-init contract, the Supabase-REST-not-Drizzle gotcha). A compilable skeleton collapses the leaf to a fill-in task and bakes the repo's gotchas in by construction.
- **Contract bundle = one signed, hashed manifest per node** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Freeze each node's contract as a single `node.contract.json` beside the worktree: typed signature, schema slice, test-file paths, golden-template path, and a content hash. Workers and the stitch step read ONE artifact; the graphify reconciler diffs as-built reality against this file's hash to detect a worker that quietly edited its own contract. **Why better:** Today the contract is implicitly scattered across types + schema + tests + template, so 'did the worker honor the contract?' is mechanically unanswerable. The repo's `reconciler.ts` already composes reality from snapshots — give it one hashed contract to diff against and the green wave becomes a real, cheap integrity check instead of a vibe.
- **Contract-readiness linter gate before the swarm spends** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A fast static pass over the frozen contract tree rejects under-specified leaves BEFORE dispatch: a leaf is 'Codex-ready' only if it has a typed signature, at least one failing test referencing that signature, and a template binding. Any leaf failing the linter bounces up to Claude to sharpen, and the scope-preview gate shows the count of not-yet-ready leaves. **Why better:** The plan-tree's recursion-stops-when-Codex-ready rule is currently an architect judgment call; a worker discovering mid-run that its contract is vague burns a whole headless process and quota. A mechanical readiness linter turns 'Codex-ready' from a vibe into a gate and surfaces the real not-ready count at the cheap scope gate, not after tokens burn.
- **Contracts carry their own rollback (transactional shell)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every frozen contract ships with an auto-generated inverse — the exact migration-down, file-deletions, and dependency-uninstalls needed to un-build it — so any subtree can be atomically reverted to its pre-contract state. For schema specifically, freeze the reversible delta (forward + tested `down`) with a round-trip test (`up` then `down` leaves the DB byte-identical), not 'these columns exist'. **Why better:** The current model has no defined way to back out a bad subtree; a wrong schema migration is a manual cleanup nightmare exactly when an autonomous swarm fails. Pairing each contract with its inverse makes the whole build transactional, matches the repo's `drizzle-kit generate` + RLS-on-every-table flow, and lets the orchestrator gamble on bold scopes knowing it can cleanly rewind.
- **Property/invariant tests, not just example tests** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For leaves whose behavior generalizes (parsers, formatters, scoring, RLS), the failing-test contract pairs a couple of concrete examples with at least one PROPERTY a fuzzer hammers with thousands of generated cases: idempotence, round-trip, monotonicity, schema-fuzz, 'RLS cannot leak across users'. A leaf is done only when the property holds under adversarial input, not when three examples pass. **Why better:** Pure example tests are the single easiest contract to overfit — a worker can literally `if (input === example) return expected`. A property describes the function's INVARIANT, not three data points, which is what 'build to spec' should mean for a behavioral leaf; properties double as the as-built graphify verification.
- **Contracts carry an explicit forbidden-list, not just a required shape** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each contract names its anti-spec: forbidden imports, banned patterns (this repo: the `db` Drizzle object in serverless, raw `gsap` import, `any`, console.log, TODO comments), and out-of-scope files the worker must not touch. The stitch step rejects any leaf that tripped a forbidden item. **Why better:** A contract that only specifies what to produce leaves infinite room to produce it wrongly — and this repo's CLAUDE.md is mostly a list of NEVERs that autonomous workers can't see. Encoding the bans INTO the per-node contract turns hard-won gotchas into mechanical rejection at stitch time instead of a human review catch.
- **The Contract IS the Executable — leaves never get "built"** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop treating contracts as a spec a worker then implements. Freeze the contract as a live, runnable artifact (a typed proxy that returns synthesized values satisfying the tests). The plan-tree boots and RUNS end-to-end the instant contracts freeze — fully mocked — so the whole app is demoable before a single leaf is written. 'Building' becomes progressively replacing live mocks with real code, with zero integration risk because the seams were always executing. **Why better:** The current assumption ships dead artifacts (signatures + failing tests) that only come alive at stitch time, making integration the riskiest moment. An executable contract moves stitch-risk to zero and gives the user a clickable product on day one of the run.
- **The contract is an executable judge you call, not a doc you read** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the frozen text bundle (signatures + schema + tests) with a running adjudicator process per node: a tiny service a worker submits its artifact to and gets back PASS/FAIL + a diagnosis. The contract is something you call, not something you read. **Why better:** First-principles: workers need a shared TRUTH only because they can't talk mid-flight, and a static doc forces every worker to re-interpret the same prose identically (they won't). A live judge gives one canonical verdict, eliminates interpretation drift, and lets the contract evolve without re-broadcasting frozen files.
- **Self-negotiating contracts — a one-hop receptor/ligand handshake** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Contracts freeze top-down but are not immutable. A leaf that discovers the signature is wrong emits a typed counter-proposal ('this field nullable / this extra method / this dep') that bubbles UP exactly one level, where the parent Claude either ratifies it into the frozen contract or rejects with a reason; affected siblings re-freeze only. Like protobuf field-reservation, or a cell's receptor refusing a ligand that doesn't fit — the interface is a handshake, not a decree. **Why better:** Top-down freezing assumes the architect is omniscient about every leaf; a single wrong signature poisons an entire subtree of dutiful conformers. A one-hop amendment protocol catches 'the contract was subtly wrong' at build time instead of at the catastrophically-expensive green-wave stitch, and keeps parallelism intact (merges the four near-identical 'renegotiable/bidirectional treaty' proposals).
- **The Million-Variant Shell — contracts fork the tree, not constrain it** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Freezing a contract doesn't pick ONE implementation target — it spawns a fan of competing contract variants (strict-typed vs. permissive, SQL-first vs. event-sourced, server-component vs. client-island). The swarm builds several in parallel against the same tests and a judge model promotes the survivor. The shell is a tournament bracket, not a single mold. **Why better:** A single frozen template bets the whole subtree on the architect's first guess being optimal. Forking contracts converts otherwise-idle quota into evolutionary search, so the user gets the empirically-best architecture rather than the first plausible one.
- **Prose-to-Contract: the user edits the shell in plain English mid-run** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The cockpit exposes the frozen contracts as readable English clauses ('this endpoint never returns another user's rows', 'the deck has exactly 12 slides'). The user edits the prose; a model re-derives the typed signatures, schema, and tests and re-freezes only the touched nodes. The contract layer becomes the steering wheel, not an opaque internal artifact. **Why better:** Today contracts are an internal architect-to-builder handoff the user can't touch without re-running ideation. Making the shell the user-facing edit surface turns course-correction into a sentence, not a restart — and it generalizes across every artifact type (app, deck, report) since prose is universal.
- **Contracts as a tradeable, reusable currency (a shell market)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every frozen-and-verified contract subtree is content-addressed and banked. The next run's architect doesn't author 'auth with RLS' or 'funnel chart' from scratch — it queries the bank and, if a battle-tested shell exists, grafts it in with its tests and golden template intact. The factory accrues a compounding library of proven shells. **Why better:** The per-stack golden template is static and singular; it never learns from the thousands of subtrees the factory has already proven correct. A content-addressed shell bank makes every successful build make the next one cheaper and safer — the factory gets smarter with use instead of starting cold.
- **Adversarial red-team baked into the freeze** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — At freeze time, alongside the failing tests, the architect emits an adversary spec: exploits, edge cases, and 'prove you didn't cheat' probes that the OTHER model must pass the leaf against before acceptance. Cross-model review isn't a separate phase — it's a clause inside every contract that the opposing model is contractually obligated to attack. **Why better:** Current cross-model review is a bolt-on pass, so a worker optimizes for the visible tests and the reviewer sees a fait accompli. Encoding adversarial probes INTO the contract means every leaf is built knowing it will be attacked on specific axes — security/RLS especially — turning review from after-the-fact into a precondition.
- **Freeze intent + acceptance; derive tests as a disposable view** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The top-down artifact is a structured INTENT+ACCEPTANCE-CRITERIA object ('this leaf must do X, observable as Y'). Typed signatures and failing tests are auto-generated PROJECTIONS of it, regenerated on demand and never hand-frozen. The contract layer holds the why; the test files are cache. **Why better:** Tests/types are a PROXY for intent we trust by habit. When proxy and intent disagree (the classic 'tests pass, wrong thing built'), frozen tests win and you ship the wrong artifact. Anchoring on intent makes the real arbiter primary and lets tests be regenerated when they prove a bad projection — instead of being load-bearing fossils.
- **Provisional contracts that tighten as evidence arrives** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Contracts start LOOSE (intent + invariants only) and progressively harden as child builds report back what shapes actually worked. A confidence score rides each clause; only clauses above threshold are binding. The shell crystallizes bottom-up even though intent flows top-down. **Why better:** Top-down freezing assumes the parent can fully specify a child it has never seen built — a planning fiction. Real systems discover the right seam during construction. Provisional contracts kill the 'frozen wrong' failure mode where one bad early signature poisons a whole subtree that all dutifully conform to it.
- **Contract and implementation as one self-verifying object** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the spec/impl separation. Each leaf is a single artifact carrying its own machine-checkable proof obligations inline (proof-carrying code / refinement types). There is no separate test file to drift from the code — the artifact cannot compile or load unless it satisfies its own embedded contract. **Why better:** We keep spec and impl apart out of human-team tradition (different authors, different review). In an AI swarm one model writes both, so the separation only creates a drift surface. Fusing them makes 'green' mean 'provably correct against its own stated obligation,' not 'a sibling test file happened to pass.'
- **Negative-space contracts: freeze the forbidden, not the required** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of fully specifying what each node MUST be, freeze the small set of things it must NEVER violate — cross-cutting invariants: never touches the Drizzle `db` at runtime, never widens RLS, never breaks the GSAP import seam, never exceeds a token budget. Workers have total freedom inside the fence. **Why better:** Full top-down specification over-constrains and burns architect tokens guessing details the leaf-builder would get right anyway. Most integration disasters are a handful of invariant violations, not signature mismatches. Fencing the disasters and freeing everything else yields more correct autonomy per token and matches this repo's actual gotcha list.
- **Per-node templates synthesized from neighbors, not a fixed golden per stack** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the single golden per-stack template. For each node, generate its scaffold on the fly by reading the graph for the 2–3 most similar already-built siblings and distilling their shared shape. The 'template' is a just-in-time, context-specific seam, not a frozen prototype. **Why better:** One golden template per stack optimizes for human-review consistency — irrelevant when machines build and a judge verifies. A fixed template under-fits the unusual node and freezes yesterday's conventions across the whole tree. Neighbor-synthesized scaffolds keep the codebase self-consistent AND let conventions evolve, using graphify as truth instead of a static file.
- **The contract is a recorded Claude-vs-Codex exchange, not a deliverable** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Freeze the ADVERSARIAL EXCHANGE that produced the contract: Claude proposes the seam, Codex attacks it, and the disagreements and their resolutions are stored AS the contract. Workers read not just the final signature but the reasoning and the rejected alternatives. **Why better:** A frozen signature throws away the most valuable thing — WHY this shape and not the obvious alternatives. Leaf-builders re-derive and sometimes 'fix' the contract back into a rejected form. Carrying the dialectic means a worker who hits friction sees it was already considered and rejected, turning the contract into guidance the swarm can't accidentally undo.
- **Golden HARNESS: a reference-render fixture, not a copy-paste seed** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of one golden template workers imitate, ship a tiny known-good input→known-good output fixture pair per node-type that every generated leaf must reproduce byte-for-byte — like a VFX color-reference frame every shot must match, or a film LUT. The leaf is graded on 'passes the reference render,' not 'looks like the template.' **Why better:** A copy-paste template encodes structure but not behavior — two workers can both 'follow the template' and still diverge semantically. A reference-render fixture pins observable behavior, which is what stitching actually depends on. The repo already proves this works: `schema.r4.test.ts` is exactly a structural reference monitor for the DB contract.
- **Capability-typed contracts: leaves declare their effects, not just signatures** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Freeze each leaf's contract with a CAPABILITY MANIFEST — not just typed in/out, but the side-effects it's ALLOWED to touch (reads table X, writes table Y, calls network, mutates global). A worker that exceeds its capability envelope trips an andon-cord poka-yoke and halts before merge, rather than producing a leaf that quietly writes where it shouldn't. **Why better:** Typed signatures constrain SHAPE but say nothing about REACH. The nastiest stitch failures are leaves that compile fine but touch shared state they shouldn't — the IPv6/Drizzle-on-Vercel gotcha in this very repo is an un-declared-capability bug. A capability envelope makes the most dangerous class of integration bug a compile-time refusal.
- **Consumer-driven contract tests: the parent authors the leaf's tests** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Flip who writes the failing tests. The CONSUMER (the parent node that will call the leaf) writes the contract test the leaf must pass, and the leaf worker is FORBIDDEN from editing it. Pact-style consumer-driven contracts: the integration point is owned by whoever depends on it, never by whoever implements it. **Why better:** If the leaf author writes their own failing tests, they test what they BUILT, not what the caller NEEDS — tests and implementation drift together and the stitch still breaks. Consumer-authored tests guarantee the contract reflects real downstream demand, and they double as the green-wave integration test for free.
- **Typed connector ports — stitching is mechanical key-matching** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every node exposes physical CONNECTOR PORTS with a typed shape (a Lego stud, a fixture pin, a strongly-typed Unix pipe). Stitching is purely mechanical: a child port type must equal the parent socket type, verified by a graph query before any code runs. No port match → the node literally cannot be placed in the tree. **Why better:** The current 'mechanical stitch' assumes the green wave just compiles up the tree but offers no structural guarantee BEFORE the build that the pieces fit. Pre-declared typed ports turn integration from 'build and pray it compiles' into 'the manifest is unsatisfiable, refuse to dispatch' — failures surface at plan time, not after the swarm has spent tokens.
- **Tolerance bands instead of exact contracts** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A frozen contract specifies a TOLERANCE BAND, not an exact value: 'returns a User-shaped object with AT LEAST these fields, p95 latency under N, may include extras.' Workers fit anywhere inside the band; the stitch only fails outside it. Like machined-part tolerances, or biological canalization where many genotypes hit the same viable phenotype. **Why better:** Exact contracts force the architect to over-specify at freeze time, when info is lowest, and any leaf that's 'right but different' gets rejected. Tolerance bands let the swarm find diverse valid solutions in parallel (more processes = more power) while still guaranteeing mechanical assembly, absorbing the natural variance between Claude-built and Codex-built leaves instead of fighting it.
- **The contract is a live graph node with a reconciler, not a frozen file** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Store each contract as a content-addressed node in graphify with a single legal read path (a reconciler), exactly like ArtLab's run-state reconciler. When a leaf is built, the reconciler diffs as-built reality against the contract hash; a mismatch becomes an orthogonal BLOCKER ('contract-drift') on that node rather than a silent break. The contract and its verification are the same object. **Why better:** A frozen file plus separate failing tests can drift apart, and nothing reconciles 'what we promised' against 'what got built' until the stitch. Making the contract a hashed graph node with a reconciler means as-built verification is continuous and queryable mid-run — the cockpit can show a contract-drift blocker climbing the tree in real time, and `reconciler.ts` already exists to host it.
- **Burn the contract layer: let the artifact be its own contract** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete frozen contracts entirely. Workers build directly off the plan-tree node's intent prose; the 'contract' is reconstructed AFTER the fact by reading what was actually built, and the stitch verifies emergent compatibility, not pre-declared signatures. No top-down freeze step at all. **Why better:** Frozen contracts bet that the architect can predict the shape of code before it exists — the most error-prone moment in the pipeline. Predicting signatures top-down for an app you've never built is hallucinating an API; you pay for being wrong twice (write the wrong contract, then force-fit code to it). Letting the artifact be its own contract removes the single most speculative artifact in the system.
- **Tests are the only contract — kill signatures, schema-freeze, and template** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Collapse the four-part contract to ONE part: the failing tests. No frozen typed signatures, no DB schema freeze, no golden per-stack template. A leaf is 'ready' iff a black-box behavioral test exists for it; the worker is free to invent any signature, any internal schema, any structure that makes the test green. **Why better:** Signatures and golden templates over-specify the HOW and strangle the model's strongest capability — finding a clean implementation shape. Behavior is the only thing the user actually contracted for. A test is an executable, falsifiable contract; a typed signature is an opinion that may be wrong. One falsifiable artifact beats four redundant ones, three of which leak implementation.
- **Freeze contracts BOTTOM-UP, not top-down** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Build the leaves first with zero contracts, let real signatures and schemas EMERGE from working leaf code, then freeze contracts upward as the green wave climbs — parent contracts are derived from children that already exist and pass. **Why better:** Top-down freezing asks the easiest-to-fool actor (an architect guessing) to specify the hardest-to-know layer (concrete leaf shapes). Inverting it grounds every contract in code that already runs. Integration bugs become impossible-by-construction because parents are written against real, observed child interfaces — not imagined ones, so 'stitch can fail' risk evaporates.
- **Merge Contracts into the plan-tree — a node IS its contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no separate contract artifact or shell phase. The plan-tree node's recursion-stop condition ('Codex-ready') simply means the node text is precise enough to act as the contract. The leaf description, when explicit enough, IS the spec; no translation into signatures/tests/templates. **Why better:** The current design keeps TWO representations of the same intent (the plan node AND its derived contract) that can drift and must be kept in sync — a guaranteed bug class. Merging them eliminates a synchronization surface and a whole phase, and makes 'Codex-ready' the single honest definition of done for decomposition.
- **No golden template — let each leaf pick its own shape, normalize after** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the golden per-stack template entirely. Each leaf worker chooses its own idioms; a cheap post-hoc normalizer (or graphify) harmonizes style afterward only where it actually matters. Stop pre-imposing a house style as part of the contract. **Why better:** A golden template freezes today's best-guess pattern into every future leaf, so the factory can never produce anything better than the template author imagined — it caps quality and ages badly. Frontier models already write idiomatic code per stack. The template is a conformity tax that suppresses the model's strongest output for a consistency goal nobody asked for.
- **Invert what gets frozen: freeze the DATA, not the code interfaces** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Freeze only the DB schema / data shapes top-down and freeze nothing else. Code signatures, function boundaries, tests, and templates all stay liquid. The data contract is the single immovable spine; everything that touches it is free to take any form. **Why better:** Schema is the one part genuinely expensive to change late (migrations, RLS, data already written) AND the one part the architect can actually reason about correctly up front. Function signatures are cheap to refactor and easy to get wrong early. The four-part freeze treats cheap-to-change and expensive-to-change as equally sacred; freeze-only-data spends rigidity where it pays and grants freedom where it pays.
- **Contracts written by the cross-model REVIEWER, not the architect** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — The architect proposes nothing frozen. Instead the OTHER model (Codex when Claude planned, or vice versa) writes the contract as an adversarial act — it must independently specify what the leaf should do, and any disagreement between proposer and contract-writer is surfaced as a scope-gate question before any build. **Why better:** A contract authored by the same agent that planned the node is self-confirming — it bakes the architect's blind spots straight into the spec the swarm obeys. Making a second, differently-biased model write the contract turns the freeze step into a cross-model disagreement detector, catching planning errors at the cheapest possible moment instead of after the swarm builds the wrong thing.

### Verification & stitching

_Current assumption: red->green leaves; a green wave climbs bottom-up; integration tests at nodes; cross-model review._

**Boldest reframe:** Reverse the wave: green ceiling drips DOWN, not a green wave climbing UP — build the whole tree stubbed top-down so the full artifact is demoable and integration-tested as a WHOLE from day zero, then real leaves replace stubs and are "done" the instant swapping them in keeps the root acceptance test green. This is the boldest because it inverts the core hypothesis itself: it deletes the bottom-up green wave (the stated mechanism), turns "integration" from a final risky big-bang into the always-on baseline, and makes the artifact shippable on day one rather than day last. Verification stops flowing up from dead parts and instead flows down from a living whole.

- **Contract-as-test: the failing test is COMPILED from the frozen contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a node's contract is frozen top-down (typed signature + schema + named behavioral properties), the red test the leaf must turn green is auto-derived from that contract — never written by the builder. 'Green' is redefined as 'contract-coverage = 100%': every clause of the contract has a derived, executing check. A leaf can't rubber-stamp itself because it goes green against the test the contract handed down, and any clause with no derived check shows up as visible under-specification. **Why better:** The hypothesis leaves WHO writes the red test unspecified — if the builder writes its own test, green just means self-satisfaction. Deriving tests from the contract makes green a real signal of contract-conformance and makes the gap between 'what we froze top-down' and 'what we verified bottom-up' visible instead of silent.
- **The stitch seam is a test, scoped to the edge — verify only adjacency** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At every internal node, the integration test exercises ONLY the seam between its now-green children (do the typed outputs actually plug into each other), never the children's internals again. Reframe these as explicit INTERFACE proof-obligations carried on each plan-tree edge ('child returns X-shaped, parent consumes X-shaped'); stitching just discharges the edge-obligations. The wave climbs by proving adjacency; leaf-correctness is already banked below. **Why better:** 'Integration tests at nodes' is vague about scope and risks re-testing everything at every level — O(subtree) per node, slow wave, combinatorial cost up the tree. Scoping to just the freshly-joined seam keeps the climb O(edges), makes a red node point at exactly which interface lied, and matches how contracts were frozen top-down.
- **Quarantine a red leaf, let the wave route around it with a typed stub** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If a leaf stays red after its round cap, mark it quarantined (with its failing proof attached) and let the green wave continue up every branch that doesn't depend on it. Where a parent does depend on it, climb PAST using a typed stub that satisfies the parent contract, marking the node 'amber' (contract met by stub, impl pending) while a dedicated rescue worker re-attacks the quarantined leaf. The run delivers a partial-but-honest artifact with labeled holes instead of freezing on one stubborn leaf. **Why better:** A strict bottom-up green wave stalls the entire artifact on a single failing leaf — the batch-and-queue antipattern. Borrowing tower-ship-gate's 'SKIP with a stated reason' honesty plus the large-org flaky-test quarantine pattern keeps the swarm at full utilization and surfaces the COMPLETE defect set in one pass, not just the earliest failure.
- **Promote the golden per-stack template to a conformance oracle** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The golden template that seeds each stack isn't just a generation seed — diff every finished leaf against it for structural drift (import patterns, file layout, the project's banned-API list: console.log, TODO, Drizzle-at-runtime) and fail the stitch on drift BEFORE the integration test even runs. The template becomes the cheapest, first, static gate. **Why better:** The hypothesis uses the golden template only as a seed. Reusing it as a verification oracle catches the cheap, high-frequency failures statically — exactly the Tower's grep-bans — before spending tokens on integration testing.
- **graphify as the as-built vs contract graph diff that gates every stitch** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before a node goes green, run graphify to extract the AS-BUILT call/dependency/symbol graph of its subtree and diff it against the CONTRACT graph frozen top-down. Any edge in code but not in contract (or vice-versa) — an undeclared import crossing module boundaries, a phantom dependency, a promised symbol reality lacks, a schema column never created — blocks the stitch. Emit a single scalar 'reality drift' score. Verification stops being 'tests pass' and becomes 'reality matches the plan we promised.' **Why better:** Tests prove behavior at sampled inputs; they don't prove STRUCTURE matches intent. A leaf can pass its test while silently calling something it was never contracted to touch. The contract-vs-as-built diff catches scope-creep, hidden coupling, missing seams, and 'green but wrong-shaped' code — exactly graphify's stated second job, and the drift Terraform/CMDB tooling learned to detect.
- **Cross-model review only where the blast radius earns it** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Tier verification spend by tag: cheap leaves get same-model self-check + the contract test; only nodes touching the irreversible set (auth, schema/migrations, payments, deploy, public output) trigger the full Claude×Codex adversarial round with a landed artifact. Auto-route by the node's tags, not uniformly. **Why better:** Uniform cross-model review everywhere is theater on trivial leaves and burns the dual quota the factory depends on. The forge adversarial-loop already reserves the full loop for code + irreversible work — making that routing automatic and tag-driven turns a principle into a built-in cost control.
- **Reviewer's counterexample becomes a permanent regression test** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When the cross-model reviewer lands a failing artifact (the edge case that breaks a leaf), capture it as a named regression test attached to the node and re-run it on every future stitch of that subtree. The verification system accumulates a memory of how each node has been broken before. **Why better:** The current loop throws away the reviewer's best output — once an objection is resolved, the breaking case evaporates. Persisting it means the same class of bug can't silently reappear during a later re-stitch or self-evolution pass, and the factory's test suite gets sharper every run instead of starting cold.
- **A signed stitch ledger: every green node carries its proof receipt** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Each node, when it goes green, writes a receipt — which tests ran, the as-built/contract graph hash, which model reviewed it, full-or-tiered check, any quarantine — and the root artifact ships with the full ledger. 'Done' becomes auditable after the fact, not a transient log claim. The cockpit shows TRUST, not just status, so a human can spot weakly-verified nodes (e.g. a degraded 'Codex unavailable' self-check) at a glance. **Why better:** The hypothesis treats green as ephemeral; nothing distinguishes a leaf that passed a real cross-model round from one that got a degraded self-check. A per-node proof receipt gives self-evolution a defensible basis for what it's allowed to touch and lets humans audit trust, not just see green.
- **Proof-carrying leaves: the contract is the only test, stitching composes proofs** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A leaf doesn't pass because tests go green — it ships a machine-checkable PROOF that its implementation satisfies the frozen typed contract (refinement types / property specs / SMT-discharged obligations). The stitch step doesn't re-run tests; it composes proofs. If every leaf carries a proof and the proofs compose, the whole tree is correct BY CONSTRUCTION and node-level integration testing becomes redundant. **Why better:** Red→green tests only sample behavior at the points you remembered; a composed proof covers the entire input space. Stitching becomes algebra instead of hoping integration tests caught the seam bugs.
- **Property + metamorphic fuzzing at every seam, not example tests** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of hand-written integration tests at each node, auto-derive PROPERTIES from the contract (idempotence, round-trip, monotonicity, schema-invariants, 'permuting independent inputs doesn't change the invariant') and run a relentless fuzzer/QuickCheck against the stitched seam. A node only goes green when the fuzzer can't break the contract after N thousand adversarial inputs the author never imagined. **Why better:** Example-based integration tests pass on the three inputs the author imagined — exactly the blind spot where cross-leaf stitching breaks in practice. Contract-derived property fuzzing (how biology validates robustness: perturb and check invariants) finds the seam bug nobody thought of.
- **Adversarial twin build: two models must DISAGREE before they can agree** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every high-stakes leaf is built INDEPENDENTLY by both Claude and Codex from the same frozen contract, blind to each other. The leaf is certified green only when both implementations pass a shared differential-test harness AND produce identical observable behavior on a fuzzed input stream. Divergence isn't a tie-break — it's a RED flag that the CONTRACT itself is underspecified. **Why better:** Current cross-model review is one model judging another's single artifact — a reviewer can rubber-stamp a subtly wrong implementation. Differential dual-build turns the two models into a built-in oracle and surfaces ambiguous contracts before they poison the stitch.
- **The green wave is a live liquid that recedes on contact (reactive flood-fill)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Verification isn't a one-shot bottom-up climb; it's a continuously-recomputed flood-fill over the plan-tree. Any leaf edit, contract change, or as-built graph drift instantly drains 'green' from every dependent node up and downstream, and the wave re-climbs only the affected subtree. The cockpit shows green as a living water level, not a checklist — green always means 'verified RIGHT NOW against current reality.' **Why better:** A one-time green wave is stale the moment a worker touches anything below it. A reactive flood-fill means you can never ship on a wave that silently went stale, and only the affected subtree re-verifies.
- **Stitch by type-directed synthesis, not gluing** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The stitch step doesn't paste leaf outputs together and pray — it SYNTHESIZES the connective tissue. Given two leaf contracts and the parent contract, the factory generates (and verifies) the adapter/wiring code automatically via type-directed program synthesis, because the seam is fully determined by the three typed signatures. Workers never hand-write integration glue. **Why better:** Today the seam is hand-written integration code — the single most error-prone part of bottom-up assembly. If contracts are frozen top-down and complete, the glue is DERIVABLE, eliminating the entire class of 'leaves are fine, the wiring is wrong' failures.
- **Metamorphic verification for the unverifiable (non-code) leaves** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — For leaves whose output has no ground truth (a deck, a research summary, an art asset, a design decision), don't fake a unit test — verify METAMORPHIC RELATIONS: rephrase the intent and the artifact must stay semantically stable; remove a source and the conclusion must weaken; raise the altitude dial and scope must monotonically grow. Green = the artifact behaves correctly under transformation, even with no 'correct answer.' **Why better:** The factory builds ANYTHING decomposable, but red→green tests only exist for code. Metamorphic relations give the non-code 80% of artifacts a real, automatable verification story instead of a human eyeball or nothing at all.
- **Time-travel bisection: the factory builds its own git-bisect oracle** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Because the whole tree is built from frozen contracts in deterministic worktrees, the factory can REPLAY any subtree at any historical contract revision. When a top-level acceptance check goes red, it doesn't debug — it binary-searches the plan-tree AND the contract-revision timeline simultaneously to pinpoint the exact leaf-or-contract-edit that broke the wave, then re-dispatches only that one. **Why better:** A failed green wave today tells you the tree is broken but not WHERE; humans bisect manually. An automatic two-axis (structure × time) bisection turns 'something downstream broke' into 'this single contract edit at this node, re-dispatching now' with zero human triage.
- **Content-addressed build cache: verification goes incremental (steal Bazel/Turborepo)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hash each leaf's full contract (signature + schema slice + tests + template + dependency hashes) into a cache key. Before any worker builds or re-verifies a node, look it up in a shared remote cache; on a hit, replay the already-green artifact and its proof instead of regenerating. Generalize this as a hash-bound CLAIM ledger on the graph: each verification claim ('signature matches', 'property P holds', 'reviewed by Codex @ contract-hash X') is bound to the hash it was made against and auto-invalidates when anything upstream changes, so only invalidated claims re-run. **Why better:** The current bottom-up wave reverifies everything on every run, and review is a perishable event — change one thing and you either blindly re-run everything or trust stale green. Content addressing makes verification incremental and HONEST: a one-line contract tweak reverifies one node, you always see which proofs are current vs stale, and cross-model review becomes reusable instead of re-paid.
- **Saga compensation for stitch failures (steal Temporal/saga pattern)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat each stitch (merging children into a parent) as a saga step with an explicit compensating action.
