If integrating sibling leaves into their parent fails verification, the orchestrator runs the recorded compensations to cleanly roll the parent back to its last-green contract boundary, rather than leaving a half-merged worktree. **Why better:** The 'mechanical stitch' assumption has no defined failure semantics — a failed merge leaves ambiguous state needing human untangling. Workflow engines solved this with compensations: every forward step knows how to undo itself, so a failed climb is always recoverable to a known-good altitude.
- **Continuous one-piece flow: integrate each leaf the instant it's green (steal Toyota)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of building all siblings then stitching at the parent, integrate each leaf into the parent the moment it goes green — one-piece flow up the tree — so the parent's integration suite runs continuously against a growing set of real children rather than a big-bang merge at the end. **Why better:** Big-bang integration is exactly the batch-and-queue antipattern lean manufacturing killed: defects pile up invisibly until the final merge, then surface all at once. Continuous flow makes the integration error visible the moment the offending leaf lands, so blame is unambiguous and the failing surface stays tiny.
- **Andon cord: a contract-bug failure escalates UP to the architect that froze it** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every worker an 'andon cord': when a leaf fails verification in a way that implicates its CONTRACT rather than its implementation (the spec is impossible or contradictory), it pulls the cord — halting that subtree's spend and escalating up the plan-tree to the Claude architect that froze the contract, with the failing proof as evidence. **Why better:** Red→green assumes failures are always implementation bugs fixable at the leaf. Some are contract bugs — a frozen signature that can't be satisfied. The andon cord routes those to the altitude that can actually fix them instead of burning tokens having a Codex worker bang against an impossible spec, the factory's most expensive failure mode.
- **Golden-frame render diffing + dailies for visual/artifact nodes (steal VFX)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — For nodes whose artifact is visual (a floor, a UI panel, an ArtLab asset, a deck slide), verification is a render-and-diff against an approved golden frame plus an automated 'dailies' contact sheet posted to the cockpit. A node is green only if its perceptual diff against the golden is under threshold or a human approves the new frame as the golden. **Why better:** The current oracle is tests, but huge swaths of this factory (Tower floors, art campaigns, decks) produce VISUAL artifacts where 'passes a test' is meaningless. VFX pipelines verify exactly this with golden-frame diffs and dailies review — giving the factory a real oracle for the half of its output tests can't touch.
- **Speculative parallel variants with a judge-selected winner (steal VFX takes / ensemble)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For high-risk leaves, dispatch 2-3 workers (Claude-built and Codex-built) to produce competing implementations of the SAME contract in parallel, run all through the identical proof suite, and let the cross-model judge pick the survivor — discarding the losers. The contract is the fitness function; the leaf is the population. **Why better:** Single-attempt red→green retries the SAME approach when it fails, often re-failing the same way. Speculative variants (how VFX shoots multiple takes, how evolution hedges with a population) turn verification into selection — you keep whichever implementation actually survives the proof, satisfying the contract on the first wave far more often.
- **Mutation gate: green is necessary, never sufficient — verify the verifier** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Forbid promoting any node on green alone. Promotion additionally requires a mutation pass: the system mutates the leaf (and even corrupts the frozen contract) and confirms the tests CATCH the mutation. If a mutant survives, the tests are theater and the leaf stays red regardless of the passing suite. A node is trusted only if tampering with it provably propagates failure; silence after corruption marks an untested, deletable node. **Why better:** Red→green is gameable — a fast leaf-builder can write vacuously-passing tests, and most generated suites would pass even against broken code. Mutation testing measures the one thing that matters (test sensitivity), closing the 'green but worthless' hole the current assumption has no defense against.
- **Two-model verification: agreement auto-passes, only DISAGREEMENT gets looked at** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Have BOTH models independently verify each leaf against the contract. Treat AGREEMENT as auto-pass and DISAGREEMENT as the only thing a human (or a third arbiter model) ever looks at. Verification attention routes entirely to the cross-model deltas. **Why better:** Single-model cross-review still trusts one judge. Independent dual verification turns the two-model router into an ensemble oracle: consensus is cheaply auto-trusted, and scarce human/arbiter attention is spent ONLY where the models diverge — exactly the high-uncertainty leaves, instead of uniformly across a mostly-boring tree.
- **Adversarial bounty per leaf: done = survived a funded attacker** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A leaf isn't done when its tests pass — it's done when a dedicated red-team worker (the OTHER model) has spent a fixed token budget actively trying to BREAK it and failed. Green = 'survived an attacker,' not 'passed the author's own tests.' **Why better:** Author-written green tests measure what the builder anticipated — exactly the blind spot. A funded adversary measures robustness against the UNanticipated, turning cross-model review from a one-shot read into an active falsification budget that catches the bugs a green suite is structurally blind to.
- **Blind adversarial tests, not prose review: the other model writes your red tests** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Eliminate the 'Codex reviews Claude's diff' read-through entirely. Replace it with: the model that did NOT build a leaf must write the failing tests that leaf has to pass, BEFORE seeing the implementation. Review becomes an executable bet, not an opinion. **Why better:** Cross-model 'review' is unfalsifiable — a reviewer LGTMs or nitpicks with no ground truth, and AI reviewing AI code tends to hallucinate approval. An adversarial test written blind by the other model is a hard, runnable contract: it either catches the bug or it doesn't, at the same token cost with vastly higher signal.
- **Verification IS the planner: the integration test is the splitting criterion** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Merge verification into the plan-tree step. A node is only allowed to split into children if the parent can state the integration test that re-composes those children — the test is the SPLITTING CRITERION, not a later artifact. No test for the recomposition? Then the node isn't actually decomposable and must stay a leaf. Decomposition and verification become the same act. **Why better:** Today decomposition and verification are separate stages that can disagree — you can split a node cleanly yet have no idea how to prove the pieces fit, discovering the gap only after the swarm spends. Making the integration test the precondition for splitting guarantees every cut is provable before any tokens are spent.
- **Reverse the wave: a green ceiling drips DOWN from a living stubbed whole** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Build the whole tree mocked/stubbed top-down so the FULL artifact is 'green' and demoable on day zero with fake leaves, integration-tested as a whole from the start. Then real implementations replace stubs from the top, and a leaf is 'done' the moment swapping it in keeps the top-level acceptance test green. Verification flows downward from a living whole, never upward from dead parts. (Contract-mocks can also let many nodes verify in parallel against children's mocks, decoupling 'does the leaf work' from 'do the pieces fit' as a separate cheap divergence check.) **Why better:** A bottom-up green wave means nothing is runnable until the very end and global integration problems surface last, when most expensive. A top-down stubbed system is always shippable, always integration-tested as a whole, and surfaces architectural mismatches on day one. Contract-mocks additionally decouple leaf-correctness (verifies fully in parallel) from integration (a cheap mock-vs-real diff).
- **Delete the green wave: make a wrong leaf un-buildable by construction** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — There is no separate verification phase. All rigor moves UP into contract-freezing: the contract is so tight (typed signature + schema + golden template + property generators) that a wrong leaf literally cannot compile or type-check against the frozen contract graph. If a worker produces something that links and type-checks, it is correct by construction; stitching becomes a no-op because nothing un-stitchable can exist. **Why better:** A climbing green wave is waste motion — it re-discovers at integration time what the contract should have made impossible at design time. Front-loading all rigor into the contract pays verification cost once, top-down, instead of N times bottom-up, and removes the slowest serial phase entirely.
- **One acceptance test, at the root, written by the user — delete the internal gates** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete every intermediate integration test in the tree. Keep exactly one verification artifact: the root acceptance criterion the human stated as intent. The swarm runs free with zero internal gates; the only question ever asked is 'does the finished artifact satisfy the original intent?' Internal correctness is irrelevant if the whole thing works. **Why better:** Node-level tests verify the PLAN's idea of correctness, not the USER's — you can pass every internal test and still build the wrong thing, the actual failure mode of code factories. Collapsing verification to the single root intent test means you can never be 'green but wrong,' and removes the overhead of authoring/maintaining tests for internal seams nobody ships.
- **Stitching is the ONLY creative act — done by one architect at the end** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the labor split. Today leaves are 'creative' and stitch is 'mechanical.' Flip it: leaves are dumb, near-templated, deterministic fills (the boring part); the genuine intelligence is reserved for a single human-grade architect pass that assembles them, where all the real design judgment lives. Stitch is the headline event, not the cleanup. **Why better:** Calling stitch 'mechanical' is the system's central lie — integration is exactly where emergent, cross-cutting bugs and design incoherence live (auth crossing 5 floors, shared state, naming drift). Treating it as the high-judgment phase puts the best model's attention where the real risk is, instead of on leaves a template could fill.
- **Merge verification into graphify: 'verified' = the two graphs are isomorphic** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no separate 'verification' aspect — it is graphify run twice (once as contract, once as as-built) with a diff, run as a live conformance daemon on every commit. 'Verified' simply means the as-built graph is isomorphic to the contract graph; a node cannot be green if its as-built shape (call edges, schema, exports, dependencies) deviates from what its contract promised, even if every test passes. A single graph-equality check is the whole stage. **Why better:** Maintaining verification as its own stage with its own tools duplicates what graphify already knows — both graphs already exist, so the only honest question is whether they match. Collapsing verification into a graph diff removes a whole conceptual surface and makes 'is it done?' a deterministic structural comparison instead of a flaky test gauntlet.

### Two-model routing & failover

_Current assumption: Codex=explicit, Claude=fuzzy; both build; quota failover._

**Boldest reframe:** Per-token Mixture-of-Experts — route every TOKEN, not every leaf: both models decode the same leaf simultaneously and a cheap arbiter blends/switches mid-generation (Codex carries the deterministic loop body, Claude takes over the instant code touches a fuzzy product decision, then hands back), so each artifact is WOVEN from two models in a single pass rather than stitched from two whole outputs.

- **Per-node model badge is a recorded decision, not a vibe** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When the router assigns a node to Claude or Codex, persist the reason (fuzzy-arch vs explicit-leaf, or 'failover from X at HH:MM') as a one-line field on the node in the manifest. The cockpit badge reads that field instead of re-deriving it. **Why better:** Treating routing as a recorded, auditable decision per node (vs an implicit 'Codex=explicit, Claude=fuzzy' property) lets you debug bad routes, replay them, and show the user WHY a node went where — turning a hidden heuristic into something you can inspect and correct.
- **Explicitness score as the route trigger, not model identity** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Compute a cheap explicitness score per node (has a frozen signature? failing test? golden template? word-count of ambiguity?) and route on the score crossing a threshold. Model assignment falls out of the score. **Why better:** A score makes the fuzzy/explicit boundary tunable, measurable against outcomes, and consistent across runs — and it directly reuses the contracts already frozen top-down (a leaf with a failing test IS explicit by definition) instead of an intuited label.
- **Pre-flight quota probe before the swarm spends** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Right before the parallel build fans out, do one tiny health/quota call to both Claude and Codex and stamp the result on the run. If one is near cap, the scope-preview gate shows it and pre-biases routing away from the strained model. **Why better:** Turns failover from a reactive mid-flight scramble (which wastes a worker process and a retry cycle) into a planning input, so the swarm is sized to the budget actually available before any spend.
- **Failover is per-node, with a sticky cool-down** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a node hits a quota/rate error on model A, fail just THAT node over to model B and mark A in a short cool-down (e.g. 2 min) so sibling workers don't all stampede the same wall. After cool-down, routing returns to the score-based default. **Why better:** A single global 'we're now on Codex' flip wastes the healthy model's capacity and can cascade. Per-node failover with a cool-down keeps both models working in parallel, drains the strained one gracefully, and recovers automatically — strictly more throughput than an all-or-nothing switch.
- **Cross-model review mandatory on the seam, optional in the middle** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Concrete rule: whoever BUILT a node, the OTHER model REVIEWS it — but only enforce this at contract seams (where two subtrees stitch) and at leaves touching schema/auth/payments. Interior leaves get cheaper same-model self-check. **Why better:** Uniform cross-model review is expensive everywhere and weak where it matters. Targeting the second opinion at the exact places integration bugs hide buys most of the safety for a fraction of the token cost.
- **Golden template carries a 'preferred builder' hint per stack** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each per-stack golden template gets a metadata header naming which model has historically one-shotted that stack best (Codex for tight typed TS leaves, Claude for prose/config/glue). The router uses it as a tiebreaker when the explicitness score is ambiguous. **Why better:** Gives the router something to break mid-range ties on besides the coarse fuzzy/explicit label — an empirical hint co-located with the template the worker already reads, so routing learns from what actually worked with zero new infrastructure.
- **A 'route receipt' line in every worker's output** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every headless worker (claude -p / codex exec) ends by emitting a structured one-liner: node id, model used, was-it-a-failover, tokens, pass/fail, duration. The orchestrator parses these into the manifest as the canonical routing/cost log. **Why better:** Replaces the Codex adapter's lossy 'last 20 stdout lines' summary with a machine-readable receipt — giving the cockpit accurate token/failover badges, making per-model cost real instead of estimated, and supplying the substrate any future routing-quality analysis needs.
- **Budget split with a shared overflow pool, not two silos** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the $200 Claude + $100 Codex as one $300 run budget with soft per-model reservations. When one model's reservation is exhausted but the other has slack, an overflow pool lets routing keep going on the cheaper-available model up to the global cap. **Why better:** Hard per-model quotas can stall a run with money left on the table on the other provider. A shared pool with soft reservations maximizes how much real work a fixed dollar budget buys per run, while still preventing either model from being silently drained to zero.
- **Per-TOKEN Mixture-of-Experts — weave the leaf from two models in one pass** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop routing whole leaves. Both models decode the same leaf simultaneously and a cheap arbiter blends/switches mid-generation — Codex carries the deterministic loop body, Claude takes over the moment code touches a fuzzy product decision, then hands control back. The artifact is woven from two models in a single pass, not stitched from two outputs. **Why better:** Picking a model per leaf is already a quantization error — most real leaves are 80% mechanical with 3 fuzzy lines. Per-token routing extracts the best of both on the exact spans where each wins, instead of paying Claude prices for boilerplate or Codex's literalism on judgment calls.
- **Self-discovered router — the routing policy is itself a forged artifact** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop the hardcoded 'Claude=fuzzy, Codex=explicit' prior. For the first hour of a campaign, send a sample of leaves to BOTH models, judge cross-model, and let the factory LEARN its own routing policy for THIS codebase/stack/user-taste. The router becomes a tiny model trained on the system's own win/loss telemetry per node-type. **Why better:** A hand-coded prior is a guess that ossifies; the optimal split differs per project and drifts as models update. Self-discovered routing re-routes automatically when one model keeps losing on a kind of leaf, turning as-built telemetry into the routing signal.
- **Adversarial twin-build — build high-stakes nodes with BOTH models and make them fight** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For high-stakes nodes, don't route — DUPLICATE. Claude and Codex each build the leaf to the same frozen contract in separate worktrees, each reviews the OTHER's implementation against the failing tests, and a judge merges the union of their bug-catches. The disagreement surface IS the QA signal. **Why better:** Failover treats the second model as a backup; this treats it as an adversary. Two strong models disagreeing on the same spec is the most reliable bug signal — free, high-precision verification you throw away when you route to only one. 2x cost on the spine, but the spine is where defects are catastrophic.
- **Quota as a planning input that SHAPES the plan-tree, not a runtime fallback** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Lift failover into the plan-tree. Before spend, the factory forecasts total Claude-tokens vs Codex-tokens the campaign needs, prices it against the remaining $200/$100, and shapes the tree to fit the wallet — collapsing leaves, biasing mechanical work to Codex, reserving Claude headroom for the fuzzy spine. Altitude dial and quota become one knob. **Why better:** Reactive failover makes you discover you're out of Claude mid-build and scramble. Quota as a first-class planning constraint lets the scope gate show 'this costs 140k Claude-tokens, you have 90k — here's the auto-rebalanced plan that fits,' turning a runtime failure into an upfront visible tradeoff.
- **A third 'referee' model that only breaks ties and never builds** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Add a permanent non-building model: a cheap fast judge that arbitrates Claude-vs-Codex disagreements, decides which twin-build output to keep, and owns the 'is this leaf actually Codex-ready?' classification. Structurally forbidden from writing code, so no skin in the game and no style bias. **Why better:** Today the builder self-judges or one builder judges the other — both conflicted. A dedicated neutral referee removes 'model grades its own homework' and makes the recursion-stop (leaf-readiness) decision consistent instead of a side-effect of whichever model looked at the node.
- **Failover across PROVIDERS and MODALITIES, not just two coding models** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Generalize failover to the whole tool fabric: Claude rate-limited → Codex; both coding quotas gone → local open model for mechanical leaves; art leaf → failover across fal.ai backbones (Kling/Flux); research leaf → across search providers. One unified resilience layer for every kind of work the factory does. **Why better:** The factory builds 'anything decomposable' — apps, art, research, decks. Scoping failover to two coding models is a category error: a campaign dies the moment its art-gen provider 429s though both LLMs are healthy. A modality-agnostic failover mesh matches the actual breadth of the subject.
- **Speculative pre-building — cheap model drafts ahead of the wave, strong model only verifies** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Run the cheap/fast model AHEAD of the green wave, speculatively drafting every leaf the instant its contract freezes (like CPU branch prediction). The expensive model then mostly VERIFIES-and-accepts a correct draft (cheap) and fully re-builds only the rare leaves the cheap model got wrong. **Why better:** Failover assumes you build each leaf once with a backup. Speculative execution flips the economics: dominant cost becomes cheap drafting + cheap verification, expensive builds reserved for genuine misses — strong-model quality at near-cheap-model cost, and the wave climbs faster because drafts are already waiting.
- **Router as a difficulty ladder with a confidence meter — escalate, don't pre-pick** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make routing graduated escalation. A leaf starts on the cheapest capable model; if its tests don't go green in N attempts, it auto-escalates to the next rung (cheap → Codex → Claude → twin-build → human), carrying the failed attempts as context. The system spends exactly as much intelligence as the leaf actually demanded. **Why better:** Deciding difficulty up-front from a static heuristic is wrong on the leaves that matter — the ones that LOOK easy but aren't. An escalation ladder is self-correcting: it can't permanently mis-route a sneaky-hard leaf and never overpays for a genuinely easy one. Difficulty is discovered, not guessed.
- **Kill the vendor stereotype — route by measured per-node-type win-rate leaderboard** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop 'Codex=explicit / Claude=fuzzy.' Every leaf and judge call logs which model won (passed contracts first, fewest revisions, cheapest). The router becomes a living leaderboard keyed by node-type (T0 component, schema, test-author, judge, art-frontend) that re-learns who actually wins, ignoring vendor claims. **Why better:** The fuzzy/explicit split is 2026 folklore that goes stale the moment either model ships an update. Empirical win-rate self-corrects and discovers non-obvious specializations (e.g. Claude may actually win explicit React leaves) a static rule will never find.
- **The work-unit is a configured AGENT, not a raw model** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Route over (model + system-prompt + tool-set + context-budget) tuples, not bare models. 'Codex-the-leaf-builder-with-tight-context' and 'Codex-the-cross-reviewer-with-full-tree' are DIFFERENT routable workers that happen to share a vendor. **Why better:** Conflating 'which vendor' with 'which job' can't express that the same model is great at one role and bad at another. Agent-as-unit makes prompt/context/tooling first-class routing levers — often a bigger quality delta than the model swap itself.
- **Race, don't fail over — N-model speculative ensemble per leaf** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For high-stakes or ambiguous leaves, dispatch BOTH models simultaneously and keep the first to pass the frozen contract/tests; discard the loser. 'Failover' becomes the degenerate case of 'always raced two, one finished.' **Why better:** Reactive failover pays a full latency penalty on every failure (build, fail, detect, re-dispatch). Racing collapses tail latency and makes a quota wall or hung process a non-event because the other runner is already producing — and power scales with processes, which the orchestrator already wants.
- **Disagreement-triggered review — reviewer is the non-builder, third opinion only on conflict** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Assign the reviewer to be whichever model did NOT build the node, and escalate to a third opinion ONLY when the two disagree. Cross-model review fires on conflict signal, not on every node. **Why better:** A fixed reviewer hierarchy spends review budget uniformly and shares blind spots when one model both builds and judges similar nodes. Disagreement-triggered review concentrates the expensive cross-check exactly where the two models see the contract differently — the only place a second opinion has information.
- **Confidence-gated solo vs. council — let the model decline the job** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before building a leaf, the assigned worker self-scores 'is this contract explicit enough for me to one-shot?' High confidence → solo build; low score bumps the node back up the tree for more contract-freezing OR triggers a two-model council. The router reads self-reported confidence as a routing input. **Why better:** The model knows better than the external dispatcher whether a leaf is actually Codex-ready. Letting it decline cheaply prevents the expensive failure-then-failover loop and feeds the recursion's stop condition directly, instead of deciding difficulty statically from outside.
- **Heterogeneous swarm-of-many, not a duopoly** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Generalize 'two models' to a fleet of N heterogeneous workers — multiple Claude tiers, multiple Codex/GPT tiers, and cheap local/open models for trivial T0 leaves and mechanical stitches. The router picks the cheapest model that clears each node's difficulty bar, reserving the expensive frontier pair for the fuzzy top. **Why better:** A strict duopoly overpays — it sends a frontier model to rename-a-variable leaves. A graduated fleet matches model cost to node difficulty, freeing the $300 frontier budget for the architecture/judgment nodes where it moves quality, and adds cheap parallel throughput on the boring majority of leaves.
- **Contracts as the only arbiter — make routing model-blind at the leaf** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Push so much rigor into the frozen contract (typed signatures + failing tests + golden template) that WHICH model built a leaf becomes invisible: any worker that turns the suite green is accepted, no model-quality assumption needed. The router optimizes only cost and speed; quality is enforced by the gate, not the routing choice. **Why better:** Routing-by-model-strength is a proxy for 'will this come out correct?' If the contract verifies correctness mechanically, the proxy is redundant and the fuzzy/explicit framing dissolves — and it future-proofs against model churn: new models plug into the same green-wave gate with zero router retuning.
- **Bid-based dispatch — a resource auction, not a hardcoded type map (Borg/Mesos)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each node posts its spec to a market; both models read just the contract and 'bid' a (confidence, est-cost, est-latency) tuple, and the orchestrator awards the leaf to the best bid. The model→node-type mapping becomes emergent and per-node instead of a global stereotype. **Why better:** Big schedulers (Borg, Mesos, K8s) abandoned pinning workloads to machine types because static maps waste capacity and mis-route edge cases. A real explicit leaf may still be Claude's strength on a given codebase; bidding routes on actual fit, not category prejudice.
- **Hedged requests on the tail — fire the backup model past p95 (Google 'tail at scale')** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't double-build everything — only hedge. If a leaf's build exceeds the p95 time for its tier, fire the SECOND model as a backup request and take the first to go green. Latency insurance triggered by the timing distribution, not by failure. **Why better:** A tiny fraction of hedged requests collapses the long tail that dominates a swarm's wall-clock. Pure failover waits for a hard error/quota wall; a model that's merely SLOW or spinning never trips it and silently stalls the whole green wave. Hedging fixes the stall-without-error case for ~2-5% extra spend.
- **Circuit breaker + bulkhead per model and per node-type (Hystrix/Temporal)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Track per-model health as a rolling failure/refusal/timeout rate per node-TYPE. When Claude's failure rate on, say, migration leaves crosses a threshold, OPEN that circuit and route migration leaves to Codex while leaving other lanes untouched — then half-open to probe recovery. Bulkhead so one model drowning in one lane can't starve the rest. **Why better:** Current failover is binary and global (out of quota → switch everything). A model can be healthy-but-bad-at-X: refusing, looping, producing red tests without ever erroring. Per-lane circuit breakers route around localized incompetence the quota signal is blind to.
- **Builder/reviewer split as a hard gate — VFX dailies, the maker never approves** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow film's rule that the artist who made a shot never approves it — a supervisor does. Whatever model built a leaf, the OTHER model MUST sign off against the contract before the node turns green and the wave can climb. Routing isn't just who-builds, it's an enforced builder/reviewer split. **Why better:** The spec treats 'Codex reviews cross-model' as optional flavor. Making model-diversity on the review edge a gate (not a courtesy) catches a class of self-blind-spot errors single-model self-grading structurally cannot — and it's nearly free since the other model is idle anyway.
- **Evolutionary A/B routing — learn the map from outcomes, explore on purpose** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Log every (node-fingerprint, model, outcome: green/red/cost/turns) and let a tiny bandit/genetic policy evolve the routing function over runs. Occasionally route a node to the 'wrong' model on purpose (exploration) to keep the map from ossifying as the models themselves change. **Why better:** Bandits beat fixed heuristics in NON-STATIONARY environments — and model capability is wildly non-stationary; every release reshuffles who's better at what. A hand-coded rule is stale the day a new model ships; a learned policy self-corrects and even discovers that Claude now wins a leaf class nobody predicted.
- **Andon cord — any worker can halt and pull in the other model as the fixer** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every worker a Toyota-style andon cord: when it detects it's stuck (repeated red tests, contradictory contract, can't satisfy a signature), it STOPS that station and escalates the exact failing leaf to the other model as a targeted rescue, with the stuck context attached. **Why better:** Failover today is triggered by an external resource wall (quota/crash). The TPS insight is that the WORKER knows it's stuck long before the system does, and stopping early beats shipping a defect downstream. The stuck-context makes the rescue a continuation, not a cold restart.
- **Saga-style compensating rollback when a model poisons a subtree (Temporal/Airflow)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat each model's work on a node as a saga step with a compensating action. If the other model's review or a parent stitch reveals the first model corrupted a shared contract/interface, automatically compensate (revert that worktree's commits) and re-dispatch the subtree — ideally to the reviewing model — rather than letting bad work propagate up the green wave. **Why better:** Quota failover only handles 'can't continue.' Sagas handle the scarier case: a model that confidently finishes a leaf wrong and contaminates its siblings — which the bottom-up stitch would otherwise amplify — by unwinding its side effects so a partial failure can't leave the graph inconsistent.
- **Cell-differentiation routing — a cheap model 'types' the leaf, the specialist expresses it** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Split routing into two cheap stages like stem-cell differentiation: a fast cheap pass reads the leaf and emits a 'cell type' + difficulty signal; that signal — not a static rule — picks the expensive builder model AND its skill attachment. The classifier is the morphogen gradient; the builders are differentiated tissue. **Why better:** Biology never has a stem cell guess its own fate from a hardcoded table — a cheap external signal differentiates it. Folding routing into the expensive builder wastes premium tokens on a classify decision and bakes prejudice into the costly step. A dedicated cheap typing pass routes more accurately AND cheaper, and naturally co-selects the right skill per node-type.
- **Delete the second model — the CONTRACT is the adversary** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill cross-model entirely. What catches bugs isn't different neuron weights — it's the failing tests + frozen typed contract + golden template the factory already produces. Run a single model where the contract, not a sibling LLM, is the relentless critic: green tests or it didn't build. The 'different model avoids blind spots' claim is an untested article of faith; the verifiable adversary is the spec. **Why better:** Removes an entire billing account, CLI, prompt dialect, the agentType:'codex'+schema crash, and config.toml landmines. If the contract is truly frozen and executable, a same-model run against a red/green wall catches more than a vibe-based 'fresh eyes' critic that rubber-stamps anyway. One throat to choke, half the ops surface.
- **Invert the role assignment — Codex architects the top, Claude builds the leaves** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The 'Claude=fuzzy/architect, Codex=explicit/builder' split is assigned by reputation, never measured. Flip it: the model with stronger long-horizon code-structure reasoning owns the plan-tree, the model with cleaner one-shot codegen-to-spec owns the leaves — and pick which is which from EVAL DATA on YOUR repo, not from priors. **Why better:** The current split is a guess dressed as a law. If the eval says the opposite, you've been routing every node to the wrong worker. Data-derived assignment per task-type beats a hard-coded folklore split that could be exactly backwards.
- **Call it what it is — a quota-arbitrage spillover queue, not a 'router'** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Strip the pretense. 'Quota failover between Claude $200 + Codex $100' reveals the real driver is BUDGET, not quality. So build a dumb spillover queue: run everything on the better model until its quota is exhausted, then spill the BACKLOG (not a curated split) to the cheaper one. No per-node model judgment at all. **Why better:** A cost-driven system disguised as a quality-driven one makes you optimize the wrong axis (clever routing) instead of the right one (don't run out of quota mid-swarm). A spillover queue is trivial, has no 'which model for this node' decision to get wrong, and is honest about what it is.
- **Merge routing INTO the altitude dial — model choice is just another tier output** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There should be no separate 'router' aspect. The altitude classifier already decides T0..T3 scope per node. Make model selection a column of that same classification — one classifier emits {tier, model, skill, budget} as a single decision — not a parallel system with its own logic, badges, and failover state. **Why better:** Two overlapping decision systems (altitude + router) both inspecting each node is redundant computation and two places for the assignment to drift out of sync. Folding model into the tier decision removes a whole aspect from the cockpit and guarantees model/scope/skill stay coherent.
- **Same model twice with hostile scaffolding beats two polite different models** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — The danger isn't shared weights — it's shared CONTEXT and a cooperative frame. Run the SAME model as builder and reviewer, but give the reviewer a cold-start context (no build transcript), an explicitly adversarial 'find the failure that ships a bug' prompt, and a bounty: scored on breaks-found, not agreements. Self-critique with amnesia + incentive, not a foreign brand. **Why better:** 'Different model = no blind spots' conflates model identity with context independence. A fresh-context, incentivized same-model critic removes the cross-vendor tax (two CLIs, two quotas, two bug surfaces) while plausibly catching more than a friendly foreign model that defaults to 'looks good.' Cheaper and meaner.
- **Forbid mid-run failover — pin one model per run, size the run to its quota** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A plan-tree where Codex built the leaves and then Codex dies (Claude finishes in a different style) yields an incoherent half-dialect artifact. Forbid mid-run failover entirely: pick ONE model for an entire run at the scope gate, size the run to fit that model's remaining quota, and never switch. No failover machinery at all. **Why better:** Mid-run failover introduces stylistic seams, re-verification cost, and 'who built this leaf' ambiguity right when you can least afford it. Pinning one model per run and pre-checking quota at the gate deletes the entire failover subsystem and produces a coherent single-author artifact.
- **Don't route by model — route by SKILL, let each skill own its model** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The factory already attaches skills per node-type (impeccable, artlab, improve-codebase-architecture, grill-me, tower-ship-gate). Push the model decision DOWN into each skill where the expertise about which model fits that job already lives. The orchestrator never thinks Claude-vs-Codex; it dispatches a skill, and the skill knows. **Why better:** A central router needs global knowledge of every task-type's model fit — exactly the knowledge that already lives, locally and more accurately, inside each skill. Decentralizing kills the router as a top-level concern and lets model choice evolve per-skill without touching orchestration.
- **Two is the worst number — go to 1, or to an N-model tournament, never exactly 2** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — 'Exactly two' is enough complexity for a router but not enough diversity to vote. Either collapse to 1 (cheapest, fastest, contract-verified) OR go to a true ensemble where 3+ cheap models build the same leaf and the contract/tests auto-elect the survivor — no human-assigned roles, no 'meaning vs proof' theater, just whoever passes. **Why better:** The 2-model split forces an artificial meaning/proof role division to justify the second model. A tournament needs zero role assignment — the test suite is the judge — and gives genuine diversity; collapsing to 1 gives genuine simplicity. Two gives the ceremony of an ensemble with none of the statistical benefit.

### Autonomous orchestrator & parallelism

_Current assumption: a main agent spawns headless worker processes in worktrees off a manifest; no human mid-run._

**Boldest reframe:** The factory is a continuous foundry, not a run — kill the discrete "run" entirely and make the orchestrator a never-sleeping standing army that perpetually re-derives the whole Tower from intent, so nudging the intent reflows the affected subtree of reality in seconds like a spreadsheet recalcing a cell. This is the boldest because it doesn't just restructure the orchestrator — it abolishes the most fundamental premise shared by ALL the other ideas (that "a build" is a bounded event with a start and end). Every other reframe still assumes you kick something off; this one makes building a permanent ambient state of the system, collapsing build-time, sync-state, and "is it done?" into a single living organism. It subsumes ghost workers, speculative builds, and content caching as mere emergent behaviors of an always-warm swarm.

- **Lease the node, not the worktree** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Port ArtLab's existing per-slot slot-leases/*.lease.json mechanism up to the plan-tree: every leaf node a worker claims gets a TTL'd lease file in the manifest dir before any process spawns. The orchestrator never dispatches a node with a live lease, and crash-reconcile releases stale leases the same way ArtLab already does at >10min. **Why better:** The current 'spawn off a manifest' assumption is silent about two workers grabbing the same node on a retry or restart. ArtLab already solved exactly this for image slots — reusing the proven lease+reconcile pattern gives idempotent dispatch for near-free instead of inventing duplicate-spend protection from scratch.
- **Heartbeat-or-reap, copied straight from ArtLab** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each headless worker writes a progress.json heartbeat every 10s (as ArtLab runs already do); the orchestrator reaps any worker whose heartbeat goes stale past a threshold, releases its node lease, and re-dispatches. A hung claude -p or codex exec can't silently stall the whole green wave. **Why better:** The bare 'no human mid-run' assumption is dangerous precisely because there's no human to notice a wedged worker. A heartbeat reaper is the cheapest possible liveness guarantee and the codebase already has the exact reconcileCrashedRuns machinery to lift.
- **Concurrency dial wired to live quota, not a fixed cap** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the static parallelism limit (ArtLab caps at 2) with a dynamic worker-pool size the orchestrator recomputes each tick from remaining Claude/Codex quota and observed token burn rate — scale up when both budgets are flush, throttle toward 1 as either model nears its cap so failover still has headroom. **Why better:** 'More processes = more power' is only true until you exhaust a budget mid-wave and strand half-built nodes. Tying fan-out to live quota keeps the swarm as wide as it can safely be without the run dying with leaves half-done.
- **Dry-run dispatch plan before any process spawns** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The orchestrator first emits the full dispatch schedule — which nodes go to which model, in what wave order, with a token/cost estimate per wave — and the cockpit renders it as a preview the same way ArtLab gates concept spend behind its brief-review gate. The actual spawn is one confirm away. **Why better:** Right now the scope-preview gate only covers altitude/tier, not the parallel dispatch itself. Showing the literal worker assignment before spend turns 'fully autonomous, hope it's right' into 'autonomous after you glance at the plan' — same no-human-mid-run promise, far cheaper mistakes.
- **Append-only events.jsonl as the orchestrator's single source of truth (durable, replayable log)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the orchestrator a deterministic state machine driven by one append-only events.jsonl per run (ArtLab already logs per-run): every dispatch, completion, failure, lease grab, and failover is an event. The orchestrator's in-memory state is a pure fold over that log, and the manifest becomes a projection of the log rather than the source of truth — so a killed-and-restarted main agent rebuilds exact swarm state by replay, never re-spawning completed leaves or losing worktrees. **Why better:** The current model implies the main agent holds swarm state in memory; if it dies mid-swarm (say at leaf 27 of 30), the worktrees are an ambiguous source of truth and the run risks a cold restart. Temporal's core insight — durable execution via replay — makes the coordinator itself crash-safe, which matters precisely because zero humans are watching to restart it manually.
- **Bounded auto-retry with cross-model escalation built into dispatch** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a worker's node fails its contract tests, the orchestrator auto-retries on a fixed ladder — same model once, then escalate to the other model (Codex leaf failing → hand to Claude to re-architect the leaf, and vice versa) — capped at N attempts before parking the node as a blocker, mirroring ArtLab's 'auto-regenerate up to 3x then escalate'. **Why better:** The two-model router today is framed as build-vs-judge and quota failover; it doesn't say what happens when a leaf just won't pass. A bounded retry-then-escalate ladder turns flaky leaves into self-healing nodes instead of stalling the green wave or requiring the human the design swore it wouldn't need.
- **Worktree janitor + lifecycle owned by the orchestrator** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The orchestrator owns worktree creation AND teardown: it provisions one git worktree per active leaf, garbage-collects worktrees for completed/abandoned nodes each tick, and refuses to spawn if the worktree count or disk would blow a ceiling. Stitch consumes from worktrees, then the janitor prunes them. **Why better:** 'Workers in git worktrees' is stated as a fact but a long fan-out run silently accumulates dozens of stale worktrees and branches that wedge git and fill disk. Making lifecycle a first-class orchestrator job keeps a multi-hour autonomous run from drowning in its own scaffolding.
- **Wave barriers / dailies gate so the stitch can't climb past unfrozen contracts** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Enforce an explicit barrier between tree levels: no node at level N is dispatched until every contract at level N+1 (its children) is frozen and their failing tests exist; the green wave only climbs a level once that level's siblings are all green. At each barrier, borrow the VFX 'dailies' ritual — auto-assemble the partial artifact and run a fast automated screening (smoke render, screenshot diff, type-check, contract-conformance) before the next level's workers spawn. Make the barrier a checkable invariant the cockpit displays; bad takes kick back to a single leaf, never propagate upward. **Why better:** The bottom-up build + mechanical stitch only works if contracts are truly frozen top-down before builders touch a node; without an explicit barrier an eager worker builds against a still-moving contract and the stitch silently mis-merges. VFX pipelines learned integration defects are catastrophic if caught at final composite — screening every stage turns a provably-ordered build and an expensive full-rebuild into a single re-spawn.
- **Critical-path scheduler, not a flat parallelism cap (Airflow/PERT)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace 'spawn up to N workers' with a critical-path scheduler that computes the longest dependency chain through the plan-tree and prioritizes those leaves first, giving them the best model and earliest slot. Off-critical-path leaves fill idle capacity. The cockpit highlights the critical path in red like a Gantt chart. **Why better:** A flat cap treats all leaves as equal, so the swarm can finish 28 cheap leaves while the one 6-deep contract chain that gates the stitch sits in the queue. Manufacturing/PERT scheduling says wall-clock time is set by the critical path alone — optimizing for it can halve end-to-end time with the same worker budget.
- **Work-stealing scheduler with leaf-size estimation (Cilk/Tokio runtime)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of a fixed N-worker pool draining a central queue, give each worker its own deque and let idle workers steal from the back of busy workers' deques. Pair it with a learned estimator of leaf 'size' (token cost / wall-time) from past runs so big leaves are split or front-loaded and the swarm self-balances without a central dispatcher bottleneck. **Why better:** A central manifest dispatcher is a single point of contention and can leave fast workers idle while one chokes on a heavy leaf. Work-stealing (Cilk, Go, Tokio) is the proven mechanism for near-optimal load balancing under irregular task sizes — and AI leaves are wildly irregular, so the payoff is large with little added machinery.
- **Supervision tree with restart strategies (Erlang/OTP let-it-crash)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Organize workers into an OTP-style supervision tree: each subtree has a supervisor process with a declared restart policy (restart-one, restart-all-siblings, escalate-up). A worker that hangs or returns garbage is killed and restarted with a fresh prompt/seed; repeated failures escalate to the parent supervisor, which can re-plan that subtree rather than retry blindly. **Why better:** ArtLab today has flat orthogonal blockers and a global retry+backoff. Erlang's let-it-crash with hierarchical supervisors localizes failure: one flaky leaf doesn't poison the run, and an unfixable subtree triggers a re-plan at exactly the right altitude instead of failing the whole factory or silently degrading. Fault-tolerance becomes structural, not ad hoc.
- **Content-addressed action cache (Bazel/Turborepo remote cache)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hash every leaf node by its frozen contract + input file fingerprints + worker/model identity, and store the resulting artifact in a content-addressed cache. Before a worker spawns, the orchestrator checks the cache: identical contract hash = instant cache hit, no process spawned. Cross-run and cross-user, so building the same 'auth form leaf' twice is free. **Why better:** The current assumption respawns a worker for every node on every run. Bazel/Turborepo prove the biggest speedup in any build graph is NOT running the work at all. Since contracts are frozen top-down, the cache key is already perfectly defined — most rebuilds touch only a few leaves, so a swarm becomes mostly cache hits and only the genuinely-changed subtree gets compute.
- **Rent the orchestrator — express the build as a durable workflow on an existing engine** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Delete the bespoke 'main agent spawns headless processes in worktrees' machinery and express the build as a durable workflow on an engine the repo already ships (Vercel Workflow / WDK plus Supabase cron/queues). Steps, retries, pause/resume, SIGTERM cancel, idempotent dispatch, crash-safety, and fan-out are the engine's job; agents are just step bodies. **Why better:** Crash recovery, idempotent dispatch, and parallelism limits are EXACTLY what ArtLab's daemon hand-rolled and is hard to get right. A durable-execution engine gives all of that for free with a battle-tested observability surface the cockpit can read instead of re-implement — building our own orchestrator reinvents a category of solved, ownable infrastructure.
- **The factory is a continuous foundry, not a run** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill the concept of a discrete 'run' entirely. The orchestrator is a never-sleeping standing army of hundreds of headless workers that hum 24/7, perpetually re-deriving the entire Tower from intent. You don't 'kick off a build' — you nudge the intent, and the always-warm swarm reflows the affected subtree of reality within seconds, the way a spreadsheet recalcs a cell. **Why better:** The current 'spawn off a manifest, finish, tear down' model has cold-start latency and treats building as an event. A continuous foundry makes the factory a live organism where artifacts are always in sync with intent — there is no 'is the build done?' because the build never stops.
- **Workers that spawn workers — fractal self-recursion** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Any worker, on discovering its leaf is actually a subtree, gets full authority to spawn its own child swarm in nested worktrees and become a sub-orchestrator. The org chart of processes grows and prunes itself to match the true shape of the problem, with no fixed depth. The factory discovers its own headcount. **Why better:** The current model has ONE main agent dispatching flat workers off a pre-frozen manifest — depth is decided up front by the plan-tree. Fractal self-recursion means the process topology IS the plan-tree, discovered live, so a leaf that turns out to be a T3 app doesn't break the run, it just blossoms more workers.
- **Speculative multiverse build — fork reality, race outcomes, keep the winner** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For every meaningfully ambiguous node, the orchestrator doesn't pick one approach — it forks N parallel worktrees that each build a DIFFERENT plausible interpretation/architecture simultaneously (CPU branch-prediction down both contract branches when a parent is unresolved), then a judge swarm races the green-test-waves and merges only the universe that wins. Throw away the losing universes. **Why better:** The current orchestrator commits to one frozen contract and one build path, so a wrong top-level guess is expensive to unwind and workers idle while the architect decides. CPUs hit >95% branch-prediction accuracy and never stall the pipeline; speculative multiverse spends compute (cheap, parallel) to buy certainty (precious) — you never bet the whole run on a single architectural coin-flip, especially near the top where one decision gates dozens of leaves.
- **The orchestrator hires temporary cloud workers — elastic to 10,000 processes** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When the plan-tree is wide, the main agent doesn't cap at your two local subscriptions — it bursts hundreds-to-thousands of ephemeral headless workers across rented cloud sandboxes for the duration of the green wave, then releases them. Scope size dials raw parallelism, not just tier classification. **Why better:** The current assumption is bounded by 'more processes = more power' on one machine with two quotas ($200 Claude + $100 Codex). Elastic cloud burst makes the altitude dial a literal throttle on a small datacenter — a whole-app T3 build finishes in the wall-clock time of a single leaf because every leaf builds at once.
- **Pre-emptive ghost workers that build before you ask** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The orchestrator watches the intent stream and predictively pre-builds the most-likely-next nodes on idle compute BEFORE the plan-tree even reaches them, holding finished artifacts in a speculative cache. When the green wave arrives, those nodes are already done — like a CPU's branch predictor for an entire app. **Why better:** The current model is strictly reactive: a node only gets a worker once the manifest marks it ready. Ghost workers hide latency completely by spending idle cycles on probable futures, so the perceived build time collapses toward zero for anything predictable.
- **A persistent veteran crew with named identities, tenure, and routing reputations** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Workers aren't anonymous disposable processes — they're a stable named cast (tied to the Tower's existing C-suite characters) that persist across runs, accumulate per-node-type domain tenure, remember their past leaves, and get routed to nodes in territory they 'own.' Each worker (and each model, Claude vs Codex) accrues a reputation from its as-built verification results — who one-shots auth leaves, who's strongest at GSAP motion, who keeps failing migrations — and the board routes each node to the highest-reputation available worker, reputations persisting across runs. The CRO-worker always builds War Room nodes and gets smarter at them over months. **Why better:** The current workers are stateless and identical, re-learning the codebase every spawn, and the two-model router is a static rule that wastes spend sending Codex to architect fuzzy tops and Claude to grind explicit leaves. Persistent reputation-routed veterans turn the router into a learning system that gets measurably better at dispatch every run — and the graphify as-built verification you already plan to run becomes the free training signal, while fusing the orchestrator's internal staff with the building's sacred visible cast.
- **Adversarial co-execution: a saboteur worker permanently embedded in the swarm** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — For every build worker, station a paired saboteur whose only job is to attack the leaf in real time — fuzz the contract boundary, inject the nastiest input, race conditions, try to make the now-passing failing-tests fail again — running concurrently rather than as a downstream review gate. A node isn't green until it survives live sabotage; build and break happen in the same instant. **Why better:** The current cross-model review is a sequential downstream pass (Codex reviews after Claude builds) and verifies at stitch time, so a leaf that passes its own tests but is subtly wrong propagates upward before anyone notices. A co-resident adversary catches contract-boundary lies the moment they're written, while context is hot and cheap to fix — turning verification from a late gate into a continuous immune response.
- **Kill the orchestrator: the manifest/graph IS the brain (blackboard, not boss)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Delete the central main agent entirely and fold orchestration into the data. The frozen contract graph IS the program — each leaf node carries its own preconditions, typed signature, failing test, template, and a claimable TTL'd lease. A dumb generic runner (any agent, even a cron) polls the board, atomically claims any node whose dependencies are green, one-shots it, and writes results back. No process 'dispatches'; gravity does. 'What to build next' becomes a query against graphify's as-built state, not a service a privileged agent runs. **Why better:** The autonomous orchestrator is the most complex, fragile, stateful component in the design and produces zero artifact — pure coordination overhead, plus a second source of truth (manifest) that drifts from the graph's as-built state. If contracts are truly frozen top-down, dispatch is a trivial topological function of the graph; deleting the orchestrator removes the one thing that can deadlock, double-dispatch, or go rogue, collapses to one authoritative model (reality), and makes the system trivially resumable by anyone from any machine.
- **The human is a worker on the bus, not a mid-run interrupt** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop 'zero human interaction mid-run' as a goal. Model the human as just another worker type that claims certain node kinds (taste calls, ambiguous specs, gate approvals). The swarm keeps building every node that doesn't depend on a human-claimed node, while human-flagged nodes sit claimable on the same board until you (async, from your phone) resolve them. The orchestrator's job becomes surfacing the 2-3 highest-leverage decision points and continuing past everything else. **Why better:** 'Sealed transaction, no humans' is batch-job thinking applied to a creative act — and ArtLab already contradicts it with two human gates that DON'T halt the world. Fully autonomous creative runs produce confident garbage; treating the human as a peer worker means the run never blocks globally on you, only the subtree that genuinely needs your taste, while everything orthogonal races ahead. One well-timed question is cheaper than a whole re-run, and you're sitting at the cockpit anyway.
- **Workers are speculative snapshots, not OS processes** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Decouple the unit-of-work from a heavyweight process+worktree. A 'worker' is a forkable context snapshot (graph state + frozen contract + scratch) that can be spawned, paused, cloned, and reverted at memory speed. Run three speculative attempts at the same leaf from one snapshot, keep the one that passes its failing tests, garbage-collect the rest. **Why better:** 'Spawn processes in worktrees' optimizes for OS isolation, but the real need is isolated context + a revertible workspace. Snapshots make speculative/branching execution cheap — you race N strategies per node and keep the winner — which is impossible when each attempt is a full process+git-worktree with startup cost. Turns parallelism from 'more boxes' into 'more bets per box.'
- **Contracts are negotiated in a live market, not frozen top-down** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Replace the frozen top-down contract with continuous negotiation: when a worker discovers its frozen interface is wrong (schema rotted, a sibling needs another field), it posts a contract-amendment bid to the board; affected neighbors auto-renegotiate, and the plan-tree heals locally without re-running the whole top-down freeze. Contracts become living treaties with versioned amendments. **Why better:** Frozen contracts assume the architect was perfectly right at the top — but mid-build discoveries are exactly when you learn the contract was wrong, and the current model's only recovery is escalate-to-human or fail. A negotiation protocol lets the swarm self-heal contract drift locally (only the affected subtree renegotiates), preserving autonomy precisely where the rigid manifest would have broken it.
- **Parallelism is an economic bid variable, not a constant** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Abolish the fixed parallelism cap. Give the run a budget (dollars + provider rate-limit headroom + a deadline) and let workers continuously bid for execution slots based on each node's expected value (critical-path weight, blast radius, retry-cost). Hot-path nodes outbid cosmetic ones; the swarm self-throttles to exactly the width the budget and rate limits allow at this instant, degrading gracefully when a provider rate-limits instead of hard-failing. **Why better:** ArtLab's 'max 2' is a static guess that's simultaneously too cautious (idle budget on a deadline) and too reckless (blows provider limits during a burst). A live bidding variable optimizes throughput-under-constraint continuously and spends compute where it changes the outcome most.
- **The conductor is an elected, disposable hat — not a designated process** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If you keep a coordinator role at all, make it ephemeral: any worker can temporarily hold the 'conductor' token, and if it dies or stalls (no heartbeat), the swarm elects a new one from whoever's idle. No process is special; the conductor is a hat anyone can wear and any worker can drop. **Why better:** ArtLab's reconciler proves the system can recover a stalled run on daemon restart — but that recovery depends on the daemon coming back. An elected, disposable conductor removes the 'main agent' as a privileged single point of failure: the swarm survives the conductor dying mid-run with no human restart, which is the whole promise of 'zero human interaction mid-run' actually delivered.
- **An internal market economy of workers bidding for nodes (blackboard auction)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Instead of a central dispatcher assigning nodes, post every ready node on a shared blackboard with its contract + altitude tags and let a heterogeneous pool of workers (Claude-architect, Codex-builder, impeccable-frontend, artlab) BID in tokens/confidence to claim the work they're best suited for. The orchestrator awards each leaf to the best-fit, cheapest qualified bidder; failover is just a re-auction, and a node nobody bids on is auto-flagged as under-specified. **Why better:** The current two-model router is a hard-coded heuristic ('Claude does fuzzy tops, Codex does explicit leaves'). A bidding market is self-tuning and emergent — routing improves as workers learn what they win at, the SKILLS-per-node-type mapping becomes dynamic, a depleted Claude quota simply means Claude stops winning bids, and silence on a node becomes a free 'this contract isn't ready' signal the dispatcher can't currently detect. It scales to many worker types without rewriting the router.
- **Zero-manifest: the swarm self-organizes by stigmergy** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete the shared manifest as a control structure. Workers coordinate ant-colony style — they leave 'pheromone' traces in the graph (this node claimed, this contract satisfied, this test green) and any worker reads the field and picks its next action. No central dispatcher, no single point of failure, no orchestrator bottleneck. **Why better:** The current design has a single main agent dispatching off a shared manifest — that agent is a bottleneck and a single point of failure for the whole run. Stigmergic self-organization removes the conductor entirely; the factory keeps building even if any node (including the 'main' one) dies, and scales without the dispatcher becoming the limit.
- **Parallelism is the bug — build strictly serial and brag about it** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert 'more processes = more power.' Run exactly one worker at a time, depth-first down the tree. Each leaf sees the FULLY BUILT context of everything before it instead of a frozen-at-plan-time contract guess. Speed comes from a faster single model and aggressive caching, not from fan-out. **Why better:** Parallel workers can only coordinate through contracts frozen before anyone has written a line — so every wrong guess at the top metastasizes across N branches simultaneously and you pay to build all of them before stitch reveals the mismatch. Serial build lets later leaves SEE earlier reality and self-correct, eliminating the entire class of 'green leaves that don't compose' failures. For a one-person factory, wall-clock rarely beats correctness, and serial is radically cheaper to debug, observe, and trust.
- **Pull, not push: a worker pool of identical agents draining a queue** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert dispatch direction. No main agent decides who does what; you start N identical headless agents that each loop 'claim the next ready leaf, build it, mark it green, repeat' against a shared lock-free queue. Scaling power = launch more drainers; the orchestrator role evaporates into a claim() primitive, and Claude-vs-Codex routing becomes a per-claim decision (claim by capability tag). **Why better:** Push-based dispatch makes the main agent a bottleneck and single point of failure holding global state about every worker. Pull-based work-stealing is how mature build/CI systems actually scale: workers are stateless and disposable, you add or kill them freely, and there's no central scheduler to crash.
- **No worktrees: build leaves in ephemeral sandboxes, never touch the repo until stitch** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the git-worktrees substrate. Workers don't get worktrees at all — each leaf is built in a throwaway sandbox/microVM (vercel:vercel-sandbox is already in the toolbox) seeded with only its contract + dependency stubs, and returns a patch. The repo only ever sees the mechanical stitch. **Why better:** Worktrees mean N agents with full repo write access running unattended — a blast-radius and merge-hell nightmare, and the source of subtle cross-contamination when one worker's uncommitted state leaks into another's view. Sandboxes enforce the contract boundary physically: a leaf literally CANNOT see anything outside its contract, making 'does it compose?' a property verified by construction rather than discovered at stitch. Cleaner isolation, safer autonomy, trivial cleanup.
- **One agent, many tabs of attention — orchestration as context-switching, not process-spawning** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Maybe the 'spawn headless OS processes' premise is wrong for this scale. A single long-lived agent with a large context window holds the entire plan-tree in working memory and round-robins its attention across ready leaves, materializing each as a focused sub-context. 'Parallelism' is the model interleaving work, not the OS forking it. **Why better:** Spawning real processes buys true CPU parallelism the factory rarely needs — the bottleneck is almost always model latency and token budget, not cores. A single agent that keeps the global plan resident never suffers the core defect of headless workers: each spawned process is amnesiac about the whole, so it can only follow the frozen contract and can't notice 'this whole branch is now obviously wrong.' In-context interleaving keeps holistic judgment alive across the entire tree, which a swarm of isolated workers structurally cannot have.

### Scope / altitude dial

_Current assumption: tiers T0-T3; auto-classify; bias bigger; scope-preview gate before spend._

**Boldest reframe:** Auto-Classify Backwards: Build the Biggest Plausible Thing, Then Ask What to Cut

- **Show the classification's reasoning, not just the tier** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When auto-classify lands on a tier, surface the one-line rationale and the top 2 signals that decided it (e.g. 'T2: touches DB schema + 3 floors'). The scope-preview gate displays this so the human corrects a misread before any spend. **Why better:** The current assumption auto-classifies silently; an opaque tier guess is the thing humans most distrust and override blindly. Showing the 'why' turns the gate from a rubber-stamp into a real correction point and catches off-by-one-tier errors cheaply.
- **One-tap tier nudge at the gate (bump up / bump down)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At the scope-preview gate, give two buttons that re-classify one tier up or down and instantly re-render the plan-tree preview at the new altitude, instead of forcing a free-text re-prompt or a full restart. **Why better:** 'Bias bigger' is a static global lean; real intent varies per request. A cheap reversible nudge respects that the human's correction is usually +/-1 tier, making the dial feel like a dial rather than a one-shot guess you have to re-argue.
- **Tier sets the spend/parallelism budget, not just the depth** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each tier carries an explicit budget envelope — max worker processes, token ceiling, wall-clock cap — that the gate shows and the orchestrator enforces. T0 spawns 1-2 workers; T3 unlocks the full swarm. **Why better:** Right now the tier only implies recursion depth, so a misclassified-big leaf could quietly drain the swarm. Binding tier to a hard budget makes 'bias bigger' safe to lean into and makes the scope-preview gate a meaningful financial decision, not just a structural one.
- **Show a worked example artifact at each tier in the gate** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The scope-preview gate displays a tiny concrete sample of what the chosen tier produces (e.g. T0 = 'a single FloorCard component', T3 = 'a full app with 6 floors + auth + DB'), drawn from a static reference set. **Why better:** Tier labels T0-T3 are abstract jargon; humans calibrate against examples, not ordinals. A worked example makes the altitude legible to a non-expert and prevents the 'I thought T2 meant the whole feature' mismatch that the current numeric scheme invites.
- **Detect and split a mixed-altitude request before classifying** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before assigning one tier, scan the intent for multiple distinct deliverables (e.g. 'build the analytics floor AND write the launch deck') and propose splitting into separate sub-factories, each with its own tier, rather than forcing one altitude over the whole thing. **Why better:** A single altitude dial assumes one homogeneous request, but real intents bundle a big build with small side-artifacts. Splitting first means each branch gets the right altitude instead of the whole job being dragged up or down to one compromise tier.
- **Confidence-gated auto-proceed: skip the gate when classification is certain** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Attach a confidence score to the auto-classify; high-confidence small jobs (T0/T1) auto-proceed with a cancellable countdown, while low-confidence or expensive jobs (T2/T3) force the explicit gate. The threshold is a single tunable knob. (Consolidates the 'Altitude Auto-Pilot' confidence-valve idea.) **Why better:** A mandatory gate on every run adds friction to the cheap, obvious cases that are the most common. Reserving the human's attention for ambiguous or expensive jobs makes the factory feel fast on trivial work without losing the safety net where it actually matters, and trains against reflexive gate-blindness.
- **Add a T-1 'edit/patch' tier below T0** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Introduce a tier beneath the component level for surgical changes to existing artifacts — rename, tweak a prop, fix a string — that skips the full plan-tree/contract machinery and routes straight to a single one-shot worker. **Why better:** The current floor (T0 component) still spins up plan-tree + contracts + tests, which is overkill for a two-line edit. A patch tier means the factory doesn't punish the most frequent real-world ask — small modifications — with disproportionate ceremony.
- **Calibrate the classifier from post-run actuals** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — After each run, log the predicted tier against what the build actually consumed (worker count, tokens, files touched, wall-clock) and surface drift so the auto-classify thresholds get retuned — starting as a simple visible report, not a black-box ML loop. **Why better:** A static auto-classifier with a fixed 'bias bigger' rule never learns that, say, ArtLab campaigns systematically land a tier high. Closing the loop with real consumption data makes the dial self-sharpening over time instead of drifting on the founder's one-time intuition.
- **The Altitude is Infinite — There Are No Tiers, Only Zoom** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill T0-T3 entirely. Scope becomes a continuous, fractal zoom — like Google Maps for ambition. The same factory that builds one button keeps zooming out through 'a feature,' 'a product,' 'a company,' 'an industry,' with no ceiling and no named tiers. You don't pick a tier; you pan and pinch a single continuous altitude axis and the plan-tree re-renders at whatever depth your viewport demands. **Why better:** Tiers are an artificial quantization of a continuous quantity. The moment you have 4 named buckets, every real request lands awkwardly between two of them and someone has to 'round.' A continuous zoom never forces a misfit and never caps the ambition — T3 'whole app' stops being the ceiling.
- **Auto-Classify Backwards: Build the Biggest Plausible Thing, Then Ask What to Cut** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the dial. Instead of classifying intent UP from a component, the factory ALWAYS first drafts the most ambitious 10x interpretation of the request (the 'maximal cone'), then the scope gate is a subtractive prune: you carve away what you DON'T want. Default is everything; you spend energy to make it smaller. **Why better:** 'Bias bigger' as currently framed is a timid nudge on a ladder that still defaults small. True bias-bigger means the default IS maximal and shrinking is the deliberate act. Users chronically under-ask; showing them the maximal cone first reveals ambition they'd never have typed.
- **The Dial Has a Past — Altitude Learns Your Personal 'Big'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Scope auto-classification is calibrated to YOU. The factory remembers that when you said 'a quick analytics view' last month you actually wanted a full sub-app, and that your 'rebuild the whole thing' usually means three components. The altitude dial drifts to match your personal exaggeration coefficient. **Why better:** A universal T0-T3 classifier treats every human's words as equivalent, but 'big' is wildly personal and context-dependent. A per-user calibrated dial turns one bad guess into a system that gets the scope right on the first try and stops re-litigating the gate.
- **Altitude as a Physical Floor in The Tower — You Ride the Elevator to Set Scope** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The scope dial isn't a slider in a cockpit — it's WHERE you are in the building. Standing in a single room = build one component. Riding the elevator up makes the factory build the floor, the wing, the whole tower. Altitude (literal vertical position) IS scope. The metaphor isn't decoration; the elevator's GSAP door-height is the spend gate. **Why better:** The current gate is a foreign 'scope-preview' modal bolted onto a spatial product whose whole identity is the building metaphor. Making altitude literal means the most important control in the factory is expressed in the one language the Tower already speaks — and the metaphor stops being broken by an abstract tier dropdown.
- **Two Dials, Not One: Decouple Scope from Boldness** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Split altitude into a 2D field: SIZE (how much surface area) on one axis, AMBITION (how rash/novel each piece is) on the other. A 'small but wild' single experimental component and a 'huge but boring' CRUD app are opposite corners, not the same T-number. The router and skills attach to the 2D position, not a 1D tier. **Why better:** T0-T3 conflates two genuinely orthogonal things — magnitude and audacity. A tiny feature can be a moonshot and a giant build can be rote. One axis forces them to share a number, mis-routing both the model (Claude vs Codex) and the skill that should own the node.
- **The Factory Negotiates Its Own Scope With a Budget, Like a Contractor** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — You don't set altitude — you set a BUDGET (tokens, dollars, or wall-clock) and a goal, and the factory bids: 'For $4 I'll give you the spine; for $40 the full tower; here's the marginal value curve.' Scope becomes the OUTPUT of a constrained optimization, not an input dial. The gate shows you the efficient frontier and you pick a point on the curve. (Consolidates the 'spend a budget, the tree allocates' and 'single cost meter' framings.) **Why better:** A tier ladder ignores the thing users actually care about — cost vs value. Bias-bigger is dangerous without a price tag attached. Framing scope as a value/spend frontier makes 'go bigger' a quantified economic choice instead of a vibe, removes the lossy human-to-tier translation, and self-justifies the swarm's spend.
- **Speculative Altitude — Build Three Scopes in Parallel, Show All, Keep One** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Refuse to classify at all. The instant intent lands, fork the factory into parallel worktrees at SMALL, MEDIUM, and HUGE interpretations simultaneously. By the time the human glances at the gate, three real (partial) artifacts already exist to compare. Pick one; the orchestrator kills the other two worktrees. **Why better:** Every gate today asks the human to judge scope from a text PREVIEW of something that doesn't exist yet — pure imagination. Speculative execution turns the scope decision into a comparison of three living things. Idle parallel compute is cheap; a wrong scope guess that runs to completion is expensive.
- **Kill the size dial — measure uncertainty, not bigness** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace T0-T3 (a size axis) with a 2x2: reversibility x novelty. The factory doesn't ask 'how big is this' but 'how wrong could this go and how new is it.' A one-line auth/migration change is tiny in size yet top-altitude; a 4000-line CRUD scaffold is huge yet near-zero altitude. Machinery (council, adversarial review, human gate) attaches to the high-uncertainty cells, raw swarm throughput to the low ones. **Why better:** Size is a proxy that systematically mis-routes the two cases that matter most — small-but-dangerous (gets under-protected) and big-but-boring (gets over-deliberated). Uncertainty is the variable that actually predicts where effort and verification pay off.
