- **Per-node altitude field, not one global tier** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Altitude is not a single number for the whole job — it's a value computed at every node of the plan-tree. A T3 app contains T0 leaves (a button) and T2 hot-spots (the payment path). The dial becomes a heatmap over the tree: the swarm reads each node's local altitude and self-selects its rigor, so a cheap node never inherits the whole app's caution and a dangerous leaf never inherits the average. (Consolidates the 'per-subtree altitude, no global tier' inversion.) **Why better:** A single top-level tier forces a blunt average: either you over-spend on the 90% boring nodes or under-protect the 10% load-bearing ones. A field lets verification and model-choice concentrate exactly where risk lives instead of being smeared uniformly.
- **Probe-first altitude — let a cheap scout discover the tier** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Don't classify from the prompt. Spend one cheap headless probe (a 30-second skeleton attempt or a graph query) and read the SIGNAL: did it one-shot cleanly, or did it fan out into unknowns and cross-file coupling? The probe's blast radius reveals the true altitude. Classification becomes empirical, not a guess the human or model declares before any code exists. (Consolidates the 'build one real leaf, then extrapolate the gate from it' inversion.) **Why better:** Up-front auto-classify is a guess made at the moment of maximum ignorance — before a single file is touched. A probe converts altitude from prediction to measurement, catching the 'looked simple, was a swamp' tasks that pre-classification always misjudges, and makes the gate empirical rather than a forecast.
- **Drop 'bias bigger' — bias toward the smallest reversible step** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the default. Instead of biasing the dial up to avoid disappointing under-scope, bias toward the smallest move that produces a real, shippable artifact and lets the human SEE it, then re-trigger. The factory ratchets up from a working core rather than committing the swarm to a big spend before any value is visible. (Consolidates the 'bias smaller, going bigger must justify itself' inversion.) **Why better:** 'Bias bigger' optimizes against the rare disappointment of under-delivery while paying a constant tax: more tokens spent before the human can course-correct, and a bigger blast radius when the intent was misread. It also contradicts forge's load-bearing 'never let process exceed output' rule. Small-reversible-first surfaces misunderstanding in minutes, not after a full swarm run.
- **Altitude as a thermostat, not a dial — it re-rates mid-flight** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Scope isn't set once at the gate; it's a control loop. As workers report (a leaf failed its golden test 3x, a node touched the schema, a contract had to be renegotiated upward), the system promotes that subtree's altitude live — pulling in a council or a human gate it didn't originally plan. The dial moves itself during the build. **Why better:** Static classification can't react to discovery. The most expensive failures are tasks that START easy and turn hard mid-build; a one-shot gate has no mechanism to escalate, so it either fails silently or burns the whole budget. A thermostat catches the escalation at the node where it happens.
- **Two dials, not one — separate 'how deep to plan' from 'how hard to verify'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The current single tier conflates decomposition depth with verification rigor. Split them. Decomposition depth answers 'how finely do I shatter this into leaves'; verification rigor answers 'how adversarially do I prove each leaf.' A boilerplate app wants deep decomposition + shallow verification; a crypto/auth tweak wants shallow decomposition + maximal verification. One dial can't express either without compromising. **Why better:** Collapsing two orthogonal knobs into one tier means every choice is a compromise on at least one axis — you can't ask for 'split it a lot but don't over-verify the parts' or vice versa. Separating them lets the factory hit corner cases the single dial literally cannot represent.
- **No tiers at all — recurse until every leaf is Codex-ready; altitude is an emergent readout** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete the altitude concept entirely as a front-door control. There is exactly one rule: recurse any node that isn't unambiguously one-shottable; stop when it is. 'Altitude' becomes a pure emergent readout — tree depth and leaf count — never an input. The human describes intent and watches the tree find its own natural size. (Consolidates both 'no tiers, just keep splitting' first-principles ideas and the 'depth is emergent' inversion.) **Why better:** The hypothesis already says the plan-tree recurses until leaves are Codex-ready. If recursion depth IS scope, a separate up-front classifier is a redundant second estimator that can only ever be WRONG relative to the ground truth the tree discovers. Deleting it removes a whole misclassification failure mode and the 'I guessed T1 but it was really T3' cliff.
- **Altitude inherited from the artifact's destination, not its description** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Classify by where the output LANDS, not what the request says. An artifact bound for production main, a billing path, or a public legal page is auto-high-altitude regardless of size; one bound for a throwaway branch, a demo page, or a research doc is auto-low. The dial reads the target's blast radius (CODEOWNERS, protected paths, prod-deploy branch) instead of trusting the prompt's self-description. **Why better:** Users describe intent unreliably and consistently understate stakes ('just a quick fix to the payment webhook'). The destination is ground truth that can't be talked down — it ties rigor to actual consequence, exactly the signal The Tower already encodes in protected paths and prod auto-deploy.
- **Content-addressed scope cache (Bazel/Turborepo remote cache)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hash every node's resolved contract (signature + schema + tests + template) into a content address; before classifying or spending on a node, look it up in a shared cache. A node whose contract-hash already built once anywhere becomes a zero-altitude 'restore', not a build — its altitude collapses to T-cache-hit. **Why better:** The current dial only sizes NEW work; it re-spends on things structurally identical to past leaves. Bazel/Turborepo prove the cheapest tier is the one you never build. This makes the altitude dial economic (skip) not just descriptive (size), and gets dramatically faster as the factory does more runs.
- **Critical-path altitude, not node altitude (CPM / Bazel scheduling)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Compute the longest dependency chain through the plan-tree and assign altitude/parallelism budget to nodes by their slack, not their intrinsic T-tier. A T1 leaf on the critical path gets a bigger, more careful build than a T3 subtree with tons of slack that can be cheaply parallelized around. **Why better:** T0-T3 sizes a node by what it IS in isolation; build systems size effort by what GATES the finish. Pouring care/model-budget into a high-slack subtree while the critical path waits is the classic assembly-line sin. Altitude-by-slack directly optimizes wall-clock to green-wave-complete.
- **Takt time as the altitude regulator (lean assembly line)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Set a target cadence (a 'takt') for how often a finished leaf should land — e.g. one green leaf per 90s. Any node whose estimated build exceeds the takt is AUTO-SPLIT one tier finer; any cluster of trivially-fast leaves is AUTO-MERGED up a tier. Altitude becomes whatever keeps the line flowing at takt. **Why better:** T0-T3 is a fixed grid the planner guesses against once. Takt makes altitude a continuous feedback controller that self-corrects mid-run instead of mis-classifying up front. Manufacturing abandoned fixed batch sizes for flow-pacing for exactly this reason — it kills both starvation and pile-ups at the stitch step.
- **Andon cord: any worker can re-classify the line (Toyota)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every leaf-worker the authority to pull an 'andon cord' — if a node it was handed is secretly too big to one-shot (or trivially too small), it halts, escalates the misclassification upward, and the orchestrator re-splits or re-merges that branch before continuing. Altitude is corrected by the floor, not just decreed from the top. **Why better:** Auto-classify is a single top-down guess with no correction loop; the first worker to discover a leaf isn't actually Codex-ready currently just fails or thrashes. Toyota's andon principle — the person closest to the work stops the line — turns every misclassification into a cheap local re-split instead of a downstream stitch failure.
- **Pre-vis / proxy altitude pass before the full-res build (VFX pipeline)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow film's pre-visualization: do a deliberate low-fidelity 'gray-box' pass of the ENTIRE tree first — stub every leaf with cheap placeholder implementations that satisfy types/tests-as-mocks — so the whole artifact stitches and runs end-to-end before a single leaf is built at full quality. Then climb tiers, replacing proxies with real builds. **Why better:** The scope-preview gate today previews the PLAN, not a working SHAPE — you still discover integration breaks at the very end. VFX never renders final-quality before the whole sequence works in proxy. A proxy pass surfaces contract/stitch mismatches when they're cheap to fix and lets the user approve a runnable skeleton, not a description.
- **Level-of-Detail dial driven by the artifact's blast radius (game-engine LOD)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace discrete T0-T3 with a continuous LOD value computed from blast radius: how many other nodes depend on this one, is it user-facing, does it touch auth/schema/payments. A 1-line helper that everything imports renders at max detail (more tests, cross-model review); a leaf-of-a-leaf nobody depends on renders at min detail (one-shot, skip review). **Why better:** T-tier conflates 'big' with 'important' — a tiny shared util is T0 yet deserves the most care, while a big isolated view is T3 but tolerates sloppiness. Game LOD spends fidelity by what's load-bearing and on-screen, not by object size. This routes review/model budget to where errors actually propagate.
- **Saga-style compensating altitude (workflow-engine rollback)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat each node's build as a saga step with a paired 'compensation': if a parent node's contract turns out wrong after children built against it, don't re-plan the whole subtree — run targeted compensations that re-derive only the children whose contracts actually shifted, and bias the RE-build a tier bigger because we now have more information. Altitude is re-assigned on the retry, informed by the failure. **Why better:** Today a frozen-top-down contract that proves wrong is catastrophic — the whole bias-bigger guess was made blind and the subtree is sunk cost. Temporal/Airflow sagas localize and compensate failures. Letting altitude RISE on retry (build the thing that surprised us with more care) turns a re-plan into a surgical, better-informed second pass.
- **Apoptosis: leaves that fail twice trigger programmed branch-death + regrowth (biology)** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — If a leaf can't be one-shot after N attempts, it's declared mis-altituded and undergoes 'apoptosis' — the node and its sibling assumptions are killed, and the PARENT regrows a fresh, finer-grained subtree from the accumulated failure signal, biasing each new leaf SMALLER. The tree heals toward the right altitude instead of retrying at the wrong one. **Why better:** Bias-bigger plus auto-classify has no mechanism for admitting it aimed too high — it just retries the same too-big leaf. Biological apoptosis prunes failed structure so the organism can regrow correctly. This gives the factory a self-healing path from over-ambitious altitude to a working finer-grained plan, rather than infinite retry at a doomed tier.
- **WIP-limited altitude via a Kanban pull, not a push gate (large-org Kanban)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop the single up-front scope-preview gate; instead cap work-in-progress per tier (e.g. max 3 T3 subtrees 'open' at once). The swarm PULLS the next node to expand only when a tier slot frees, so altitude expansion is rate-limited continuously by downstream capacity rather than approved once and then unleashed. **Why better:** One scope-preview gate is a binary commit-the-whole-spend decision; if the estimate is wrong you over- or under-commit the entire run. Kanban WIP-limits let the factory throttle spend continuously as real costs come in, surfacing a runaway big-tier subtree early instead of after the gate already greenlit it.
- **Merge altitude into the router — the model that judges the node already knows its altitude** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't have a standalone scope/altitude stage. Fold it into the two-model router: when Claude looks at a node to decide 'do I architect this further or hand it to Codex to build?', that exact decision IS the altitude call. 'Codex-ready leaf' = T0. 'Needs me to split it' = higher. One model pass produces both the routing decision and the depth — they're the same judgment. **Why better:** The hypothesis already has the router asking 'is this leaf explicit enough to one-shot?' at every node. That question and 'what tier is this?' are the same question asked twice. Merging them removes a redundant pipeline stage, eliminates the chance the classifier and the router disagree, and means altitude is computed by the smartest available judge instead of a cheap heuristic.
- **Auto-classify is the wrong default — make the human DECLARE altitude in one word, and freeze it as a contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert auto-classification: the system never guesses scope. The user says 'component' / 'feature' / 'app' (or just a budget) as part of intent, and that declaration is FROZEN like the typed contracts are — the swarm is forbidden from exceeding it without re-asking. Scope becomes a user-owned constraint, not a machine inference. **Why better:** Auto-classify's whole risk is silent scope creep — the machine decides 'this is really a T3' and spends 10x. Treating altitude as a declared, frozen contract (consistent with the hypothesis's top-down contract-freezing) makes runaway spend structurally impossible and puts the one decision the human actually cares about back in human hands, where the bias-bigger pressure can't quietly inflate it.
- **The dial should be DOWNWARD-only — a big intent must be allowed to collapse to nothing** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the bias-bigger framing into its sharpest case: the most valuable altitude move is realizing the answer is ZERO tiers — 'you don't need to build this, here's the one-line config change / existing feature / three-sentence answer.' Bake a mandatory 'can this whole tree collapse?' check at the root before any planning, and reward it. **Why better:** A factory that biases bigger will dutifully build a 40-node tree for something that wanted a one-liner — the single most expensive class of mistake (perfectly executing the wrong-sized thing). A downward-biased dial that actively hunts for 'actually, no build needed' is the highest-leverage move in the system and the exact opposite of what bias-bigger optimizes for.

### Skills as workers

_Current assumption: existing skills attach per node-type; backend-build skills are a gap._

**Boldest reframe:** The factory grows its own workers (skill mitosis) — when a node has no matching worker, the factory dispatches skill-creator to AUTHOR, eval, benchmark, and attach a brand-new skill on the spot. This is the boldest reframe because it dissolves the entire premise of the aspect: there is no fixed "skills as workers" roster to route over. The worker population becomes emergent and self-manufacturing, so the named "backend-build gap" stops being a gap to fill and becomes the first thing the factory mints the instant a backend node appears. It scales the workforce with demand rather than with human authoring weekends, and it folds the existing skill-creator eval harness into the runtime as a self-healing organ — the single largest leap in agency the set proposes.

- **Node-type → skill routing manifest the orchestrator reads** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Add an explicit declarative manifest (workers.json) mapping each node-type tag — frontend-floor, db-schema, api-route, migration, art-asset, research, decision — to the skill that staffs it (impeccable, ui-ux-pro-max, artlab, improve-codebase-architecture, grill-me, tower-ship-gate). The orchestrator dispatches by lookup instead of every worker re-deriving its role from a generic 'smith' prompt. Routing becomes a machine fact, not prose in CLAUDE.md. **Why better:** Makes 'skills attach per node-type' provable and editable in one line, so the dispatcher can show it staffed the right worker instead of every unit re-deriving its own role from identical prose.
- **A first-class backend-build skill (tower-api-smith)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Author one Tower-flavored backend skill that bakes in the stack's hard constraints — Supabase REST not runtime-Drizzle, Zod v4 validation, RLS auth.uid()=user_id on every table, timestamp withTimezone, UUID PKs — so an api-route/schema/migration leaf gets a worker that already knows the gotchas instead of a generic lane re-learning them from CLAUDE.md. **Why better:** Directly closes the stated gap by encoding the most expensive recurring failure (runtime-Drizzle 'Tenant not found', missing RLS) as default behavior, so backend leaves stop being the weakest workers in the swarm.
- **Skills declare the contract shape they consume and emit** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every worker-skill a tiny contract-IO header: what frozen artifact it expects (typed signature, failing test, DB schema, golden template) and what it must hand back (file paths, a green test). The orchestrator checks a leaf is 'staffable' before dispatch and rejects a node whose contract matches no skill's input shape. **Why better:** Turns the top-down contract freeze and the worker layer into one type-checked handshake, catching mis-routing before the swarm spends tokens instead of after a lane silently fails.
- **tower-ship-gate as a per-leaf gate, not just an end gate** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Run a scoped, parameterized slice of tower-ship-gate (tsc + lint + the leaf's own failing test, skipping e2e/build) on each leaf the moment a worker reports done — making the gate the definition-of-done attached to every node rather than a single final whole-app check. The green wave literally climbs the tree, leaf by leaf. **Why better:** A parallel swarm needs each leaf independently provable before stitching; a failing leaf is caught and re-dispatched in isolation so one bad leaf can't poison the stitch — far cheaper than discovering it at final assembly.
- **A shared worker capability registry for cockpit and dispatcher** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Maintain one source-of-truth list of worker-skills with their model bias (Claude vs Codex), node-types covered, and cost/latency notes — queried by the dispatcher to pick a worker and by the Forge cockpit to render the model badge and which-skill-on-which-node. Skills not in the registry are simply not dispatchable. **Why better:** The badge the human sees and the worker the dispatcher picks become literally the same record, eliminating drift between what's displayed and what ran.
- **Skill-level acceptance recipes so verify lanes aren't improvised** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each worker-skill ships a default acceptance recipe for its node-type — the api-route skill's is 'curl the route, assert the Zod-validated shape + RLS denial for a foreign user_id'; the frontend skill's is 'render the floor, assert no console errors + a11y aria check'. The Codex verifier inherits that recipe instead of a free-text acceptance field per unit. **Why better:** Binds verification rigor to the node-type's skill so it's consistent across leaves and the planner doesn't have to hand-author acceptance for every leaf.
- **Seam-aware backend pass (improve-codebase-architecture pre-flight)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before the swarm builds, run a lightweight improve-codebase-architecture pass that tells each backend leaf where the existing seams/adapters live — which Supabase client wrapper, which query module to extend — so workers build INTO the existing architecture instead of each minting a parallel data-access path. **Why better:** Prevents the classic failure where N parallel backend lanes each invent their own DB helper, keeping the as-built graph matching the contract graph — what graphify is meant to verify, prevented up front.
- **graphify as the mandatory pre-dispatch query every worker runs first** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make 'query the code/knowledge graph for the symbols, schema, and neighbors your leaf touches' the mandatory first step in every worker-skill's prompt — one shared graphify lookup, identical for frontend, backend, and art workers — replacing each lane's ad-hoc recall+grep. **Why better:** Gives every worker the same authoritative view before writing a line, cutting redundant token-heavy exploration and reducing the chance two leaves contradict each other.
- **The factory grows its own workers (skill mitosis)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — There is no fixed skill roster. When a node has no matching worker, the factory dispatches a meta-worker (skill-creator, which already has the eval harness) that AUTHORS, evals, and benchmarks a brand-new skill on the spot, then attaches it. The backend-build 'gap' isn't filled by hand — it's the first thing the factory manufactures the instant a backend node appears. Skills become an emergent, ever-growing population. **Why better:** Turns every new domain (backend, mobile, ML, infra) from a manual authoring chore that bottlenecks on you into a self-manufactured organ — the roster scales with demand, not your weekends, covering domains you never anticipated with zero human lag.
- **Skills evolve under selection (a genetic pool, not a roster)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every skill keeps a fitness record across runs (leaf passed first-try? Codex review flagged it? token cost?). Low-fitness skills get mutated, forked, A/B'd against challengers, and culled; high-fitness ones breed variants. The skill library is a living gene pool under continuous selection, with the eval harness as the fitness function. **Why better:** Static skills rot — frozen at authoring time, improving only when you notice and edit them. Selection pressure makes workers measurably better every run with no human in the loop, and dead weight removes itself instead of accumulating as catalog cruft.
- **Every leaf gets a council, not a single skill** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of attaching THE one matching skill, each node convenes a small council of competing workers (impeccable vs a design-variant skill; Claude-built vs Codex-built; conservative vs bold) that build in parallel, then a judge-skill picks or merges the winner. The worker for a node is a tournament, not an individual — forge's top-level best-of-N pushed all the way down to every leaf. **Why better:** Single-skill-per-node bets everything on one worker's first guess; a council with adversarial judging dramatically raises the quality floor at the cost only of compute the orchestrator already parallelizes.
- **Skills bid in an auction with price and reputation** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each skill bids to take a node — quoting expected tokens, expected pass-rate, and confidence. The orchestrator runs an auction weighted by reputation (past fitness); cheap confident specialists win routine leaves, expensive generalists win only the hard ones. The router stops being a hand-written table and becomes an economy. **Why better:** A static lookup can't price the tradeoff between a cheap specialist and an expensive generalist; an auction lets the right worker self-select per leaf on real economics and track record, and absorbs new skills without touching a routing file.
- **The cast becomes the workforce (CRO writes the War Room)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The Tower's own AI characters ARE the build workers for their floors. CRO builds and reviews War Room features; CMO builds the Writing Room; CFO builds the Observatory. Each persona's voice, domain expertise, and memory become the system prompt of the skill that builds its own floor — the building literally constructs itself, character by character. **Why better:** A character-as-worker carries the floor's domain semantics and history natively, makes the build self-dogfooding, and fuses the product's sacred building metaphor with the factory — the workforce IS the cast, not a separate toolbox.
- **Passive apprenticeship — skills learn from every build they didn't write** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After each run, a distiller watches the diffs and reviews and silently writes lessons back into the relevant skills' bodies — new gotchas, golden snippets, anti-patterns. CLAUDE.md gotchas, stitch failures, and Codex review notes all flow back into the workers automatically, so every skill is strictly smarter on run N+1. **Why better:** A hard-won lesson (an IPv6 DB gotcha, a stitch failure pattern) today reaches a skill only if you manually edit it; passive apprenticeship makes the workforce institutionally remember every mistake, compounding competence with no authoring step.
- **One universal worker that composes a bespoke specialist per leaf** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the roster. ONE meta-skill, given a node, instantiates the exact specialist it needs by composing capabilities on demand — design judgment + backend contract + the floor's domain — into an ephemeral worker that exists only for that leaf and is discarded after. Infinite specialists, zero standing catalog. (Subsumes the self-emitting/throwaway-skill and parameterized-single-worker variants.) **Why better:** A finite roster forces every task to be approximated by the nearest pre-built skill and makes the frontend/backend seam integration friction; an on-demand composer makes a perfectly-fit worker per leaf with no approximation, no roster maintenance, and no artificial split the assumption itself names as weak.
- **Workers can refuse and renegotiate the plan-tree (andon cord)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A worker assigned a leaf can push back UP the tree: 'this node is mis-decomposed / the contract is impossible — re-split it.' The signal doesn't just fail the node, it propagates to the Claude-architect that froze the contract, pausing dependent siblings before they waste tokens building against a broken spec. Workers are domain experts with veto power, not passive executors. **Why better:** Contracts frozen top-down can be subtly wrong; today a leaf just fails locally and the orchestrator retries blindly. Veto-and-escalate turns a leaf's hard-won discovery into upstream contract-repair, killing whole subtrees of wasted parallel work.
- **Capability marketplace, not a node-type lookup table** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the static frontend→impeccable / backend→grill-me map. Each plan-node DECLARES the capability it needs ('needs visual-design judgment', 'needs concurrency-safety judgment', 'needs cited-source verification') and skills publish what capabilities they supply. The router matches declared-need to advertised-supply at dispatch, so an unmet need surfaces itself as a visible MISSING-SUPPLY signal. **Why better:** The current map is the author's frozen pre-decision and can't express a need no skill covers; a marketplace makes the backend gap a first-class visible signal and lets new skills slot in without rewriting the router.
- **Split every skill into a generator half and a critic half** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A skill bundles judgment, build, and verify for ONE in-the-loop human — wrong granularity for a swarm. Split each into two dispatchable primitives: a GENERATOR (impeccable-build, schema-build) and a CRITIC (impeccable-audit, schema-audit). The factory recombines them freely — Claude-generator + Codex-critic, or Codex-generator + impeccable-critic — mixing models AND skill-halves per node. **Why better:** Lets the contract-frozen factory pair ANY builder with ANY adversary, generalizing 'Claude builds, Codex proves' to 'any worker builds, any catalog-armed critic proves' — and lets a frontend critic attack a backend-built node.
- **There is no backend-build gap — build a backend JUDGMENT catalog instead** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The base model already builds backends; what's missing is the backend equivalent of ui-ux-pro-max — a queryable catalog of schema patterns, concurrency hazards, failure modes, idempotency/migration rules. Don't author a backend WORKER; author the corpus the verifier consults, exactly as impeccable consults ui-ux-pro-max. **Why better:** Mislocates and corrects the gap: a skill's value is the taxonomy the model lacks, not the act of building. A catalog is cheaper to author, reusable by every backend node's critic, and doesn't duplicate generation the model already does free.
- **Skills become tools the worker CALLS, not roles the worker IS** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of spawning a worker that 'is' impeccable, spawn a generic builder that can CALL impeccable / ui-ux-pro-max / artlab / graphify as queryable services mid-task. The node-type no longer pre-binds one skill to one worker; any worker reaches for any skill the moment the work demands it. **Why better:** A real floor node needs design judgment AND schema AND copy AND art; binding one skill per node forces artificial splitting, while callable services let one worker assemble exactly the judgment it needs when it needs it.
- **Contracts ARE the workers; skills only fill them in** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Reframe so the frozen contract (typed signature + failing test + golden template) is the autonomous agent. The contract-node spawns itself, pulls whatever skill/model best satisfies its failing tests, self-reports green, and swaps skills freely until tests pass — the skill is interchangeable plumbing, not the protagonist. **Why better:** In a contract-frozen top-down factory the load-bearing invariant is the contract, not the skill; inverting agency to the contract makes the swarm robust to any single skill being weak or absent.
- **Toolchain resolution, Bazel-style: nodes request capabilities, a resolver picks the skill** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal Bazel's toolchain resolution: a node declares a required CAPABILITY ('build-rls-postgres-table', 'design-judgment', 'verify-e2e') and a resolver matches it against registered skills by platform/constraints and picks the best. The same plan-tree builds on a different stack by swapping the toolchain, not rewriting nodes; the backend gap is just an unresolved capability the resolver flags. **Why better:** Decouples WHAT a node needs from WHICH skill provides it, makes gaps machine-detectable, and lets the factory retarget stacks for free — versus hard-coding skill identity into the tree.
- **Hermetic skill sandboxes with declared inputs (Bazel hermeticity)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Force every worker skill to declare its full input set up front — the contract slice, the graph queries it's allowed, the files it may touch — and run it in a sealed worktree that physically cannot read anything undeclared. Needing more means amending the declaration, which the orchestrator logs. Outputs become content-addressed and cacheable. **Why better:** A skill that greps whatever it wants makes parallel builds non-reproducible and lets leaves drift from contract; declared hermetic inputs make every leaf cacheable, reproducible, and provably the skill's fault when wrong.
- **Versioned skills with a pinned lockfile (Nx/Turborepo)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat each skill as a semver package with an explicit interface version; the plan-tree pins skills in a skills-lock.json (the forge dashboard already has one). A run records exactly which skill versions built which leaves, so upgrading impeccable to 4.0 mid-campaign can't silently change last week's outputs. **Why better:** Per-node attachment is implicitly 'whatever version is installed now'; pinning makes builds reproducible across days and machines and turns a skill upgrade into an explicit reviewable bump.
- **Skills as durable Temporal Activities (retries, heartbeats, timeouts)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Wrap every worker not as a fire-and-forget agent call but as a durable Activity: idempotent, auto-retried with backoff, required to heartbeat or be declared dead, with a hard timeout. The orchestrator becomes a workflow that survives crashes and resumes the exact half-built tree, re-dispatching only the activities that didn't complete. **Why better:** A swarm of headless processes will have stragglers, hangs, and partial failures and the current model has no failure semantics; durable-activity semantics give crash-safe resume and automatic recovery instead of a wedged run that needs a human.
- **Specialist departments with strict handoff formats (VFX pipeline)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Model the worker layer like a film VFX house: distinct DEPARTMENTS (layout, modeling, lighting, comp → schema, API, UI, prose, verify), each with its own lead, its own definition-of-done, and a strict handoff format between departments. A node flows department-to-department; backend isn't a 'gap' but the under-staffed modeling+rigging department you explicitly stand up (improve-codebase-architecture as rigging lead, a new RLS/migration lead). **Why better:** A flat pool has no notion of inter-stage handoff quality or per-stage done-ness; departmentalizing makes the backend gap a named staffable org chart with explicit handoff contracts, each department improvable independently.
- **Morphogen gradients — workers read ambient context they didn't ask for** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Like cells differentiating by signal-concentration gradients, each worker reads an ambient context field the orchestrator broadcasts — design tokens, the canon graph, naming conventions, the altitude tier, sibling decisions made so far — without explicitly requesting it. A UI leaf next to an already-built sibling inherits its register; a leaf deep in T3 behaves differently from the same leaf in a T0 run. **Why better:** Identical per-node attachment yields locally-correct but globally-incoherent output (the swarm tell); a diffusing context field makes workers position-aware so parallel leaves cohere into one artifact instead of N stylistic islands.
- **Builder+antagonist pair as the atomic unit of work (two-person rule)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Make cross-model adversarial review structural, not a separate phase: every node is dispatched as a PAIR — a builder skill and an antagonist skill from the OTHER model whose only job is to break the leaf against its failing tests and contract. A leaf is not green until its paired antagonist signs off. Pairing rules are declared per node-type (impeccable builds UI, a Codex critic attacks it; Codex builds RLS, grill-me/improve-codebase-architecture attacks the seam). The worker's identity IS its adversary, not its specialty. **Why better:** In an autonomous swarm with no human watching, the ADVERSARY guarantees quality, not the builder; embedding the pair per leaf makes 'Claude builds, Codex proves' atomic, catches contract violations before they stitch upward, and turns the two-model router into a quality mechanism, not just quota failover.
- **Delete the skill layer: a worker is a system prompt + the contract** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill 'skills as workers' entirely. A worker is nothing but a fresh model process pointed at one leaf's frozen contract (typed signature + failing tests + golden template). The contract already contains every constraint a skill would inject; a hand-curated skill on top is redundant scaffolding that drifts from the contract it serves. **Why better:** Removes a whole layer that can disagree with the verifiable contract; fewer moving parts, no skill-vs-contract conflict, and any leaf becomes one-shottable by any model.
- **Generalists beat specialists at leaf scale — the gap is imaginary** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the premise: at leaf granularity (small enough to one-shot) a specialized backend skill adds nothing a precise failing-test + schema contract doesn't. Specialization pays for fuzzy judgment (design taste, plan-hardening), not explicit mechanical leaves. Spend the budget on better contract-generation, not a backend-skill catalog. **Why better:** Stops you building skills you don't need; better contracts yield more than a backend-skill catalog ever would because the rigor lives in the verifiable test, not unverifiable prose.
- **Merge skills into node-types: the node-type IS the worker** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't attach skills to node-types — make the node-type definition carry its own build/verify recipe inline. A 'frontend-component' node-type embeds its checks; a 'migration' node-type embeds its safety gates. The plan-tree's typed nodes replace the skill roster entirely, collapsing two registries into one. **Why better:** Today node-type points at a skill that points at behavior — an indirection that can mismatch; inlining makes the tree self-describing and dispatch trivial, with no routing table to maintain or get wrong.
- **Skills as JUDGES only, never as builders** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Flip skills out of the build path. Builders are anonymous interchangeable processes; skills like impeccable, grill-me, and tower-ship-gate live ONLY on the verify/judge edge of the green-wave stitch. The build is dumb and parallel; the taste lives exclusively in the gate. **Why better:** Separates the cheap-and-parallel (building) from the expensive-and-serial (judging); judgment belongs at the gate where reality meets the contract, not smeared across every worker where it serializes and bloats the swarm.
- **The graph replaces skills: workers query graphify, not a pre-attached skill** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If graphify is the shared code/knowledge graph every worker queries first, the worker's expertise should come from the graph, not a pre-attached skill. Delete the skill-attachment step; the worker's first act is a graph query returning the conventions, neighbors, and contracts it needs — always as-built-accurate because derived from the real graph. **Why better:** A single source of expertise that can't go stale, versus skills that encode stale generalities — and the graph already has to exist for the factory, so you don't pay to curate a parallel knowledge base.
- **Route by MODEL, not by skill — collapse the double router** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — The two-model router already decides Claude-vs-Codex per node on the exact fuzzy-vs-explicit axis skills claim to serve. Adding a skill layer is double-routing on overlapping axes. Collapse it: model choice + contract is the entire dispatch decision; 'which skill' is a question that shouldn't be asked. **Why better:** Two routers competing on overlapping axes is where surprise behavior lives; one router decided by altitude/explicitness is simpler and has no seam to mismatch.

### Code / knowledge graph layer

_Current assumption: graphify as the shared map + as-built verification._

**Boldest reframe:** The graph IS the build — code files become a read-only projection of the graph, not the source of truth. Workers mutate typed semantic nodes/edges and a deterministic renderer emits the TSX/SQL, so as-built reality literally cannot drift from the contract (it's generated from it), and 31-way merge conflicts become three-way-mergeable graph-edge conflicts instead of textual clobbering.

- **Contract-IDs as the graph's primary key + impact-radius BOM** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every frozen contract (typed signature, DB table, failing test, golden template) gets a stable contract-ID the moment it's frozen top-down, and the graph is keyed on those IDs. Every built node, file, symbol, and worker output is tagged back to the contract-ID it satisfies, with an immutable lineage edge to the exact prompt/model/parent that produced it. As-built verification becomes a trivial set-diff (satisfied / missing / conflicting), and one first-class query — 'who breaks if I change this contract?' — returns the transitive set of dependent nodes and their owning workers for the scope gate, merge ordering, and re-dispatch on drift. **Why better:** Keying on contract-IDs makes 'does reality match?' mechanical (set membership, zero LLM cost) instead of a judgment call, and the lineage/impact-radius layer turns the graph from a passive lookup into the orchestrator's operational instrument: a contract change yields a precise rework list, not a full re-verify sweep.
- **The plan-tree IS the graph (one object, two views), seeded before any code** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop treating graphify as a separate map workers consult. Make the recursive plan-tree and the code/knowledge graph the SAME persistent object: each plan node carries its frozen contract as graph edges from the instant of freeze — before any code exists — and as workers build, their as-built code attaches to the very same node. The cockpit's 'live plan-tree' and the 'embedded graph' are literally one data structure rendered two ways, and the planned-vs-built delta IS the as-built verification. **Why better:** Two artifacts kept in sync guarantees drift, and a code-extracted graph is empty during the most parallel, most chaotic phase. Unifying them means a node can never be 'planned but ungraphed,' workers have something to query from the very first leaf, and the green wave climbing the tree and the verification become one traversal.
- **30-second incremental indexer keyed to git diff, with freshness-stamped answers** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Never re-index the world. After each worker merges into its worktree, re-index only the changed files (tree-sitter/ast-grep on the diff) and patch the graph in place so it's at most one merge stale. Every query returns its answer plus the commit SHA it was computed at and a confidence (exact / stale-by-N-merges / not-indexed); on 'stale' or 'not-indexed' the worker is explicitly told to fall back to grep for that slice. **Why better:** With N parallel workers committing constantly, a full re-index is always stale and workers silently fall back to grep anyway — defeating the premise. Incremental-on-diff keeps the graph trustworthy enough to beat grep, and freshness stamps let a worker trust it when fresh and degrade gracefully when not, instead of blindly trusting a stale map or never trusting it.
- **Per-worker scoped capsule: the smallest correct subgraph, not the whole graph** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When dispatching a leaf, the planner that freezes its contract also computes the minimal dependency cone via an 'affected' query and hands the worker a sealed capsule: its own contract, its direct dependencies' frozen signatures, the 3-5 symbols/files it may touch, and nothing else. The worker queries within that boundary by default and must explicitly ask to widen; a leaf reaching outside its capsule is a loud planning error, not a silent traversal. **Why better:** Giving every headless worker the full graph wastes context, invites hidden cross-leaf coupling the stitch can't reconcile, and caps how many parallel workers fit on the same quota. A scoped capsule keeps context tiny (cheaper, faster), enforces the contract boundary structurally, and makes scope-escape catchable instead of invisible.
- **Failing tests are graph edges; the graph DERIVES them from edge topology** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Each failing test becomes an edge connecting a consumer node to the producer node it tests, so the as-built green wave is literally those edges flipping red→green and a still-red edge after its subtree is 'done' names both endpoints of a precise stitch failure. Go further: instead of the architect hand-writing each failing test, the graph derives the boundary tests automatically from a node's typed signature plus its parents' contracts — it's a test-generator over the contract topology. **Why better:** Unifying tests and graph makes the 'green wave climbing the tree' a real, observable mutation rather than a metaphor, and deriving tests from topology catches every contract boundary by construction — including ones between leaves the architect never co-considered — turning the slowest, most error-prone part of the freeze into the thing that makes the wave trustworthy.
- **Graph-diff as the cockpit's primary live view, with confidence-weighted edges** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The Forge cockpit renders the plan-graph with each node colored by as-built state (planned / building / built / verified / drifted), headlined by 'contracts satisfied vs frozen.' But edges carry a confidence weight, not a pass/fail bit — test coverage, which model built it, whether a cross-model review confirmed it — so the green wave is a HEAT map. The scope-preview gate prices a run partly on how much red-uncertainty the graph predicts, and the orchestrator spends remaining quota on the least-certain frontier. **Why better:** Making the planned-vs-built graph-diff the primary view answers the exact question the cockpit exists for — is the swarm converging? — and weighting edges stops treating 'merely compiles' and 'Codex-built + Claude-adversarially-reviewed' as equally green, which is how silent rot ships and how quota gets spent uniformly instead of on the real risk surface.
- **The graph IS the build — code is a read-only projection of typed nodes** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the relationship: code files become a deterministic, read-only PROJECTION of the graph, not the source of truth. Workers mutate typed nodes/edges (this function calls that, this component renders that prop, this test asserts that contract) and a renderer emits the actual TSX/SQL. graphify isn't a map OF the code — it's the substrate the code is generated FROM. **Why better:** Kills the entire as-built verification problem: reality can't drift from the contract because reality is generated from it — the graph cannot disagree with itself. Merge conflicts become semantic, three-way-mergeable graph-edge conflicts instead of textual ones, so 31 parallel workers never clobber each other's lines.
- **Self-healing contract graph — edges carry executable invariants that auto-repair drift** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every edge isn't just 'A depends on B' — it's a live, executable invariant (a typed predicate + a failing test + a repair recipe). Seed lightweight 'sentinel' invariants from the frozen contracts that continuously patrol as-built code DURING the run, not just at final verify. When reality violates an edge, the graph spawns a micro-worker that proves the violation and either repairs the leaf locally or escalates with a minimal repro. The graph defends its own contracts. **Why better:** Passive end-of-run verification means a deep violation only surfaces after the swarm has built on top of it. Continuous, distributed, self-correcting edges (an immune system) catch drift at the leaf that owns it with the smallest possible blast radius — orders of magnitude cheaper than a giant stitch-time diff — and the green wave never has to re-run wholesale.
- **Time-travel graph — every node remembers all the versions it could have been** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The graph is append-only and superpositional: each leaf holds not one implementation but the full lattice of candidate implementations the swarm/council generated, with their verdicts, token cost, and which contracts each satisfied. 'Choosing' is collapsing the superposition; reverting or A/B-ing a feature is re-collapsing a subtree to a different branch — instantly, with no rebuild. **Why better:** A single as-built snapshot throws away every alternative the council considered. Keeping the whole decision lattice lets the factory re-decide any node months later ('actually use the risk-first take here') without re-running the swarm, and turns the graph into institutional memory of WHY every node is the way it is.
- **Cross-run accreting graph as content-addressed cache and moat** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — graphify isn't per-build — it's a single persistent graph that accretes every node, contract, and proven leaf the factory has ever produced, identified by a hash of its frozen contract (signature + schema slice + golden test). A new intent first queries 'has this subtree been built before, anywhere?' — if two subtrees across runs/projects/modalities hash identically, they're the SAME node and the build is cloned + re-typechecked instead of re-derived. The factory gets monotonically faster and cheaper the more it builds. **Why better:** Scoping the graph to one run means every build starts from grep-zero and re-spends tokens on leaves built identically last week. Content-addressed accretion turns the graph into a global memoization layer — the 100th 'Stripe webhook handler' is a subgraph clone — and compounds into an ever-growing library of verified, contract-attached blocks no competitor can cold-start.
- **The graph routes the swarm — topology IS the orchestrator (event-sourced)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Dissolve the separate 'main agent + manifest' into the graph itself. The graph is the single event-sourced source of truth: node state transitions, worker dispatch, failover, and stitch-readiness are all edges/events in the same graph workers query. A node becomes 'ready' the instant all its upstream contract-edges are green, and readiness directly triggers a worker spawn — pure dataflow, like a spreadsheet recalc where each cell is a headless claude -p / codex exec. **Why better:** A passive map the orchestrator consults has a scheduler bottleneck and a manifest that can drift from reality. Fusing them makes maximum parallelism fall out for free (every node fires the moment it CAN), makes the green wave literally the recalc frontier, makes 'more processes = more power' automatic, and lets a crashed orchestrator resume from the graph with zero reconciliation.
- **Critical-path scheduler over the dependency graph** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Compute the critical path (longest contract-dependency chain) through the plan-tree and dispatch workers to critical-path leaves first, with idle workers stealing non-critical leaves. The graph isn't just a map — it's a schedule that minimizes wall-clock time to the green-wave stitch. **Why better:** graphify says nothing about ORDER, so 'autonomous parallel build' can starve on the one deep chain that gates the whole stitch while capacity sits idle on shallow leaves. CPM/Airflow-style bottleneck-first sequencing directly shrinks time-to-finished-artifact, which raw parallel fan-out alone cannot.
- **Semantic + structural fused graph the worker THINKS in, not greps** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Split graphify into a precise structural layer (SCIP-style symbol/def/ref edges for exact signatures and call sites, zero hallucination) and a fuzzy semantic layer (embeddings over node contracts/intent). A worker asks in intent — 'what already enforces auth.uid() = user_id' or 'where would a new floor's contract attach' — and the graph resolves intent-to-location, returning the 3 most relevant nodes with their contracts pre-loaded into context. The worker never greps, never reads a whole file, never blows context on irrelevant code. **Why better:** Name/path lookup forces a cold leaf-builder to already know the structure it's trying to find, but it only knows its intent. Intent-resolution is strictly closer to why a worker consults the graph, works identically across code/art/prose nodes, and auto-injecting only load-bearing neighbors slashes per-worker context — directly raising parallel-worker count on fixed quota.
- **Adversarial red-team daemon that hunts contracts the graph FORGOT** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Run a permanent adversary whose only job is to find MISSING edges: contracts that should exist but don't — the auth check nobody wired to the new route, the migration with no rollback edge, the component prop with no test edge. It proposes 'phantom edges' the graph lacks and proves each gap with a failing case. **Why better:** As-built verification only checks reality against the contracts you THOUGHT to write — it's blind to the one you forgot, which in a top-down-frozen factory is exactly where the dangerous security/migration/concurrency bugs hide. A standing red-team makes the graph's completeness self-questioning instead of trusting the human's plan-tree captured every invariant.
- **Holographic graph — navigate the build's source as floors of the Tower** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Render the code/knowledge graph IN the Tower metaphor: the live plan-tree and dependency graph are an actual explorable floor. You ride the elevator into 'the blueprint room' and walk the graph spatially — edges as corridors, contract-violations as flickering lights, the green wave as the building powering on floor by floor. The cockpit isn't a separate dashboard; it's a room in the world. **Why better:** A 2D dashboard bolted beside the building breaks the sacred spatial metaphor the whole product rests on. Making the graph a navigable floor means the factory's internals obey the same world-rules as everything else, the user inhabits their build instead of monitoring it, and watching your skyscraper light up as the swarm finishes becomes the single most cinematic surface — the demo that sells the whole thing.
- **Verification runs backwards — reality amends the contract** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — As-built verification shouldn't only ask 'does reality match the contract?' and fail when it doesn't. When a Codex leaf discovers the frozen contract was wrong (an unsatisfiable type, an impossible test), the graph becomes the ADJUDICATION venue: the leaf files a 'contract dispute' edge upward, a Claude judge node rules, and the contract mutates with a recorded provenance trail. The graph stores the negotiation, not just the verdict. **Why better:** Top-down frozen contracts assume the architect was right and every disagreement from below is a build error — but the fuzzy top is exactly where errors hide and leaves have ground truth. One-directional verification turns every architect mistake into a wall of red leaf-tests no one can fix; bidirectional verification lets reality correct the plan without a human un-freezing it by hand.
- **The graph is a write-medium (ownership/locking fabric), not a read-cache** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Reframe graphify from 'the map every worker reads before grepping' to 'the coordination ledger every worker WRITES to.' Each node/edge has an owner-lease; a worker claims a subtree before building, the graph rejects conflicting claims, and parallel workers negotiate boundaries through it. Knowledge lookup becomes a side effect of a system whose primary job is preventing two headless processes from stitching the same seam. **Why better:** The autonomous orchestrator's hardest problem isn't 'where is the auth code' — it's 'two worktrees both edited the contract boundary and the stitch is a merge conflict.' A read-only knowledge graph does nothing for that; a write-medium graph makes the swarm's parallelism safe by construction, which is the actual bottleneck of zero-human-interaction runs.
- **One polymorphic graph over ALL modalities, not a code graph** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — An art slot, a DB column, a deck section, a research claim, and a TSX component are all just NODES with a contract and an as-built. Make graphify modality-agnostic: 'does the war-room background fill its slot' and 'does this function satisfy its type' are the same edge-resolution operation. ArtLab's slot_audit and the code graph collapse into one substrate, queryable by intent identically across all of them. **Why better:** The subject is a factory for ANYTHING decomposable, but a 'code/knowledge graph' silently re-narrows it to software. A campaign, a deck, a decision have dependency structure too; a code-only graph forces every non-code modality to invent ad-hoc tracking, recreating the universal-factory promise per-modality. One graph keeps the universality the whole thesis rests on.
- **Provenance / color-of-money edges (who, which model, reviewed?)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Tag every node and edge with provenance: which model touched it (Claude/Codex), whether a human gate approved it, what license/source any snippet came from, and a confidence score. The graph then answers as a single query: 'show me everything Codex wrote that no human reviewed,' or 'isolate AI-slop-risk subtrees,' or 'quarantine everything with an unlicensed source.' **Why better:** graphify checks structural conformance but is blind to WHO/HOW — and VFX/GDPR pipelines live or die on per-asset provenance so a bad node can be quarantined. Provenance edges give the cockpit a trust-weighted view and let scarce review effort target exactly the unverified, cross-model, or unlicensed subtrees instead of the whole tree.
- **Delete the graph — the frozen contract IS the map** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — If contracts are truly frozen top-down (typed signatures + schema + failing tests + golden template), a worker already has everything it needs to build its leaf in isolation. A shared code graph is redundant scaffolding that only exists because we secretly don't trust the contracts to be complete. Remove it: if a worker has to consult a graph, that's a bug in the contract, not a feature of the system. **Why better:** Forces the contract layer to be genuinely sufficient instead of letting workers 'query around' an under-specified contract. Removing the crutch makes the real defect visible (an incomplete contract surfaces immediately) and leaves one less subsystem to build, sync, and keep correct.
- **Workers grep / let the LLM be the index — no pre-built graph** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert 'query the graph before grepping' to 'grep first, never build a graph.' Modern models are extremely good at ripgrep + reading files on demand, and a frontier model with a large context window CAN hold the relevant slice in its head. A static graph is a stale, lossy cache of what grep gives fresh and what in-context attention computes live, task-specifically, with no build step. The model's attention IS the graph, recomputed every call. **Why better:** Eliminates an entire staleness class of bug (graph says X, file says Y) and avoids reimplementing a worse version of what the model does internally for free. Grep is zero-maintenance and always current; the graph's only edge is speed on huge repos, but leaves are small by construction, so that win never materializes.
- **The graph is an OUTPUT, never an INPUT (write-only-at-the-end)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop letting workers READ a shared graph mid-run (it couples them, creates contention, and leaks cross-leaf knowledge that breaks isolation). Build-time: zero graph. Post-build: EMIT a read-only graph purely for the human cockpit's comprehension and the as-built map. **Why better:** Preserves the genuinely useful thing (a human-readable as-built map in the Forge) while deleting the dangerous thing (workers sharing mutable state mid-run). True parallel isolation requires that no two leaves consult a common live structure; making the graph write-only-at-the-end restores that isolation by construction.
- **The graph causes architectural conformity — that's a bug, delete it** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A shared knowledge graph makes every worker reuse whatever patterns and helpers it finds — which entrenches whatever happens to be there first and suppresses the better local solution a fresh leaf would invent. Delete it to let leaves be genuinely independent clean-room one-shots judged only against their contract, and let the STITCH phase (not the graph) reconcile them. **Why better:** Real parallelism means each leaf is judged only against its contract; a graph turns the swarm into a herd converging on the incumbent pattern. Removing it raises solution diversity, and since the contract (not the graph) guarantees fit, conformity should be enforced at the seam, not pre-seeded everywhere.
- **Demote graphify to a forensic tool summoned only on a stitch FAILURE** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't run or maintain a graph on the happy path at all. Only when the green-wave stitch fails to climb does the orchestrator lazily build a graph of the conflicting subtree to localize the break. 99% of runs never touch it; it exists solely as an on-demand diagnostic — and can be lossy without consequence. **Why better:** Pays the graph's cost only when it actually earns its keep (a real conflict) instead of taxing every leaf and every run. Reframes graphify from load-bearing infrastructure on the critical path to a cheap, occasionally-summoned forensic instrument.
- **Merge as-built verification into the test layer — kill graphify's second half** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The 'does reality match the contract?' job is exactly what the FAILING TESTS already do — green tests ARE the as-built proof. A separate graph-diff verifier is a second, weaker oracle that verifies structure (a proxy) and can disagree with the tests, which verify behavior (what matters). Fold the verification half away; keep one oracle. **Why better:** When the two oracles conflict you trust the tests anyway, so graph-diff verification adds confusion and zero decisive authority. One oracle simplifies the stitch gate to a single green-wave criterion and removes a whole subsystem whose only job duplicates the test suite.

### The cockpit (rebuilt Forge)

_Current assumption: a clarity-first local dashboard: live tree, model badges, status, gates._

**Boldest reframe:** The Cockpit Is the Factory (you speak buildings into being, no launch step, no terminal)

- **Scope-gate as an editable, line-itemized diff (plan-vs-apply)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The pre-swarm scope preview is a Terraform-style dry-run: a concrete manifest of every leaf the plan-tree will spend on — its tier (T0–T3), assigned model, estimated tokens/dollars, and fan-out width — rendered on the graph itself. Checkboxes defer or kill individual leaves; pruning a branch or dialing altitude updates a running cost total live. Only 'apply' releases the workers. The gate becomes a surgical per-branch cost-editing surface, not a whole-run accept/reject. **Why better:** The brief's scope gate is vaguely 'show a cheap gate before the swarm spends.' A whole-run yes/no is a blunt instrument; the proven plan-vs-apply UX turns the single highest-leverage cost-control surface into something the user steers leaf-by-leaf before any worker spawns.
- **Failover as a first-class timeline event with a live ledger** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a node fails over Claude→Codex (quota or error), draw it as an annotated transition on that node — old model pill struck through, new pill lit, reason on hover ('Claude 5h limit hit', 'error retry'). The run header keeps a live failover ledger ('3 nodes moved to Codex, ~$0.40 saved on Claude budget'). **Why better:** A static model badge shows the current model but hides the most operationally important moment in a two-model run: when and why the router switched. Surfacing failover as an event makes quota exhaustion legible before it stalls the whole swarm.
- **The green wave is the hero animation, literally** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the bottom-up stitch — the 'green wave climbing the tree' — the cockpit's signature live motion: as leaves go green and merge upward, a visible wavefront of completion propagates from leaves to root, so a glance tells you exactly how close the whole artifact is to done. The root node fills last and pulses when the full stitch lands. **Why better:** Clarity-first doesn't mean motionless. Tree-plus-status-colors shows per-node state but no gestalt of overall progress. The wave makes 'how done is the whole thing' instant, reusing the existing status-color channel instead of a competing progress bar.
- **Altitude dial: collapse the plan-tree to a chosen depth** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A single altitude slider/keyboard control collapses the recursive plan-tree to a chosen depth — T3 root only, down through sections, bullets, leaves — so the user reads the run at the altitude they care about and expands a branch only on demand. Default opens at the tier the run was auto-classified to. **Why better:** A genuinely recursive plan-tree is visually overwhelming fully expanded. An altitude control keeps the clarity-first promise at any tree depth, which a flat always-expanded tree cannot.
- **Click a stalled node → jump to its worktree, last 20 lines, and failing test** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every node deep-links to its git worktree path and shows the worker's last ~20 transcript lines inline, with one-click 'open files it touched' and 'open worktree in editor.' For an erroring node, the error and the failing-test output are pinned to the top of that panel. **Why better:** The orchestrator spawns headless workers in worktrees with zero mid-run interaction — so when something stalls, the first need is 'where is this and what did it just do.' Wiring transcript-tail and open-files per node turns watching into debugging without leaving the cockpit.
- **Dual-budget run meter with projected cost-to-finish** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A persistent header readout: live spend split across the two real quotas — Claude $200 and Codex $100 — as twin depleting gauges, with burn rate and projected cost-to-finish based on remaining leaves. It turns amber when projected cost would exhaust either budget mid-run. **Why better:** The brief names the two budgets and quota failover as core mechanics but the cockpit assumption only lists 'token/failover' vaguely. A budget-aware meter that projects to completion lets the user pull the scope gate or change altitude before a run dies broke.
- **The contract grid: watch the spec turn red→green, not workers turn busy** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the frozen CONTRACTS the visualized object. Every typed signature, schema slice, and failing test is a cell in a coverage grid that flips red→green as the bottom-up build satisfies it. Clicking a node opens its frozen contract — signature, schema slice, the failing tests it must turn green, the golden template it was handed — with a per-test red→green indicator. You watch the SPEC getting fulfilled, not 31 workers being busy. Doubles as the graphify as-built verification surface. **Why better:** The factory thesis is contracts-frozen-top-down + failing-tests, but a status/badge tree mirrors the machine's topology, not the user's question ('did it build what I asked'). The contract IS what was asked, frozen and checkable; a red-to-green grid is a falsifiable readout of progress-toward-intent, and verifies 'done' against tests rather than against 'an agent stopped.'
- **Per-node live-tap inspector (workflow-engine debugger)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Clicking any leaf opens a Temporal/Inngest-style inspector: the frozen contract, the live tool-call stream, the exact dispatch prompt, the retry/failover history (Claude→Codex), and a 'tap in' button to inject a steering note into the worker's next turn. The tree is the map; the inspector is the oscilloscope on any one cell. **Why better:** For the BUILDER running the factory, a plain-language gloss isn't enough to debug a failing leaf. Workflow engines prove you need raw inputs/outputs/retries plus a steering tap — without it, a stuck worker is a black box you can only kill and re-run. This also natively solves the 'inject into a live turn' control problem.
- **graphify per-node: what did this worker query, and does its output match the contract graph?** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Since every worker queries the shared code/knowledge graph before grepping, surface that per node: which graph symbols/files each leaf pulled, plus an as-built green/red 'matches contract graph?' verdict comparing what the node produced against the contract it was frozen to. **Why better:** graphify is defined as both the shared query layer AND the as-built verification spine. A clarity-first dashboard that ignores it is blind to the system's verification backbone; showing queries + a contract-match verdict makes 'does reality match the plan' visible where the user is already looking, not a separate audit step.
- **Critical-path burndown spine (Bazel/Nx critical-path overlay)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Compute the longest dependency chain through the live plan-tree and render THAT path as a thicker, brighter trunk, with an ETA that sums only the critical-path leaves, not the whole tree. Everything off the critical path visually recedes; the one chain that gates completion glows. **Why better:** Equal-weight cards hide which stalled leaf is actually holding up the green wave versus which is incidental. Bazel's insight is that only the critical path sets wall-clock — surfacing it turns 'lots of stuff happening' into 'THIS is the bottleneck' at a glance.
- **Andon cord + blast-radius (Toyota stop-the-line)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Any worker or the user can 'pull the cord' on a node, which freezes that subtree, halts the downstream stitch comet at that junction, and raises a full-width andon banner. Crucially the cockpit then shows the BLAST RADIUS — which sibling leaves and parent stitches are now blocked — so the user sees the cost of the stop before deciding to clear it or route around. **Why better:** Today an error just tints one card red. TPS's andon makes a defect a first-class, propagating, line-level event with visible blast radius — a bad contract gets caught and contained at its station instead of silently shipping defective leaves to the stitch where they're 10x costlier to fix.
- **Backpressure & WIP-limit lanes (Kanban + Airflow pools)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Render the cockpit as horizontal capacity lanes — model quota, worktree slots, token budget/min — each with a visible fill level, and show leaves QUEUED behind a saturated resource rather than pretending everything runs at once. When Claude's quota nears cap its lane reddens and leaves back up into a waiting buffer the user can watch drain. **Why better:** The tree implies unbounded parallelism ('more processes = more power'), but real runs are gated by model quota, worktree count, and token budget. Hiding the constraint causes thrash; showing WIP limits lets the user see why the swarm slowed and decide whether to raise a limit, switch failover model, or wait.
- **The Dailies Room (VFX review-and-approve ritual)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow film/VFX dailies: instead of approving the whole run, the cockpit batches every leaf that went green since you last looked into one scrollable review reel — artifact preview, diff, and the contract it was built against, side by side — and you sweep through approving/kicking-back each like a VFX supervisor at a screening. Kicked-back leaves re-enter the swarm with your note attached. **Why better:** The cockpit is watch-only with two coarse gates. VFX solved 'how does one supervisor quality-gate hundreds of parallel shots without becoming the bottleneck' — the dailies reel makes human judgment scale to a 200-agent swarm by batching and contextualizing it, instead of approve-everything or approve-nothing.
- **Taste-harvest instead of approval-clicks** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace yes/no gates with a preference surface: at each gate the cockpit shows 2–3 divergent micro-options (this scope vs that, this component variant vs that); you pick or nudge, and it records the choice as a reusable taste-vector that pre-biases future runs. The human's interaction is expressing preference, not granting permission. **Why better:** We modeled the human as an approver, but the human's scarce, non-automatable resource is TASTE, not consent — a machine can ask for approval, it cannot supply taste. A cockpit that spends every interaction harvesting irreversible preference compounds across runs; one that spends them on approve-clicks throws the most valuable signal away.
- **Adversarial split-screen: watch Claude and Codex argue** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Make the model duality the spine. A two-column live cockpit shows Claude's architecture/judgment stream on one side and Codex's build/review stream on the other, with the moments they DISAGREE (failed reviews, contract violations, failovers) surfaced as the headline content — disagreement is the signal, agreement is silent. **Why better:** A model badge is decoration; the two-model router's whole value is cross-model adversarial verification, currently buried in a status dot. Foregrounding the agreement/disagreement boundary shows exactly where trust is being earned or contested — the highest-information moments of the run.
- **A cockpit that earns the right to be closed** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the goal from 'best window to watch' to 'make watching unnecessary.' Every run computes and shows a single live 'confidence to walk away' meter from contract-coverage, test-pass density, cross-model agreement, and unresolved-blocker count. When it crosses threshold it literally says 'safe to close me' and pings you only if confidence later drops. **Why better:** You watch live to catch failures the system can't judge for itself — but the honest objective is a dashboard whose success metric is the fraction of runtime you DIDN'T need to look at it. A beautiful window that demands your eyes is a failure dressed as a feature.
- **Ambient one-symbol presence; the full cockpit is the rare drill-down** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — For the 90% case there is no dashboard to open — the run radiates ambiently: a menubar glyph / Dock badge / one push / a soft sound the swarm hums while healthy that sours on a red leaf, encoding the whole run in one symbol (calm = healthy and autonomous, amber = wants your taste, red = stuck). The full visual cockpit materializes only on demand or when intervention is genuinely required. **Why better:** 'See everything' and 'know if I'm needed' are different needs, and 99% of glances are the second. Even a clarity-first dashboard is a screen you must consciously parse; an ambient one-symbol status answers the real question (am I needed?) with a peripheral glance and zero parse — respecting that the best autonomous run is one you don't watch.
- **The cockpit is a single status line, not a dashboard** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Radically shrink it. The entire live view is one persistent line: '▰▰▰▱▱ 14/31 nodes green · 2 codex 1 claude building · $4.10 · 0 failovers · gate:PASSED'. No tree, no cards, no canvas — a progress bar with a cost meter. For detail you grep the worktrees, but the default is glanceable ambient awareness. **Why better:** 'Clarity-first' taken to its extreme is the minimum signal that says 'on track / overspending / stuck', not a beautiful multi-panel dashboard. One line costs almost nothing to build, never drifts, fits next to the chat, and avoids the v1-maximalist trap the user already rejected once.
- **The artifact is the surface; the plan-tree is a peelable underlay** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the process-tree as the primary view. Show the emerging artifact itself — a live-assembling app preview, the growing report, the deck filling in — updating in place as leaves go green, with the plan-tree demoted to an underlay you summon only when something is wrong. **Why better:** You watch the tree because structure equals comprehension, but you only care about structure to judge the output's quality — which the tree never shows. Nodes flipping color tell you nothing about whether the result is good. Center-staging the artifact answers the only question that matters: 'is this turning out right?' not 'is the machine busy?'
- **Post-mortem-first: the run is a navigable replay, live is just t=now** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat every run as a Temporal-style append-only event history and make the cockpit a deterministic replay over that log. A global scrubber drags to ANY past moment and the whole tree re-renders to its exact state then — which contract was frozen, which worker was mid-build, what the token spend was. You always land on a scrubbable story of the whole run; 'live' is just the scrubber pinned to the present edge. **Why better:** Intervention is rare; most runs are reviewed after they finish or stall, and a per-run spotlight walk isn't true state reconstruction. Event-sourcing makes the cockpit a debugger for the factory itself — rewind to the moment a contract froze wrong and watch the failure propagate — and makes live a trivial special case of review rather than a separate mode.
- **Cost-and-confidence heat field painted on the tree** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Render the plan-tree as a landscape where token spend, model-confidence, failover risk, retry count, and model-disagreement are baked into the terrain — expensive subtrees glow hot, low-confidence leaves shimmer, a region of trouble shows as a spreading stain BEFORE any single node goes red (a morphogen-style diffusing health gradient). The scope gate stops being a modal and becomes terrain you see coming and defuse by reshaping. **Why better:** Discrete per-card status is binary and read one card at a time. Biology coordinates millions of parallel agents with continuous fields; a diffusing heat field lets you grasp where money, doubt, and danger are across hundreds of leaves at a single glance — anomaly detection by ambient color, and intervention before the spend, which a per-card list can never surface in time.
- **Talk to the run: a conversational control channel instead of a panel grid** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Replace the watch-surface with natural-language control. You ask the orchestrator 'how's the auth leaf?', 'why is node 12 red?', 'pause the frontend lane', 'bump that to a bigger tier' — it answers and acts. The cockpit is a chat with the foreman, not a screen of the floor. **Why better:** A graphical dashboard can only show what its designers anticipated; mid-run questions are open-ended ('stuck or just slow?', 'did the contract change?'). An LLM orchestrator can answer arbitrary questions about its own run better than any fixed panel, the user is already in a text interface, and conversation natively solves the hard 'inject into a live turn' control problem.
- **The cockpit talks back: a resident coordinator you argue with** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The orchestrator has a face and voice — a Tower character in the CEO/Mara register seated at the apex — who narrates intent in plain language, flags where it's least sure, and lets you interrogate any decision ('why did you split contacts into three leaves?'). It defends or revises the plan-tree in conversation, turning oversight into dialogue. **Why better:** The current viewer narrates one-way and you watch passively.
