A two-way resident agent makes the cockpit answerable — you steer by conversation and challenge — which fits the Tower's character metaphor and turns 'watch the run' into 'run the run with a colleague.'
- **Kill the separate dashboard: the factory narrates itself inline in chat** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Delete the second surface entirely. The plan-tree, model badges, status, and scope gate are a single live, self-rewriting markdown/ASCII block rendered inline in the same chat where you typed the intent — it overwrites itself as the green wave climbs, expanding to the full tree only on demand. No port, no window, no daemon, no tab-switch. **Why better:** A dashboard is fundamentally a second pane you must context-switch into, breaking flow during an autonomous run and adding a whole daemon/SSE/CDN stack. Inline-in-chat keeps attention where intent lives, ships with zero infrastructure, and works identically over SSH, in CI logs, and in the Telegram bot the rest of the system already uses.
- **The cockpit is the git history, not a UI** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert 'watch the build' into 'read the build.' Every worker commits to its own worktree branch with a structured trailer (node-id, tier, model, contract-hash, test-status). The cockpit is `git log --graph` plus a tiny formatter — the plan-tree IS the commit DAG, the green wave IS branches going green and merging, and you inspect a node by checking out its branch. **Why better:** A bespoke dashboard invents a parallel, lossy state representation that can drift from reality (the on-disk structure is already shallower than the imagined tree, roles inferred not authoritative). Git is the durable substrate the orchestrator writes to anyway — making it canonical means the view can never disagree with ground truth, survives crashes free, is diffable/blameable/bisectable, and needs no live process.
- **Merge the cockpit into graphify — verification IS the view** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no 'cockpit' and 'graph' as two things. The contract graph already knows every node, its signature, its tests, and its as-built status. The view is just graphify rendered — a query, not a product. 'Show me the run' = `graphify --watch`. The same graph workers query before grepping is the same graph you look at. **Why better:** The current assumption embeds the graph INTO the cockpit as a panel — backwards. The graph is the load-bearing source of truth for both workers and humans; collapsing them removes a whole sync layer, guarantees the human sees exactly what workers see, and means one artifact to build and harden instead of two.
- **No live cockpit — only a scope gate and a final report card** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete continuous monitoring. Humans get exactly two surfaces: (1) the cheap scope-preview gate BEFORE the swarm spends, and (2) a rich post-mortem report card AFTER it finishes — what was built, contracts honored, tests green, cost, failovers, diffs to review. Nothing in between. The run is a sealed box you approve into and judge out of. **Why better:** The system's premise is ZERO human interaction mid-run, so a live real-time dashboard contradicts the autonomy it was designed to not need watching — watching a swarm you shouldn't steer is anxiety theatre. Spending the whole design budget on a killer gate and a killer report puts effort where human judgment actually changes the outcome and deletes the most expensive, least load-bearing piece.
- **The cockpit lives in the IDE/editor, not a browser** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Reverse 'standalone web app' into 'annotate the code you're already in.' The run's state is projected as editor decorations: each file/function that's a node gets a gutter badge (model, tier, test status), the active worker's file glows, failing contracts show as inline diagnostics. You watch the build by looking at the codebase it's building. **Why better:** A separate dashboard forces you to look away from the artifact to watch the artifact being made — spatially incoherent. The factory's output is code, so the truest cockpit is the code surface itself; the spatial overlay maps 1:1 to the thing you'll actually inspect and ship, instead of an abstract node-graph you must translate back into files.
- **Perfect the one terminal watcher; cancel the second cockpit** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A shipped, zero-dependency terminal 'mission control' (forge-watch.mjs) already exists and is verified. The contrarian move: that IS the cockpit, full stop. Cancel the greenfield web dashboard and pour all remaining effort into making the terminal watcher excellent — focus pane, the authoritative run-lane marker, solo mode — rather than building a second, heavier browser representation of the same shallow data. **Why better:** The web dashboard's own handoff admits it must lift all its discovery/state logic from the terminal watcher that already works — the hard part is done and the web layer is mostly re-skinning. Maintaining two cockpits over the same artifacts doubles the surface that can drift and splits attention; perfecting the one that ships in every terminal/SSH/CI context is strictly less to build and harder to break.
- **Spectator broadcast: one run, two surfaces (operator cockpit + cinematic feed)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Lean into the non-technical-viewer insight and build a true broadcast layer — a calm, narrated, cinematically-shot 'documentary feed' anyone can watch like a nature film, with a camera that auto-pans to wherever the drama is (a leaf going red, a green wave cresting), commentary, and chapter cards. Operator cockpit and spectator broadcast run off the same data. **Why better:** The dashboard already pivoted toward a watch-only window for a non-technical viewer but kept it static and diagrammatic. A directed, auto-cinematographed broadcast turns the hardest existing constraint (clarity for a non-coder) into a feature anyone would want to watch.
- **Plan-tree as an inhabitable Tower under construction** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop drawing the run as an abstract pipeline and render it as a skyscraper rising: each altitude tier is a floor, leaves are rooms being furnished by worker-characters you watch through the windows, the green stitch-wave is literally the lights coming on floor by floor as the building completes. The cockpit becomes another floor of the Tower, not a foreign tool grafted on. **Why better:** The current cockpit is a clarity-first dashboard living outside the sacred building metaphor — a tool, not a place. Making the factory itself a building you watch rise reinforces the Tower vision, and gives non-technical viewers an intuitive 'is it built yet?' read (floors lit = done) no node-graph can match.
- **Multiplayer cockpit: several operators (or your own agents) fly one build together** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the cockpit a shared space — several people, or several of YOUR own agents, inhabit the same live plan-tree at once, each able to grab a branch, propose a contract change, or veto a scope-gate, with presence cursors and a lightweight 'who's steering what.' A build becomes a piloted-together flight rather than a solo watch. **Why better:** The current design is single-operator, one daemon serving read-only tabs. Multiplayer turns the cockpit into a control room where a team or a council of your agents can co-pilot a large run — distributing oversight across the tree instead of bottlenecking every gate decision on one human at one screen.
- **Time-scrubber across every possible build (the run is a 4D object)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the whole run as a navigable 4D artifact: a timeline scrubber that also branches sideways into every counterfactual the swarm explored or could explore. Drag back to before a contract froze, nudge a signature, and watch a parallel green wave re-climb a ghosted version of the tree beside the real one — keep whichever future you like. Past, present, and unbuilt futures all on one canvas you fly through. **Why better:** A linear timeline only scrubs the one run that happened. A branching multiverse turns the cockpit from a flight recorder into a decision instrument — you steer outcomes by editing history and previewing forks, instead of waiting for one linear run to finish before judging it.
- **The Cockpit Is the Factory (you speak buildings into being)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — There is no separate 'launch the run' action and no terminal. The cockpit IS the orchestrator's living body: you say what you want into the canvas, the plan-tree crystallizes in real time, and you reach in and reshape it — drag a node bigger to bump its altitude tier, pinch two leaves to merge contracts, slice a branch to fork a variant — and the swarm re-dispatches off your gesture instantly. The dashboard stops being a window onto a process and becomes the process's only surface. **Why better:** Every other idea treats the cockpit as an observer pane (read-mostly, or a control surface) bolted onto a CLI-launched run. Collapsing intent, plan, dispatch, and control into one direct-manipulation surface removes the entire 'kick it off in the terminal, then go watch' seam — the cockpit becomes where the work actually lives, not a mirror of it. It's the boldest because it dissolves the very category the whole brainstorm assumes ('a cockpit that watches a run') into 'the cockpit is the run.'

### Factory for everything (generality)

_Current assumption: domain-agnostic; art/research/decks/decisions, not just code._

**Boldest reframe:** The Factory That Builds Factories (Meta-Generality) — you hand it a domain it has never seen and it forges a bespoke sub-factory (node-types, contracts, worker skills, verification graph) for THAT domain, then runs it and forgets it. This is the boldest because it makes the domain menu infinite and self-extending instead of a fixed list, and it relocates generality from the artifacts to the meta-layer that manufactures specialist factories on demand — true domain-agnosticism means never enumerating domains at all.

- **Domain packs, not a domain-agnostic monolith** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Ship generality as a small set of named 'domain packs' (app, art-campaign, research-report, deck, decision) where each pack supplies its own leaf-classifier vocabulary, contract type, and stitch rule. The core stays generic; the pack is the concrete adapter. Each pack is independently shippable, so pack #1 delivers value before pack #5 exists. **Why better:** Pure domain-agnosticism is a lie that leaks: a 'failing test' means nothing for a research report and everything for code. Naming 5 packs makes generality real and testable instead of an aspiration.
- **'Contract' is a typed interface with per-domain implementations** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Generalize 'contract' so the factory freezes the RIGHT frozen artifact per domain: code freezes signatures + failing tests; a deck freezes slide outline + per-slide acceptance bullet; a report freezes a claim-list + required citations; a decision freezes criteria + rubric. Same top-down-freeze mechanism, domain-specific payload. (Merges the contract-shape, hermeticity, and 4-tuple 'pack' framings — all say: make the frozen unit a declared-inputs + verifier interface, not a test file.) **Why better:** Today's spec hard-codes a code-shaped contract (failing tests, golden stack template) and bolts other domains on awkwardly. A typed contract interface keeps the recursion/stitch engine identical while making art and research first-class, not second-class — and it exposes which claimed domains (decisions, decks) still lack a verifier and aren't really supported yet.
- **A per-domain 'verify' adapter so the green wave isn't code-only** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — graphify's as-built check needs a domain verifier: tests + build/lint (code), 'every claim has a live citation' (report), 'every slide renders + matches its acceptance bullet' (deck), ArtLab byte-protection + slot_audit (art). The stitch wave goes green only when the domain verifier says so. Reuses real assets you already own (slot_audit, byte-diff CI, deep-research's adversarial verification). **Why better:** The whole value prop is 'does reality match the contract?'. Without a per-domain verifier, generality silently degrades to 'it generated something' for non-code domains.
- **Reuse existing Tower skills as the per-domain workers** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Map each domain to an existing skill as the leaf-builder: art -> artlab + impeccable + ui-ux-pro-max; research -> deep-research; deck -> frontend-slides; decision -> grill-me; code -> codex/forge. Generality is 'route the leaf to the right existing skill', not 'invent a generator per domain'. **Why better:** Generality is cheapest as orchestration over capabilities you've already battle-tested, and it keeps each domain at the quality bar those skills already enforce instead of a generically-mediocre output across all five.
- **Capability probe before the scope gate ('can I even build this domain?')** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before the swarm spends, the scope-preview gate runs a cheap capability check: does a verifier + worker + contract-shape exist for the classified domain? If not, degrade gracefully ('I can plan + draft this report but can't auto-verify citations yet') instead of faking full factory power. Surfaces a visible coverage matrix in the cockpit. **Why better:** Honest 'partial support' beats a confident bad artifact in an unsupported domain, and it tells you exactly which domain pack to build next.
- **One committed golden run per domain as the acceptance bar** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Generality is 'proven' only when one end-to-end golden example is committed per domain — a real app feature, art campaign, cited report, deck, decision — each run through the same intent->tree->contract->build->stitch loop and checked into the repo as a regression fixture. **Why better:** Right now generality is asserted, not demonstrated. A committed golden run per domain converts the claim into a re-runnable test, catches the moment a core change breaks the deck path while fixing the code path, and gives the cockpit five concrete demos.
- **Auto-detect domain, but make it a one-tap correction at the gate** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Classify domain from intent (you already auto-classify altitude), show it as an editable chip at the scope-preview gate ('Domain: Research report — change?'), and let the user flip it before any spend. Misclassification becomes a 1-second fix, not a wasted swarm. **Why better:** The biggest failure mode of a 'factory for everything' is silently picking the wrong everything. A cheap correctable gate makes generality safe to be aggressive about without a wrong-domain blowing the budget.
- **Native mixed-domain runs: one plan-tree whose leaves span domains** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Let one run contain leaves of different domains under one frozen top contract — a new floor's code leaf, its ArtLab character (art leaf), comp data (research leaf), launch email + analytics event (ops leaf) — with the interfaces between subtrees (the slot the art fills, the route the email links) themselves being contracts. The router dispatches each leaf to its domain's worker; one green wave stitches across domains. **Why better:** Real Tower work is already mixed-domain (a new floor = code + ArtLab character + UX copy + comp research). A factory that does one domain per run isn't general — it's several narrow factories sharing a logo, forcing the user to fan out and re-stitch by hand. Cross-domain trees are where the generality actually pays rent.
- **The Factory That Builds Factories (Meta-Generality)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The supreme artifact a universal factory emits is not an app or a deck — it's another factory. Hand it a domain it has never seen (legal contracts, molecular designs, supply-chain plans) and it forges a bespoke sub-factory: the right node-types, contracts, worker skills, and verification graph for THAT domain, then runs it and forgets it. Generality is not 'it knows many domains'; it's 'it manufactures a new specialist factory on demand.' (Subsumes the 'factory-factory / stamping press' inversion — same move from the meta-layer.) **Why better:** The current assumption treats domains as a fixed menu (art/research/decks/decisions). This makes the menu infinite and self-extending: every novel intent grows the repertoire instead of hitting a wall it wasn't pre-wired for. True domain-agnosticism means never enumerating domains at all — and each emitted factory is allowed to hardcode its domain ruthlessly.
- **Artifact-Agnostic: it doesn't know what it's making until it's made** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill the altitude dial's artifact taxonomy. The factory never classifies intent as 'app' vs 'report' vs 'decision' — it only sees a plan-tree of contracts and decomposes until leaves are one-shottable. Output TYPE is an emergent property of which leaves got built, so a single intent can yield a deck AND the app it pitches AND the legal entity to ship it, because no boundary said 'this is a slides job, stop here.' **Why better:** Current generality is a SWITCH between known artifact types — still a finite set of lanes. Removing the lanes lets cross-type artifacts (the report that is also the working prototype that is also the decision memo) fall out naturally instead of needing three disconnected runs.
- **The factory forges the physical world, not just files** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Extend 'decomposable artifact' past the screen. A leaf can be a 'manufacturable object' (route to CNC/3D-print/PCB-fab API), a 'physical event' (book the venue, hire the caterer, ship the kits), or a 'biological assay' (order the synthesis). The plan-tree spans bits AND atoms; the stitch wave climbs from a soldered board to a shipped product the way it climbs from functions to an app. **Why better:** Every current example (art, research, decks) is a file — the assumption is implicitly digital. If the factory is REALLY for 'anything decomposable,' the world's most decomposable thing is the physical world. Stopping at the file boundary is an arbitrary ceiling, not a law.
- **Generality across TIME — the factory that runs forever on one intent** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Some artifacts are never 'finished' — a company, a research program, a personal brand, a garden. Let an intent compile into a STANDING factory: a perpetual plan-tree that re-decomposes as reality changes, re-freezes contracts when the world shifts, and keeps a green wave climbing indefinitely. 'Build me a startup' isn't a run that ends; it's a factory that stays lit and ships until you tell it to stop. **Why better:** Current generality is bounded in TIME — intent in, artifact out, run terminates. The most ambitious artifacts are ongoing processes, not static deliverables. A factory that only makes finished things can't make a living thing.
- **Inverse Factory: hand it the artifact, get back the intent** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Run the machine BACKWARDS. Feed it any existing thing — a competitor's app, a famous deck, a published paper, a building — and it reverse-decomposes it into the plan-tree, contracts, and golden templates that WOULD have produced it. The factory becomes bidirectional: manufacture from intent, or extract the intent (and rebuild recipe) latent inside any finished object. Re-decompose your own output and check the trees match = the perfect verifier. **Why better:** The current assumption is one-directional. A truly general factory should be invertible — and the inverse is wildly valuable (clone, learn-from, audit, remix anything) while doubling as a self-verifier.
- **One intent, a Pareto FRONTIER of worlds** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of producing THE artifact, build the whole neighborhood of artifacts the intent could become — a navigable space of variant worlds (the bold version, the cheap version, the trust-over-speed version) all built in parallel in their own worktrees. You don't pick before the swarm spends; you walk the finished frontier and choose by living in each. **Why better:** Current generality assumes one intent collapses to one artifact (with a scope-gate guess up front). The hardest part of any 'factory for everything' is that intent is ambiguous — so build the ambiguity out in full and let the user resolve it by inspection rather than prediction.
- **The factory with no operator — intent comes from the world** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the 'intent in' requirement. The factory watches a live signal stream (calendar, inbox, repo, market, news, your half-finished projects) and AUTONOMOUSLY originates intents, decomposes them, and ships before you ask — a deck for tomorrow's meeting, the feature users keep churning on, the decision memo for a fork you haven't noticed. (Merges with 'intent selection as the top tier above T3': the factory decides WHAT, you ratify with one tap.) **Why better:** Every current framing starts at 'intent ->', capping the factory at your typing speed and imagination. Promoting intent-selection to the top tier turns a tool you operate into a system that proposes — generality of the WANT, not just the work.
- **Self-hosting: the factory is a standing artifact it maintains** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The cockpit, router, skills, and graphify layer become a plan-tree the factory continuously rebuilds. It adds node-types when it meets domains it lacks, rewrites its own contracts when verification keeps failing, and forges new worker-skills for itself the way it forges features for you. It is the first artifact on its own assembly line. **Why better:** Current generality is a static set of mechanisms applied to varied inputs — the MACHINE doesn't grow. A factory truly 'for everything' must include itself in 'everything,' so its generality compounds instead of being capped at whatever was hand-built on day one.
- **Verifier-agnostic, not domain-agnostic: every leaf must supply its own oracle** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop 'works on any domain.' The real axis is: can this node supply its own oracle? Code brings tests/tsc; an art campaign brings a rubric-judge or human gate; a decision brings a falsifiable prediction with a check-date. A node with no verifier is not factory-eligible — it routes to a 'draft only, human owns truth' lane and is visibly marked unverified in the cockpit. (Re-anchors the recursion rule too: keep splitting until verifying a leaf is cheaper than building it — domain-agnostic by construction.) **Why better:** The current assumption silently treats a green test suite and a vibes-judged deck as equivalent. 'Who is the oracle for this leaf' tells you exactly which domains the factory can autonomously close vs. only assist — the difference between a factory and a fancy autocomplete. And 'cheap to verify' as the stopping rule has meaning in any domain, unlike 'one-shottable.'
- **The terminal node is an EFFECT (with an inverse), not a file** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For non-code domains the last node isn't 'produce artifact.txt' — it's 'take the action and stage a rollback': send the email (draft + undo window), book the calendar slot, open the PR, push the price change behind a flag. Every leaf declares its side-effect class and its inverse; stitch = committing effects in dependency order with a single tree-wide rollback. (Pairs with a reversibility tag orthogonal to altitude: the dial picks SIZE, reversibility picks AUTONOMY — run hot on reversible leaves, auto-gate irreversible ones.) **Why better:** For decisions and ops, a generated document is worthless theater — value is the change in the world. First-class effects with inverses generalize the factory beyond 'things that compile,' and separating size from reversibility lets it be aggressive where mistakes are free and cautious only where they're permanent, instead of one global timidity setting.
- **Generality as a compounding CORPUS + content-addressed leaf cache** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop defining the factory as 'an orchestrator you point at anything.' Define it as a growing, queryable library of frozen contracts, golden per-stack leaf templates, and past judge verdicts. Hash every leaf by its frozen contract + inputs and cache the built artifact globally — a 'pricing table', a 'competitor SWOT' bullet, a 'CTA hero', a 'cream-owl idle pose' built once is reused verbatim whenever an identical contract recurs, across runs, projects, AND domains. graphify becomes a memoized function of contracts; 'factory for everything' becomes 'everything reachable in N steps from what we've already frozen.' **Why better:** Process-generality plateaus — every new domain is a cold start. Corpus + content-addressing compounds: decomposition makes leaves repeat constantly (every app has a settings page, every deck a 'market size' slide, every report a 'methodology' section), so run N+1 is cheaper and more consistent than run N. Generality stops being a capability you claim and becomes an asset that visibly grows in the cockpit.
- **Bazel toolchains: one plan-tree grammar, swappable per-domain rulesets** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal Bazel's separation of the BUILD graph from the toolchain that executes each rule. The plan-tree, contracts, stitch, and green-wave stay 100% domain-agnostic; what makes a leaf 'an app component' vs 'a deck slide' vs 'a research claim' is a swappable RULESET (rules_code, rules_deck, rules_research, rules_art) declaring that domain's leaf-shape, contract type, 'one-shot' builder, and definition-of-green. Adding a domain = registering a ruleset, never touching the engine. **Why better:** The assumption asserts generality but smuggles code-specific machinery (typed signatures, DB schema, failing tests, per-stack templates) into the core — that's a code factory with art bolted on. Bazel proves ONE graph engine can have N toolchains, making generality structural and provable rather than aspirational.
- **VFX 'department + dailies' pipeline: domains as departments, review as the gate** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Model the factory like a film VFX house: a single shot (the artifact) flows through departments (layout->anim->FX->lighting->comp), each with its own tools but a shared shot-tree and a daily 'review' gate where a supervisor signs off before it advances. Map domains/skills to departments, the scope-preview gate to 'dailies'. A mixed artifact (an app WITH art WITH a launch deck WITH a research-backed pricing decision) is ONE shot routed through multiple departments on the same tree. **Why better:** Current generality is parallel-but-siloed, implying separate runs for code vs art vs research. VFX pipelines routinely composite heterogeneous outputs into ONE deliverable with one review cadence — exactly what 'build my launch: the feature + its art + the announce deck + the go/no-go' needs.
- **USD/OpenAsset: one composable scene-graph every domain layers onto** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow Pixar's USD: a single composable graph where each department contributes a non-destructive LAYER and the final artifact is the composited stack. The plan-tree IS the scene; code, copy, art, and data each write a layer keyed to the same node; graphify becomes the composition engine. Any domain can 'override' a node's layer without forking the tree, and stitch is a deterministic layer-merge — the same node carries a typed signature AND a Figma frame AND a cited rationale AND a Lottie. **Why better:** graphify is currently code-centric (a code/knowledge graph + as-built verification). USD-style layering makes the graph genuinely multi-modal and non-destructive — which is what 'factory for anything' actually requires structurally.
- **Cell differentiation: stem-leaves that specialize from a morphogen gradient** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every leaf starts as an identical 'stem unit'; its domain identity (code/art/research/copy) is INDUCED by a positional signal flowing down from the root intent (the 'morphogen gradient'), not assigned by a brittle classifier. A leaf near 'visual identity' differentiates into art; one near 'pricing rationale' into research — and a leaf can TRANS-differentiate if the gradient shifts mid-run (intent edit). **Why better:** The altitude dial auto-classifies node TYPE statically and up front, but real intents are mixed and shift. Gradient-driven late-binding handles ambiguous and changing intent gracefully — type emerges from context rather than being guessed once and frozen wrong.
- **Temporal/LangGraph: domain-agnostic durable engine, domain logic only in 'activities'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Adopt Temporal's hard wall: the WORKFLOW (tree traversal, contract freeze, dispatch, stitch, failover, retry, replay) is pure orchestration that knows nothing about domains; all domain knowledge lives in pluggable ACTIVITIES with a uniform contract->artifact signature. The engine gets durable replay and crash-safety for free, and proving generality reduces to 'the workflow never imports a domain.' **Why better:** Right now generality is an assumption with no enforcement boundary, so code-isms leak into the core (per-stack template, DB schema in the contract). The workflow/activity split gives a TESTABLE invariant for domain-agnosticism plus durable resume — turning 'it's general' into an architectural guarantee, with crash-recovery as a bonus.
- **Andon cord + poka-yoke: stop the line, and make wrong-domain stitches physically impossible** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — From the Toyota Production System: give every worker an ANDON CORD to halt the green-wave when a leaf's contract is unbuildable in its domain, surfacing it at the cockpit instead of shipping a defect upward; and add POKA-YOKE — domain-typed connectors so an art artifact literally cannot be stitched into a code socket (the slot's shape rejects it). Generality is enforced by mechanical fit, not convention. **Why better:** A universal factory's scariest failure is cross-domain mis-stitching (a research bullet welded where a component belongs) propagating silently up the green-wave. Andon + poka-yoke catches the one bug class that ONLY exists because the factory is general, and stops it at the source.
- **Spotify-model 'guild' skills: domain expertise travels horizontally** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of hard-binding one skill to one node-type, model skills as GUILDS any node can consult across the tree. An 'accessibility guild' reviews the app floor AND the deck AND the research figures; a 'narrative guild' tightens copy in code UI, slide text, and report prose alike. Cross-cutting concerns (a11y, brand voice, security) span domains horizontally rather than being re-implemented per domain. **Why better:** Per-node, per-domain skill-attachment silos quality and forces every new domain to re-derive cross-cutting standards. Guild-style horizontal skills mean adding a research or deck domain instantly inherits the a11y, brand, and security guilds instead of starting from zero.
- **Kill 'everything.' Build the one-domain factory that actually wins.** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Delete the generality claim outright. The factory manufactures exactly ONE class of artifact end-to-end (e.g. a Tower floor / a Next.js feature) and knows that domain to the bone — its DB schema, ship gate, golden template, review rubric. 'Factory for anything' is the feature that guarantees it does nothing excellently. **Why better:** The only parts of this system with real teeth are the specialized ones — ArtLab's 10-phase state machine, byte-protected assets, tower-ship-gate. A factory's value is in the jigs cut for ONE part. A deep single-domain factory ships finished artifacts; a universal one ships an orchestration framework that still needs a human to fill in the domain every time.
- **It already exists and it's called forge — merge, don't invent** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The 'universal factory' is forge (already deliberately domain-agnostic: 'best way to use AI agents on ANY task') with a plan-tree and a swarm bolted on. There is no second product. Fold the recursion + contracts + orchestrator into forge's T3 tier and the cockpit into forge's existing dashboard, and stop pretending it's a new thing. **Why better:** Two domain-agnostic 'do anything' engines in one toolkit is one too many — they'll fight over the same triggers (forge already routes into impeccable/artlab/grill-me, exactly what the factory claims). Merging removes a whole conceptual surface, a router-collision class, and a parallel cockpit.
- **Honest scope: it's ONLY a factory for typed, testable, stack-bound code** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Frozen top-down contracts = typed signatures + DB schema + failing tests + golden per-stack template. None of that exists for an art campaign, report, deck, or decision. Invert the claim: the factory is ONLY a factory for code where the green-wave guarantee actually holds. Everything else is an LLM with extra steps wearing a factory costume. **Why better:** The single mechanism that makes autonomous parallel build safe (failing tests turning green) is undefined for non-code domains. Claiming generality smuggles code's rigor into domains where it evaporates, so the swarm runs blind and you discover slop at stitch time. Honest scope = the guarantee actually holds.
- **Generality is a demo, not a product — delete the cross-domain examples** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The art/research/decks/decisions spread exists to sound impressive in a pitch, not because anyone needs an AI deck-factory with frozen DB contracts. Cut every non-core example. If 90% of real runs are 'build/change a Tower feature,' the rest is demoware taxing the classifier, router, and test suite for zero shipped value. **Why better:** Every domain you claim to serve is one you must classify, route, gate, and regression-test, multiplying the failure surface and the classifier's confusion (is 'write the war-room copy' a deck, a feature, or art?). Narrowing to observed demand makes the classifier nearly trivial and the whole thing more reliable.
- **Invert the I/O: an artifact CRITIC, not an artifact factory** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop manufacturing from intent. Ship the reverse, valuable, genuinely-domain-agnostic half: take an EXISTING artifact (a built floor, a draft deck, a written decision) and verify it against a contract graph — graphify's 'does reality match the contract?' Let humans/other tools build; you own the verification. **Why better:** The verification half IS domain-agnostic (any artifact can be checked against any contract) and is where almost no tool competes, whereas 'intent in, app out' is the most crowded category in AI right now. Inverting from generator to critic moves generality to where it's defensible and where the moat (the contract graph) actually lives.
- **One altitude only: delete T0–T3, the factory is T3-or-nothing** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — The altitude dial spanning T0 component to T3 whole-app is itself a bet on generality across scales. Invert it: a factory is justified ONLY where a swarm pays for itself (whole app / whole campaign). Anything smaller isn't a factory, it's a chat — route it out, no dial, no scope-preview gate, no swarm overhead. The cost gate becomes 'is this T3? yes/no.' **Why better:** The scope-preview gate, auto-classifier, and 'bias BIGGER' logic exist only because the system spans scales it shouldn't. forge's own law — process never exceeds output — is violated the instant you spin up worktrees and manifests for a T0 component edit.
- **Generality is the user's job — sell a kit, not a factory** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Ship a specialized, opinionated kit (the Tower-feature factory) PLUS the raw primitives — plan-tree recurser, contract-freezer, swarm orchestrator — and let advanced users assemble their OWN narrow factory for THEIR domain. The product is honest: specific-plus-a-toolbox, never a magic everything-machine. **Why better:** This relocates generality from a fragile runtime promise ('it'll figure out your domain') to a composable build-time capability ('here are the parts, here's one assembled example'). Users who need art or research wire their own gates and golden templates — which they understand better than any auto-classifier could infer — and the primitives stay sharp because each assembly is allowed to be specific.

### Learning across runs

_Current assumption: OPEN — does the factory learn? does the golden template evolve over time?_

**Boldest reframe:** The factory dreams (offline self-play between runs). Borrowing AlphaZero, the factory invents its own synthetic intents while idle, builds them in throwaway worktrees, has Codex adversarially attack the output, and harvests failures into template/test improvements — so it gets sharper on its own compute budget with zero real work. This is the boldest because it severs the learning rate from your usage entirely: every other idea learns FROM runs you trigger, but this one MANUFACTURES the runs to learn from, turning idle quota into a permanently rising quality floor. It is the single reframe that makes the factory an autonomous self-improving organism rather than an instrumented tool.

- **Outcome ledger: stamp every leaf with what actually happened** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After each leaf finishes, append one line to a per-project ledger: node-type, model that built it, one-shot vs rounds-to-green, stitch outcome (clean / contract-broke), and wall-time. This is exhaust the factory already produces but currently throws away. It becomes the substrate every later learning idea queries. **Why better:** The current memory only keeps human-curated decisions and gotchas; it has zero record of build OUTCOMES. Without a ledger of what worked, the factory literally cannot learn anything quantitative across runs — this is the missing primary key.
- **Golden template changelog → one-click fold-in** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf built from the golden per-stack template needed >1 round to pass review, capture the diff between the template and the green result as a candidate template patch. Surface those patches for a one-click 'fold into golden' so the template provably improves run over run instead of staying static. **Why better:** The hypothesis freezes golden templates top-down and never feeds them back. If every run reveals the same boilerplate gap and the template never absorbs it, the factory re-pays the same fix tax forever. A changelog turns the template into a learning surface.
- **Recall the closest PAST plan-tree as a starting skeleton** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before decomposing a new intent, retrieve the most similar prior plan-tree (by intent embedding) and offer its shape as a starting skeleton — same sections, same leaf boundaries — for the architect to adapt rather than decompose cold. **Why better:** Today recall returns loose one-line facts. The expensive, judgment-heavy part of a run is the decomposition itself, and similar intents decompose similarly. Reusing a proven tree shape is the single highest-leverage thing to carry across runs.
- **Self-tuning two-model router learned from win-rate (bandit)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat every node-type → model assignment as a bandit arm. Per leaf-category, track which model (Claude vs Codex) produced first-pass-green output more often, faster, and cheaper, and continuously reallocate routing — discovering non-obvious splits like 'Codex wins Zod schemas, Claude wins copy-under-constraints, but only above altitude T2.' The $200/$100 quota flows to where each model is empirically strongest, without overriding the human's altitude choice. **Why better:** Routing is currently a fixed heuristic (Claude architects fuzzy tops, Codex builds explicit leaves) frozen by a human's prior. A bandit that learns real win-rates per category extracts more quality and more dollars from the exact same two models, and adapts as the models themselves change under you.
- **Stitch-failure auto-authors its own gotcha note** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a contract breaks at stitch (the green wave stalls because a signature didn't match the frozen contract), auto-author a gotcha note describing the mismatch class — so the next contract-freezing pass is warned before it makes the same shape of mistake. **Why better:** Gotchas are currently hand-remembered by a human noticing a trap. Stitch failures are the factory's own most painful, most repeatable traps and it has perfect ground truth on them. Self-authoring closes the loop with zero human curation.
- **Scope-preview gate calibrated from past actuals** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The cheap pre-swarm scope gate currently estimates leaf count and spend from heuristics. Feed it the actual leaf-count and token totals from completed runs of the same altitude tier so the estimate it shows the human gets more accurate every run. **Why better:** The scope gate is the moment the human decides whether to spend. An estimate that learns from its own past misses is strictly more trustworthy than a fixed heuristic — and trust at that gate is what lets the human bias bigger with confidence.
- **Per-stack 'failing-test starter kit' that accretes (Andon cord)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Keep, per stack, the set of contract/acceptance tests that have caught real regressions across runs (RLS-on-every-table, JSX-namespace import, no-Drizzle-in-serverless, IPv6 DB, Tailwind v3). Borrowing the Toyota Andon cord + poka-yoke: when a leaf fails review or a human reverts post-stitch, the failure writes a new permanent failing test / lint rule / graphify invariant into the contract layer, so new runs of that stack inherit the accumulated set as a baseline and that exact defect can NEVER recur. **Why better:** Contracts are frozen fresh per run today, so a trap caught last run isn't caught this run unless someone re-remembers it. An accreting, executable test kit makes the factory's safety net monotonically stronger and makes the lesson load-bearing and unskippable — the swarm physically cannot ship the old bug, vs. merely being reminded not to.
- **End-of-run retro digest the human can accept or reject** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At run completion, emit a 3-line digest — 'what the template/router/tests should learn from this run' — drawn from the ledger, and gate each line behind a single keystroke before it's written durable. Learning stays opt-in and auditable, never silent drift. **Why better:** The current remember step is the agent's unilateral guess at what mattered. A structured, accept/reject retro makes cross-run learning legible and reversible — so the factory can learn aggressively without the human fearing it's quietly poisoning its own templates.
- **Golden template as a fitness-selected genome (fork, shadow-run, keep the winner)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every per-stack golden template is a living, git-versioned genome, not a frozen asset. Each run forks it; the swarm's diffs, verifier PASS/FAIL, post-stitch green-wave time, reviewer edits and human reverts become a fitness score. Periodically promote the highest-fitness mutation to canonical, run variant templates in shadow on the next eligible leaf, keep the winner, and archive the lineage so you can roll back a regression. The 'golden' template at run 100 is provably better than the one a human wrote at run 1. **Why better:** Today the template is hand-frozen and static — it can only decay relative to reality, and a single evolving template hill-climbs into local optima. A fitness-selected population means the single highest-leverage artifact in the whole factory compounds automatically and can discover structures a human would never author, with the run itself as the fitness function and a safe rollback lineage.
- **Failure-to-Contract distillation (the factory writes its own failing tests)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every time a leaf fails Codex verification or a stitch breaks the green wave, the factory distills the root cause into a NEW reusable failing test plus a contract clause and injects it into the relevant golden template's test suite forever. The corpus of failing tests is the factory's scar tissue — it can never make the same class of mistake twice across any future run. **Why better:** Current memory stores human-written one-line gotchas that a model must remember to recall and apply. Encoding the lesson as an executable FAILING TEST inside the frozen contract makes the learning load-bearing and unskippable — the swarm physically cannot ship the old bug, vs. merely being reminded not to.
- **Cross-run pattern mining → automatic skill birth** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A background process clusters every leaf the factory has ever built. When a cluster crosses a frequency threshold (e.g. 'idempotent Stripe webhook with replay guard' built 8 times), the factory auto-synthesizes a new parameterized worker/skill for that pattern, with its own golden template and tests, and routes future matching leaves to it instead of one-shotting cold. The factory grows its own org chart of specialists. **Why better:** Today every leaf is one-shot cold against a generic stack template, repeating work. A factory that crystallizes recurring leaves into purpose-built specialists turns repetition into permanent leverage — the 9th instance of a pattern is near-free and higher quality than the 1st.
- **The Regret Replay Engine** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Keep an immutable log of every plan-tree the factory ever expanded. Periodically re-plan a PAST intent with the CURRENT (now-smarter) factory and diff the two plan-trees: where would today's factory decompose differently, freeze a different contract, or stop recursion sooner? That structural delta — 'regret' — is itself a training signal that retunes the planner's altitude and decomposition heuristics. **Why better:** Current learning only captures explicit facts a human chose to remember. Regret replay learns from the SHAPE of past decisions — the decomposition and altitude calls invisible to the note store — so the planner's judgment, not just its facts, compounds, which is where most factory quality actually lives.
- **Shared graphify as a federated learning brain across all factories** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The code/knowledge graph isn't per-repo — it's a federated organism. Every user's factory contributes anonymized, abstracted build patterns (contract shapes, decomposition strategies, which two-model route won), and every factory pulls down the global priors. A solo founder's 50th-percentile factory inherits the accumulated taste of 10,000 runs it never made. **Why better:** Today the graph and memory are siloed to one repo and one user, so learning is bounded by personal run-count. Federating the graph turns the factory into a network-effect organism where every run anywhere makes every factory everywhere smarter — a moat the static-template approach can never have.
- **Taste injection — learn the OWNER's aesthetic, not just correctness** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every human edit AFTER a run ships (the diffs Armaan applies to the factory's output before he's happy) is captured as a 'taste gradient.' The factory learns a personal style model — naming, file layout, comment density, which abstractions you actually like — and bakes it into the golden templates so future output arrives already in your voice, needing fewer post-edits each run. **Why better:** Current learning captures correctness facts and explicit decisions but is blind to the silent tax of 'technically right but not how I'd do it.' Learning from post-ship human edits closes the gap between 'passes tests' and 'I'd have written this myself' — the difference between a tool and a co-founder.
- **The factory dreams (offline self-play between runs)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When idle, the factory runs synthetic intents against itself — invents plausible feature requests, builds them in throwaway worktrees, lets Codex adversarially attack the output, and harvests the failures into template and test improvements. Like AlphaZero self-play, it gets better while you sleep, so Monday-morning's factory is sharper than Friday's even with zero real work in between. **Why better:** Today the factory only learns from real runs you happen to trigger, so learning rate is capped by your usage. Offline self-play decouples improvement from demand — the factory compounds on its own compute budget, turning idle quota into a permanently rising quality floor.
- **The template is a moving average of what shipped green, not a file** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the hand-authored golden per-stack template. Replace it with a synthesized artifact that is literally the moving average of leaf code that shipped green and survived cross-model review unedited. Each run nudges the template toward what actually passed; choices that got rewritten in review decay out. **Why better:** The current assumption treats 'best practice' as a fixed thing a human wrote once; we never instrument which template choices survived. A template that is a measured artifact of what shipped green is always current and never drifts from reality, because reality is its only input.
- **The run IS the gradient — no separate learning loop** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the idea that learning is a distinct phase. Every run already emits failing-test deltas, review diffs, and failover events. Treat the shared manifest as the weights and each run's scar (a contract that broke, a leaf that mis-cut) as a gradient step applied immediately. There is no 'train' vs 'infer' — running and learning are one act. **Why better:** The OPEN assumption inherits ML's train/infer split for no reason — the factory isn't a model, it's a process that already produces dense signal. Collapsing the two means improvement is continuous and free, not a batched offline job nobody remembers to run.
- **Learn the scar tissue, not the green waves** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Mine learning exclusively from where the recursion mis-cut a leaf, where a contract had to be renegotiated mid-build, and where a model failed over. Build a 'mistake graph' that the planner queries BEFORE freezing contracts, so it pre-avoids cuts that historically broke. Clean runs teach nothing; the re-plans teach everything. **Why better:** Current thinking would learn from successes because green=good. But a green run only confirms a path already worked — zero new information. The expensive, novel signal lives in the renegotiations. Learning from scars is strictly higher information-per-byte.
- **Local idioms beat global best practice** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the dominant learned artifact a per-repo idiom sheet (this codebase's naming, this reviewer's recurring nits, the Tower's 'never break the building metaphor' rule) rather than a universal stack template. The factory learns the GRAIN of the specific tree it's building in, queried by every worker via graphify. **Why better:** The assumption that learning is global conflates 'this stack' with 'this codebase.' The costliest review churn is almost always local convention violations, not framework misuse. Learning the local grain eliminates the churn that a global template can never see.
- **Every verifier VERDICT trains the planner's altitude dial** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Feed the verifier's pass/fail and the count of mid-run re-plans back into the auto-classifier that picks T0-T3 and decides where recursion stops. If leaves at a given altitude keep coming back red, the dial learns to cut smaller there next time; if they one-shot clean, it learns to cut bigger. **Why better:** Today the altitude/stop-recursion heuristic is static. But 'is this leaf Codex-ready?' is exactly a prediction graded every single run by whether the leaf built. Closing that loop makes the single most consequential decision (where to stop splitting) self-calibrating instead of a fixed guess.
- **Learn the router division-of-labor itself, not just the templates** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the Claude-architects / Codex-builds split as a learnable policy, not a law. Log which model actually produced the leaf that survived review for each node-type and let the router shift dispatch over time — maybe Codex should architect a certain fuzzy node-type, maybe Claude should build a certain explicit one. **Why better:** The two-model router currently hard-codes 'Claude fuzzy top, Codex explicit leaves' as an axiom, but every run grades that assignment via review survival. Making the router learn from outcomes could overturn the very split the factory was designed around — exactly the kind of assumption a first-principles pass should expose.
- **A forgetting curve so the factory un-learns stale lessons** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every learned fact (idiom, scar, template choice) a decay rate tied to how recently it was confirmed by a green run. Lessons that stop being reinforced fade; a framework upgrade or a reversed convention lets old learning die instead of fossilizing into cargo-cult rules. **Why better:** Every 'learning' design silently assumes accumulation is good. But a factory that only accumulates eventually drowns in obsolete rules from dead framework versions and reverted conventions. Active forgetting keeps the learned corpus honest and small — the calmest corpus and the most correct corpus are the same corpus.
- **Bazel remote-cache for plan-subtrees (content-addressed leaf memoization)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hash every leaf-contract (typed signature + schema slice + failing test) into a content-addressed key, exactly like Bazel/Turborepo cache build artifacts. If an identical or near-identical leaf was ever built green before, replay the cached artifact instead of re-spending a model. The cache key IS the contract, so reuse is provably safe. **Why better:** The current assumption rebuilds everything every run. Bazel-style content-addressing turns repeated leaves (a CRUD endpoint, a glass card, a Zod schema) into near-free cache hits, so cost and latency drop monotonically as the factory sees more work — learning without any fragile 'training' step.
- **Temporal-style durable run history as the training corpus** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Like Temporal/Inngest persisting every workflow event, log the full event history of each factory run — every contract frozen, every leaf dispatched, every failover, every reviewer verdict — as replayable, queryable records. 'Learning' becomes querying this history before each new run to retrieve the closest past run and reuse its decisions. **Why better:** Beats a vague 'does it learn?' by making memory concrete and auditable: the factory learns by REPLAYING durable history, so every improvement is traceable to a specific past run, and you can re-derive why a template changed instead of trusting an opaque weights blob.
- **VFX 'shot library' of reusable golden leaves with render-farm dedup** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal the film/VFX asset-library pattern: maintain a curated library of blessed leaf artifacts (a 'shot') tagged by node-type, stack, and visual/functional register. Before the swarm spends, a render-farm-style dispatcher checks the library and pulls an existing shot, only generating net-new shots. **Why better:** Unlike a single monolithic golden template, a SHOT LIBRARY lets the factory learn at fine granularity — the best war-room table, the best onboarding empty-state — and compose proven parts, which is how VFX houses ship faster every film without re-animating from scratch.
- **LangGraph-style per-skill episodic 'lessons' vector store** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give each worker skill (impeccable, artlab, improve-codebase-architecture) an episodic memory store of compact 'lessons' distilled from past runs ('CRO floor tables always need a sticky header', 'this Drizzle pattern breaks on Vercel IPv6'). Before building, the worker retrieves the top-k lessons for its node-type and prepends them to its spec. **Why better:** Improves on a one-size golden template by letting learning be PER-SKILL and retrieval-based — exactly like the repo's own pull-based memory system — so a frontend worker learns design lessons while a schema worker learns migration lessons, without bloating every prompt with irrelevant history.
- **Nx affected-graph: learn co-fragile leaves and pre-bundle their contracts** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Mine the run history for which leaves historically break together when one changes (Nx's 'affected' dependency analysis). Learn these co-fragility clusters and, on future runs, freeze their contracts as a single coordinated bundle and assign them to one worker, instead of discovering the coupling mid-build. **Why better:** The current bottom-up parallel build can thrash when hidden cross-leaf coupling surfaces late. Learning the affected-graph from history lets the factory anticipate coupling and partition work correctly up front — turning painful re-stitches into a planning-time decision, which a golden template alone never captures.
- **Amnesia by design: the factory should never learn** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete cross-run learning entirely. Every run starts from a frozen, hand-curated golden template and zero memory of prior runs. The only thing that 'improves' is the human-edited template in git, reviewed like any other code. **Why better:** A learning factory accumulates silent drift — it overfits to your last 20 internship-app-shaped runs and quietly gets worse at the next novel thing, with no one able to point at WHICH commit made it dumber. Statelessness makes every run reproducible, auditable, and bisectable; quality becomes a code-review problem (which you're already good at) instead of an unobservable ML-ops problem you'd have to build a whole eval/rollback apparatus to babysit.
- **The golden template is a liability — kill it; let the contract be the only spec** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Remove the per-stack golden template as a first-class artifact. Stop maintaining a canonical 'how a leaf should look.' Let the contract (typed signature + failing tests) be the ONLY spec, and let each worker write idiomatically from scratch against it. **Why better:** A golden template is a second source of truth that fights the contract. The moment they disagree, workers cargo-cult the template's stale shape over the test's intent. If tests fully pin behavior, the template adds nothing but a calcified style that resists the next framework upgrade. Deleting it forces the contract to be genuinely complete — the real invariant you want — and removes an entire maintenance surface.
- **Learning belongs to the model vendor, not your factory** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert ownership: assume Claude/Codex get materially better every few months on their own. Your factory's job is to stay a thin, stateless harness so it inherits those gains for free — any learning layer you build is a moat you'll be maintaining against your own suppliers' roadmap. **Why better:** A bespoke learning loop is depreciating capital: every base-model upgrade can obsolete or actively conflict with the patterns you've memorized (your 'learned' 2026 React idioms become anti-patterns by 2027). Betting the improvement curve on the frontier labs, while you stay thin, means you compound their R&D budget instead of your own — and you never have to migrate a learned corpus across model generations.
- **Merge 'learning' into graphify and delete it as a separate aspect** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no learning subsystem. The only memory is graphify's as-built code/knowledge graph of the CURRENT repo. Workers 'learn' purely by querying what already shipped in this codebase — not by abstracting patterns across unrelated runs. **Why better:** Cross-run learning and graphify are the same instinct (reuse prior knowledge) implemented twice. Graphify is grounded — it points at real, compiled, tested code in THIS repo. A separate learning store is ungrounded — it stores 'lessons' that may not even typecheck anymore. Collapsing learning into 'just query the graph of what's real' gives you all the reuse benefit with none of the hallucinated-wisdom risk, and one fewer subsystem to build.
- **Learn what to FORGET, not what to keep (a growing denylist)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the polarity of the learning objective. Instead of accumulating successful patterns, the factory only ever records anti-patterns to ban — a growing denylist of 'never do this again,' enforced as lint rules and failing tests, never as suggested-defaults. **Why better:** Positive learning ('do it like last time') causes regression to the mean and homogenizes output toward whatever was merely adequate before. Negative learning is monotonic and safe: a banned mistake can't make a novel run worse, only stops a known failure. It also converts fuzzy 'wisdom' into hard, testable gates — the only form of learning your green-wave/contract architecture can actually verify.
- **Per-run learning, incinerated at the run boundary** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Keep aggressive in-run learning (a worker that solved a leaf teaches its siblings within the same plan-tree) but burn ALL of it the instant the run completes. Memory's lifetime equals one run's lifetime; nothing crosses the run boundary. **Why better:** Almost all the real value of 'learning' is local — sharing the contract interpretation and gotchas among the 30 workers building ONE app right now, judged by the same run's green wave. Cross-run persistence is where the danger lives (drift, staleness, leakage between unrelated projects). Scoping memory to the run captures the upside and structurally eliminates the downside.
- **The factory should get DUMBER on purpose (scheduled template bankruptcy)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Periodically reset the template and any heuristics back to a deliberately naive baseline ('template bankruptcy'), forcing the system to re-derive solutions from first principles against current models and current docs. **Why better:** Accumulated learning is accumulated assumption — it bakes in 'this is how we've always done it' and blinds the factory to better approaches the ecosystem invented since. A scheduled amnesia event is the only forcing function that guarantees the factory periodically re-evaluates against the present instead of compounding the past.
- **Learning is a UI feature, not a factory feature — human-gated promotion** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The factory never auto-applies anything it 'learned.' Instead, the cockpit shows the human candidate patterns ('you've now built 6 floors this exact way — promote to template?') and the ONLY way a lesson enters the system is a deliberate human click that writes it to git. **Why better:** Autonomous learning is where a 'universal factory' silently goes off the rails with no kill switch. Routing every lesson through a human gate keeps the architect as sole curator of taste, makes every improvement an inspectable diff, and turns 'learning' from an opaque self-modifying loop into a high-signal recommendation feed — full autonomy DURING a run, human-in-the-loop ACROSS runs.

### Cost, budgets, quotas

_Current assumption: balance Claude $200 + Codex $100; token budgets per run._

**Boldest reframe:** One model, no failover — the two-economy is the bug. The entire cost subsystem (balancing, failover, cross-model StructuredOutput gotchas, quota arbitrage) exists only because two providers were chosen. Collapsing to a single capable model deletes the whole aspect — the router's job shrinks from "pick a model and watch its wallet" to "pick a skill." It's the boldest because it doesn't optimize the cost machinery, it argues the cost machinery is self-inflicted and should not exist; every other idea presupposes the two-wallet world this one refuses.

- **Scope-gate quotes dollars, not vibes** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the existing pre-swarm scope-preview gate print a concrete cost+time estimate before spending — e.g. 'T2, 14 leaves, ~9 Codex + 5 Claude, est. $3.10 / ~480K tokens, ~12 min'. The estimate comes from per-tier historical averages (tokens-per-leaf by node-type) recorded from past runs. **Why better:** The current assumption is an abstract per-run token cap. A dollar+time quote at the one gate the human already looks at turns budget from an invisible guardrail into an actual decision, and prevents surprise spend on a misclassified T3.
- **Per-node-type token budgets summed bottom-up, not one run budget** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the single per-run token budget with a small budget table keyed by node-type/skill (a ui-ux leaf gets X, an architecture leaf gets Y, a Codex verify lane gets Z). The orchestrator sums leaf budgets bottom-up to derive the run budget instead of guessing one number — which also makes the scope-gate estimate additive and accurate. **Why better:** A flat run budget either starves heavy leaves or over-funds trivial ones. Budgeting at the unit where work actually happens (the leaf) makes the cap meaningful and the estimate composable.
- **Failover triggers on quota headroom reserve, not just hard 429** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Add a soft threshold: when Claude's $200 (or Codex's $100) window drops below a reserve (~15%), the router proactively shifts NEW leaf dispatch to the other model and keeps the scarce one for what only it can do (Claude for fuzzy-top architecting, Codex for explicit builds). **Why better:** Hard-429 failover means a run stalls or degrades mid-swarm with no warning. A reserve threshold keeps the scarce model available for the work it's uniquely good at, so you never burn your last Claude headroom on a leaf Codex could have built.
- **Run-level kill-switch budget with graceful stitch to the green subtree** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Let the user set a hard ceiling ('don't spend more than $5 / 1M tokens'). When the orchestrator projects a breach, it stops dispatching NEW leaves, finishes in-flight ones, and stitches whatever green subtree exists — returning a partial-but-coherent artifact plus a 'resume from here for $X more' note. **Why better:** A pure token budget that halts mid-build leaves a broken tree and wasted spend. Degrading to the largest already-green subtree means money spent always buys a shippable partial, and the run is resumable rather than thrown away.
- **Cheap-model dry-run pass before the expensive swarm** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before freezing contracts, run plan-tree decomposition and contract-drafting on the cheapest tier (Haiku / Codex-mini), then escalate to the $200/$100 tiers only for actual leaf builds. The expensive models judge the cheap draft rather than authoring it from scratch. **Why better:** Premium quota is currently spent on both the fuzzy-top structuring AND the build. Drafting structure cheaply and reserving premium quota for leaf execution and judgment stretches both budgets — decomposition is far more forgiving of a weaker model than one-shot leaf code is.
- **Persist a self-calibrating cost ledger per run and per node-type** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After every run, write actual tokens+dollars by node-type and model to a small ledger (forge's retrieval-based memory can hold it). The scope-gate estimator reads it back, so estimates and per-node budgets self-calibrate over time instead of relying on static priors. **Why better:** Static per-tier averages drift as the codebase and prompts change. A self-updating ledger makes both the dollar quote and the per-node budgets get more accurate every run — the one thing static config can never do.
- **Charge retries to a separate retry/rework budget and surface it** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Track build-and-verify failure loops (Codex fails a leaf, Claude rebuilds) against a distinct rework budget separate from first-pass build spend. Surface 'rework spend' in the cockpit so a flaky contract eating 40% of the run in retries becomes visible and attributable. **Why better:** A single run budget hides WHERE money goes; rework from a bad frozen contract is the most expensive failure mode and is invisible today. Splitting it out turns 'we're over budget' into 'leaf X's contract is bad — fix it,' which is actionable.
- **Quota-aware altitude downgrade at the gate** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — If the user picks T3 (whole app) but combined Claude+Codex headroom can't cover the estimate, the altitude dial offers a downgrade path: 'You have budget for ~T2 today — build the 3 highest-value floors now, queue the rest?' rather than starting a run it can't finish. **Why better:** The 'bias bigger' default plus a finite $300/month of combined quota means ambitious runs can start and die half-built. Tying altitude to actual remaining headroom converts an unaffordable big run into an affordable sequenced one — strictly better than a stalled swarm.
- **The budget is a binding QUOTE, with a bid-to-build internal spot market** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before the swarm spends, the factory produces a binding quote: 'this app, this quality bar, ~$4.10, 22 minutes.' The user accepts a price, not a token cap. Internally each leaf is an open job that Claude-workers and Codex-workers 'bid' on with estimated cost/confidence, and the cheapest qualified bidder wins. Model-routing arbitrage is hidden under a guaranteed deliverable price. **Why better:** Current assumption treats budget as a defensive ceiling you hope not to hit. A quoted price makes cost a first-class product promise, turns the $200+$100 pools into a tradable supply curve, and lets the system self-optimize which model does each job on price/quality — instead of a static 'Claude tops, Codex leaves' rule.
- **Speculative execution budget — spend a recon slice to kill branches early** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Allocate a small 'recon' slice (~5%) to fire cheap parallel probes down MULTIPLE plan-tree branches before committing, then kill the losers and pour the remaining 95% into the winner. Like CPU branch prediction: deliberately waste a little compute up front to avoid the catastrophic cost of building the wrong architecture and discovering it at stitch time. **Why better:** Per-run budgets meter a single committed plan. But the worst failure mode isn't token waste — it's building 40 leaves against a wrong contract and finding the mismatch at stitch. Spending recon to de-risk the contract is dramatically cheaper than a full rebuild.
- **Quota weather forecast + pre-buy at the cycle boundary** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Model the combined $200 Claude + $100 Codex pool as a single depletable reservoir with a burn-rate forecast, warning 'at this pace both pools dry out Thursday 4pm — this run alone consumes 18% of remaining monthly headroom.' Defer non-urgent leaves to off-peak windows and pre-stage prompts so they fire the instant quota refreshes. **Why better:** The current 'balance Claude + Codex' assumption is reactive — failover only fires once a wall is hit. A forecast turns quota into a managed runway with visible runout dates, so the user makes informed go/no-go calls instead of getting surprised mid-build by a dead pool.
- **Value-weighted spend — pay more where a bug is catastrophic** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Tag each node with a blast-radius/criticality score (auth, schema, payments, the contract layer = expensive; a marketing footer = cheap) and route the premium model + extra review passes ONLY to high-stakes nodes, deliberately starving low-stakes leaves. Money flows to risk, not uniformly across the tree. **Why better:** A flat per-run token budget spends the same on a login flow as on a 404 page. Concentrating dollars where a bug is catastrophic — and one-shotting trivial nodes with the cheapest model — buys more correctness per dollar than uniform metering.
- **The self-funding factory — a content-addressed parts warehouse** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each completed run leaves reusable contract-frozen artifacts (golden templates, verified leaves, graphify subgraphs). Treat the contract hash (signatures + schema + failing tests + golden template) as a Bazel/Nx-style cache key: any leaf whose hash matches a prior build is a cache HIT and costs ZERO tokens, pulled from the artifact store. graphify becomes the cache index; rebuilds only spend on the dirty subtree. **Why better:** Per-run budgets assume every node is built from scratch every run. Build systems prove most of a graph is unchanged between runs; content-addressed caching cuts spend by an order of magnitude on iterative runs and makes the 100th 'add a CRUD floor' a fraction of the 1st — something a fixed dollar split with no memory can never do.
- **Negative-cost reviewers — credit avoided-rework, review to break-even** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat cross-model review as deflationary, not additive. When Codex reviews Claude's output and catches a defect, credit the budget with the avoided rebuild cost — so a productive reviewer has negative net cost. The orchestrator dispatches MORE review the more review is paying for itself, dynamically, until reviews stop catching anything. **Why better:** Today Codex review is a pure add-on line item against the $100 pool. Accounting for avoided-rework makes the system spend on verification exactly up to its live-computed break-even — the economically optimal amount of adversarial checking, instead of a fixed pass.
- **Infinite-budget mode — overflow onto the whole internet's compute** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When local $200+$100 pools are exhausted but the user needs the artifact NOW, transparently overflow onto any reachable capacity — Vercel AI Gateway routing, the project's fal.ai FAL_KEY for generative leaves, spare API keys, paid spot inference — treated as one elastic quota pool with a unified dollar meter. The two named plans become just the cheapest tier of a bottomless supply. **Why better:** The two-plan assumption makes $300/mo a hard wall that stalls a run dead. An elastic overflow pool means budget never blocks a deliverable — you pay the marginal rate above subscription and the factory always picks the cheapest available watt per leaf.
- **The cost X-ray — a spend heatmap over the finished artifact** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Render the artifact with a per-component spend overlay in the cockpit: hover any component to see which model built it, tokens, retry/review cycles, and dollar cost. The plan-tree doubles as a P&L where you spot the one node that ate 40% of the budget and ask why. Pair with EVM-style CPI/SPI burndown for live forecasting. **Why better:** Today's per-run budget is one opaque number — you know you spent it, not WHERE. Per-node attribution turns budget from a meter into a debugging tool, surfacing pathological nodes (retry loops, over-reviewed leaves) so the next run's budget is allocated from evidence.
- **Budget time, not money — the rolling rate-limit window is the real currency** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop counting sunk dollars; count against each subscription's rolling token window. Model the two windows as refillable buckets and schedule the swarm to maximize work-done-before-refill, not work-done-per-dollar. Give each model a synthetic shadow-price that rises as its window depletes so the router load-balances both lanes instead of slamming one until 429. **Why better:** The assumption optimizes a phantom resource — marginal dollars are ~$0 on a flat subscription. The genuinely scarce resource is throughput inside the rate-limit window; budgeting the real constraint (and shadow-pricing it) lets more processes run without ever stalling on a wall.
- **Contracts carry a price tag — cost is frozen top-down with the spec** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a node's contract freezes (signatures + schema + failing tests + golden template), attach a predicted token cost to it. The whole-tree budget is the SUM of leaf prices, known before the swarm spends a token, making the scope-gate an itemized estimate and overruns attributable to specific leaves — budgeting becomes planning rather than panic-stopping. **Why better:** A per-run cap is a blunt circuit-breaker that fires at a meaningless boundary. Pricing each contract makes the gate show a real itemized number, makes overruns attributable, and turns budgeting into planning.
- **A near-free local third lane absorbs the trivial deterministic leaves** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Route the smallest, most deterministic leaves (boilerplate, type stubs, mechanical stitches, lint-fixes — the cheap ~60%) to a free local model lane, reserving the paid $200/$100 windows for the fuzzy-top architecture and cross-model review. **Why better:** Treating quota as two fixed buckets makes the constraint binary. A near-free elastic lane for the cheap majority of leaves multiplies effective capacity without touching the bill, and reserves the expensive models for the work only they can do.
- **Speculative overspend on the critical path (PERT/CPM critical-path budgeting)** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Compute the plan-tree's critical path and deliberately overshoot on the longest-pole subtree — more parallel workers, redundant racing attempts, larger context, premium model, retries — while starving off-path branches with the cheap model and one shot. Re-derive the critical path live as nodes finish, the way a factory re-plans around its current bottleneck. Budget is reallocated toward the bottleneck, not a uniform ceiling. **Why better:** A flat per-run budget spends evenly across branches with wildly different value-to-finish. Concentrating spend on the critical path finishes the whole artifact sooner — the only metric that matters when dollars are already sunk.
- **Self-funding runs authorized by an ROI value ledger** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A run may spend extra budget IF it produces an artifact that justifies it (a shipped feature, a sellable deck, a closed decision). The cockpit tracks a running value ledger so a high-yield run can borrow against future cheap windows, and low-value runs get rate-limited. Budget by ROI instead of a uniform cap. **Why better:** The fixed $200+$100 framing treats every run as equal-cost, equal-value. Tying spend authorization to output value pours resources into the runs that matter and throttles the ones that don't.
- **Drop the spend cap; cap only the irreversible blast radius** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Remove the token budget as a spend-limiter and replace it with a SAFETY budget: how many files/migrations/external calls a run may touch before a human checkpoint. Cost takes care of itself via the rolling window; the only thing worth capping is damage. **Why better:** The per-run token cap conflates two fears — 'too expensive' and 'too dangerous' — and the expensive fear is already void on a subscription. Splitting them lets cost run free while the real risk (autonomous changes) gets the guardrail it actually needs.
- **Auction the swarm — nodes bid worker slots with value-per-token** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the central budget allocator with a continuous internal market (steal Borg/Mesa spot schedulers). Each ready node bids (estimated value of finishing / estimated token cost); the orchestrator clears bids against the remaining pooled budget every tick and funds the highest value-per-token nodes first. Failed/abandoned nodes refund their unspent allocation; low-value speculative work runs only when budget is slack. **Why better:** A static split has no notion of marginal value — it'll spend the last $5 on a throwaway icon.
