A clearing market routes every dollar to its highest-value claimant in real time, reallocates as the tree unblocks, and naturally throttles speculative fan-out when budget is tight.
- **Preemptive token CPU scheduler with hierarchical (cgroup) quotas** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat Claude-$200 and Codex-$100 as two CPUs and tokens as time-slices. A central scheduler (steal Linux CFS / Kubernetes resource quotas) hands each node a token 'quantum,' preempts a worker that blows past its slice, and uses cgroup-style hierarchical quotas so a subtree can't starve its siblings. Priority = altitude tier x judged-risk, so T3 architecture nodes get fat slices and T0 components get thin ones automatically. **Why better:** The static $200/$100 split is a fixed partition that wastes idle capacity and lets one greedy node monopolize a model. A preemptive hierarchical scheduler keeps both models near 100% utilization, prevents subtree starvation, and makes 'more processes = more power' actually true under a fixed budget.
- **Quota circuit breakers + bulkheads + per-run error budget** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Wrap each model behind a circuit breaker that trips on 429/cost-velocity spikes and instantly fails over, with a bulkhead so a runaway subtree can only consume its compartment's budget before being isolated. Add an SRE-style per-run error budget: once burn-rate exceeds the SLO, the orchestrator auto-degrades (smaller model, fewer retries) instead of dying. **Why better:** Quota exhaustion mid-swarm can cascade and abort the whole run (large-output runs already abort in practice). Breakers + bulkheads contain the blast radius to one compartment and keep the green wave climbing on degraded power rather than collapsing.
- **Two-phase render-farm bid — measured pre-flight quote, batched passes** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Mirror a VFX render farm's per-frame cost estimate: each leaf does a near-zero-cost dry-run that estimates token cost and confidence, the cockpit aggregates these into a farm-wide cost+ETA quote shown at the gate ('~420K tokens / ~$3.10 / 18 min') before any expensive call, and cheap deterministic leaves get batched into one render pass to cut per-call overhead. **Why better:** The current scope-preview gate is a guess; render farms never spend without a per-frame estimate. A measured pre-flight quote turns the gate from vibes into an approvable number, and batching deterministic leaves cuts per-call overhead.
- **ATP energy economy — continuous metabolic backpressure** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Give the factory a single regenerating 'ATP' pool that refills at the real $/min quota velocity. Every model call burns ATP; when low, the system down-shifts metabolism (pause speculative branches, switch to cheaper model, coarsen leaf granularity) rather than hard-stopping; when flush, it spins up more parallel workers. Budget becomes a continuously-sensed pressure, not a hard wall. **Why better:** Static caps are binary — you have budget or you're dead. A metabolic pool gives graceful continuous backpressure that keeps a run alive and producing at reduced throughput instead of aborting, and auto-scales parallelism to whatever headroom exists.
- **Delete budgets entirely — run to done, read the bill after** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill the per-run token budget and pre-spend scope gate. The factory runs every node to completion and reports cost as a fact afterward, the way you read an AWS bill, not a permission slip you sign before each job. The real cap already lives at the subscription provider's $200/$100 hard stop; re-implementing a budget inside the orchestrator just rebuilds a wall one layer too high. **Why better:** Every budget check is a place the run can stall, mis-estimate, or refuse to finish what the user wanted. The plans already cap spend; a second internal cap only adds the failure mode of a half-built app that quit at 80% because a counter tripped. 'Intent in, finished artifact out' is violated the moment a token meter can veto the artifact.
- **Invert the gate — budget is an output, approve the costed tree not a guess** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The scope-preview gate currently asks 'may I spend?' before work. Flip it: the gate never blocks; the FIRST thing the swarm produces is a real costed plan-tree (the contracts pass is cheap), and the human approves the actual frozen-contract shape, not a guessed dollar figure. Cost is whatever it took to reach the green wave — a contractor's final invoice, not a pre-authorization. **Why better:** Pre-spend estimates on a recursive, data-dependent plan-tree are nearly always wrong, so the gate either over-blocks good runs or rubber-stamps bad ones. Approving the concrete frozen-contract tree is a real decision; approving a fictional token estimate is theater users learn to click through.
- **One model, no failover — the two-economy is the bug** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Collapse to one capable model for the whole tree. The Claude-$200 + Codex-$100 balancing and quota-failover machinery only exist because two providers were chosen; a single economy eliminates failover edge cases, the balance to tune, quota-arbitrage logic, and the known cross-model StructuredOutput/schema failure. The router's job shrinks from 'pick a model and watch its wallet' to 'pick a skill.' **Why better:** Half the cost complexity is self-inflicted engineering tax to load-balance two wallets you created — and the two-model split already causes real recurring failure (Codex+schema dies). A single economy has no failover edge cases, no balance to tune, and no arbitrage to maintain: the cheapest budget system is the one you never build.
- **Merge cost into altitude — they're the same dial** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Budget shouldn't be its own aspect; it's a shadow of the altitude dial. T0 is cheap, T3 is expensive by definition. Delete the separate token-budget config and let the altitude tier BE the cost contract — choosing T2 already chose your spend envelope. One honest scope dial (how big), not two that can disagree (how big AND how much). **Why better:** Two independent dials secretly measuring the same thing create incoherent states ('T3 app, but $5 budget') the system must reconcile with ugly heuristics. Folding cost into altitude means the user makes one scope choice and the money follows — exactly how they already reason.
- **Spend MORE on purpose — redundancy beats frugality** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the goal from minimizing tokens to maximizing first-wave trust: deliberately overspend by running the most uncertain leaves 3x in parallel and keeping the best, and having BOTH models build the same critical contract and diffing. The factory's pitch is 'more processes = more power'; a budget that fights that fights the thesis. **Why better:** A wrong artifact is infinitely more expensive than the tokens to triple-check it — the user's time re-running a failed swarm dwarfs any model bill. Treating spend as the constraint optimizes the cheap resource (tokens) at the expense of the precious one (a correct finished artifact you trust on the first wave).
- **No per-run budgets — one global standing burn-rate thermostat** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop scoping money per-run. The factory is always running something; give it ONE global burn-rate dial ('spend up to X/hour across all worktrees') like a thermostat, and let runs draw from the shared pool without any owning a budget. Runs never see a wallet; the orchestrator just throttles concurrency to hold the line. **Why better:** Per-run budgets fragment a continuous resource into dozens of wrong pre-allocations, stranding money in finished runs while a starving run blocks. A single burn-rate pool is self-balancing — hot work gets the tokens, idle worktrees cost nothing — and it's one number to reason about instead of N.
- **Make cost invisible — bake it into per-tier pricing, never surface it** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If the factory becomes a product, delete cost from the user's mental model entirely: charge a flat price per artifact tier and absorb token variance as a margin problem, the way a restaurant prices a dish, not the grams of flour. Budgets/quotas/failover become an internal ops concern — no meter, no gate, no balance bar in the cockpit. **Why better:** Surfacing token economics trains users to optimize their bill instead of their intent and makes the cockpit a spreadsheet. Every successful creative tool hides unit cost behind a tier; pricing-as-abstraction means the budget aspect shouldn't exist in the UX at all — it's a margin lever, not a feature.
- **Quota failover backwards — drain the CHEAP model first, by law** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert failover from a panic move into the primary routing rule: deliberately drain the Codex-$100 quota first on every explicit leaf because it's the cheaper, more fungible builder, and reserve the Claude-$200 budget exclusively for the fuzzy architect/judge top where it's irreplaceable. Failover stops being an emergency and becomes the default policy. **Why better:** Treating failover as exceptional means the system spends scarce Claude tokens on grunt leaf-work until something breaks, then scrambles. 'Spend the cheap, fungible quota first, hoard the irreplaceable one' as the explicit law turns the whole balance-and-failover apparatus into one deterministic policy — no balance-watching, no mid-run surprise, and the expensive model is always there when only it will do.

### Failure & recovery

_Current assumption: OPEN — retries / escalation when a leaf cannot go green._

**Boldest reframe:** The Escalation Auction — failures bid for help in an open market

- **Classify the failure before you retry it** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf fails to go green, run a cheap classifier on the failure signal (tsc error vs failing assertion vs lint vs timeout vs provider 5xx vs contract-violation) and route each class to a different recovery path instead of one blind retry. The leaf must emit a TYPED diagnosis of which part of its contract it violated (signature mismatch / failing assertion / missing dep / hallucinated API / impossible constraint), and recovery is a router keyed on that cause, not a fixed N-attempt loop. ArtLab already distinguishes 4xx-pass-through from transient-5xx-retry in provider-batch.ts; this generalizes that triage: a flaky timeout gets a plain re-run, a tsc type error gets a fix-and-rebuild on the same model, a contract-violation escalates immediately because retrying won't help. **Why better:** The current 'retry N times then escalate' assumption treats every failure as transient — the brain-retry model only catches 429/timeout/network and cannot distinguish 'flaky' from 'fundamentally wrong,' so it burns identical expensive rebuilds on unfixable leaves and slow-escalates failures that needed a human two attempts ago. A typed cause matches recovery cost to failure type.
- **Cross-model failover as a recovery move, not just quota failover** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If a model's leaf build fails its tests on a logic/spec failure (not a quota error), hand the EXACT failing tests + frozen contract + the failed diff + a one-line postmortem to the OTHER model (Claude<->Codex) as a fresh adversarial attempt, rather than re-prompting the model that already proved it can't satisfy this spec. The frozen typed signature + failing tests + golden template make a clean handoff packet. The other model isn't a backup for outages — it's a different mind on a stuck problem, since a blind spot for one model is often trivial for the other. **Why better:** Re-running the same model on the same prompt mostly reproduces the same failure. The two-model router already exists for quota failover; reusing it for COMPETENCE failover via model diversity turns a stuck leaf into a second independent attempt for free, and the model split is exactly where a frozen contract pays off.
- **Escalate UP the plan-tree, not just to a human (the Andon Cord)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf exhausts cause-appropriate attempts — or detects its own frozen contract is unbuildable (signature impossible, failing test unsatisfiable) — it pulls an 'andon cord' that halts the green-wave on that subtree and pages the Claude architect for that BRANCH, not the operator. The parent re-examines whether the leaf was mis-decomposed: maybe it wasn't 'Codex-ready,' maybe the contract was wrong, maybe it should split into two smaller leaves, and re-issues corrected child contracts. Only after the parent also fails does it bubble to the human. **Why better:** The current assumption escalates straight to a human, treating every leaf failure as an execution problem. But Toyota's insight is that a persistently failing station means a defect was injected UPSTREAM — many leaf failures are DECOMPOSITION problems the tree created and the tree can fix, keeping the zero-human-interaction promise intact for a whole new class of failures instead of re-rolling an impossible leaf forever.
- **Quarantine the red leaf so the green wave keeps climbing** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A leaf that can't go green is marked QUARANTINED and stubbed with its contract's typed signature throwing 'not-yet-implemented' (satisfying types/signatures), so the stitch/green-wave climbs AROUND it and the rest of the tree completes and ships. Like Bazel/CI quarantine lanes, one stubborn leaf is never allowed to block its siblings. The quarantined leaf becomes a tracked punch-list item with its contract preserved, collected into the cockpit. **Why better:** Today one un-greenable leaf can stall the green wave and strand all completed sibling work in an all-or-nothing run; serial blocking escalation can stall a 200-node run on one leaf. Quarantine converts a binary run into a 95-98%-shipped artifact plus a small known gap — far more useful output that preserves the parallel work already paid for, and the user gets a walkable building tonight plus a crisp todo, not a dead run at 3am.
- **Failure ledger / immune memory — antibodies that vaccinate every future build** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every red-to-green transition is distilled into a reusable 'antibody': the failure signature (error class, contract shape, stack), the root cause, and the fix-shaped patch/guard, written to a ledger in graphify. Before freezing contracts on the next run, the planner queries it; before escalating, recovery queries 'have we beaten this exact signature on this stack before, and how?' and applies the known fix automatically. A matching fingerprint gets the proven fix injected as a precondition immediately. Antibodies decay if unused; the human is the LAST resort, not the second. **Why better:** Plain retry has zero memory — the 50th identical 'Drizzle in serverless' failure costs exactly as much as the 1st, and the friction detector only reacts after five identical recurrences within a single run. An immune bank makes the factory get monotonically better at NOT failing across runs and projects — recurring failure classes become zero-touch, hard-won gotchas (like this repo's IPv6/REST-client rule) become reusable antibodies, and recovery's cheapest form is the failure that never happens.
- **A failure BUDGET per subtree, not fixed per-leaf attempt caps** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Recovery is governed by a token/dollar/time budget allocated to a whole SUBTREE plus an explicit attempt budget per leaf; whichever trips first halts retries and routes to escalation. A cheap 5-cent leaf can retry many times; an expensive 50-dollar one gets one shot then degrades to quarantine+report. Tie into the existing budget ledger so a single thrashing leaf can't silently burn the run's whole spend, and surface 'leaf X consumed $Y over Z attempts' in the cockpit, reusing the engine's Telegram budget-cap-approval gate before asking for more. **Why better:** Fixed maxAttempts=3 with fixed backoff treats a 5-cent and a 50-dollar leaf identically and has no global ceiling — a pathological leaf can burn the run's budget invisibly or loop infinitely on an impossible leaf. An economic budget makes failure bounded, visible, and cost-aware: spend where retries are cheap and likely, cut losses where they're expensive and stuck.
- **Capture the green diff as a regression test promoted into the golden template** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf finally goes green after N failed attempts, automatically snapshot the failing-then-passing transition as a pinned test case attached to that NODE TYPE and promote it into the golden per-stack template, so any later rebuild of a similar leaf inherits the assertion that previously caught the bug. Recovery doesn't just fix this leaf — it hardens the contract template for every future leaf of its kind. **Why better:** The current assumption recovers a leaf and moves on, learning nothing structural. Promoting the recovery diff into the golden template means the same failure mode is contract-blocked next time, shrinking the future failure surface instead of just patching the present one.
- **Heartbeat-driven dead-worker reclaim with mid-flight warm resume** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat a silent worker (crashed claude -p / codex exec, no heartbeat past threshold) as a DISTINCT failure class from a red leaf: release its worktree lease, refund its budget reservation, and re-dispatch the SAME contract to a fresh worker with the partial work-in-progress diff as a warm start. ArtLab's reconcileCrashedRuns already does stale-heartbeat lease-release + reservation-refund at the run grain; this brings it to the per-leaf worker grain. **Why better:** The open assumption conflates 'leaf produced wrong output' with 'worker died' — but a dead worker should be replaced, not its often-correct approach abandoned. A warm-resume reclaim recovers the crashed leaf without re-paying for work already done, and the engine already has the lease/refund primitives proven at the run grain.
- **Hermetic re-roll with a fresh worker (no context contamination)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf fails, don't retry in the SAME process whose context window is now poisoned with failed attempts and anchored on its own wrong approach. Bazel-style hermeticity says spin a brand-new headless worker with a CLEAN context: just the contract, the failing test, and a one-line 'previous attempt failed because X' postmortem. Optionally re-roll on the other model. **Why better:** A dumb retry reuses the same agent that's now anchored on its own wrong approach and keeps digging the same hole. Hermetic fresh-context re-rolls escape local minima the way a clean rebuild escapes stale-cache bugs — same retries, radically different outcome.
- **Circuit breaker on the model/quota, not just the leaf** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — From Hystrix/microservice resilience: track failure RATE per model and per subtree, not per individual leaf. If one model's leaf-failure rate on a subtree crosses a threshold (it's the wrong tool for this stack), TRIP a breaker that reroutes all of that subtree's remaining leaves to the other model and stops even attempting the failing one there. The breaker half-opens later to retry routing. **Why better:** Per-leaf retry is blind to systemic failure — it'll keep feeding leaves to a model failing 80% of them on this stack, wasting the whole quota. A circuit breaker recognizes the PATTERN and reroutes wholesale, protecting the dual-model quota budget the entire failover system depends on.
- **Predictive triage — cheap green-probe BEFORE the swarm spends** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before the swarm spends, a fast judge dry-runs each Codex-ready leaf: 'can the failing tests plausibly be made to pass under this contract, given the antibody history?' It predicts each leaf's probability of going green and pre-emptively reshapes the doomed ones — splitting too-fat leaves, pre-attaching the likely-needed skill, or routing the model most likely to one-shot it. Most red leaves are never spawned; they're prevented, and this folds into the scope-preview gate the user already sees. **Why better:** Current recovery is purely REACTIVE — you only learn a leaf is unbuildable by burning a full build + adversarial rounds, then a human. Pre-flighting risk spends a few cheap tokens to avoid the most expensive failure mode entirely; the cheapest recovery is the build that never happens, and the altitude dial already shows a scope-preview gate to extend.
- **Quarantine becomes the artifact's self-critique surface in the cockpit** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every quarantined / degraded leaf is surfaced in the Cockpit as a first-class 'known gap' card — typed cause, what was tried, the cheapest path to green, and a one-click 'spend more to fix this.' The red leaf isn't a failure to hide; it's the artifact telling you exactly where it's weakest, a precise prioritized punch-list of where human attention actually pays off. **Why better:** The current assumption ends recovery at a human escalation with no structured handoff — the human inherits a stuck run and must re-derive context. Making each gap a self-explaining, actionable card turns failure from a dead-end into the most valuable view in the run.
- **The Self-Healing Contract — failure rewrites the spec, not the code** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When a leaf can't go green after a cause-appropriate attempt, the factory stops assuming the LEAF is wrong and considers the CONTRACT is wrong. It runs an inversion: hold the worker's best attempt fixed and ask the parent 'what frozen signature/test/schema would make THIS green and still satisfy the siblings?' If a coherent relaxation exists, it renegotiates the contract UPWARD — a controlled un-freeze, loosen a type, split the leaf, drop a constraint, re-freeze a smaller sub-tree — with sibling re-verification, instead of beating the leaf or doing a global reset. **Why better:** Contracts are frozen top-down and immutable on the assumption the planner's contract is always satisfiable, so an over-constrained or subtly-wrong contract turns into infinite retry then a human halt. Most real failures are bad specs, not bad code — current recovery can only push DOWN (retry the worker) with no path to fix the actual root cause UP the tree. This turns a dead leaf into a localized re-plan that recovers a whole class of failures retries can never fix.
- **Time-Travel Recovery — every node is a checkpointed VM you can rewind and re-roll** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Each leaf build runs in a snapshotted micro-environment (worktree + filesystem + token/context state) checkpointed at each step. On failure, recovery isn't 'retry from scratch' — it's 'rewind to the last green checkpoint and re-roll only the divergent step,' optionally with a different model, seed, or temperature. The whole plan-tree becomes a save-scummable game. **Why better:** Current retries throw away all partial progress and restart the leaf cold, wasting tokens and re-introducing the same mistakes. Branching from the last-good checkpoint makes recovery cheap, surgical, and monotonic — you never re-pay for the 80% that already worked.
- **The Escalation Auction — failures bid for help in an open market** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A stuck leaf doesn't halt to a human; it posts a bounty describing its blocker to a MARKETPLACE of recovery strategies — a fresh model, a different skill, a council debate, a from-scratch re-plan, a contract renegotiation, even a real human — each of which 'bids' a confidence and cost. The factory spends the recovery budget on whichever bid maximizes expected-green-per-token, pricing recovery against the value of the leaf. The human is just one bidder of last resort. **Why better:** Today escalation is a single hardcoded ladder ending in a human JSON file — one strategy, fixed order, no economics. An auction lets the SHAPE of the failure pick its own remedy and prices recovery against the leaf's value, so trivial leaves never wake the human and critical ones get the whole swarm thrown at them.
- **Negative-Space Synthesis — when a leaf is impossible, fail the build into a better design** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A leaf that provably cannot go green is treated as a signal that the DESIGN is wrong, not a thing to retry. The factory treats the impossible leaf as a constraint, re-runs the parent's planner with 'this is unbuildable as specified — find the architecture where it isn't,' and lets a genuinely impossible requirement bubble all the way up as a renegotiation with the ORIGINAL INTENT, propagating impossibility as design pressure. **Why better:** The current system has no concept of 'this leaf SHOULDN'T exist' — it retries then dumps it on a human with no alternative. Letting impossibility propagate as design pressure means the factory can discover that the real answer was a different decomposition, turning a hard stop into an architectural insight.
- **Chaos Drills — the factory attacks itself to build recovery muscle** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A background daemon (and a dry-run pass during the scope-preview gate) deliberately injects failures into healthy runs — kills a worker mid-build, corrupts a contract, starves a model's quota, flips a passing test red — purely to exercise and score the recovery machinery. The cockpit's scope gate reports a 'recovery resilience score': which subtrees have no fallback, which contracts are single-points-of-failure, so the user funds the run knowing where it's brittle. Untested recovery paths are assumed broken. **Why better:** Current recovery (cap then escalate) is an untested happy-path that only runs when something genuinely breaks, so its edge cases rot and weaknesses are discovered only mid-spend. Adversarially exercising recovery — Netflix Chaos Monkey for plan-trees — means by the time a REAL failure hits, every escalation branch has been proven on thousands of synthetic ones, turning recovery from a reactive scramble into a budgeted, visible property hardened up front.
- **The Green Wave That Heals Backwards — stitch failures spawn a reconciliation leaf** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When the green wave hits a node that won't INTEGRATE (siblings built fine, but the mechanical stitch fails on shared-type drift or individually-green-but-jointly-inconsistent contracts), the conflict itself becomes a new mini-intent: the factory spawns a 'reconciliation leaf' whose only job is to make the conflicting children cohere, plans IT recursively, and inserts it into the tree. Integration failure becomes just another buildable node, not a dead end. **Why better:** The stitch is described as 'mechanical,' but mechanical stitches DO fail on real cross-cutting concerns, and there's no recovery path except a human. Treating integration failure as a recursively-solvable intent keeps the autonomous loop closed all the way to the root.
- **Saga compensation — every leaf ships its own undo** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrowing the Saga pattern from Temporal/Inngest: when a node's contract is frozen, it also freezes a COMPENSATING action (how to cleanly rip out this node's partial artifact, migration, or DB row). If a subtree can't go green, the orchestrator runs compensations bottom-up to roll the tree back to the last green frontier — never leaving a half-stitched building with orphaned files or applied-but-unused migrations. **Why better:** Naive retry leaves zombie half-built artifacts when escalation finally gives up. Sagas guarantee the factory always rests in a consistent state, so a partial failure degrades gracefully instead of corrupting the worktree.
- **VFX pre-viz proxy — ship a low-res leaf, refine later** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — From film pipelines where a shot that won't finish renders as a gray proxy so the edit proceeds: a leaf that can't reach full-green degrades to a typed PLACEHOLDER implementation that satisfies the contract and tests-as-stub, flagged 'proxy.' The green wave stitches the whole building with proxies in place; a second, lower-priority pass re-renders proxies to final quality. **Why better:** All-or-nothing green means a single un-renderable leaf can block delivery of an otherwise-shippable artifact. Proxy-and-refine separates 'does the building stand up' from 'is every fixture final,' so the user always gets a walkable structure with refinement decoupled from blocking. (Distinct from quarantine: a proxy is a working low-fidelity impl with a refine pass, not a thrown stub.)
- **Delete recovery entirely — a red leaf is a planning bug, re-plan at the parent** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There is no retry layer, no escalation ladder, no recovery subsystem. A leaf that can't go green is definitionally proof the CONTRACT was wrong (too big, ambiguous, under-specified). The only response is to throw the leaf away and bounce the failure UP to the planner that minted the contract, which re-decomposes. Recovery is replaced by re-planning at the parent node. **Why better:** The current assumption treats failure as a worker problem to be retried away, masking the real signal: the plan-tree mis-sized a leaf. Folding 'failure' back into 'planning' removes a whole subsystem and makes every red leaf improve the decomposition heuristics instead of burning quota retrying an impossible spec.
- **Invert the trigger: green is the alarm, red is the resting state** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop building machinery to handle the rare red leaf. Assume EVERY leaf is red until a passing test + cross-model review flips it green, and the only event the system reacts to is a leaf turning green (which advances the stitch wave). 'Failure' isn't an exception path — it's the default that never fires a special handler because it's already the steady state. There's only un-completed work, handled by the same dispatch loop that handles all work. **Why better:** A recovery aspect only exists because we framed green as default and red as the deviation needing handling. Inverting the default means there's no 'unexpected failure' to recover from — one mechanism (the dispatch loop) instead of two (build + recovery).
- **No per-leaf recovery — nuke the whole subtree and re-fan-out fresh** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When a leaf can't go green, don't retry or escalate it. Abandon its ENTIRE parent subtree, regenerate that subtree's contracts from scratch with the failure as new context, and re-dispatch the whole branch in parallel. Coarse-grained blast radius, zero fine-grained recovery state. **Why better:** Per-leaf retry/escalation needs fragile bookkeeping — which leaf, how many attempts, what failover state — that couples siblings. Nuking and regenerating the subtree is stateless and exploits the cheap parallel swarm already built. The factory's superpower is throughput, not careful repair; spend compute, not orchestration complexity.
- **Merge recovery into the contract-freeze step — make leaves un-failable by construction** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Move all the energy budgeted for recovery into the top-down freeze: a leaf is not dispatched until its failing test + golden template + typed signature are SO tight that a one-shot is near-guaranteed. The recovery aspect dissolves into a stricter 'Codex-ready' gate. If a leaf would need recovery, it wasn't ready to be a leaf. **Why better:** Recovery is downstream firefighting; tightening the readiness bar is upstream prevention. The same review compute spent re-trying a vague leaf five times is better spent once, before the swarm — cheaper in total, and it strengthens the contract graph the whole system already depends on.
- **Kill escalation-to-human; cross-model failover IS the entire recovery story** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Delete the escalation ladder and any human-in-the-loop recovery rung. The only recovery primitive is: if model A can't green a leaf, model B gets it (Claude<->Codex), and if both fail, ship-as-stub. No retry counts, no backoff curves, no escalation tiers — just one cross-model handoff, then done. **Why better:** Elaborate escalation ladders are where autonomous orchestrators go to die — they reintroduce the human interaction the whole design swore off. Collapsing recovery to a single 'try the other brain once, else stub' uses an asset we already have and keeps the run truly hands-off.
- **Recovery belongs to graphify's reconciliation pass, not to a leaf** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Remove failure handling from the build phase and relocate it entirely to the as-built verification. Workers never 'recover'; they run to completion or stop. Afterward, graphify diffs reality against the contract graph, and EVERY mismatch (failed leaf, drift, missing seam) is treated identically as a reconciliation delta fed into a fresh re-build wave. There is no 'failure' category — only 'graph doesn't match contract yet.' **Why better:** Splitting 'failed leaf' from 'as-built drift' creates two recovery codepaths for one fact: reality != contract. Merging recovery into graphify gives a single reconciliation loop, makes failure detection identical to verification (no extra detector), and lets the green-wave abstraction handle repair the same way it handles the first build.
- **On red, lower the altitude dial and re-quote the user** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A leaf that can't go green doesn't trigger retries — it triggers a SCOPE event: the factory automatically drops the altitude tier for that branch (T3->T2, etc.), re-runs the cheap scope-preview gate, and bounces a 'this branch is smaller/cheaper than promised, proceed?' card back to the cockpit. Failure feeds the scope dial, not a recovery engine. **Why better:** The current assumption hides cost blowups inside silent retries. Routing failure into the altitude/scope-gate you already built keeps the human's mental model honest (they watch scope shrink in real time), turns every failure into a money-saving decision instead of a quota-burning loop, and reuses the gate rather than inventing recovery logic.

### Invocation & human gates

_Current assumption: a 3-sentence idea -> scope preview -> autonomous run._

**Boldest reframe:** Make the human a worker node, not a gatekeeper — the plan-tree marks certain leaves human-only (taste calls, brand decisions, legal sign-offs) and the orchestrator dispatches THOSE to you exactly like it dispatches code leaves to Codex. This is the boldest reframe because it doesn't tweak the gate, soften it, move it, or learn it away — it dissolves the human/machine boundary entirely. The human stops being a turnstile in front of the swarm and becomes a typed tool inside it, pulled in only where their judgment is irreplaceable and nowhere else. Every other idea still treats the human as categorically outside the build, approving or steering it; this one says the human is just another worker with a specialty, dispatched on demand by the same manifest that runs everything else.

- **The scope preview is a one-screen contract receipt, not prose** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The gate shows a fixed, scannable card: altitude tier (T0-T3), plan-tree depth + leaf count, the frozen contracts it will write (signatures, DB tables, test names), the skills it attaches per node-type, and a token/$ + wall-clock estimate with a failover note. No paragraphs — a receipt you approve like a checkout cart. **Why better:** The current 'scope preview' is undefined as to WHAT it shows. A non-technical owner can't reason about an autonomous spend from prose; a fixed receipt makes the one go/no-go decision legible in five seconds and forces the system to commit its plan before spending.
- **Two-line entry, not three-sentence — auto-elaborate the rest** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Accept an intent as short as one line ('rebuild the war room empty state'); the architect silently expands it into the canonical goal/constraints/done-criteria frame and shows that expansion AS PART of the scope receipt for the human to correct. Intent capture and intent confirmation become the same step. **Why better:** Demanding a well-formed 3-sentence brief front-loads work onto the human and bounces lazy asks. Letting the machine elaborate then showing its interpretation catches the real failure — 'it built the wrong thing flawlessly' — at the cheapest possible moment, before any leaf spends.
- **The gate is editable, not just approve/reject** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At the scope preview the human can prune or pin individual subtrees ('skip analytics', 'lock the DB schema, I wrote it', 'don't touch auth'), bump or drop the altitude one notch, and swap a node's skill — then re-confirm. Approval mutates the manifest in place rather than kicking back to a fresh re-prompt. **Why better:** A binary go/no-go forces a full restart when 90% of the plan is right, training the human to over-specify the prompt. An editable manifest turns the single gate into a steering wheel and captures the human's cheapest, highest-leverage input — structural corrections — exactly where they cost nothing.
- **A dry-run 'contract-only' tier below the full swarm** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Add an explicit intermediate gate: 'freeze the contracts and write the failing tests, then STOP and show me before any leaf builds.' The human reviews the typed signatures, schema, and red test suite — the skeleton — and only then releases the autonomous parallel build. **Why better:** Contracts are the load-bearing decision; a wrong contract poisons every parallel leaf simultaneously. The single scope gate approves scope but lets the build run on unreviewed contracts. A cheap contract-freeze checkpoint is where one minute of attention prevents the most expensive class of swarm failure.
- **Confidence/regret-gated autonomy: cheap reversible work skips the gate** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Auto-classify the run; if it's T0/T1 (reversible, small surface, low novelty, under a $/token floor) it runs immediately with NO gate and just reports the result. The owner sets a regret/cost ceiling ONCE (e.g. $4, 20 worker-minutes); only runs that would breach it surface for a glance. The gate stops being about scope-correctness and becomes a pure economic tripwire. **Why better:** Gating a trivial one-floor tweak the same as a whole-app build is process exceeding output. Reserving attention for spends that warrant it keeps the gate meaningful instead of click-through theater, makes the factory feel instant for the common small case, and means ~90% of invocations need no human at all.
- **One canonical 'go' phrase, mirrored from ArtLab** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Standardize the release on a single explicit phrase — like ArtLab's 'approved for app' — e.g. 'approved to build'. Approval is never inferred from a vague 'ok' or 'sounds good'; the autonomous swarm refuses to spend until the exact phrase appears. **Why better:** ArtLab already proved an exact-phrase gate prevents accidental irreversible spend, and the codebase treats that as non-negotiable. An ambiguous 'yes' is dangerous when the next step is N headless processes burning quota; one unambiguous verb makes the thinking/committing boundary impossible to cross by accident.
- **Mid-run guardrail gates: budget tripwires, not just a start gate** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Beyond the start gate, set automatic pause-and-ask tripwires DURING the run — if a subtree exceeds its token/$ estimate by N%, if the adversarial loop hits its 3-round cap on a leaf, or if failover flips models mid-build, the orchestrator surfaces a single 'continue / re-scope / abort this branch?' card instead of silently grinding. **Why better:** 'Zero human interaction mid-run' is great until a runaway branch quietly eats the whole budget on a leaf that will never go green. A handful of tripwires preserve autonomy in the common case while giving the human a brake exactly when reality diverges from the approved contract — supervised autonomy instead of blind trust.
- **Replay the gate as a saved estimate-vs-actual receipt** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After a run, show the same scope receipt with actuals overlaid on estimates: tokens, $, wall-clock, leaves that needed extra adversarial rounds, failovers. The next gate's estimates are calibrated from this history, so the preview gets more honest run over run. **Why better:** A scope preview the human can't trust is a rubber stamp. Estimates with no feedback loop drift; a visible estimate-vs-actual ledger both builds trust to approve bigger autonomous runs and tightens the numbers the gate asks them to approve, making every future gate decision cheaper and better-informed.
- **Ambient intent capture — the factory listens, you never 'invoke'** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill the invocation event entirely. The factory passively ingests your whole working stream — Telegram, commits, half-written docs, voice memos, browser tabs, the meeting you just had, the open repo diff, the failing test, the floor with no character art — and continuously maintains a ranked queue of 'things you clearly want built.' You wake up to scope-previews already waiting because it inferred intent from your life. Invocation becomes 'yes, that one' instead of 'here is what I want.' **Why better:** The 3-sentence-idea gate assumes the bottleneck is the build. The real bottleneck is articulation — turning a fuzzy want into a crisp brief. The strongest intent signal already exists in your repo, backlog, and working stream. Surfacing-then-confirming has far higher intent-fidelity than blank-page articulation, and the factory is never idle waiting to be told.
- **The Counter-Brief — factory argues back before it builds** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invocation is a 30-second adversarial volley: you state the idea, the factory instantly returns the version it thinks you ACTUALLY want (bigger, sharper, with the obvious feature you forgot) plus the two cheaper things that would kill this idea's need to exist. You accept its counter-brief or defend yours. The gate is a debate, not a form. **Why better:** A raw 3-sentence brief encodes your blind spots and lands you in a polished build of the wrong thing. Forcing one round of model pushback at the gate catches scope errors when they cost one sentence to fix instead of a whole swarm-run to redo.
- **Speculative pre-building — it's already half-done when you ask** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The factory continuously runs cheap speculative builds on its top-N predicted intents during idle quota, like CPU branch prediction. When you finally state the real intent, it doesn't start cold — it pattern-matches to a warm speculative tree that's already partway built, and your invocation just confirms a branch. 'Generate' feels instant because the work happened before the request. **Why better:** The current flow is strictly request-then-work, so every run pays full latency from zero. Speculative pre-building amortizes the build across idle time, collapsing felt time-to-artifact and turning the human gate from a 'start' button into a 'confirm' tap.
- **One human gate, ever: 'Was this what you meant?'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Collapse all gates — scope preview, direction approval, promotion — into a SINGLE post-hoc judgment. The factory autonomously runs to a finished, reversible draft with zero mid-run checkpoints, then shows you the actual artifact and asks one question: keep, or re-spin? No approving plans you can't visualize; you only ever judge real output. **Why better:** Humans are terrible at approving abstract plans and great at reacting to concrete artifacts. Every pre-build gate asks you to predict; this asks you to react. It removes the cognitive tax of gating things you can't yet see and trusts cheap reversibility instead of expensive permission.
- **Intent Futures Market — bet on what to build, factory clears the trades** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invocation becomes a portfolio, not a request. You (and eventually teammates) stake a budget of attention/quota across many competing intents; the factory runs them all at proportional scope and the 'winners' are whatever produces the highest value per token. You express a whole desire-distribution and let the factory clear it. **Why better:** Single-intent invocation forces premature commitment to one idea before you know which is worth it. A futures market lets the factory explore many intents cheaply in parallel and surfaces the winner empirically, so the human picks from proven artifacts instead of guessing upfront.
- **The Dream Gate — narrate the finished thing, not the task** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the task-shaped brief with a first-person 'walk through the finished world' narration: you describe USING the thing on its best day ('I open the war room and the pipeline already re-sorted itself by which interview is closest...'). The factory reverse-engineers the plan-tree, schema, and contracts from your lived description of the outcome. **Why better:** A 3-sentence brief is implementation-flavored and leaks assumptions about HOW. Narrating the outcome captures the actual want — the felt experience — which is denser, harder to fake, and far more decomposable into contracts than a terse task description. It matches how people actually know what they want.
- **Trust Dial replaces gates — earn your way to zero human touch** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — No fixed gates; a single per-domain trust score the factory has earned with you. Low trust = it asks at every fork. High trust = it ships to production while you sleep and tells you after. The dial moves automatically based on its track record in that domain, so frontend might be fully autonomous while migrations still gate hard — without you ever configuring a thing. **Why better:** A one-size scope-preview gate is either annoying on trivial work or reckless on dangerous work. A self-tuning trust dial spends attention only where the factory hasn't proven itself, so invocation friction shrinks to zero exactly where it's safe and stays high exactly where it isn't.
- **Biometric invocation — the factory triggers on your frustration, not your words** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The strongest 'build me this' signal isn't a typed brief — it's the moment you sigh at a manual task, rage-quit a spreadsheet, or do the same tedious thing a third time. The factory watches for those friction-spikes and pre-emptively offers: 'Want me to make this floor do that for you, permanently?' Invocation is triggered by your pain, captured at the instant it's most legible. **Why better:** Deliberate invocation only fires for wants you've consciously formed and bothered to articulate. Most high-value automations die because you never stop to ask. Triggering on friction-in-the-moment captures the enormous backlog of 'I should automate this someday' at the one moment the need is undeniable and fully specified by the pain itself.
- **Gates dissolve into a learned taste-model** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every approve/revise/reject you ever give is training data for a personal taste-model. Once it can predict your gate decision with high confidence, the factory ACTS as you at that gate and only escalates the low-confidence cases. The gates aren't removed by fiat — they're earned away, one confident prediction at a time, each with a visible 'I answered this as you would have' receipt. **Why better:** Today's gates are a permanent tax that never decreases no matter how predictable you are. A gate is really a placeholder for a missing model of your judgment. Learning that model means the factory gets MORE autonomous the longer you use it, while the receipts keep you in the loop without blocking you.
- **Standing commissions instead of one-shot runs** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Separate durable INTENT from momentary invocation. You register standing commitments — 'every floor without character art should get art', 'keep the analytics charts matching the latest data', 'the landing page should always reflect current pricing' — and the factory continuously satisfies them, spinning up runs on its own whenever reality drifts from the commitment. Invocation becomes subscription, not transaction. **Why better:** One-idea-one-run inherits the chat paradigm and treats every build as a fresh ask. But most real intent is a standing condition you want maintained, not a single artifact. Standing commissions make the factory do the watching and re-triggering, collapsing dozens of future invocations into one durable statement of intent.
- **Invoke by pointing, not by describing** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invoke by gesturing at an artifact instead of writing a brief: drop a screenshot of a competitor's feature, point at an existing floor and say 'another one of these for X', circle a region of the cockpit, or forward a Slack thread. The factory reverse-engineers intent and scope from the referent. Description-from-scratch becomes the rare fallback, not the default front door. **Why better:** Prose briefs are lossy and slow — humans are far better at recognition and comparison than specification. Pointing at a concrete referent carries dense, unambiguous intent (this layout, this density, this tone) three sentences can't, so the plan-tree starts from a much higher-fidelity seed and needs fewer downstream gates.
- **The gate moves to the leaf, not the top** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop gating once at the top before the swarm spends. Let the run go fully autonomous AND interruptible: any worker that hits genuine ambiguity at a leaf raises a tiny, hyper-specific question ('CRO's badge: gold or crimson?') that you answer in two taps, while the rest of the tree keeps building around it. Approval is distributed, lazy, and only ever about the specific fork that's actually undecidable. **Why better:** A single up-front scope gate forces the human to adjudicate the WHOLE plan before any of it exists, when most of the plan is obvious and only a few leaves are genuinely ambiguous. Pushing gates to the leaves means the human only ever decides things that actually need deciding, in context, without blocking the 95% of the tree that's unambiguous.
- **Two-way invocation: the factory bids for your attention** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Flip who initiates. Instead of the human invoking and the factory waiting at gates, the factory maintains a ranked queue of things it WANTS to build and 'bids' for a slice of your attention — 'I can ship the missing Observatory charts for $2 and 15 min, want it? [go] [later] [never]'. Your inbox becomes a feed of pre-scoped, pre-priced proposals you approve or dismiss with one tap. **Why better:** The current model makes the human do the expensive cognitive work (notice the need, phrase it, scope it) and reserves the cheap work (say yes) for the factory. Inverting it puts the heavy lifting on the always-on factory and reduces the human's role to one-tap triage of fully-formed proposals — strictly less human effort per unit of output.
- **No gate — a fully reversible factory with an undo wave** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Remove human gates entirely and make the cost of being wrong near-zero instead. Every autonomous run lands behind a flag on a worktree/preview, never on main; a single 'undo this run' command unwinds the whole green wave atomically. Safety comes from cheap, total reversibility, not pre-approval — you review the FINISHED artifact and discard it for free if it's wrong. **Why better:** Gates exist because a wrong run is assumed expensive and hard to unwind. But the factory already builds on worktrees behind flags — if every run is atomically reversible, pre-approval buys almost nothing while costing a synchronous human stop every time. Reviewing real output and undoing the misses is higher-signal and lower-friction than approving abstractions up front.
- **BUILD --target-pattern: invoke by querying the graph, not writing a sentence** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal Bazel's target patterns. Instead of starting from a 3-sentence English idea, you invoke against the EXISTING graphify graph: `forge //floor/war-room:pipeline-card` builds one node, `//floor/war-room/...` a whole subtree, `//... --changed-since=HEAD~3` rebuilds only what a diff invalidated. The idea sentence becomes a NEW-node constructor; everything that exists is addressable as a path you point the swarm at. **Why better:** A sentence is a lossy way to say 'redo the comp panel and everything downstream of it.' A target pattern is exact, re-runnable, diff-aware, and lets you re-invoke a sub-tree of a prior run with zero re-prompting — the factory becomes incremental instead of always-from-scratch.
- **The signal/await gate: approve via a Temporal-style signal, not an interruption** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow Temporal's signals + durable timers. A gate isn't a blocking modal — it's a named await-point in the durable workflow (`awaitSignal('approve-direction')`) with a timeout-default baked in ('auto-approve after 30m' or 'auto-pick option 2'). The human can send the signal from anywhere (Telegram, cockpit, CLI), hours later, and the run resumes exactly where it parked — or proceeds on the default if they never show. **Why better:** Today's 'scope preview → run' is a synchronous fork; if the human is away the whole factory stalls. Signals decouple the gate from attention: the run survives the human being asleep, gates carry a sane default so absence never deadlocks, and approval works from any channel. ArtLab's exact-phrase gates become durable signal handlers.
- **Dailies: gates are a scheduled screening room, not per-decision pings** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal the VFX/film 'dailies' ritual. Instead of stopping to ask at each gate, the factory BATCHES every pending decision and finished sub-artifact into a once-or-twice-a-day review reel: a single scrollable session where you see each in-flight node's latest state side by side and approve/reject/redirect them all in one sitting. Workers keep building speculatively against the un-approved nodes in the meantime. **Why better:** N gates scattered across a run = N context-switches that each kill flow. Dailies converts an unbounded stream of interruptions into one predictable, high-bandwidth review window — the exact mechanism film pipelines invented because the same swarm-vs-one-reviewer bottleneck exists there. Better throughput AND a calmer human.
- **The manifest is the gate — invoke by editing a checked-in BUILD file** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Make the primary invocation surface a file, not a chat box. The plan-tree compiles to a human-editable manifest (`tower.factory.yaml`) listing every node, its tier, assigned skill/model, and a `state: proposed|approved|frozen`. You 'approve scope' by editing that file (flip nodes to approved, delete unwanted nodes, retier one) and the orchestrator dispatches off the diff. The 3-sentence idea just SEEDS the first draft. Invocation becomes a PR, not a prompt. **Why better:** A scope-preview you can only accept/reject wholesale is low-resolution; a manifest you can surgically edit lets you prune, re-tier, and reassign before a single token is spent — and it's version-controlled, diffable, and reviewable like any other code.
- **Quorum sensing: the swarm self-gates by density, escalating only at thresholds** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Steal bacterial quorum sensing. Each worker emits a confidence/risk 'signal molecule' as it finishes a leaf. The factory aggregates these; while local consensus is high and risk-signal low, it auto-approves and keeps climbing the tree with NO human gate. Only when accumulated risk crosses a threshold (many low-confidence leaves clustered, or a contract-mismatch spike) does it 'go human' and surface a gate. **Why better:** A fixed scope-preview gate fires on EVERY run regardless of stakes — annoying on the trivial, insufficient on the dangerous. Quorum sensing makes gate frequency proportional to actual measured risk: invisible when the swarm is confident, insistent exactly where reality is fighting the contract.
- **Andon cord: any worker (or the human) can halt the line and trigger a 5-whys swarm** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow Toyota's andon cord + jidoka. A single primitive lets ANY node — human or worker — pull the cord: it freezes new dispatch line-wide, surfaces the offending node front-and-center in the cockpit, and auto-spawns a tiny root-cause sub-swarm (grill-me / systematic-debugging) on it. Pulling the cord is the inverse gate: not 'may I proceed' but 'everyone stop, something's wrong here.' **Why better:** Current gates are all go/no-go BEFORE the swarm runs; there's no first-class STOP once it's running. An andon cord gives the human (and the workers) a single, instant kill-and-diagnose lever mid-flight, so a contract drift caught at leaf 40 doesn't silently corrupt the 200 leaves downstream.
- **Poka-yoke invocation: the idea box won't let you start a mis-shaped run** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal assembly-line poka-yoke (mistake-proofing fixtures that physically reject the wrong part). The invocation surface actively refuses under-specified or contradictory intent: if your sentences lack an acceptance criterion, name no target stack, or conflict with a frozen contract, the box blocks 'Run' and offers the 2-3 grill-me questions that would un-block it — like a connector that only mates one way. **Why better:** Today a vague idea still launches a scope preview, burning a planning pass to discover it was under-specified. Poka-yoke moves that check to time-zero and makes it impossible to start a doomed run — the cheapest possible place to catch a defect, exactly where manufacturing learned to put it.
- **Trigger graph: runs fire from events, like Inngest/Airflow sensors** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow Inngest event-triggers + Airflow sensors. Register standing invocations: `on(merge to main where floor/* changed) -> re-run tower-ship-gate subtree`, `on(new ArtLab character promoted) -> regenerate slot-audit + integration snippets`, `on(schema diff) -> re-freeze affected contracts + failing tests`. The human 3-sentence idea is just ONE event type among many that can start a factory run. **Why better:** Pure human-typed invocation means the factory only runs when you remember to push the button. An event/sensor layer makes it reactive — the building maintains itself as upstream facts change, and the human gate shifts from 'start everything' to 'approve the standing rules,' a far higher-leverage place to spend attention.
- **Two-key launch + change-advisory altitude: the gate's ceremony scales with blast radius** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal large-eng-org change management (CAB / two-person rule / progressive rollout rings). The altitude dial doesn't just size the build — it sizes the GATE. A T0 component auto-runs with no gate; a T2 feature needs one scope-ack; a T3 whole-app or anything touching frozen contracts/migrations/payments requires a two-key launch (a second confirming signal) and a canary ring (build 10% of leaves, prove them green, then release the rest). **Why better:** A single uniform scope-preview gate is simultaneously too heavy for a one-component tweak and too light for a 200-leaf app rewrite. Tying gate ceremony to measured blast radius means trivial work is friction-free while genuinely dangerous runs get the canary + second-signer real eng orgs require — proportionality the flat gate can't express.
- **Delete the scope-preview gate entirely — the run IS the preview** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the pre-spend scope gate. The first thing the swarm produces is a real, navigable contract-tree skeleton (typed signatures + failing tests + one golden leaf, no full build) costing ~2% of the run, and the human approves or kills THAT instead of a textual estimate. A preview that's an actual artifact can't lie about scope the way a token/tier estimate can. **Why better:** The scope preview's whole job is to stop the swarm spending wrong, but a textual/cost estimate is exactly what models hallucinate confidence about. A real frozen-contract skeleton is cheap, un-fakeable, and the human gates reality instead of a guess — same protective function, zero new abstraction, and it doubles as the first real build phase.
- **Invert the gate: the human's one checkpoint is at the contract freeze, not the start** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Reverse where human attention lives. Don't gate at intent (front) — let a 1-sentence intent fire instantly into an autonomous run, and inject the SINGLE human checkpoint at the moment of maximum irreversibility (schema freeze / contract lock), because that's the only decision expensive to undo. Early scope is cheap to throw away; a wrong frozen contract poisons the whole parallel build. **Why better:** The current gate spends judgment on the cheapest-to-redo moment (scope) and zero on the most expensive-to-redo moment (the contract freeze the entire bottom-up swarm builds against). Putting the one gate where mistakes are catastrophic and removing it where they're trivial is strictly better allocation of the one scarce resource: human attention.
- **Merge invocation into graphify — reconcile to desired state like Terraform** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Fold the entire invocation/gate aspect into the code-knowledge graph. You don't 'invoke a run'; you mutate the desired-state graph (declare a node that should exist), and the orchestrator continuously reconciles reality toward the graph like a controller loop. 'Human gates' become graph diffs you approve, identical to a Terraform plan. **Why better:** It collapses invocation+gates and graphify into one mental model the user already understands — declare desired state, review the diff, apply. No bespoke scope-preview UI, no separate 'autonomous run' concept; the same reconcile-and-approve loop covers a one-line tweak and a whole-app build, and the gate is automatically the diff, which is always honest.
- **Two gates, not one — and the second (stitch-to-trunk) gate is non-negotiable** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The assumption that ONE front gate suffices is the flaw. Mandate a second gate at the stitch boundary: the green-wave climbs the tree autonomously, but the final merge-to-trunk always stops for a human. Front gate guards spend; back gate guards what becomes real. Skip either and you've either burned money or shipped unreviewed code. **Why better:** ArtLab already proved this exact two-gate pattern works ('approve direction' + 'approved for app') — the factory inheriting only ONE gate is a regression from a battle-tested system in this very repo. A purely front-loaded gate means a perfectly-scoped run can still autonomously merge subtly-wrong code; the back gate is where 'oversight' is actually earned.
- **Gate by spend velocity — a thermostat, not a turnstile** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Remove the discrete pre-run gate and replace it with a continuous budget governor: the run goes fully autonomous until it crosses a spend/time threshold YOU set, then pauses and asks to continue. No upfront scope estimate at all — you cap the blast radius, not the plan. **Why better:** Scope previews are notoriously inaccurate, so gating on predicted scope means gating on a number that's wrong by the time the swarm runs. A live spend ceiling is grounded in actual tokens burned, can't be gamed by an optimistic estimate, and right-sizes naturally: tiny tasks never hit the ceiling and run gate-free, runaway runs self-halt.
- **Make the human a worker node, not a gatekeeper** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the human's role from 'approve then get out of the way' to 'be the model assigned to the nodes only you can do.' The plan-tree marks certain leaves human-only (taste calls, a real password, a brand decision, a legal sign-off) and the orchestrator dispatches THOSE to you the same way it dispatches code leaves to Codex. You're a tool in the swarm, not a turnstile in front of it. **Why better:** A single front gate forces the human to front-load every judgment they'll ever need, before the plan-tree even exists to reveal which judgments matter. Treating the human as a typed worker pulls their scarce attention in exactly where and when it's irreplaceable — and nowhere else — a far higher-resolution use of oversight than one all-or-nothing yes/no at the start.
- **Delete the autonomy promise — default to a paired, narrated run** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the headline value prop. 'Zero human interaction mid-run' is the wrong default for most real work; make the default a co-pilot run that thinks out loud and pauses at every fork, and make full-autonomous an explicit opt-in flag ('--unattended') you reach for only on runs you already trust. Autonomy is the advanced mode, not the resting state. **Why better:** Full autonomy is most valuable precisely when you ALREADY trust the factory — which is never on day one or on a novel task. Autonomy-by-default means the user's first, trust-defining experiences have the least oversight and the highest chance of an expensive silent failure. Earning trust through narrated paired mode first, then graduating to unattended, builds the confidence that makes autonomy actually usable.

### Self-improvement / factory builds factories

_Current assumption: OPEN — the factory improves itself over time._

**Boldest reframe:** The Factory Forks Itself and Runs an Evolutionary Tournament Against Itself

- **Close the ledger loop: actually write the friction the detector reads** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every runner/worker already emits a failureCode on a blocked or failed leaf, but VERIFIED IN THE REPO nothing appends those events to ledgers/improvements.jsonl — the exact file friction-detector.ts reads (it returns empty whenever the file is absent, which is always). Add one thin recordFriction(failureCode, severity, runId, context) helper and call it at each existing failure-emit site (concept-runner, production-runner, strict-qa-runner, canary-runner, coherence) so the half-built loop finally has a data source. **Why better:** Confirmed dead today: zero non-test writers to improvements.jsonl, so detectFriction can never return an actionable group and the loop can never fire. This single wire turns inert dead-code into a live feature — strictly dominates the current aspirational-but-empty state.
- **Wire the scheduler into a real cron so the factory improves on a clock** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — runSelfEvolutionScheduler is fully tested but VERIFIED to have zero non-test callers. Add a /api/cron/self-evolution route mirroring the existing unprompted-ceo / warmth-decay cron pattern (both confirmed present in src/app/api/cron/) that invokes it nightly with the existing 1-hour cooldown intact. **Why better:** A self-improving factory that only improves when a human manually calls a function isn't self-improving. Reusing the proven in-repo cron pattern makes the loop autonomous with near-zero new surface area.
- **Did-the-fix-work verification: mark friction resolved before re-summoning** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — After a fix branch merges, the same failureCode should stop recurring. Add a resolvedAt marker: when a failureCode hasn't appeared for N runs after its fix branch, log it resolved; if it reappears, escalate severity and tag the new summon as a 'regression' so Codex knows the last fix was insufficient. **Why better:** The loop currently fires open-loop — it summons a fix and never checks if reality changed, so it can re-summon identical branches forever. Adding the as-built check (graphify's 'does reality match the contract' half) turns blind generation into measured improvement.
- **Unify the scattered failureCode strings into one typed taxonomy** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — failureCode literals are sprinkled across concept-runner, production-runner, strict-qa-runner, canary-runner, coherence, etc. as freeform strings. Promote them into a single exported FailureCode union with severity defaults, so the detector groups cleanly and a typo can't spawn an orphan bucket that never crosses threshold. **Why better:** Group-by-string silently fragments friction (one typo = a group that never reaches 5 = a fix that never fires). A typed registry makes the friction signal trustworthy and the whole loop auditable, beating the implicit drift-prone convention.
- **A standing 'self-improvement' floor in the Cockpit, not a hidden cron** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Surface the friction ledger, actionable groups, open fix-branches, and resolved/regressed counts as a panel in the rebuilt Forge Cockpit — the factory's own improvement backlog rendered next to the live plan-tree. **Why better:** Self-improvement is invisible today: it runs in the dark and a human only finds fix branches by stumbling on them in git. Making it observable lets the founder steer ('this one's wrong, drop it') instead of trusting blindly — turning autonomous-with-oversight from a slogan into a control surface.
- **Promote-with-evidence: every self-fix branch ships its own green proof** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The summon prompt already demands a regression test + tsc + lint + vitest. Capture that gate output into the branch description as a machine-readable evidence.json (which test now catches the bug, before/after run counts) so a human reviewing the branch sees proof-of-improvement, not just a diff. **Why better:** Branches-only safety means a human reviews everything — but today they review a raw diff with no context on whether it closes the friction. Attaching evidence makes the gate fast and confident, which is what keeps an autonomous loop from being throttled by review fatigue.
- **Self-improvement as a managed investment portfolio with a budget line** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give the factory an explicit 'improve myself vs. ship the user's work' allocator. It estimates each candidate self-edit's expected token/time savings from its own history and only spends idle quota on improvements whose projected ROI beats a threshold, logging realized-vs-predicted savings to recalibrate. Pair it with a 'taste budget': risky self-surgery (router logic, manifest schema, stitch algorithm) is unlocked only after banking enough credit on safe wins, so autonomy scales with proven competence instead of a flat never-auto-merge ceiling. **Why better:** The current trigger is a blunt count-of-5 with a one-hour cooldown — no notion of whether a fix is worth making or whether the factory has earned the right to attempt it. An ROI allocator concentrates effort where compounding payoff is largest and rate-limits reckless self-modification by track record.
- **Critical-path bottleneck profiler that re-engineers only the slowest node-type** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow Theory of Constraints / line-balancing: across all runs compute the plan-tree's critical path and identify the systemic bottleneck node-type ('frontend leaves take 3x longer because impeccable keeps re-iterating', 'schema contracts are the serialization point everything waits on'). Each self-improvement cycle targets ONLY that constraint — fatten its template, add a sub-skill, parallelize it, pre-warm it — then re-profile to find the next constraint. **Why better:** The 5x-friction trigger improves wherever pain happens to repeat, which can be a trivial node irrelevant to throughput. A constraint profiler guarantees every cycle spends on the ONE change that shortens wall-clock and cost for the whole factory — maximal leverage per evolution.
- **Every failed run leaves a permanent capability — improvement is synchronous, not a 5x background loop** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf fails to one-shot, the run doesn't just retry — it distills WHY (missing skill, ambiguous contract, wrong altitude) into a durable artifact: a new worker-skill, a sharper contract template, or a reclassification rule. The factory is provably stronger the instant the run ends, not after friction recurs five times. **Why better:** The 5x trigger throws away the first four lessons and acts only on the fifth. Capturing every failure synchronously means zero wasted signal, a factory that compounds per-job instead of per-week, and it deletes the arbitrary magic number 5.
- **Improve the DECOMPOSITION, not the leaves — learn where the plan-tree keeps cutting wrong** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Mine the history of stitch failures and human re-splits to learn the factory's recurring decomposition mistakes (this feature should never have been one leaf; that contract should have been frozen one level higher). The factory improves by getting better at WHERE to cut the tree, since a bad split poisons every leaf below it. **Why better:** Friction-driven refactors fix symptoms at the leaf, but the highest-leverage error in a recursive plan-tree is a bad cut near the top — it cascades. Learning the splitting policy fixes a whole class of future failures upstream, where one template tweak can't.
- **The Factory predicts the next failure and pre-builds the fix** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — From its history the factory forecasts which failure code is statistically about to cross threshold next, drafts and benchmarks the candidate fix branch in advance, and surfaces 'you will likely hit X soon' to the cockpit as a leading indicator — so the moment friction recurs the patch is already proven and one keystroke from merge. **Why better:** The 5x threshold is purely lagging — by the time it fires the failure has already cost five real runs. Forecasting turns self-improvement from reactive to anticipatory, eliminating friction before it accrues instead of after.
- **Dailies + a Supervisor reviewer node that mines rejections into new contract clauses** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal VFX dailies: add a permanent SUPERVISOR node-type the green wave must pass at each tier boundary (leaf→section→app) — a Codex-or-Claude reviewer judging built work against the contract AND canon, whose verdicts are logged. The factory improves by mining the supervisor's recurring rejection reasons into NEW contract clauses for future runs. **Why better:** The current loop catches friction only after 5 repeats of a coded FAILURE — it's blind to silent quality drift that still passes tests. Dailies convert subjective 'this is wrong even though tests pass' judgments into hardened contract clauses, so the factory's DEFINITION of good tightens, not just its speed.
- **Hypermutation under stress: couple the self-evolution rate to the factory's own distress** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Steal bacterial stress-induced mutagenesis (SOS response): when a run shows high failover churn, repeated stitch failures, or quota thrash, the factory temporarily WIDENS self-evolution — proposing more and bolder changes to its prompts/templates/routing across several parallel worktree variants — then contracts back to conservative single-change mode once green-wave throughput recovers. **Why better:** The current loop evolves at one fixed cadence (5x, one branch) regardless of how badly it's struggling. Coupling mutation rate to distress makes the factory explore hard exactly when stuck and stop fiddling when healthy — beating a constant-rate tinkerer that's too slow in a crisis and too noisy when fine.
- **Friction harvesting from the human gates, not just from crashes** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the human approval gates ('approve direction <n>', 'approved for app', PLAN-review rejections) as friction signals: when a human repeatedly rejects the same kind of output or hand-edits the same generated section, log a low-severity friction event so the factory learns from taste-corrections, not only hard failures. **Why better:** The current loop only sees machine failures and is blind to the most valuable signal in the system — where the human keeps overriding it. Mining the gates turns every correction the founder already makes into training signal, so the factory converges on the founder's taste, not just on 'tests pass'.
- **Let the factory evolve its own MOLDS — propose contract/template fixes, not just bug fixes** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Extend the summon beyond 'harden against failure X' to a recurring-class fix: when many leaves fail at the SAME contract boundary or a golden per-stack template keeps needing manual patching, the factory diffs 'the template I started from' vs 'what actually shipped after human edits' and promotes the delta into the template library — a higher-tier change requiring explicit human approval. The codebase barely changes; the priors compound. **Why better:** The current loop patches downstream symptoms one failureCode at a time, but the highest-leverage improvements are upstream in the shared contracts and golden templates every worker inherits. Fixing the mold once fixes every future leaf — and the very next build of that type starts closer to done. Taste is the asset that transfers across every job; a plumbing refactor is local and one-time.
- **Every build is a training example — the factory distills its own reflexes** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each completed plan-tree, with its contracts, failures, fix diffs, and final green state, is logged as a (situation → winning-decision) pair. Periodically the factory builds a retrieval-policy (or fine-tunes) from this corpus so that next time it sees a similar node it skips deliberation and acts from learned reflex. Over months the fuzzy top of the tree gets cheaper because the factory has memorized how it tends to win. **Why better:** Today nothing is learned across runs except a JSONL ledger counting failure codes. This turns every build — successes included — into permanent capability, so the factory gets faster and cheaper the more it's used, not merely less broken.
- **The Factory rewrites its own SKILLS, not just app code** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the workers themselves (impeccable, grill-me, the contract-freezer, the router prompt, tower-ship-gate) as first-class build targets. When a node-type repeatedly produces weak output, the factory drafts a new version of that SKILL's prompt/logic, A/Bs it on held-out past nodes, and promotes the winner. The factory edits its own organs. **Why better:** Current self-evolution only drafts branches for the codebase under construction; the toolchain that does the building is frozen. Letting the factory improve its own skills is the actual 'factory builds factories' — the multiplier compounds on every future job, not just one feature.
- **Self-improvement as a first-class node TYPE in the plan-tree (dogfood the recursion)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — 'Improve the factory' is itself a decomposable intent the factory runs on itself: intent ('make T2 floor builds 30% cheaper') → plan-tree → contracts (the regression vault as failing tests) → autonomous parallel build → stitch — with the same scope-gate and model-router as any user job. **Why better:** Today self-evolution is a bolted-on side-channel with different mechanics than the main pipeline. Making it a node type aims the factory's strongest machinery — recursion, contracts, parallel build, the cockpit — at improving the factory, and any pipeline upgrade automatically upgrades self-improvement too.
- **The Regression Vault — improve against a held-out, replayable suite of past intents** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every completed job is frozen as a replayable benchmark (intent + contracts + the shipped artifact) — an event-sourced run history in the Temporal sense.
