Given a known-good run and a now-broken one, `factory bisect` binary-searches the decision DAG — holding all other leaves at their last-green artifact and flipping suspects to the new build (or replaying under the old run-card) — until the failing test goes green, naming the single culprit leaf AND the worker decision/prompt that produced it. **Why better:** Today a regression is untraceable to any worker decision because the unit of history is a human commit but the unit of CAUSE is a worker's invisible leaf choice. This gives git-bisect-grade root-causing over the swarm, collapsing a manual hunt across hundreds of parallel changes into a logarithmic, automatic search.
- **Provenance-as-contract: graphify verifies the run-card too** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Extend the as-built graphify check so it not only confirms reality matches the contract graph, but also that every shipped artifact has a valid, non-stale run-card whose input_hash matches the current contract — flagging any 'orphan' code with no recorded provenance or a contract that drifted out from under it. (Counterpoint variant: rather than a parallel manifest, fold the one useful fragment — contract-hash per node — INTO graphify and delete the standalone provenance system entirely, so there is only one source of truth about the build.) **Why better:** Makes provenance a first-class invariant of 'done' rather than a nice-to-have log, so the factory can refuse to call a build complete if any leaf can't prove how and from what contract it was made — while the counterpoint variant removes the risk of two systems disagreeing about the same build.
- **The Bit-Exact Replay Cassette (replay re-derives the artifact without calling an LLM)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every factory run records a 'cassette' so complete — model version hashes, exact prompts, sampled token logprobs, tool-call results, golden-template hash, RNG seeds, even wall-clock and quota-failover events — that replaying it re-derives the identical artifact byte-for-byte without ever calling an LLM again. Replay is pure: the cassette IS the build. Re-running an unchanged subtree is a no-op proven by hash equality, not a gamble. **Why better:** Kills the same-intent-twice problem at the root: an unchanged subtree can NEVER silently rewrite working code because replay is deterministic playback, not regeneration — turning 'why did this leaf come out this way' into a scrubbable recording you step through frame by frame.
- **Git-for-Intent: the Plan-Tree is the Repo** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Version-control the PLAN-TREE itself, not just the output code. Each node has a content-addressed hash over (its contract + its inputs + the cassette that built it). You `git diff` two runs of the same intent and see exactly which leaves diverged and why; you `git blame` a line of generated code back to the worker, prompt, and seed that emitted it; you `git bisect` a regression down to a single worker decision. **Why better:** Replaces the one-way 'intent in, artifact out' street with a navigable history. Regressions become bisectable to a worker decision instead of a black box, and diffing two builds of one intent becomes a first-class operation.
- **Content-addressed leaf cache (the 'Nix/Bazel for LLM builds')** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Treat every leaf build as a pure action keyed by a hash of its FULL closure — frozen contract (signature + schema + failing tests), golden-template hash, model id+version, system prompt, toolchain versions, and the resolved subtree of dependency contracts. Identical hash = cache hit, check out the exact prior artifact byte-identical; different hash = rebuild only that leaf. A global cross-project store means a leaf built last week (or in another factory) is reused verbatim instead of re-rolled. **Why better:** Makes re-running on an unchanged subtree provably free AND provably non-destructive — same intent twice MUST yield the same artifact when the closure is unchanged (determinism by construction), plus massive incremental-build speed/cost wins from sharing, which is the entire reason Bazel/Nx/Turborepo exist.
- **Deterministic Twin: a frozen local model checkpointed into every run** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Pair every non-deterministic frontier build with a distilled, weight-frozen local 'twin' model checkpointed alongside the run. The frontier model architects; the twin is the part of the manifest that guarantees reproducibility — because its weights are literally in the cassette, the build replays identically even after Claude/Codex silently ship a new version next month. **Why better:** Defeats the deepest reproducibility killer — provider models mutating underneath you. A manifest that pins a model 'version string' is a lie once the provider re-rolls weights; pinning actual frozen weights makes a build replayable for years.
- **The Notarized Build: cryptographic provenance you can subpoena** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Sign every run manifest into a tamper-evident, append-only ledger — a 'birth certificate' per artifact attesting which model, prompt, seed, template, and human gate produced it, countersigned by the model providers. Anyone (an auditor, a customer, a court) can verify an artifact's full lineage without trusting you. **Why better:** Elevates provenance from internal debugging convenience to externally trustable attestation. In a world of AI-generated everything, 'prove how this was built and that it wasn't tampered with' becomes a competitive moat the one-way-street model can never offer.
- **The Build Manifest is the real artifact; code is a cache** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert what the factory considers its output. Each run emits a signed, content-addressed manifest (plan-tree hash, and per-leaf: model id+version, full resolved prompt, sampling params/seed, tool versions, golden-template hash, input-context hash, worker id, output blob hash). The code on disk is treated as a rebuildable CACHE of that manifest, not the source of truth — like a lockfile-plus-build-cache rather than a one-way emission. **Why better:** Three whys deep: a run is a one-way street because we treat code as the deliverable; code is the deliverable because that's what a human reads and ships; but a human reading it doesn't make it the source of truth — it's just the most legible projection. Once the manifest is the canonical object and code is a derived projection, replay, diff, audit, and selective re-run all become trivial reads of a stored object instead of impossible archaeology over non-deterministic text.
- **Event-sourced run manifest with deterministic replay (Temporal-style history log)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't store the artifact as the source of truth — store the ORDERED LOG of every decision: which router chose which model, every prompt sent, every tool call + its result, every seed, every retry. The artifact is a pure projection of that log. A run becomes 'replayable' the way a Temporal workflow is: feed the same history back and you deterministically reconstruct exactly what happened, even though the live LLM calls were non-deterministic. **Why better:** Provenance stops being metadata bolted on afterward and becomes the build's substrate. You answer 'why did this leaf come out this way' by reading its slice of the log, and re-run a single failed leaf against the EXACT recorded inputs of its neighbors — which the current one-way-street model throws away the moment the artifact lands.
- **Seed-pinned leaves: determinism is a per-node contract, not a global wish** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make every leaf declare its own determinism contract as part of its frozen contract: a pinned model snapshot, temperature 0 where it must be reproducible, a fixed random seed, and a hash of the exact context window assembled for it. 'Creative/explore' leaves stay free but are explicitly tagged as non-reproducible; 'mechanical/reproduce' leaves are pinned and become re-runnable functions. Re-running a pinned leaf with the same contract MUST yield a byte-identical (or semantically-frozen) artifact, or the factory flags a provenance breach. **Why better:** Same intent twice is non-deterministic because we treat the LLM as a creative oracle, not a function, and never separated 'this leaf is creative' from 'this leaf is mechanical.' Assigning determinism per node is exactly what lets you re-run ONE failed leaf without silently rewriting the working ones around it.
- **Hermetic worker sandboxes: pin the toolchain so 'works on my worker' can't happen** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal hermeticity from Bazel/Nix: each headless worker runs against a pinned, declared toolchain (node/tsc/test-runner/dep-lockfile versions) recorded in the run manifest, and is FORBIDDEN to touch anything outside its declared inputs. Two runs with the same manifest hit the same toolchain, so a green leaf today is still green when replayed next month. **Why better:** LLM non-determinism is only HALF the reproducibility problem — the other half is environment drift (a dep bumps, tsc changes, a test runner updates) silently breaking a replay. Pinning the environment means provenance actually buys you replayability, instead of a manifest pointing at a world that no longer exists.
- **Lineage tree + mutation rate: phylogeny of artifacts, per-worker drift** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow from biology: every artifact has parents (the contract + template it descended from) and you record its 'mutation rate' — how much a leaf's output changes across re-runs at fixed inputs. Workers/models/templates with high variance for the same contract are quarantined or pinned to a fixed seed; low-variance lineages are trusted to auto-merge. The factory LEARNS which parts of itself are reproducible. **Why better:** The current model treats all non-determinism as uniform and unmeasured. Tracking lineage + per-node variance lets the factory spend its determinism budget where it matters — pin the flaky leaves, let stable ones run free — instead of trusting everything blindly or freezing everything.
- **The factory must reproduce ITSELF before it earns trust to build** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before a real run, the factory replays a canonical golden manifest from cold storage and asserts it reproduces the known-good artifact. If today's model snapshots/tools/templates no longer reproduce yesterday's golden output, the factory refuses to start (or forces a re-baseline) — a self-provenance gate, analogous to ArtLab's concept-critique-fallback blocker. **Why better:** A factory with no reproducibility check can't tell when its own substrate changed underneath it. Proving it can rebuild a known-good artifact before spending the swarm converts a silent regression from a model update into a loud, run-blocking failure — the same fail-closed instinct as the project's existing blockers.
- **The artifact is the only provenance worth keeping (delete the manifest)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete the run manifest entirely. The committed code + its tests + git history ARE the record. If a leaf passes the frozen contract's tests, WHY it came out that way is irrelevant; the green test is the provenance. Stop trying to capture seeds, prompts, and model versions that explain a thing the tests already certify. **Why better:** Provenance metadata ages into lies the instant a model version rotates, while a passing test is eternally re-checkable. You're already paying for contracts-as-tests; a manifest is a second, weaker truth that competes with the first and rots faster.
- **Replayability is a category error — LLM builds are not compilations** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Abandon the goal of deterministic re-run of a single leaf. Same seed + same prompt does NOT reproduce output across model patch versions, temperature drift, or tool-version skew, and providers rotate models on their schedule, not yours. Embrace builds as one-way, non-reproducible events — like a casting, not a make target — and redirect all that engineering toward re-verification. **Why better:** The current assumption smuggles in a compiler mental model that's physically false for hosted LLMs. Chasing bit-reproducibility you can never achieve wastes engineering and creates false confidence; admitting non-determinism redirects effort to the thing that actually protects you — re-verification, not replay.
- **Re-verify, never re-run — the lock is on the gate, not the build** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert provenance into its opposite: instead of recording HOW a leaf was built so you can rebuild it, freeze WHAT it must satisfy and forbid the factory from ever rebuilding a leaf whose contract is unchanged and whose tests are green. No manifest needed; an unchanged-subtree is simply skipped, not replayed. **Why better:** Directly kills 'silently rewrite working code' without any provenance capture at all. A content-hash of the contract + a green test is cheaper than a full manifest and gives a stronger guarantee: working code is structurally untouchable, not merely diffable after the fact.
- **Non-determinism is a feature: re-running SHOULD yield a different, better artifact** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Flip 'same intent twice = scary divergence' into 'same intent twice = a free second draft.' Treat re-runs as a generative ensemble — run the subtree N ways on purpose, keep the one that scores highest against the contract, discard the rest. Variation is the asset, not the bug. **Why better:** Pinning the swarm to one deterministic path throws away the only thing it does that a Yeoman/codegen template can't — explore — and a best-of-N selector beats a single replayable run on quality every time.
- **Bisecting to a 'worker decision' is a question nobody will ever ask** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Drop the premise that you'll want to forensically blame a 2-week-old prompt for a leaf regression. When a leaf regresses, you re-throw it at a fresh worker against the same contract and take the green result. The cheapest debugger is regeneration, not archaeology — so don't hoard the manifest that the investigation would need. **Why better:** Manifests are justified by an investigative workflow that costs more (read logs, reconstruct context, reason about a stochastic decision) than the alternative (just rebuild the leaf, seconds, deterministic gate). You're hoarding evidence for a trial that never happens.
- **Provenance secretly re-introduces the human into a zero-human run** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — A run manifest exists to be READ by a human auditor — which contradicts the core promise of a fully autonomous orchestrator with ZERO human interaction mid-run. If no human is in the loop, there's no audience for provenance; treat its existence as a signal that the gate is under-trusted, and fix the gate rather than paper over it with logs. **Why better:** Exposes provenance as a hedge against not trusting the autonomy you just built: either the gate is good enough to run unattended (then you don't need the manifest) or it isn't (then fix the gate). The aspect is a symptom of an under-trusted gate, not a real need.
- **Capture intent-lineage, kill build-lineage** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — If anything is recorded, record only the PLAN-TREE and the frozen contracts (the upstream 'what'), never the build trace (the downstream 'how'). The plan-tree is deterministic, author-owned, and diffable; the build is stochastic and disposable. Provenance lives entirely ABOVE the swarm, nothing below it. **Why better:** Salvages the one genuinely diffable, re-runnable thing — the intent/contract layer — and explicitly trashes the part that can't be made reproducible. You answer 'what did we ask for and how did that change' (the real audit question) without pretending you can answer 'how exactly did the model build it' (the impossible one).

### Mid-run steering & interruptibility (beyond the one scope gate)

_Current assumption: The seed collapses all human involvement into ONE upfront scope-preview gate, then 'ZERO human interaction mid-run.' Unstated: a long autonomous swarm WILL drift, and the only modeled human touchpoints are the entry gate and the final artifact. There is no notion of a steering interrupt (pause/redirect/kill a subtree without killing the run), no surfacing of a 'this leaf is thrashing — look at me' signal mid-flight (ArtLab needed exactly this: the needs-human blocker + 10s heartbeat), and no way to inject a correction that the swarm respects. 'No human mid-run' is an aspiration that breaks the first time a subtree goes sideways for 40 minutes._

**Boldest reframe:** Time-Travel Steering: Rewind a Subtree to Any Checkpoint and Fork — because every leaf builds in its own git worktree against a frozen contract, the whole run is a replayable event log; scrub the cockpit timeline back to before a subtree drifted, branch a what-if with a corrected contract, and re-swarm forward, optionally racing the original against the fork and keeping whichever goes green first. This is the boldest because it doesn't just make the frozen top-down contracts interruptible — it makes them REVERSIBLE, turning a wrong high-altitude decision from an irrecoverable catastrophe into a cheap undo + A/B, and it reconceives the entire run as a durable, replayable event log rather than a one-way process. It subsumes pause, redirect, fork, and the standing-tribunal gate while preserving every token of cheap downstream work.

- **Per-leaf heartbeat with a thrash counter** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every worker emits a 10s heartbeat (ArtLab already does this) carrying not just 'alive' but a cheap progress signal: attempts, test-pass delta, and tokens-since-last-green. A leaf whose attempt count climbs while its failing-test count stays flat is 'thrashing' and gets flagged in the cockpit without any human watching it live. The cockpit surfaces a ranked 'look at me' queue ordered by wasted-tokens-per-minute. **Why better:** The seed has zero mid-flight signal — a leaf can burn 40 minutes invisibly. This reuses an existing ArtLab mechanism (10s heartbeat) and adds one derived metric, turning silence into an actionable 'look at me' the moment a subtree goes sideways, not at the final artifact.
- **needs-human as an orthogonal blocker on any node** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Adopt ArtLab's exact pattern: a leaf or subtree can self-raise a `needs-human` blocker (ambiguous contract, repeated test failure, missing decision) that PAUSES just that node and its descendants while the rest of the swarm keeps building. The run state becomes (node, phase, blocker?) per the same reconciler model ArtLab uses. **Why better:** Replaces the binary 'zero human interaction or kill the whole run' with a surgical pause. The factory's own sibling system already proved this is the right shape; the seed simply forgot to carry it across. One stuck leaf no longer forces an all-or-nothing choice.
- **Subtree-scoped pause / redirect / kill without aborting the run** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Three first-class verbs at every node, extending `artlab cancel` from run-level to node-level. PAUSE freezes this branch and frees its worktrees and quota back to the pool. REDIRECT swaps the node's contract/skill/model and re-dispatches only its descendants. KILL prunes the subtree and lets the green wave route around the hole — while siblings keep building. The run is a forest of independently-controllable limbs, not one atomic pass between two gates. **Why better:** 'Kill a subtree without killing the run' is named in the aspect as the missing primitive. ArtLab already releases slot leases and refunds reservations on cancel — node-scoping that machinery makes mid-run surgery a scalpel instead of a guillotine, preserving 90% of in-flight work when the first branch goes sideways.
- **Steering inbox / standing-orders doc the swarm re-reads at every node boundary** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A persistent, human-writable steering document keyed by node-path ('node 3.* — prefer Postgres enums over text'; 'whole run — stop gold-plating tests'; 'use Zod v4 not v3'; 'match the war-room palette'). Workers reconcile against their relevant entries when they pick up or hand off a node, so a correction lands deterministically at the next safe checkpoint rather than racing a running process. A soft, propagating correction that needs no kill, no gate, no barrier. **Why better:** The seed has no way to inject a correction the swarm respects — it's deaf between gates. Checking at node boundaries (not mid-keystroke) makes injection safe and ordered, gives continuous low-latency steering that compounds across the whole forest, and keeps the manifest the single source of truth the orchestrator already dispatches off.
- **Drift watchdog: auto-pause a leaf at a token/time budget per altitude tier** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each leaf carries a soft budget derived from its altitude tier (a T0 component leaf shouldn't cost what a T3 subtree does). Crossing the soft cap doesn't kill it — it auto-raises a `needs-human` blocker and parks the worker, so the 40-minute runaway becomes a 5-minute 'this leaf exceeded budget, decide' card. **Why better:** Turns the unbounded-drift failure mode into a bounded, self-surfacing event. ArtLab's stale-at-10-min reconciler is the precedent; budgeting per altitude tier (which the seed already classifies) makes the threshold meaningful instead of a flat global timeout.
- **Recurring scope gate: a standing tribunal that re-fires on re-plan and at every altitude transition** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The upfront scope gate doesn't close — it re-opens automatically on material re-plan (a worker discovers a contract is wrong and the tree must regrow a branch with new leaves/schema), at every altitude transition (T3→T2→T1→T0), and at any cost-threshold crossing. 'Steering' is just the same cheap scope-preview gate firing again for only the changed subtree, never a new mechanism. **Why better:** The seed treats scope as a one-time upfront decision and mid-run steering as a separate system — but they're the same problem at different depths. One recurring gate is far simpler to build than a gate plus a bespoke interrupt/heartbeat/inject subsystem, and it puts human attention exactly where spend escalates instead of at arbitrary 'this feels off' moments.
- **Live cockpit pause/resume/re-prompt on any subtree** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The rebuilt Forge cockpit gets a per-node pause/resume/re-prompt control wired straight to the blocker model — click node 5.1, it parks; type a correction, it resumes from where it stopped. Mirrors ArtLab's `artlab continue <runId>` but at node granularity and through the UI the seed already plans to build. **Why better:** The cockpit is already in scope as the live plan-tree view; giving it hands (not just eyes) is the smallest possible bridge from 'watch the swarm drift' to 'steer it.' It makes interruptibility a first-class, discoverable affordance instead of a CLI afterthought.
- **Confidence-gated escalation so only low-confidence leaves interrupt** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each leaf self-reports a confidence score (contract clarity, test coverage, retry count). High-confidence leaves run fully autonomous and never bother the human; only leaves that dip below a threshold raise themselves into the steering queue. The human's attention is rationed to exactly the nodes that need it. **Why better:** Naive interruptibility risks death-by-a-thousand-prompts, which would just recreate the per-leaf-approval hell the seed was trying to escape. Gating escalation on self-reported confidence preserves the 'mostly autonomous' spirit while guaranteeing the genuinely-stuck 40-minute leaf is the one thing that surfaces.
- **The Plan-Tree IS the Steering Surface (Direct Manipulation Mid-Flight)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The live plan-tree in the cockpit isn't a read-only monitor — it's a hot-editable document the swarm continuously re-reads. You drag a node to re-parent it, strike a leaf to kill that subtree, retype a contract signature, or pin a note onto a branch — and the running workers respect the edit on their next checkpoint with no restart. Steering is just editing the same artifact the swarm is building. **Why better:** Replaces the single upfront gate with continuous, structural control. The current model treats the plan as frozen-on-launch; making it a live mutable substrate means a 40-minute drift becomes a 5-second drag-and-strike instead of a kill-and-rerun.
- **Drift Radar: the swarm surfaces 'look at me' and it bubbles UP the tree** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every leaf emits a heartbeat carrying not just 'alive' but a self-assessed convergence/thrash score (retries, test-flapping, contract-violations, token-burn-per-green, time-over-estimate, self-doubt phrases in its own reasoning). When a subtree's score crosses a threshold it raises a soft `needs-human` flag that bubbles UP the tree and pulses in the cockpit — pause-on-detect optional. The factory tells YOU where to look instead of you babysitting eight worktrees. **Why better:** Directly imports ArtLab's hard-won `needs-human` blocker + 10s heartbeat. The current model only knows done/not-done, so a thrashing leaf is invisible until the final artifact or never. Measuring convergence rate makes the silent 40-minute drift the LOUDEST event in the cockpit instead of the quietest, and turns 'zero human mid-run' into 'zero REQUIRED attention, instant SUMMONED attention.'
- **Steering Without Stopping: a whisper channel the swarm obeys, compiled into a constraint amendment** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A persistent 'whisper channel' lets you type a natural-language correction mid-run ('these leaves should all use Zod v4, not v3'; 'make the war-room floor darker') that gets compiled into a constraint amendment and broadcast to the relevant subtree. Workers absorb it at their next checkpoint; in-flight leaves that now violate it self-flag for redo. No pause, no rerun — the run bends. **Why better:** The seed has exactly one human input (the scope gate) and zero correction path. A respected mid-run injection means you don't have to choose between 'let it finish wrong' and 'kill everything' — you nudge a moving system, and the correction self-propagates to exactly the leaves it invalidates.
- **Time-Travel Steering: rewind a subtree to any checkpoint and fork** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Because every leaf builds in its own git worktree against a frozen contract, the whole run is a replayable event log. You can scrub the cockpit timeline back to 'before this subtree drifted', branch a what-if at that point with a corrected contract, and let it re-swarm forward — optionally racing the original against the fork and keeping whichever goes green first. **Why better:** Transforms a mistaken contract from a catastrophe into an undo. The seed's top-down frozen contracts have no escape hatch when a high node was wrong; time-travel makes the freeze reversible without losing the cheap downstream work.
- **The Standing Order Sheet: pre-authorize how the swarm should drift, with a reserved intervention budget** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Alongside the scope gate, you write a short 'rules of engagement' — thresholds and policies the swarm consults autonomously ('if a leaf retries 3x, downgrade scope and flag, don't keep burning tokens'; 'never touch byte-protected assets'; 'if cost passes $X, pause the whole run'). You also reserve an explicit mid-run intervention budget at scope time (e.g. 3 steer-tokens + 15 operator-minutes) so the architecture carves out capacity to re-plan and re-dispatch. It's steering you pre-declare so the swarm self-corrects within your guardrails. **Why better:** Pushes human judgment EARLIER and makes most mid-run interrupts unnecessary, while still honoring 'zero human mid-run' for the common case. The seed's single gate captures WHAT to build but nothing about HOW the system should behave when it goes sideways — and pre-budgeting corrections makes a steer a planned event the system expects rather than a shock that destabilizes the manifest.
- **Two-Way Interrogation: the swarm grills YOU mid-run when it's unsure** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of a worker silently guessing on an ambiguous leaf (and drifting), it pauses that leaf and fires a single sharp question up to the cockpit — 'CRO floor: pipeline-stage colors — match the gold accent or introduce a status palette?' — with 2-3 concrete options and a default it'll take on timeout. You answer in one tap; the leaf resumes. The grill-me dynamic, but the swarm initiates. **Why better:** Converts the worst drift cause — confident wrong guesses on under-specified leaves — into a cheap clarifying micro-gate. The seed assumes recursion makes every leaf 'explicit enough', but the first genuinely ambiguous leaf needs a question, not a guess, and there's currently no channel for one.
- **Ambient Steering: walk a live build-out of the Tower and object to anything** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stay true to the building metaphor — the cockpit renders the in-progress artifact as a literal tower under construction (floors filling in, characters appearing, rooms lighting up as leaves go green). You walk the building as it's built and gesture-object at anything that looks wrong; the objection geolocates to the responsible subtree and pauses it. Steering by inhabiting the thing being made. **Why better:** Makes mid-run oversight intuitive and continuous rather than a JSON-tree-staring chore, so you actually catch drift instead of zoning out. The seed's cockpit is a status dashboard; an inhabitable live artifact makes the human's attention naturally land on what's wrong, which is the real bottleneck of any 'watch the swarm' model.
- **The human is a sensor, not a gate — invert the attention model** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Stop modeling the human as an approver who blocks at boundaries and start modeling them as the swarm's highest-bandwidth anomaly detector. The factory's job mid-run is to PULL the operator's eyes onto the ~5% of leaves that are diverging, and actively keep eyes OFF the 95% behaving. 'Zero human interaction' becomes 'zero human interaction you didn't earn' — silence is the reward signal, an alert is the system spending your attention on purpose. **Why better:** The current assumption frames every human touch as friction to eliminate, so it eliminates the one thing that catches a 40-minute drift. Reframing the human as a sensor means the system gets MORE autonomous (fewer false interrupts) while becoming steerable — the two goals stop being in tension.
- **Contracts are versioned hypotheses, not frozen truth — the amendment wave** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A mid-run correction is a versioned edit to the frozen contract (a typed signature, a test, a schema field) committed to the contract graph, not a chat message. The reconciler (ArtLab's 'only legal read path') detects which leaves' contracts changed, propagates a 'contract changed @ node X, v2' wave: descendants that depended on v1 are invalidated and re-dispatched, untouched subtrees keep their green. A steer becomes a LOCAL re-freeze that ripples, causally binding because headless workers are structurally forced to reconcile to the graph. **Why better:** Today the plan-tree is frozen top-down and untouchable, so the only way to fix a wrong contract is to kill the whole run. A prompt-injected note can be ignored by a headless worker. Routing steering through versioned contracts + the reconciler makes the redirect mechanically scoped and un-ignorable, with cost proportional to the blast radius instead of the size of the whole run.
- **Incremental re-dispatch on edit — affected-graph for human corrections** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a human (or a failing stitch) changes one node, compute the 'affected set' the way Nx/Turborepo compute affected projects from a changed file, and ONLY re-run those leaves — reusing cached green output for everything downstream-independent. Killing/redirecting a subtree never re-runs the cousins. **Why better:** Currently a mid-run change implies either ignore-it (drift) or restart (waste). An affected-graph makes 'redirect a subtree without killing the run' the cheap default — the green wave only re-climbs the branches the correction actually touched.
- **Designed steer-points — checkpoint barriers the swarm parks at** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Pre-declare safe interruption points in the plan-tree (at each tier boundary, after contracts freeze but before the swarm spends, at every stitch-wave crest). At a steer-point the swarm parks at a consistent state and briefly opens a write window: redirect a subtree, kill it, re-scope, inject a correction — then resume. Mid-run control becomes a scheduled affordance at a clean seam, not an emergency abort. **Why better:** Right now interruption is unplanned, so the only interrupt available is SIGKILL on the whole run, which can corrupt a worktree mid-write. Designed barriers mean you can always steer at a clean seam where state is consistent, instead of yanking a worker mid-keystroke.
- **Checkpoint-and-fork: pause a subtree, snapshot its worktree, build speculative variants in parallel** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Steal VFX dailies + Temporal's durable replay: every leaf checkpoints to its git worktree at each contract milestone. A human can pause a subtree at a checkpoint, FORK it into 2-3 speculative variants (different approaches to the same contract), let the swarm build all forks in parallel, then pick the survivor at the next stitch. Interruption becomes branching, not just stopping. **Why better:** The seed offers no way to inject judgment mid-flight except up front. Checkpoint-and-fork turns a steering moment into a cheap A/B at the exact altitude where taste matters, instead of forcing the human to guess the right approach before the swarm has spent a single token.
- **The dailies reel — a scheduled mid-run review window the human can ignore** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow film's 'dailies': at fixed intervals (or each time a stitch wave completes a tier), the cockpit assembles a 2-minute digest — what each subtree built, diffs against contract, thrash flags, token spend — as a glanceable reel. The human can scrub it and drop steering notes onto specific frames (nodes); silence = implicit approval and the swarm rolls on. **Why better:** It replaces the false binary of 'one gate then go dark' with optional, low-friction, recurring visibility. Steering becomes available continuously without being mandatory — the human stays a director who reviews dailies, not a foreman who must watch every station or none.
- **Supervisor agents with kill/restart authority — an Erlang/LangGraph supervision tree** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow OTP supervision trees + LangGraph's supervisor pattern: each internal (non-leaf) node runs a lightweight supervisor agent whose ONLY job is to watch its children's heartbeats and enforce a restart policy (retry-with-backoff, restart-with-fresh-context, escalate-to-human, or circuit-break the subtree). Most drift is caught and reset machine-to-machine; only policy exhaustion pages a person. **Why better:** The seed has one human supervising N headless workers with no intermediate authority — the human IS the supervisor for the whole tree, which doesn't scale past a few thrashing leaves. A supervision tree absorbs routine drift automatically and reserves human interrupts for genuine escalations, making 'more processes = more power' actually true under failure.
- **Per-subtree circuit breaker that mints a context-rich steering ticket** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow Hystrix/Inngest circuit breakers + manufacturing WIP limits: each subtree gets a failure budget (attempts, tokens, wall-clock). Tripping the breaker auto-pauses that subtree and converts it into a human-steering ticket carrying full context (last 3 attempts, the contract, the diff). The human spends a finite 'steering budget' across open tickets, forcing triage instead of whack-a-mole. **Why better:** Without a breaker, a sideways subtree burns the shared quota ($200 Claude + $100 Codex) silently for 40 minutes. A per-subtree breaker caps the blast radius and turns drift into a pre-packaged, prioritized decision — the human steers the few that matter rather than discovering the bill at the final artifact.
- **Drift autopilot — a cross-model self-arrest reflex before any human is paged** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before escalating to a human, the orchestrator runs a cheap drift-arrest reflex: a divergent leaf is auto-paused, its context snapshotted, and the IDLE model (Codex if Claude was building, or vice-versa) does a 60-second 'is this off the rails?' second-opinion. Most drifts self-correct or self-kill; only genuinely ambiguous ones spend an operator interrupt. The human becomes the LAST resort, not the only sensor — using capacity the seed already pays for. **Why better:** The current model has exactly two human touchpoints and nothing in between, so EITHER drift runs for 40 minutes OR a human must babysit. A cross-model self-arrest reflex fills that void using the second frontier model already on the books, preserving 'zero human mid-run' for the cases that genuinely deserve it.
- **Delete steering entirely — make the run cheap enough to throw away** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — There is no pause/redirect/kill-subtree machinery at all. If a run drifts, you don't steer it — you let it finish (or hard-kill the whole run) and re-roll from a sharpened intent. The factory's job is to make a full run so cheap and fast that correcting via a fresh run beats the cognitive and engineering cost of mid-flight surgery. **Why better:** Mid-run steering is the most complex, bug-prone part of any swarm (consistency of a half-redirected plan-tree is a nightmare). Killing it forces the real fix — cheaper runs and a sharper entry gate — instead of papering over a bad gate with live intervention. A drifted subtree you 'corrected' at minute 40 is usually worse than a clean re-roll.
- **Invert who watches whom — the swarm steers the human, never the reverse** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the human-initiated interrupt entirely. The only mid-run human action is answering questions the swarm chooses to raise (a needs-human blocker it owns). The human cannot reach in unprompted; they can only respond when a leaf declares itself stuck. Attention flows up on the swarm's terms, never down on the human's whim. **Why better:** Human-initiated steering invites micromanagement that destroys the 'zero interaction mid-run' value prop and creates race conditions (you redirect a node that just finished). ArtLab already proved the right primitive is the worker-raised needs-human blocker, not a human-held joystick that fights the scheduler.
- **Ban the kill switch — drift is the most valuable artifact; harvest it** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Remove pause/kill of subtrees outright. A subtree that 'goes sideways for 40 minutes' is producing the single most valuable output the factory can generate: a worked example of where the contract, router, or decomposition is wrong. Let it run to completion and harvest the failure into the taste/contract ledger that ArtLab's reject path already writes. **Why better:** Killing a thrashing subtree throws away the only evidence of WHY it thrashed, so the next run thrashes identically — you've optimized latency at the cost of never learning. A kill switch severs the learning loop; better to be slow-and-learning than fast-and-amnesiac.
- **Make leaves structurally incapable of thrashing — one-shot or re-decompose** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Drop the whole 'surface a thrashing leaf' apparatus (heartbeats, stall detectors, attention signals). Instead, hard-cap every leaf at one shot with a fixed token/time budget: a leaf either one-shots green against its failing tests or it's instantly marked failed and bubbles up for re-decomposition. Nothing runs long enough to need a 'look at me' signal. **Why better:** The 'this leaf is thrashing for 40 minutes' problem only exists because leaves are allowed to grind. If a leaf can't one-shot, the decomposition was wrong — the fix is to split it again, not watch it suffer. A one-shot budget makes the thrashing-detector, heartbeat, and steering-interrupt all unnecessary by construction, and enforces the seed's own definition of a leaf.
- **Steering belongs to Codex, not the human — cross-model review IS the interrupt** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete the human steering channel and hand interrupt authority to the idle model. The two-model router already has Claude judging and Codex reviewing; promote that to a live watchdog — the non-building model continuously reviews in-flight subtrees and is the ONLY actor with pause/redispatch power. The human is removed from the mid-run loop entirely. **Why better:** A human steering at minute 40 is slow, inconsistent, and asleep half the time; the factory has a second frontier model sitting idle that can watch every subtree at machine speed. Putting the interrupt in Codex's hands makes mid-run correction continuous and automatic, using the $100 Codex capacity already paid for rather than a human-facing steering UI nobody can staff 24/7.
- **Replace mid-run steering with a fork: never redirect, always branch** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — There is no 'redirect a subtree without killing the run.' If the human disagrees mid-flight, the only action is fork-the-run: snapshot the worktree-backed plan-tree, spawn a divergent run from that node with the new intent, and let both race to a finished artifact you compare at the end. **Why better:** Redirecting in place corrupts the plan-tree's consistency and forces fragile 'respect this correction' logic into every worker. Forking is git-native (worktrees already exist), keeps every run internally coherent, and turns a steering decision into a cheap A/B you judge on finished output instead of guessing mid-process — you stop steering blind and start choosing between two real artifacts.

### Recursion termination & runaway tree growth

_Current assumption: The plan-tree 'recurses until a leaf is Codex-ready' and the altitude dial 'biases BIGGER' — both assume recursion naturally terminates and bigger is safe. Unstated: there is no GOVERNOR on tree explosion. A fuzzy node can recurse into hundreds of leaves; 'bias bigger' compounds this; a node that's hard to make Codex-ready can recurse forever or be over-decomposed into busywork. Nothing defines a max depth/breadth, a node budget, a 'this is decomposing pathologically — stop and ask' tripwire, or detection that two sibling subtrees are re-deriving the same leaf. Combinatorial blow-up is the default failure mode of recursive planners and it's unmodeled._

**Boldest reframe:** Delete recursion entirely — the factory has exactly two altitudes, never N

- **Node budget that bias-bigger spends FROM, not into** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give every run a single up-front leaf budget (e.g. 40 leaves) shown at the scope-preview gate. 'Bias bigger' then means spending fewer, fatter leaves out of that fixed pool — so going bigger REDUCES tree size instead of compounding it, and the budget is a hard ceiling the swarm cannot exceed without re-gating. **Why better:** Reframes the dangerous coupling: today 'bias bigger' and 'recurse to Codex-ready' both push leaf count UP. A shared budget makes 'bigger' and 'more leaves' trade against each other, which is the actual safe direction, and reuses forge's existing scope-preview gate.
- **Hard depth+breadth caps borrowed from the verify-loop's 3-round cap** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Forge already has a 'hard 3-round cap → then decide and move on' on the adversarial loop. Apply the identical pattern to recursion: max depth (e.g. 4) and max children-per-node (e.g. 7); on hitting either, stop decomposing and ship that node as one fat leaf with a flag rather than splitting further. Hitting a cap can also pause at the scope-preview gate so the human deliberately extends the fuse per branch. **Why better:** The current assumption has no terminal condition at all; a node 'hard to make Codex-ready' can recurse forever. A cap guarantees termination, reuses a cap pattern the codebase already trusts rather than inventing new control theory, and wired to the existing scope gate makes blow-up a visible, priced decision instead of a silent default.
- **Codex-ready confidence score, not a boolean gate (diminishing-returns stop)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the binary 'is this leaf Codex-ready?' with a 0-1 readiness score from the architect. Recurse only while score is improving meaningfully between levels; if a child's readiness barely beats its parent's, decomposition isn't helping — stop and ship the parent as the leaf. **Why better:** Directly attacks the 'over-decomposed into busywork' failure: a hard boolean can chase tiny gains forever. A diminishing-returns signal stops the recursion exactly when splitting stops paying for itself, which a boolean can never detect.
- **Sibling-dedup pass before freezing contracts** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before contracts freeze top-down, run one cheap pass that hashes/embeds every leaf's normalized intent and flags near-duplicate leaves across sibling subtrees, collapsing them into one shared contract that both parents depend on. **Why better:** The aspect explicitly calls out 'two sibling subtrees re-deriving the same leaf' as unmodeled. One dedup pass turns that silent waste (two workers building the same thing, then conflicting at stitch) into a shared seam — and graphify already gives you the embedding/graph substrate to do it.
- **Pathological-decomposition tripwire that escalates to the human** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Watch a running ratio: leaves-produced vs leaves-predicted-at-the-gate, plus readiness-velocity per node. If a node blows past ~2x its predicted subtree size, OR keeps recursing without readiness gains (e.g. 3 levels deep, 12 leaves, no leaf yet Codex-ready), freeze that branch and surface a 'this is decomposing pathologically — pick: ship fat, cap depth, or rescope' card/blocker in the cockpit before the swarm spends tokens. **Why better:** Adds the missing 'stop and ask' tripwire the aspect names. It converts runaway growth from a silent token bonfire into a single human decision at the cheapest possible moment — pre-build. ArtLab's orthogonal-blocker model (needs-human, budget-blocked) is the exact proven mechanism to reuse, and it fits forge's 'process must never exceed output' rule.
- **Two-pass plan: estimate the whole tree cheaply BEFORE decomposing for real** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Run a fast 'shape estimate' pass that predicts depth, leaf count, and token cost for the whole tree using rough heuristics, render it at the scope gate, and only then do the expensive real decomposition — and only down the branches the human greenlit. **Why better:** Today the blow-up is discovered DURING the expensive recursion, after tokens are spent. A cheap estimate-first pass makes combinatorial explosion visible and vetoable while it's still free, turning the altitude dial's 'bias bigger' into an informed bet instead of a blind one.
- **Per-leaf size floor so 'bias bigger' can't manufacture trivial leaves** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Define a minimum substance threshold for a leaf (e.g. must justify >N lines of real work or it's busywork). Any candidate leaf below the floor is force-merged back into its parent instead of becoming its own node, worker, and stitch point. **Why better:** Attacks blow-up from the bottom: the cost isn't just deep trees, it's a swarm of one-line leaves each carrying a worker-spawn + contract + stitch tax. A floor caps the WIDTH of busywork directly, complementing the depth caps.
- **Decomposition complexity-class auto-classifier with a regular-tree default** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before recursing a node, classify its decomposition shape: linear (a list of N siblings), bounded-tree, or open-ended/fractal. Default aggressively to the bounded shapes; only an open-ended node is allowed deep recursion, and only after an explicit human OK, so the fractal nodes are the rare exception not the default. **Why better:** The current model treats every node as freely recursive, so the worst-case (fractal) growth is the default path. Classifying shape up front makes the safe, terminating shapes the default and isolates unbounded recursion to a tiny, gated minority of nodes — flipping the failure mode from default to exceptional.
- **The Tree Has a Metabolism (energy budget, not depth limit)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Kill the idea of a depth/breadth cap entirely. The root intent is granted a fixed quantum of 'metabolic energy' (tokens, dollars, wall-clock — one fungible currency). Every act of decomposition COSTS energy and every node must metabolize its parent's allowance; a node can only split into children it can afford to feed. When a subtree runs hot, it literally starves — recursion stops because there's nothing left to burn, not because a counter hit a magic number. **Why better:** A max-depth/max-breadth governor is a guess that's wrong for every intent. An energy budget is self-adapting: a deep-but-narrow plan and a shallow-but-wide plan are governed by the SAME scarce resource the user actually cares about (spend), so blow-up becomes economically impossible rather than arbitrarily forbidden.
- **Speculative Over-Decomposition With Ruthless Pruning (beam-search the tree)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Lean INTO explosion on purpose. Decompose every fuzzy node three or four different ways in parallel, generate all the competing subtrees, then run a tournament that keeps only the cheapest subtree that still satisfies the parent's contract and discards the rest. The tree grows wildly for a few seconds, then collapses to its fittest shape. **Why better:** The current model commits to the FIRST decomposition it finds and recurses down it — if that split was bad, you get busywork forever. Treating decomposition as a search problem with a fitness function means runaway growth is harnessed as exploration, and the survivor is provably the leanest tree, not just the first plausible one.
- **Global Leaf Memoization — content-addressed leaf cache across all runs** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every leaf the factory has ever produced (and every sibling currently in-flight) is hashed by its frozen semantic contract (typed signature + schema slice + test fixture, like a Bazel/Nx action key) and stored in a shared, content-addressed cache spanning ALL runs, ALL projects, forever. Before any subtree decomposes, it queries the cache; if an equivalent leaf exists or is in-flight, the node resolves to a pointer/edge, not a new subtree. The factory gets monotonically smarter — the millionth app barely decomposes at all. **Why better:** The aspect explicitly names 'two sibling subtrees re-deriving the same leaf' as unmodeled. Build systems solved this decades ago with content-addressable action graphs. Memoization doesn't just detect duplicate leaves — it makes duplication structurally impossible, turns the factory's history into a deflationary force on tree size, and means bigger-biased trees stop scaring you because most leaves are cache hits.
- **The Decomposition Oracle: predict the tree before you grow it** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Before a single node splits, a learned model looks at the root intent and forecasts the FULL shape of the tree that's about to grow — leaf count, depth distribution, total cost, blow-up probability — the way a chess engine evaluates a position without playing it out. If the forecast is pathological, the factory refuses to start and shows the user the predicted explosion instead of discovering it the expensive way. **Why better:** The scope-preview gate today is a cheap guess AFTER some decomposition. An oracle moves the governor BEFORE the recursion — you never enter a runaway tree because you saw its silhouette and declined. It converts 'discover blow-up by living it' into 'predict blow-up and veto it.'
- **Pathology Tripwire as a First-Class Agent ('The Auditor')** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Spawn a dedicated adversarial agent whose ONLY job is to watch the live tree for decomposition pathologies: a node that's recursed N times without getting more Codex-ready, a subtree whose children are smaller than the cost of having split them, branches re-deriving siblings, fractal busywork. When it smells a pathology it doesn't warn — it HALTS that subtree and forces a 'stop and ask the human / re-architect from a higher altitude' interrupt. **Why better:** The aspect's exact gap is 'no tripwire that says this is decomposing pathologically — stop and ask.' Today nothing is looking. A standing auditor agent makes the tripwire a real, ever-present actor in the swarm rather than an assumption that recursion 'naturally terminates.'
- **Convergence-Gated Recursion: a node may only split if its children are provably simpler** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make 'getting closer to Codex-ready' a HARD, measured invariant, not a hope. Each node carries a fuzziness/complexity score; a decomposition is only ACCEPTED if the children's combined score is strictly, meaningfully lower than the parent's. A split that doesn't reduce fuzziness is rejected and the node is forced to either one-shot itself or escalate. Recursion is mathematically guaranteed to terminate because every legal step strictly decreases a bounded-below quantity. **Why better:** Today termination is an ASSUMPTION ('recurses until Codex-ready'). This makes it a THEOREM: like a loop variant, you cannot recurse forever because you'd have to keep decreasing a number that has a floor. A node that 'can't be made Codex-ready' provably cannot split, so it surfaces immediately.
- **Bias Bigger, But Pay an Insurance Premium (risk-priced altitude)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Keep the 'bias bigger' instinct but make every node BUY its own ambition. A bigger node must post 'collateral' — extra contract specificity, extra failing tests, a checkpoint, a rollback plan — proportional to the blow-up risk it introduces. Cheap, well-specified bigness is nearly free; speculative bigness is taxed until the factory either gets the specificity it needs or the node shrinks. **Why better:** 'Bias bigger is safe' is the unexamined compounding assumption. Risk-pricing keeps the upside of aiming high (fewer, fatter, one-shottable units) while making the DANGEROUS kind of bigness — vague, under-specified, explosion-prone — automatically expensive and therefore self-limiting.
- **The Tree That Grows Toward Light (demand-pull lazy decomposition)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop growing the whole tree up front. Decompose a node ONLY when a downstream builder actually reaches for it and finds it too fuzzy to one-shot — lazy, demand-pulled, just-in-time. Branches no one needs never get born; the tree materializes along the critical path the builders actually walk, and entire speculative subtrees that 'bias bigger' would have eagerly spawned simply never exist. **Why better:** Eager top-down recursion is the engine of combinatorial blow-up — it grows every branch whether or not it's needed. Lazy decomposition makes the worst case proportional to work ACTUALLY done rather than work imaginable, so a fuzzy node can't explode into hundreds of leaves unless hundreds are genuinely consumed downstream.
- **Charge for every node at birth — a conserved decomposition ledger** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Mirror the existing ArtLab budget/ledger pattern but for STRUCTURE, not provider spend: the root gets a finite 'planning token' / leaf budget (e.g. 120 leaves) granted at run start. When a node decomposes it must SPLIT its budget across children — a node with 8 budget can spawn at most 8 leaves beneath it. Recursing spends from the parent's allotment; a node can only spawn children it can pay for. When the ledger hits zero the tree physically cannot grow and the run raises a 'budget-blocked' blocker, forcing one-shot-what-you-have or escalate-for-more. **Why better:** The current assumption treats decomposition as free, so it has no natural floor. ArtLab already proves per-run caps and monthly ceilings work for spend; a budget that is conserved as it splits makes blow-up a hard accounting impossibility — total leaves can never exceed the root grant, and over-decomposed busywork visibly bankrupts its own subtree first.
- **Terminate on value-density, not clarity** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop recursing when a node's expected-value-per-token stops rising, even if the node is still 'fuzzy'. A clear-but-trivial node and a fuzzy-but-low-value node both halt: the recursion floor is 'further decomposition no longer improves the artifact,' measured, not 'the node is now Codex-ready.' **Why better:** 'Recurse until Codex-ready' conflates explicitness with worth, so it over-decomposes hard-to-specify-but-low-value nodes into busywork forever. Keying on marginal value makes the dial's 'bias bigger' safe — bigger only wins where it pays.
- **Plan as a deduplicating DAG, not a tree** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before any node recurses, hash its normalized intent and check a run-wide registry. If a sibling or cousin already produced (or is producing) an equivalent leaf, draw an EDGE to it instead of re-deriving. The 'tree' becomes a graph where shared leaves are built once. **Why better:** The current tree expands every node in isolation, so two siblings silently re-derive the same leaf — pure combinatorial waste. The codebase already leans on 'graphify' as the shared knowledge graph; making the PLAN itself a DAG turns re-derivation from a runtime accident into a structurally impossible one.
- **Decompose breadth-first with a frontier budget (anytime planner)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Abandon depth-first 'recurse this node until it's a leaf.' Instead expand the WHOLE frontier one level at a time against a global node budget, always keeping a buildable plan. If budget runs out mid-expansion, you ship the best coarse plan you have rather than one over-resolved branch and twelve untouched ones. Optionally cap in-flight open nodes per altitude tier (Kanban WIP limits) so the frontier grows in leveled waves, not a thundering herd. **Why better:** Depth-first recursion can pour the entire budget into one runaway subtree and starve the rest. A budgeted breadth-first frontier guarantees an always-valid, balanced plan, makes 'we ran out of room' degrade gracefully, and a per-tier WIP cap makes the scope-gate's cost estimate trustworthy because frontier width is bounded.
- **Two-judge decomposition gate: a 'split' must be defended** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make decomposing the EXCEPTION, not the default. A node stays a leaf unless the architect model produces an explicit, reviewable justification for splitting it — and a second model (cross-model, Codex) can veto the split as over-decomposition. Splitting requires winning an argument; staying whole is free. **Why better:** Right now recursion is the default and termination is the thing that must be earned — exactly backwards, which is why trees explode. The codebase already runs cross-model adversarial review (Claude builds, Codex proves); applying that same skepticism to STRUCTURE makes every unnecessary split die at birth.
- **Compost the tree: prune leaves that don't change the artifact** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Periodically run a backward pass that asks of each leaf 'if this didn't exist, would the parent's stitched output meaningfully change?' Leaves that don't move the artifact are merged upward or deleted. The tree is continuously gardened down, not just grown. **Why better:** Every governor so far only constrains growth; none ever SHRINKS a tree that already over-decomposed. A pruning pass turns the plan-tree into a self-correcting structure where busywork leaves are reabsorbed, so the green-wave stitch climbs fewer, denser nodes.
- **Scope-Preview as a Hard Budget Auction, not a glance (the gate gets teeth)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The cheap scope-preview doesn't just SHOW the projected tree — it requires the planner to fit estimated leaf count and token cost under a pre-committed ceiling. If 'bias bigger' overshoots, the gate auto-prunes the lowest-value subtrees (or demands the user raise the ceiling) before any worker spawns. The dial biases big; the auction enforces affordable. **Why better:** Right now the gate is informational while the dial actively biases BIGGER — a gas pedal with no brake. Making the gate a hard budget constraint that bigger must satisfy turns 'bias bigger, show a preview' into 'bias bigger UNTIL the ceiling, then prune' — exactly how cloud build/CI systems cap fan-out spend per job.
- **Apoptosis: subtrees that stop earning their keep self-destruct (biology)** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow programmed cell death. Each node carries a 'value-to-cost' survival signal that must stay above a threshold to keep dividing; a subtree whose marginal leaves stop adding contract surface (pure busywork) receives no survival signal from its parent and is pruned automatically, its partial work folded back up. The signal decays with depth (a morphogen gradient), so over-decomposition becomes self-limiting instead of self-perpetuating. **Why better:** Biological development is the canonical bounded recursive process: cells divide explosively but apoptosis and morphogen gradients ensure a hand has five fingers, not a tumor. The current planner has only division, no death — the literal definition of cancerous growth. A decaying survival gradient guarantees growth that doesn't pay for itself necessarily dies.
- **Title-only 'previs' spike before the real recursive build (VFX previsualization)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal VFX previs: before committing a fuzzy node to a real recursive build, the planner produces a cheap, throwaway low-fidelity decomposition — a 'previs' tree of titles only, no contracts — so depth and breadth are KNOWN before any expensive contract-freezing or worker spawns. If the previs shows 300 leaves, that's caught at sketch cost, not build cost. **Why better:** Film/VFX pipelines never light and render a sequence before previs locks the shot count. 'Recurse until Codex-ready' commits to expensive contract-freezing AS it discovers the shape, so blow-up is only visible after spend. A title-only spike makes the full tree's size a known quantity at the gate, where pruning is nearly free.
- **Org-Chart Span-of-Control + Escalation Ladder (the Tower's own C-Suite)** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Impose the management rule the Tower already preaches: a single node may directly own at most ~7 children (span of control). Need more than 7? You must insert a 'manager' intermediate node — forcing a coherent grouping AND a deliberate human-legible layer rather than a flat explosion of 40 siblings. Depth is bounded by an escalation ladder where each new level must justify a distinct altitude. **Why better:** Large eng orgs cap span-of-control precisely to keep a recursive hierarchy navigable and force intentional layering — and the Tower's own governance doc ('CEO must not own every detail') already encodes this. Forcing a manager-node at 7 caps breadth, makes the tree readable in the cockpit, and turns runaway width into a deliberate, costed structural decision.
- **Delete recursion entirely — the factory has exactly two altitudes, never N** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Kill the recursive plan-tree. Replace it with a flat, fixed two-level structure: a human (or Claude once) writes a hand-sized list of leaves directly, and that's the whole tree. If a 'leaf' turns out too big, you don't recurse — you reject the whole intent as too big for one run and tell the user to split it themselves. No depth, no breadth, no governor needed because there is nothing to govern. **Why better:** The entire governor problem (max depth, node budget, runaway detection, sibling dedup) is a tax you only pay because you chose recursion. A factory that physically cannot recurse cannot blow up. ArtLab — the one real autonomous engine in this repo — already works this way: a fixed 10-phase machine, not a tree. The repo has zero recursive planners shipped, which is evidence recursion is a want, not a need.
- **Invert 'bias bigger' to 'bias smaller until it hurts'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The altitude dial's default should pull DOWN, not up. Always classify an intent one tier smaller than it looks and force the user to manually upgrade it. The factory's resting state is 'do the smallest coherent thing and stop,' and growth is an explicit, friction-laden human act. **Why better:** 'Bias bigger' is the accelerant on the combinatorial fire — it makes every node spawn more children and every run spend more before the human sees value. Biasing smaller means the failure mode is 'did slightly too little' (cheap, recoverable, user re-runs) instead of 'recursed into 400 leaves of busywork.' Small runs also build user trust before the system is allowed to attempt big ones.
- **No leaf check — the builder declares 'too big' at runtime, one split per attempt** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop trying to recurse 'until a leaf is Codex-ready.' That predicate is the unbounded loop. Instead, hand any node — at any size — directly to Codex and let IT either one-shot it or return 'too big, here are the 3-5 pieces I'd split it into.' Decomposition becomes a side effect of a builder hitting a wall, capped at one split per attempt, not a planning phase that runs to a fixed point. **Why better:** 'Codex-ready' is judged by the architect (Claude) GUESSING what the builder can handle — a guess that can be wrong forever, which is exactly what lets a node recurse infinitely. Letting the actual builder declare 'too big' grounds termination in real capability instead of a predicted one, and 'one split per failed attempt' is a hard, self-evident governor that needs no separate budget system.
- **Merge recursion-governance INTO the scope gate — they're one feature** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The 'runaway tree' problem and the 'cheap scope-preview gate before the swarm spends' are not two aspects; the gate IS the governor. Delete recursion-governance as a standalone concern and make the scope gate the only place tree size is ever bounded: it shows leaf count + token estimate, and a tree simply cannot exist until a human approves its final size at the gate. **Why better:** A separate 'max depth / node budget / tripwire' subsystem is duplicate machinery — it polices the same number (total leaves) the scope gate already shows the human. One gate, one human approval on one number, is simpler and harder to bypass than a constellation of automatic limits that each need tuning and each have edge cases.
- **Let it explode, then prune — generate-and-cut, not plan-and-cap** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Don't prevent over-decomposition. Deliberately over-decompose into a deeply redundant tree, then run a single ruthless pruning pass that merges duplicate sibling leaves, collapses single-child chains, and deletes busywork — turning the messy big tree into a lean one before any building starts. Explosion is a feature of the draft; pruning is the discipline. **Why better:** Trying to stop blow-up DURING recursion forces the planner to make irreversible size decisions while it still knows the least. Letting it sprawl and then pruning with full visibility of the whole tree is where sibling-rederivation and busywork are actually DETECTABLE — the current design names sibling-dedup as a worry but offers no point in the pipeline where you can ever see two siblings at once. This creates that point.
- **One hard leaf-count quota; let nodes fight over it (no separate depth/breadth dials)** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Forget max-depth and max-breadth as separate dials. The factory gets ONE number: a global leaf budget (say 40). Recursion goes any shape it wants, but every new leaf decrements the shared budget; when it hits zero the tree freezes as-is and remaining fuzzy nodes are marked 'manual.' Depth and breadth self-regulate because they spend one shared currency, shown in the cockpit as a fuel gauge. **Why better:** Two independent caps interact badly — a tree can satisfy both and still be huge, or a legitimately deep-but-narrow task gets killed by a depth cap it didn't deserve. A single fungible leaf budget directly bounds the only thing that costs money (leaves = builds), is trivial to display, and converts 'runaway tree' from an unbounded risk into a known, prepaid ceiling.
- **Recursion is for art and prose, not code — forbid it on the build path** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Split the factory in two. The fuzzy-domain side (research reports, decks, art campaigns) MAY recurse because its leaves are cheap and idempotent. The code-build side may NOT — it gets a flat, contract-frozen leaf list only. The 'universal' factory stops pretending one recursion policy fits a research bullet and a database migration. **Why better:** The cost and blast radius of a runaway leaf are wildly different by domain: an extra research paragraph is free and deletable; an extra mis-decomposed migration leaf can corrupt the contract graph the whole swarm builds against. A single governor tuned for the average is wrong for both extremes — domain-split policy bounds the dangerous path by construction and keeps the safe path flexible.
- **No tripwire — a wall clock and a wallet are the only governor** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete every structural runaway-detector (max depth, sibling-dedup heuristics, pathological-decomposition sniffers). Replace all of it with two dumb limits: a planning-phase time budget and a dollar cap. Recursion runs until the timer or the meter trips, then whatever tree exists is what you build. The system never reasons about its own tree shape at all. **Why better:** Structural tripwires require the planner to correctly recognize its own pathology — but a recursive planner that's blown up is, by definition, mis-judging structure, so it's the worst possible judge of 'am I blowing up.' Time and money are external, model-agnostic, impossible to rationalize around, and they're the two limits the user actually cares about anyway ($200 Claude + $100 Codex).

### Per-leaf confidence & risk-weighted verification depth

_Current assumption: Verification is treated as uniform — every leaf goes red→green, every node gets integration tests + cross-model review, as if all leaves carry equal risk. Unstated: a cheap boilerplate leaf and an auth/migration/payments leaf get the SAME verification, which is simultaneously wasteful on the trivial ones and dangerously shallow on the load-bearing ones. There is no confidence score attached to a built leaf, no risk classifier that DIALS verification depth (more rounds, human gate, property tests) for high-blast-radius nodes, and no 'green but I'm not sure' state distinct from 'green and proven'. Green-ness is binary when trust should be graded._

**Boldest reframe:** The Insurance Market — leaves are underwritten before they can join the stitch, and the premium a separate underwriter model quotes (in token budget) IS the risk signal. The leaf nobody will cheaply insure is, by definition, the dangerous one, and the factory keeps spending verification until the premium drops to an acceptable floor. This is the boldest because it abandons the entire idea of a risk classifier — author-imagined categories, static tags, even self-reported confidence — and replaces it with emergent cross-model price discovery. Risk stops being a property anyone declares and becomes a market price that aggregates many models' fear, surfacing cross-cutting danger no taxonomy would ever have a column for.

- **Blast-radius tag frozen into every leaf's contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — When a leaf is created during top-down contract-freezing, the architect stamps it with a blast_radius enum (trivial / standard / load-bearing) right next to its typed signature — auth, migration, payments, money, deletes, and shared-schema touches auto-classify to load-bearing. Verification depth then reads that tag instead of guessing at review time. This is the single risk decision, made once at plan-freeze where the architect already knows the schema, and carried as a queryable field every downstream worker honors (the codebase's decision-log + node metadata already exists to hold it). **Why better:** Today the adversarial-loop table hand-waves 'reserve the full loop for code + irreversible work' with no machine-readable signal, so depth is decided ad hoc per reviewer. A tag set at contract-freeze makes risk a first-class, build-time field the dangerous leaves inherit on the FIRST pass, instead of learning a leaf was dangerous only after spending build budget on shallow verification.
- **Verification depth is a lookup on the risk tag, not a constant — encoded as a per-tier policy object** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Replace the flat 3-round cap with a declarative verification policy per risk tier (the way Temporal/Inngest attach a retry/backoff policy to an activity): trivial = 1 self-review pass, no human; standard = the normal Claude-build/Codex-verify single pass; load-bearing = unlimited rounds until proven + mandatory property/edge-case test + mandatory human gate + no auto-promote. The orchestrator reads the policy object off the node; the cap stops being one hard-coded number and becomes data. **Why better:** The current uniform cap is simultaneously wasteful (a boilerplate prop-drill leaf pays for a full cross-model round) and shallow (an auth leaf gets the same 3 rounds as a button), and bakes '3 rounds then escalate' into code for everything. A per-tier policy lets a trivial leaf stop early to save tokens while forbidding a payments leaf from ever auto-passing on round 3.
- **A graded leaf state machine: green-proven vs green-unverified vs amber (binning, like a semiconductor wafer test)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give each built leaf a confidence/trust state distinct from its build state, stamped into the manifest like a chip-fab bin: PROVEN/Bin-A (checks green AND an adversarial evidence artifact landed — property-tested, cross-model agreed), GREEN-UNVERIFIED/Bin-B (checks green, single-model, no adversary artifact yet), AMBER/Bin-C (built but the builder self-flagged low confidence, or the two models disagreed on approach). The stitch wave may climb over GREEN-UNVERIFIED on trivial leaves but blocks on AMBER or on any unverified load-bearing leaf — a Bin-C node blocks promotion of its parent until upgraded. **Why better:** Green-ness is binary today, so 'it compiles' and 'it's proven correct under attack' are indistinguishable on the plan-tree — chip fabs never ship a die as merely 'works', they bin it by tested margin. A graded state surfaces the 'green but I'm not sure' leaves the current model erases and lets the orchestrator and cockpit treat trust as earned rather than assumed.
- **Builder-emitted confidence score on every leaf handback, then adversarially re-priced** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Require each worker (claude -p / codex exec) to return a 0-1 confidence number plus the single sentence of WHY it's unsure ('mirrors an existing pattern' = high; 'novel concurrency I couldn't fully test headless' = low) alongside its diff. The orchestrator never trusts that number raw — Codex re-prices Claude's confidence and vice versa, and the GAP between self-confidence and cross-model confidence is itself the trigger for deeper verification. Low-confidence or high-gap leaves auto-escalate regardless of their static risk tag. **Why better:** The static blast-radius tag is set before the code exists and can't know the builder hit something gnarly mid-build; self-reported confidence captures that dynamic surprise. Re-pricing it cross-model turns the two-model router (already running review uniformly) into a cheap targeted signal — a leaf where the builder says 0.9 but the reviewer says 0.4 is exactly the load-bearing uncertainty uniform review drowns in noise.
- **Risk-rationed human gate that only fires on the load-bearing few** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Auto-classify reserves human checkpoints for only the highest-blast-radius leaves (the migration, the payments call, the irreversible delete) — these pause for an explicit approve before stitch, while everything else stays fully autonomous. The gate is rationed by risk, not sprinkled everywhere — ArtLab's two precise gates ('approve direction', 'approved for app') ported as a risk-triggered gate instead of either zero gates or a gate per leaf. **Why better:** A zero-human-interaction run will happily ship an irreversible migration on the strength of integration tests. A risk-rationed gate gives oversight exactly where a wrong autonomous leaf is unrecoverable, without breaking the zero-human-interaction promise on the other 95%.
- **Confidence and risk badges rendered live in the cockpit plan-tree** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The rebuilt Forge cockpit colors each node by (risk tag × confidence state) — load-bearing-but-unverified glows hot, trivial-and-proven goes quiet — so a glance shows WHERE the trust is thin, not just which leaves are red or green. A filter pins 'show me only the load-bearing unproven nodes', turning the cockpit from a progress bar into a risk map. **Why better:** A binary red/green tree hides the most important question — which green nodes are load-bearing yet only shallowly checked. Visualizing graded trust lets the operator spend their scarce human attention on the few nodes that can sink the build.
- **Run can't be declared done while any load-bearing leaf is below PROVEN** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Extend the definition-of-done gate (tower-ship-gate) so a green wave is only allowed to reach the root if every load-bearing leaf is PROVEN with an evidence artifact — trivial leaves may finish GREEN-UNVERIFIED, but the high-blast-radius set must be attacked. The final 'shipped' claim is gated on weighted trust, not on uniform green. **Why better:** tower-ship-gate treats all green as equal at the finish line, so a run can report 'done' while its riskiest leaf was never actually attacked. Making done-ness contingent on the load-bearing subset being proven closes the dangerous-shallowness hole exactly where it costs the most, without slowing the trivial majority.
- **Escalation ladder when a load-bearing leaf fails verification at the cap** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — For trivial leaves, hitting the round cap means 'record tradeoff and move on' (today's behavior). For load-bearing leaves the cap instead triggers an escalation ladder: add a second independent reviewer model, then a human gate, before it's allowed to ship or be abandoned. Risk decides what 'we hit the cap' MEANS. **Why better:** The current single cap-behavior ('pick best-evidenced option, record the unresolved objection, move on') is reasonable for boilerplate but reckless for a migration where an unresolved objection is a data-loss bug. Branching the cap behavior on blast radius keeps trivial leaves fast while load-bearing disagreements get more eyes instead of being silently shipped.
